# 道德真经注疏

**Daoist Classic of the *Dao De Jing* with Commentary**
**Title:** *Dao De Jing* with Commentary
**Original Attribution:** Attributed to Master Gu Xuan of Wu Commandery, though likely composed by a Song-era scholar.
**Eight volumes.**
**Source:** *Zhengtong Daozang* (Orthodox Daoist Canon), *Dòngshén Bù* (Mystic Treasures Section), *Yùjué Lèi* (Jade Precepts Category); Supplementary: Dunhuang fragment S.4430.

---

**Volume One of the *Dao De Jing* with Commentary**
**Narrated by the Wu Commandery recluse Gu Xuan**

**Chapter 1: The Dao that Can Be Spoken of**
**Commentary:** This refers to the Dao as expounded in the *Jing* for the cultivation of political and religious teachings.

**The Dao that Cannot Be Spoken of**
**Commentary:** This is not the Dao of natural longevity and immortality.

**The True Dao** (that cannot be spoken of) is to:
- Nourish the spirit with non-action (*wúwéi*),
- Keep the people at ease without affairs (*wúshì*),
- Hold the luminous within and conceal its brilliance (*hán guāng cáng huī*),
- Erase one’s traces and obscure one’s origin (*miè jì nì duān*),—
so profound it cannot be named or described.

**Exegesis:**
The term *Dao* signifies the principle of emptiness and fluidity (*xūtōng*), while *cháng* denotes the state of profound stillness (*zhànjì*). The **Dao of the Boundless** is the innate nature (*zhèngxìng*) of all beings. When it speaks of "the Dao that can be spoken of," it refers to **named teachings**—the methods that can be articulated.

Though it may be called "the Dao that can be spoken of," one must adapt to circumstances (*suí jī qiè dàng*) and speak accordingly, yet it remains merely a manifestation of sound and discourse (*yǒu shēng yǒu shuō*), not the **true, immutable, and eternally still Dao**.

The **True Dao** transcends named teachings and cannot be grasped by thought or reasoning (*bùkě míngyán biàn*). Its subtlety is unparalleled (*miào zhuó xīyí*), its essence elusive to the point of bewilderment (*lǐ qióng huǎnghū*). Thus, only beyond the realm of words and symbols (*zhī yán xiàng zhī biǎo*) does one truly align with the **Dao of immutable stillness**.

What can be spoken of and named is the **Dao that is not the True Dao**.

**The Name that Can Be Named**
**Commentary:** This refers to the **names of wealth, honor, and worldly eminence** (*fùguì zūnróng gāoshì zhī míng*).

**The Name that Cannot Be Named**
**Commentary:** This is not the **name of natural, eternal permanence**.

The **True Name** (that cannot be named) should be like:
- The infant’s unspoken innocence (*yīngxiōng zhī wèiyán*),
- The undivided egg (*jīzǐ zhī wèifēn*),
- The pearl still within the oyster (*míngzhū zài bàng zhōng*),
- The gemstone hidden in stone (*měiyù chǔ shíwèn*),—
where its inner radiance is clear (*zhāozhāo*), yet its outer appearance is dull as ignorance (*wài rú wán yú*).

**Exegesis:**
**Name** is **teaching** (*jiào*). Earlier, the Dao that can be spoken of was expounded with utmost clarity (*shèng míng yú lǐ*). Now, the Name that can be named is a secondary revelation through teaching (*cì xiǎn yú jiào*). Since the True Dao transcends words and symbols, and even teaching surpasses mere discourse (*shuōshì*), the essence of the Dao and its teachings are **one without distinction** (*bùyī bùyì*).

Yet the **Supreme Dao** is too profound to measure (*bùkě yá liàng*), neither void nor existent (*fēiwú fēiyǒu*), neither fixed nor eternal (*bùduàn bùcháng*), yet it holds **nuanced distinctions** (*yì yǒu yìyáng*), and **teaching** accommodates both gradual and sudden methods (*jiào cún jiàndùn*). Thus, the True Dao is posited to transcend what can be named, hence the statement: *"The Dao that can be named is not the True Dao."*

When discourse reaches its utmost, it rests in **the realm of neither naming nor not naming** (*wúkě wúbùkě*). Therefore, the *Yùjīng Jing* (Jade Capital Scripture) states:
*"Neither naming nor not naming, thought merges with the subtlest essence."*

**The Nameless**
**Commentary:** The **Nameless** refers to the **Dao itself**, which has no form and thus cannot be named. **The Beginning** signifies the Dao’s emission of *qi* and manifestation of transformation (*tǔqì bùhuà*), arising from the **Void** (*xūwú*) as the primordial source of heaven and earth.

**Exegesis by Master Wang:**
Though the Dao is **Nameless**, it yet **emits *qi* and manifests transformation**, emerging from the **Void** to become the **origin of heaven, earth, and all things**.

**Exegesis:**
**The Beginning** is the **Source** (*běn*). The **Void and Nameless Dao** molds and refines all things (*táoyǒng zhōngwàn wù*), from the **Two Poles** (*èr yún*) to the **Three Manifestations** (*sān jǐng*), all deriving from it. Thus, the **Nameless** is the **root of all things**, and though it can be spoken of, its clarity is evident.

**The Named**
**Commentary:** The **Named** refers to **heaven and earth**, which possess form, position, yin and yang, and softness and hardness—these are their **Names**.

The **Mother of All Things** is heaven and earth, which contain *qi* and give birth to all things, nurturing them to growth and maturity, like a mother rearing her child.

**Exegesis:**
The **Mother** has **form and traces** (*yǒu míng jì*). The **Dao of the Profound Void** originates as the **Nameless**, yet descending from its source, names and distinctions arise. Thus, the Sage establishes the **Named** to elucidate the **Nameless**, desiring to **nurture beings back to their origin**—through compassion and care (*cíbēi jūyǎng*), akin to a mother’s role.

**Master Gu states:**
The **Named** refers to **yin and yang**, while the **Nameless** is the **True Dao**. The True Dao has no substance (*wú tǐ*), hence it is **Nameless**. Yin and yang are differentiated (*yǒu fēn*), hence they are **Named**.

**The Beginning** is taken from its **timelessness** (*wú xiān*), while **Mother** is taken from its **function** (*yǒu gōng*). Timelessness renders the origin unsearchable (*běn bùkě xún*), while function makes its principle explicable (*qí lǐ kě shuō*). For yin and yang contain *qi* and give birth to all things, nurturing them to growth and maturity—like a mother rearing her child—hence they are called **Mother**.

**Constantly without desire, one observes its subtlety.**
**Commentary:** *Miao* means **essential unity** (*yào*). When one constantly remains without desire (*wú yù*), one can perceive the **core principle** of the Great Dao.

**Essential unity** is **oneness** (*yī*), which issues forth and manifests transformation (*míng dào zàn shū*), distinguishing right and wrong (*míng shìfēi*).

**Exegesis by Jiéshuò:**
This refers to **preserving the Void** (*shǒu xūwú*).

**Exegesis:**
*Miao* means **profound and refined** (*jīngwēi*). **Observation** is **illumination and scrutiny** (*zhào chá*). **Its** refers to **oneself** (*jǐ*). When one constantly remains without desire or action (*wú yù wúwéi*), in **absolute emptiness and stillness** (*zhì xū zhì jìng*), one can then **discern the subtleties of one’s own Dao** and **perceive the refined essence of ultimate principle**.

**Constantly with desire, one observes its return.**
**Commentary:** *Jiǎo* means **alignment** (*guī*). Those who constantly harbor desire (*yǒu yù*) can observe the **tendencies of the worldly realm**.

**Master Yu states:**
Humans are born in stillness (*jīng*), which is the **nature of Heaven**. When stirred by things (*gǎn wù*), they move—this is the **desire of nature**. If one constantly upholds **purity and stillness**, releases the mind and frees the spirit (*jiěxīn shìshén*), and reflects back upon one’s true nature (*fǎn zhào zhèngxìng*), then one observes the **refined origin**.

**Exegesis:**
When one cannot remain in **non-action and serenity** (*wúwéi tiándàn*), fails to **guard the refined and uphold the true**, but instead **arises with greed and indulges in worldly attachments** (*wàngqǐ tānqiú, sìqíng rǎnzhì*), one merely sees the **existence of worldly conditions** (*shìjìng zhī yǒu*), yet does not grasp the **emptiness of immediate existence** (*jìyǒu zhī kōng*). Thus, one fails to perceive the **refined essence of the Dao** and only witnesses the **alignment toward life and death**.

Earlier, the distinction between the **Nameless and Named** was clarified; now, the **superiority of those without desire over those with desire** is revealed.

**Master Zhang states:**
The **highest attainments** (*shàng dá zhī rén*) take and discard with equanimity (*qíngyí*), maintaining **purity and emptiness without desire** (*qīngxū wúyù*). Their **spirit and emotions** are illuminated and scrutinized (*shénqíng zhào chá*), thus they attain the **subtleties of the Dao**.

**The two aspects** (of desire and non-desire) **emerge from the same source yet bear different names.**
**Commentary:** The **two aspects** refer to **those with and without desire** (*yǒu yù wúyù*). **Same source** means they both arise from **human nature** (*tóng chū rén zhī xīn*). **Different names** signifies their **distinct outcomes** (*suǒ míng gèyì*).

Those without desire endure, while those with desire perish their bodies.

**Commentary:**
The observed realm is singular, yet the observing wisdom varies—
when these two modes of perception differ, their subtleties earn distinct names.
They are called **Dao/Way** together.
**Note:** *Dao/Way* refers to **Heaven**.

It is said that those with desire and those without share the same origin in **Heaven’s qi**.

**Commentary:**
*Dao/Way* signifies depth and boundlessness, also a name for non-adherence.
With two minds—having and not having—both trace back to the same **Dao/Way**, emerging yet differing.
This differing yet singular **Dao/Way** is called **depth and boundlessness**.
The boundlessness of depth: its principle lies in non-adherence.
Neither adhering to having nor to not-having—both non-adherence—
thus it is called **Dao/Way**.
**Dao/Way**, again **Dao/Way**,
**Note:** *Heaven within Heaven.*

It is said that the reception of qi varies in thickness—
those who attain **harmony and nourishment** become sages and worthies,
while those who receive **turbulence and impurity** become greedy and licentious.

**Commentary:**
Those with desire cling only to having,
while those without desire cling to not-having—
thus, **Dao/Way** is spoken of to dispel both fixations.
Yet fearing practitioners might cling to this **Dao/Way**,
another **Dao/Way** is spoken of to remove the later affliction.
Now, not clinging to clinging nor to not-clinging—
this is dispelling again, hence **Dao/Way**, again **Dao/Way**.

**Master Gu states:**
Though all receive qi from Heaven,
humans differ in elevation, and qi in purity—
Heaven is ever higher and purer, Earth ever lower and fouler.
Those who receive pure qi become the wise and virtuous,
while those who receive impure qi become the common and foolish.
The wise and virtuous are without desire, perceiving its subtleties.
The common and foolish are with desire, perceiving only its entanglements—
this is natural.
**The Gateway to All Subtleties.**
**Note:** To know that Heaven contains Heaven within it,
that the reception of qi varies in thickness—
if one can eliminate emotions and desires,
and abide in **harmony**,
this is called the **Gateway to Knowing the Dao**.

**Commentary:**
*Gateway* is the **Law-gate**.
Earlier, with the singular **Dao/Way** of the center,
the dual fixations of the two extremes were dispelled.
Once the dual afflictions are removed,
the medicine of the center is itself dispelled—
when medicine and affliction vanish together,
this is the utmost subtlety, exhausting principle and essence.
It is not merely the doorway of all sages,
but also the **Law-gate of all subtleties**.

All under Heaven know beauty as beauty,
**Note:** *To exalt oneself, making it manifest.*

**This evil is already present.**
**Note:** There is peril and ruin.

**Commentary:**
*Heaven under Heaven* is the comprehensive name for the worldly realm. *Beauty* means delight and affection. The *Sutra of the Upper Primordial* states:
*"All beneath the heavens, all above the earth—
within them dwell beings, called the worldly realm."*
It speaks of all living creatures, none who do not cling to dusty passions, falsely arising in beauty and ugliness. What contradicts the heart gives rise to resentment—this is called *evil*. What aligns with the will inevitably breeds attachment—this is called *beauty*.
Yet unaware that all dharmas are both existent and empty, beauty and ugliness being empty, what is there to love or hate?

Thus *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The fur-lined walls and lovely consorts—
what mortals call beautiful—
fish see them and dive deep,
birds see them and take flight.
Again, the beautiful deems itself beautiful,
but I do not know its beauty.
The ugly deems itself ugly,
but I do not know its ugliness."*
By this standard, where is there beauty or ugliness?
Thus, those in the world who cling to beauty as beauty—
this is itself the root of evil.

**Gu says:**
All beings know they indulge in the beauty of sensory objects—
the five desires, sounds, colors, and all dusty passions—
and this unceasing beauty brings suffering to the body.
Thus, this evil is already present.

**This goodness is already present.**
**Note:** There is fame and status.

**This non-goodness is already present.**
**Note:** It is what people contend for.

**Commentary:**
The vulgar masses, lost in true principle,
not only indulge in lust and color
but also covet fame and reputation,
pursuing name at the cost of life,
serving self while harming others,
unaware that fame and virtue are hollow constructs.
Thus the text below asks: *Which is closer to you, name or body?*
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"To do good without seeking fame,
and again, what they prize and spare—
nothing but fame and virtue."*
Thus, it is clear that clinging to fame and virtue
contradicts true principle.
Only by forgetting good and evil and abiding in the center
does one attain the Way of *wuwei*.

**Luo says:**
All beings, receiving form,
cherish their bodies and crave fame—
what they treasure is nothing but fame and virtue.
This "goodness" without goodness
only invites suffering.
Though fame may be attained,
what benefit does it bring to oneself?

**Being and non-being generate each other.**
**Note:** Seeing being as non-being.

**Commentary:**
The two names, *being* and *non-being*,
arise interdependently.
Pursuing principle to its end,
they are both empty in essence.
Once it is known that being and non-being generate each other,
it is clear that all dharmas are without substance.

**Wang says:**
Craving desire is the mind of *having*;
emptiness and stillness are the mind of *non-being*.

**Difficulty and ease arise together.**
**Note:** Seeing difficulty as ease.

**Commentary:**
With a mind of emptiness and wisdom,
there is neither ease nor difficulty.
With discriminating attachment,
there is both ease and difficulty.
Thus, the two dharmas of difficulty and ease
mutually arise and depend on each other.

**Wang says:**
To suppress nature is difficult;
to follow desire is easy.
To suppress ease and follow difficulty is good;
to abandon difficulty and follow ease is disastrous.
Disaster and perversity manifest outwardly—
thus it is said they arise together.

**Long and short define each other.**
**Note:** Seeing shortness as length.

**Commentary:**
To measure length by length reveals no length;
to compare shortness with shortness reveals no shortness.
Thus it is said that long and short define each other.

**Wang says:**
Self is length, others are short—
each is self and not the other.

**High and low topple each other.**
**Note:** Seeing high as low.

**Commentary:**
*Topple* means to usurp.
Where there is high, there must be low;
without low, there is no high.
How so?
Compare a *chi* with a *cun*—
the *chi* becomes high,
compare a *zhang* with a *chi*—
the *chi* becomes low.
What was once high is now low—
thus it is clear that high and low have no fixed nature,
mutually usurping, all empty.

**Sounds harmonize each other.**
**Note:** When one sings above, the other must respond below.

**Commentary:**
The five tones and bamboo pipes
harmonize to form music.
Pursuing their intrinsic nature,
they are all empty in essence.
By analogy, all phenomena are likewise empty and false.

**Before and after follow each other.**
**Note:** When one moves above, the other must follow below.**

「○Commentary: When one looks back from today to the past, today exists because of the past; when one looks forward from the past to today, the past is named because of today. Yet today is not truly today—how could it give rise to the past? The past is not truly the past—how could it give rise to today? Since there is neither past nor present, what is before or after? Thus, it is known that though there are before and after, the three times follow one another yet have no ultimate substance.

○Master Wang said: Beginning and end pursue each other, never far apart—like accumulating virtue yields blessings, accumulating vice invites calamity. Good and evil act in the present, while fortune and misfortune follow in their wake.

Thus, the Sage governs through *wuwei* (non-action).
**Note:** Governance through the Dao.
**Explanation:** This means naturalness.

○Commentary: The Sage is one who embodies the Dao and aligns with the True. "Sage" also means "rectified"—able to rectify oneself and, in turn, others. Therefore, they are called Sage. This Sage, with compassion and mercy, rescues all beings, upholding the wondrous law of *wuwei* to govern the desiring masses. The governance is near—referring to the ability to govern mentioned earlier, which belongs to the Sage. Continuing from before, it states: Thus, the Sage governs through *wuwei*, being still yet active, active yet still, still yet active, and through *wuwei* yet able to act.

Active yet still—this means, in the world, not abandoning *wuwei*. Thus, *wuwei* is indeed action, and action is indeed *wuwei*. How could there be distinctions between marketplace and mountain valley, or separations between bowing in silence and governing the world? Hence, it is said to govern through *wuwei*.

Practicing the teaching of *wu* (non-speech);
**Note:** Leading by personal example.

○Commentary: The wondrous embodiment of the True Source transcends speech and form. Though dwelling in stillness, its transformations are boundless. Not only does the True not contradict response, but speech does not hinder silence. Having emerged from speech and silence, their essence is one. Silent and composed, yet filling the world with influence—how could one say the Sage "does not speak" by sealing the lips? Thus, *Zhuangzi* says: *"One who speaks sufficiently can speak all day and exhaust the Dao; one who speaks insufficiently can speak all day and exhaust things."*

All things arise,
**Note:** Each moves spontaneously.
yet without complaint.
**Note:** Not repelling or obstructing.

○Commentary: All things are all living beings. *Action* means response and movement. *Beginning* means first. The Sage, without intention, responds to stimuli—like a clear mirror or an empty valley. Responding only after being moved, not acting before things, hence *Zhuangzi* says: *"Ever harmonious yet never leading."*

Bringing forth yet not possessing,
**Note:** The Original Qi gives rise to all things yet does not possess them.
acting yet not relying,
**Note:** The Dao’s actions are not done with expectation of reward.

○Commentary: *Relying* means depending on. The great Sage, with an empty heart, seizes opportunities to benefit beings, treating self and others equally, forgetting both self and others. Though achieving great deeds, they never depend on them, forgetting their accomplishments.

When deeds are complete, they do not dwell upon them.
**Note:** When deeds are done and affairs settled, they withdraw, not claiming position.

○Commentary: Covering and nourishing all things, their deeds reach heaven and earth, illuminating the people with brilliance surpassing the sun and moon—yet they attribute their deeds to others, not dwelling in their virtue.

Only because they do not dwell upon it,
**Note:** Only because their deeds are complete and they do not claim position,
is it that it does not depart from them.
**Note:** Their blessings and virtue remain ever-present.

This speaks of the Sage’s non-action, which cannot be followed; their non-speech, which cannot be known. The previous six lines—high and low, long and short—Master Wang opens one source, from which a hundred branches arise. The transformations of these hundred branches never fail to bring chaos.

○Commentary: *Fu* marks the beginning of speech; *wei* emphasizes uniqueness. Only the Sage creates and transforms heaven and earth, nurtures all sentient beings, achieves great deeds, yet attributes them to others. Who but the Sage could do this? Because they recognize their deeds, their great deeds are ever distant, their Sage virtue ever present—thus it does not depart.

Not elevating the worthy,
**Note:** The "worthy" refers to worldly scholars—eloquent in speech, skilled in texts, yet detached from the Dao, prioritizing form over substance.

Not elevating means not honoring them with rank or office.
**Explanation:** Master Wang says: Elevating the worthy creates distinctions; following distinctions sparks contention. Thus, the people do not contend.

**Note:** Not contending over fame and position, returning to naturalness.

○Commentary: This does not mean the ruler does not value the worthy. It is that people practice humility, placing others before themselves, not exalting themselves while belittling others. Speaking of "not contending"—if everyone exalts themselves while belittling others, then vain competition flourishes. If all retreat from self and prioritize others, then contention naturally ceases.

Not treasuring difficult-to-obtain goods,
**Note:** The ruler does not hoard treasures—gold abandoned in mountains, pearls cast into abysses.

Thus, the people do not steal;
**Note:** The ruler’s purity descends, so the people have no desire for noble things.

**Explanation:** This means evil *qi* does not arise, thieves do not enter. A single firm grasp dispels evil *qi*.

○Commentary: Difficult-to-obtain goods refer to treasures like Zhao pearls and Jing jade. If treasures are universally prized, thieves will arise. If treasures are universally despised, petty thefts will not occur. Hence, it is said there is no theft.

Not seeing what is desirable,
**Note:** Banishing *Zheng* music, distancing flatterers.

**Explanation:** This means *wuwei*.

Thus, the heart does not become disordered.
**Note:** No licentiousness.

**Explanation:** Ever maintaining the One.

○Commentary: What is desirable refers to worldly sights and sounds—objects of greed and attachment. The Sage does not see them—not because they block the ears, but because the wondrous embodiment perceives the emptiness of dust and phenomena, finding no true desirable objects. Exhausting the roots of dust, they do not align, hence no desire arises. Since there are no desirable objects, the ears and eyes wander freely, yet the heart remains ever empty and still. Thus, it is said the heart does not become disordered.

Hence, the *Xisheng Jing* says: *"Like shadows in a mirror—visible yet untouchable."* It also says: *"To see is to see nothing; to listen is to hear nothing."* The previous statements on non-contention and non-theft are specific; this statement on not seeing what is desirable is comprehensive, dispelling the three activities and all phenomena. It solely reveals the Sage’s empty heart benefiting beings.

Thus, the Sage’s governance—whether of the realm or of the self—
*○节解 (Jiejie)* says: *"Of the self, it means:*
To empty the heart, to fill the belly—
*○注 (Zhù)*: *to discard cravings and dispel distractions.*
To embrace the Dao/Way and hold to the One, to preserve the Five Spirits.
*○御 (Yù)*: *The heart, undisturbed by desirable objects, is thus emptied.*

*○节解 (Jiejie)* says: *"To empty the heart is to have no wicked thoughts.*
To fill the belly is to close the qi and nurture the essence.*

*○郭 (Guō)*: *When all evil is transformed and exhausted, all virtues arise naturally.*
To embrace the Dao/Way and hold to the One is to maintain purity and harmony within—
*○疏 (Shū)*: *Having no desirable objects without, and no desiring heart within,*
The heart and its objects both forgotten—
*○疏*: *This is the state of "heart without heart."*
Though the external realm be illusory and the heart be emptied,
Yet there remains a luminous awareness—
So it is said, *"The wondrous embodiment perceives emptiness yet responds without distinction,"*
And *"virtue is full within."*
To weaken the will—
*○注 (Zhù)*: *to soften, yield, and humble oneself,*
Not to seek power or dominance.
*○御 (Yù)*: *When the heart is emptied, the will naturally weakens.*

*○疏*: *Though the inner self is filled with wisdom,*
The outer self expands to meet all beings,
In the world yet unsoiled by dust—
*○疏*: *Gentle and yielding, retreating from strife.*

To strengthen the bones—
*○注 (Zhù)*: *to cherish essence and value qi,*
So the bones are full and firm.
*○御 (Yù)*: *When the belly is filled, the bones naturally grow strong.*
*○顾 (Gù)*: *Bones rely on containing essence;*
When essence is scattered, the bones grow weak.
Preserving essence and valuing qi—
Then the bones naturally strengthen.

*○床化 (Chuáng Huà)*: *The Sage, though outwardly demonstrating harmony and brightness,*
Remains inwardly ever still and undisturbed.
To make clear that movement does not harm emptiness,
And response does not depart from truth—
Thus it is said, *"To strengthen the bones."*

Ever keep the heart in ignorance and without desire—
*○注 (Zhù)*: *returning to simplicity and guarding purity.*

**○御 says:**
*To reject contention and curb the knowing,*
*To sever greed and extinguish desire.*

**○疏:**
*Knowing* refers to the distinctions of sound and name,
*Desire* is the label for avarice and seeking.
It speaks of how the Sage, ever empty yet benefiting the people,
Turns upside-down ones away from divisive thoughts,
Quells the vulgar’s greedy cravings.
Thus, those who know dare not act.

**注:**
*Deep in thought, speaking not lightly.*

**To act without action,**
**注:**
*Not contriving, moving only in accordance with the Way.*

**Then nothing remains ungoverned.**
**注:**
*Virtue’s cultivation is pure; the people dwell in peace.*

**○疏:**
Having already abandoned desire in seeking non-desire,
Yet fearing those without desire might stagnate in empty views,
The Way of non-action is spoken of—
Yet to say *"dare not act"* is to dispel non-desire.
Fearful of clinging to this *"not acting,"*
Thus it follows with *"dare not"* as well.

*Governance* means rectification;
If one but first dispels having-desire,
Then dispels non-desire,
This is the double-dispelling of the two extremes,
The wondrous embodiment of the One Way,
Where self and other merge,
And phenomena and wisdom both vanish—
Thus governance, with nothing left unrectified.

**The Way is void yet applied,**
**注:**
*Void—this means central.*
The Way conceals itself in name, its use lies within.

*A household says:*
*The Way’s use lies in its void and harmony—*
Thus it is called *void.*

**○饰解 says:**
*Void* means *one*—
That the One abides within, ever acting.

**Or it does not overflow.**
**注:**
*Or*—always.
The Way is ever modest, never full.

**○饰解 says:**
*This means guarding stillness.*

**○疏:**
The Sage’s transformations are manifold;
No words are more fitting than *"central"*—
Thus, *"The Way is void yet applied."*
This is to take *centrality* as use,
Yet to say *"or it does not overflow"*—
Toward the One Central Way,
Breaking the two extremes,
Once the extremes are removed, the One returns to dispelment.

Now, lest those who cling to teaching
Dwell in the One Centrality,
And take it as fullness—
To say *"does not overflow"* is to dispel the meaning of *centrality.*

**Deep, like the source of all things.**
**注:**
*The Way’s depth is unfathomable—*
Like the ancestral root of all things.

**○疏:**
*Deep*—still water.
To liken the Sage’s words:
Still water can reflect people,
Sage-wisdom illuminates all dharmas—
Thus the Master Carpenter takes his measure from still water,
All beings find their ultimate in the Sage—
Yet it is called *"like"*:
To clarify that there is no fixed source,
Yet the source is sourced.

Thus *Zhuangzi* says:
*"No one mirrors in flowing water,
But all mirror in still water."*
And again:
*"Still water is clear and deep—*
The Great Teacher’s ultimate essence."*

**To blunt its sharpness,**
**注:**
*Blunt*—to halt.
*Sharp*—advancement.
When human desires are sharp and pressing,
Seeking fame and gain—
This must be halted.
The Way does not reveal itself.

**To dissolve its anger.**
**注:**
*Anger*—resentment.
One must ponder the Way’s non-action to dissolve it.

**○节解 says:**
*This means not harboring wrath.*

**○疏:**
Having already emphasized the profound practice,
Self-benefit’s Way is complete,
As the teacher for all things—
Thus below, it clarifies benefiting others.
Realizing phenomena and objects are empty,
It calms those who press forward in seeking.
Yet to say *"dissolve anger"*—
*Anger*—bitter resentment,
*Solve*—to disperse.
Anger arises from defiance and compliance,
Defiance and compliance stem from deluded mind.
The Sage penetrates the emptiness of defiance and compliance,
The non-existence of deluded mind—
Thus able to guide the people,
Leading them back to true reality,
Dispersing their grudges,
Free from hatred and delusion.

**To harmonize its light,**
**注:**
*Though possessing singular insight,*
One must blend with obscurity,
Not to confuse others with brilliance.

**○节解 says:**
*The Sage’s Way is ever to harmonize divine radiance.*

**○疏:**
*Light*—the illumination of wisdom.
The Sage’s wisdom shines like sun and moon,
Yet conceals its brilliance, hides its path,
Aligning with ordinary understanding—
Virtue subtly pervades, not dazzling things.
Thus below, it says: *"Light yet not blinding."*

**To equalize its dust.**
**注:**
*One must share the vulgar’s filth,*
*Not set oneself apart.*

**○疏:**
*Dust*—the six defiling senses of color, sound, and so on,
Like the dust of the world, which can stain both pure and impure objects.
The laws of sound and color, too, can mar virtuous conduct,
Hence they are called *dust*.

Yet *equalizing* means this:
When the Sage descends into the world,
He hides his traces and conforms to the vulgar,
Having already concealed that radiant wisdom,
He also blends with this defiling dust.
Sound and color make no distinction,
Eyes and ears are the same—
Yet he remains untouched by defilement,
This is how he differs.

Deep and tranquil, as if something exists.
I do not know whose child he is,
Yet he precedes the Divine Prototype.
Heaven and Earth are not kind,
Treating all things as mere fodder for the ox.
The Sage is not kind,
Treating the people as mere fodder for the ox.
Between Heaven and Earth—
Is it like the vast expanse of a keyhole?
Empty yet unyielding,
Moving yet ever emerging.

Many words lead to exhaustion,
Better to hold to the Center.
The Spirit of the Valley does not die,
This is called the Mysterious Mother.
The Mysterious Mother’s gate—
This is called the root of Heaven and Earth.
Endless as if she exists,
Yet she is effortless to use.
Heaven endures, Earth lasts long—
Heaven and Earth endure so long
Because they do not generate themselves,
Thus they can everlastingly generate.

Therefore, the Sage places himself last,
Yet his actions are first;
He sets himself outside,
Yet his essence remains.
Is it not because he has no selfishness?
Thus he can fulfill his true purpose.

The highest virtue is like water:
Water benefits all things without striving,
Dwells where others abhor,
Thus it nears the Dao.
The Sage’s dwelling is virtuous,
His heart is deep like the abyss,
His conduct is benevolent,
His words are trustworthy,
His governance is effective,
His deeds are accomplished,
His actions are timely.
Only because he does not strive,
He incurs no blame.

Clutching and filling—
Better to let it be.
Prodding and sharpening—
Cannot be long preserved.
Gold and jade filling the hall,
No one can guard them.
Riches and honor lead to arrogance,
Only to invite one’s own misfortune.
When merit is complete and fame achieved,
He withdraws—
This is the Dao of Heaven.
Carrying the vital essence and embracing unity,
Can one remain unseparated?

**注:** *To focus the qi and attain softness,*
**注:** *To focus the mind and close the breath,*
So that it does not scatter,
Then the body can respond with pliability.

**能如婴儿乎？**
**注:** *Can one be like an infant?*
**注:** *Can one be as pure as an infant?*
**注:** *Can one be without inner thought or outer affairs,*
Then the spirit will not depart.

**○节解曰:** *This speaks of embracing the Original Qi without exertion.*
*The Great Dao flows freely,*
*Like an infant’s innocence.*

**○御曰:** *To be like an infant—*
*Is to make no distinctions.*

**○张曰:** *To focus the mind—*
*This is the breath of the Central Void, harmonized.*
**○张曰:** *To focus the mind is to be one.*
*Qi*—the breath of life.
*Attain*—to grasp.
*Softness*—harmony.
*Infant*—the cessation of knowledge and perception.

The breath moves eastward and life begins,
The breath scatters and death ensues.
Humans must focus the mind and cherish the breath,
Attaining the Way of softness and harmony,
Only then can they internally still the divisions,
Externally sever the knowledge of the Dao,
So that the body is not seen as the body,
And the body itself is extinguished.
The heart does not know the heart,
And the heart itself is serene,
Hence it is said: *Can one be like an infant?*

**○疏:** *Focus*—the mind’s essence.
*Qi*—the Dao’s breath.
*Attain*—to grasp.
*Softness*—harmony.

**政**—*virtue/power attained.*
**柔**—*harmony.*

It is only through focusing the mind on the Dao’s *qi* and attaining the principle of softness and harmony that one becomes like an infant, free from desire.

*Purify and inspect the Mysterious Vision*—
*Note: Wash the heart to make it pure and clear.*
The heart dwells in the Mysterious Void, inspecting all things—hence it is called *Mysterious Vision*.
Can one be without blemish?
*Note: Free from licentiousness and vice.*

**○疏:**
*Purify*—to wash.
*Except*—to dispel.
*Inspect*—to examine.
*Blemish*—disease.
To purge the Six Palaces and dispel the Five Emotions, so that the spirit-*qi* becomes empty and mysterious, one can then examine subtle principles. Internally and externally, pure and tranquil, without blemish or disease.

Then the body does nothing, the heart takes nothing,
Neither clinging to life nor rejecting annihilation.
Using this, how could there be blemish or disease?
This is the clarity of self-benefit.

*Love the people and govern the state*—
*Note: To govern the body, cherish the breath to preserve the spirit; to govern the state, love the people to ensure its stability.*

Can one be without action?
*Note: For governing the body, inhale and refine the vital *qi*, ensuring no sound is heard.
For governing the state, dispense virtue and benevolence, ensuring none below discern it.*

**○疏:**
Having already expounded the Dao of self-benefit, now one must turn to interacting with others. The first step in interacting is to govern the state and love the people.
*Know*—discriminating wisdom.
Compassion and pity, nurturing the people—this is loving the people.
Dispensing governance and cultivating transformation—this is governing the state.
The ruler must be sincere and unadorned, teaching the Way of non-action, blocking deceit and closing the path of discrimination. Thus, the *strike-the-clod* harmony may be achieved, and the *knotted-cord* governance may be restored.
The following text states: *"To govern the state is to eliminate its bandits, not to use wisdom to cultivate its virtue."*

*Illuminated and all-reaching*—
*Note: The Dao is illuminated like the sun and moon, reaching all four directions and filling the world. Beyond the eight extremes, it is unseen and unheard, yet radiant in all ten directions, dazzling and brilliant.*

Can one be without knowing?
*Note: None can know the Dao’s fulfillment across the world.*

**○节解:**
*Four-reaching*—knowing the eight directions.
Close the heart and block thoughts, enduring eternally.
If you can practice this, you will attain communion with the Ancient Sovereigns.

**○疏:**
*Illuminated*—capable of illumination.
*White*—pure and unadorned.
*Four*—the four directions.
*Reaching*—unobstructed.
The Sage’s emptiness and wisdom are illuminated, subtly penetrating profound principles, with no knowledge left unilluminated and no realm left unpenetrated. Thus, mentioning the four directions suffices to clarify the eight extremes.
Moreover, in stillness yet able to respond, this is the four-reaching.
Response does not contradict stillness—constantly abiding in non-action.

**○蔡:**
*Four-reaching*—recognizing body, life, and wealth.
*Without knowing*—the heart holds no discrimination.

*The Heavenly Gate opens and shuts*—
*Note: The Heavenly Gate refers to the Northern Pole’s Purple Micro mansion.
Opening and shutting refer to the five critical junctures of beginning and end.*
For governing the body, the Heavenly Gate is the nostrils—opening is panting, shutting is breathing.
**○王:**
Heaven has the Northern Pole star within the Purple Micro mansion, where the Five Virtues’ sovereigns successively reign. Thus, the Gate has opening and shutting.
Shutting is *Negation*, opening is *Prosperity*.
Spring sees the Green Sovereign’s Gate open, while the others shut—this applies to the three seasons.
The five critical junctures refer to the rise and fall of the Five Virtues, their cyclical succession. Each era has opening and shutting, each succession has *Negation* and *Prosperity*.
The Sage abides within this, ever guarding the receptive and tranquil, thus not swayed by the shifts of beginning and end.
For governing the body, the Heavenly Gate is the nostrils and mouth—opening is panting, shutting is breathing.
Cultivating life and guiding the people, the breath’s inhalation and exhalation must not be heard. Ever able to remain receptive and tranquil, one naturally attains the Way of non-action.

Can one be receptive?
*Note: Governing the body should be like the receptive female—tranquil, soft, and weak.
Governing the state should respond to changes, harmonious yet not leading.*

**○疏:**
The *Riverbank Master*’s text reads *Heaven and Earth*.
Heaven and Earth’s opening and shutting refer to the rise and fall of cosmic cycles.
The Sage blends into the two poles, unaffected by the three calamities. Though cycles rise and fall, the heart remains eternally empty and tranquil. Hence, *Zhuangzi* says:
*"When the great flood submerged Heaven, I did not drown.
When the great drought made metal flow and earth mountains wither, I did not feel the heat."*

*Nurture and raise*—
*Note: The Dao gives birth to all things and nurtures them.*

*Give birth yet not possess*—
*Note: The Dao gives birth to all things, taking nothing for itself.*

**○郭:**
*Yīnyūn* merging and transforming, all things arise.
Displaying benevolence while concealing action—this is the trace of *having-for-doing*, yet the merit is not claimed, hence *"not possessing."*

**○疏:**
The Sage, having benefited themselves through the Dao, fills others with virtue, thus able to give birth and nurture all sentient beings. Hence, the following text states:
*"The Dao gives birth to them, virtue nurtures them, and things shape them.
Though molding all things and refining the Three Talents, the true body remains empty—thus no living beings can be transformed."*

*Act yet not rely*—
*Note: The Dao’s actions are not relied upon for reward.*

**○疏:**
Since there are no living beings left to transform, acts of benefiting others are without expectation of gratitude.

*Nourish yet not dominate*—
*Note: The Dao nourishes all things, yet does not carve or control them for use.*

**○疏:**
*Dominate*—to rule.
The Sage nourishes all beings, truly their transformative sovereign, yet forgets merit and abandons the self—hence *"not dominate."*

**○顾:**
The Dao nourishes yet does not dominate; the Sage dominates yet does not sever.
Perfecting all things, truly the transformative sovereign, yet forgetting merit and abandoning the self—hence *"not dominate."*

This is called *Mysterious Virtue*.
*Note: The virtue is profound and inscrutable, unseen, meant to make the heart align with the Dao.*

**○张:**
*Mysterious*—deep and distant.
*Virtue*—the highest virtue.
Praising this self-forgetting Sage, obscured in transformation, truly profound and the highest virtue.

**Mysterious Virtue is also Heaven**—
*Heaven* is the Dao in its profound and boundless form.
It acts yet does not cling—
*It performs its function without attachment.*
This aligning with Heaven is called *virtue*.
*Note: The Sage’s virtue harmonizes with the Dao’s boundless nature.*

**○疏:**
*Mysterious*—deep and distant.
*Virtue*—the highest virtue.
Praising this self-forgetting Sage, obscured in transformation, truly profound and the highest virtue.

**Thirty spokes unite at a single hub**—
*Note: In ancient times, a chariot had thirty spokes, corresponding to the thirty days of a month.*

The hub has a hollow core—
*Hence, all spokes converge upon it.*
For governing the body, one must purge emotions and desires,
*Thus emptying the five viscera,*
So that the spirit may return to it.
For governing the realm, one must wield few abilities to command the many,
And use weakness to support strength.

**○严、顾等曰:**
Ancient chariots symbolized the thirty days of the month—
*The moon hangs in the sky, flowing without rest,*
While the chariot wheels upon the earth—
*Yet also carries without ceasing.*
Thus, ancient chariots were made to emulate the moon.
Thirty spokes unite at a single hub—
*Just as thirty days complete one month,*
So too do the many spokes depend on the one hub,
Illustrating how all teachings, though diverse,
Converge upon a single principle.
This one principle, though few in form,
Encompasses all teachings.
For governing the realm,
*When many opinions arise, the wise follow the few;*
Using weakness to support strength,
*Hence, the single hub—few in number—*
Commands the many spokes.

**○疏:**
The *Zhou Li·Kao Gong Ji* states:
*"Thirty spokes represent the thirty days,
Thus completing one month."*
When it is *without*—
*The hub’s core is empty,*
Hence it can bear the weight of all beings.
Moreover, the chariot is but a borrowed name for the myriad greens—
*The harmonized earth,*
Which, though dismantled and examined,
Reveals an illusion of form.
All the more so for every law—
*They too are thus.*
When it is *without*—
*There arises the chariot’s function.*
*Note: "Without" means emptiness; the hub’s emptiness allows the wheels to turn, the carriage’s emptiness permits the conveyance of people.*

**Molded clay becomes a vessel**—
*Note: "Molded" (涎) means harmonized; "clay" (坛) refers to earth.*
Harmonized earth serves as a vessel for food and drink.
When it is *without*—
*There arises the vessel’s function.*
*Note: The vessel’s emptiness allows it to contain and receive.*

**○节解曰:**
This refers to how ancient people shaped clay vessels without firing them—
*If exposed to water, they would crumble,*
And thus fail as vessels.
If one seeks the Dao yet relies on worldly methods—
*Entering chambers to refine the form at set times—*
Then one is a commoner, bound to perish.

**○疏:**
The *Kao Gong* says:
*"Molded" (涎)—harmonized.
"Filled" (填)—adhered.*
Harmonized water and clay, fired to form a vessel,
*Its emptiness within allows it to contain and receive.*
All the more so for the Sage’s empty heart—
*Thus able to embrace all things.*

**○张曰:**
Earth has the nature of a vessel;
*Harmonized, it becomes a vessel.*
Man has the nature of the Dao;
*Cultivated, he becomes the Dao.*

**Pierce doors and windows to make a chamber**—
*Note: Refers to constructing houses.*
When it is *without*—
*There arises the chamber’s function.*
*Note: Doors and windows are empty spaces,*
Through which people may enter and exit,
And within which they may dwell—
This is its function.*

**○疏:**
Piercing and carving doors and windows to make houses,
*The chamber’s emptiness allows habitation.*
All the more so for the Sage’s empty heart—
*Thus able to embrace all things.*
Piercing doors and windows,
*They permit passage and exchange,*
And all the more so for the empty, permeable roots and orifices—
*Hence, their use without obstruction.*

The three things—chariot, vessel, chamber—
*Their meanings are analogous,*
Yet they are repeated because:
*The chariot moves yet does not rest,*
The chamber rests yet does not move,
*The vessel both moves and rests—*
These three distinctions,
*Hence their repetition.*

**Thus, the "having" brings benefit**—
*Note: Things profit the form; vessels contain objects, chambers hold people, lest they be destroyed. The spirit dwells within the abdomen, lest the form perish.*
**○节解曰:**
This refers to the Dao.
The "without" brings function.

The annotation states:
*"The empty void can be used to contain and receive all things—*
*Thus it is said: 'The empty and formless can govern the formed.'*
*The Dao is the empty void.*

○ *Node Interpretation says:*
*'This refers to the Sage’s adherence to Oneness, acting naturally, and employing nothing.'*

○ *Exegesis:*
*'The "having" relies on the "without" for benefit,*
*The "without" depends on the "having" for function—*
*These two principles interdependently form the chariot and such.*
*The Sage must rely on both the "having" and the "empty,"*
*Using the empty to guide the having,*
*The having and empty mutually supporting,*
*Lest the heart incline to excess,*
*Thus achieving the Sage’s benefit and utility.*

○ *Master Gu says:*
*'Benefit (利) means advantage.*
*Within the chariot’s hub is the axle,*
*Within the vessel is food,*
*Within the chamber is a person,*
*Within the body is the spirit—*
*All these are things that bring advantage,*
*Hence it is said: "The 'having' brings benefit."*

*Yet the spirit’s advantage to the body,*
*The "having" within the "without,"*
*The "having" itself is invisible,*
*Thus it returns to the "without."*

*The spirit is the advantage for sustaining life,*
*The void is the means for nurturing the spirit.*
*This clarifies that the Dao is neither "having" nor "without,"*
*The "without" achieves function,*
*The "having" brings benefit to things.*
*Benefit to things lies in the "having,"*
*Function lies in the "without."*

*The "without" is clarity and emptiness,*
*The "having" is spirit and luminosity—*
*Yet common scholars fail to grasp this,*
*All following Laozi’s doctrine of complete non-action.*

*If the Dao were entirely non-action,*
*How could it benefit things?*
*Now it is clear that the Dao’s benefit lies in*
*Using form for advantage,*
*The "without" for function,*
*Thus containing all things through emptiness.*

#*End of Volume One of the True Classic of the Dao and Its Exegesis*
#*Original text missing; restored from common editions.*

「#2 **Qi/Godly Spirit***: Originally written as *shēn* (a variant of *shén*), corrected to *shén* (Godly Spirit), based on the Tang Dynasty Emperor Xuanzong’s imperial annotation of the *True Classic of the Dao and Its Exegesis* from the Daoist Canon.

**Volume Two of the True Classic of the Dao and Its Exegesis**
*Narrated by the Wuyun recluse Gu Xuan*

Five colors blind the eyes,
*[Note:]* Indulgence in lustful desires and vibrant hues consumes vital essence, dims clarity, and prevents one from perceiving the formless *color*—thus, the eyes grow blind.

**[Node Solution]:** It does not mean the eyes are physically blind, but that one’s own vision is obscured. Though they see only the *xuányuán* (the primal yellow of the cosmos) and the *jūyù* (jade-like radiance of the Dao), the brilliance of the Godly Spirit is lost, and the five colors’ dazzling glare obscures all else—so the eyes are blind.

**[Exegesis]:** The five colors are blue, yellow, red, white, and black.
It states that one cannot internally illuminate the true source of existence, yet externally pursues fleeting sensory realms. Though they see the illusory colors of the world, they remain unaware of the formless *color*—so what difference is there between blindness and this?

Five tones deafen the ears,
*[Note:]* Delight in the five tones scatters harmonious *qi*, preventing one from hearing the soundless *sound*—thus, the ears grow deaf.

**[Node Solution]:** It does not mean the ears are physically deaf, but that one’s own hearing is dulled. Though they hear only the clamor of bells and drums, they fail to perceive the Godly Spirit’s voice—so it is called deafness of the ears.

**[Exegesis]:** The five tones are *gōng* (yellow bell), *shāng* (major second), *jué* (minor third), *zhǐ* (major fourth), and *yǔ* (major fifth).
When the mind indulges in strings and pipes, the ears become clogged by the five tones, unable to return and listen to the subtle *sound* of the Dao—so it is called deafness.

*Zhuangzi* says: It is not only the body that has blindness and deafness, but the mind as well.

**[Wang Bi’s Commentary]:** If one is ensnared by them, then licentious sounds will harm the Godly Spirit and disrupt the true nature. When one’s speech is filled with lamentation and sorrow, it is the same as being deaf.

**[Gu Xuan and Shi Le’s Commentary]:** Though they hear only discordant clamor, it is still called not being deaf—yet they fail to recognize that the true nature of sound is emptiness, and thus are no different from the deaf.

Five flavors make the mouth insensible,
*[Note:]* *Shuāng* (insensible) is the same as *wáng* (lost).
Indulgence in the five flavors leads to reckless speech, straying from the Dao.

**[Exegesis]:** The five flavors are sweet, bitter, sour, pungent, and salty.
*Shuāng* means deviation and loss. When one indulges in rich delicacies and savors the rank, they cannot taste the Dao’s essence or discuss its mysteries—so it is called insensibility of the mouth.

Those who indulge in reckless pursuits and hunting,
Their minds grow deranged.

*[Note:]* The human spirit prefers tranquility; if one gallops and breathes wildly, the spirit scatters and dissipates—so the mind grows deranged.

**[Node Solution]:** It refers to the dead returning to the earth, their kin lamenting in sorrow, while the living rush about in haste, weeping and wailing. The departed are buried in tombs, the living scatter in the fields, the close kin are deranged with grief, the distant kin are sorrowful—so it is said that their minds grow deranged.

**[Imperial Annotation]:** Those who chase after worldly affairs, clinging to artificial actions, like hunters who seek only to slay, wage daily battles with their minds, racing after sensory realms—when they pause to reflect, they are struck by madness.

**[Exegesis]:** *Chíchéng* (reckless pursuits) is akin to *zǒuzòu* (rushing about). Hunters, driven by greed, chase after beasts, their hearts wild and unrestrained—like madmen. Those who invert the Dao’s principles, their desires chase after sensory realms, swift as galloping steeds, their minds as deranged as hunters.

Thus, *Zhuangzi* says: There are five ways to lose one’s true nature—one of which is when the five colors blind the eyes, preventing them from seeing clearly.

Hard-to-obtain treasures bring hindrance to one’s conduct.
*[Note:]* *Fáng* (hindrance) is the same as *shāng* (harm).
Hard-to-obtain treasures—such as the *Zhào Bì* (Bi of Zhao) and *Suí Zhū* (Pearl of Suí), precious gold, silver, jade, and pearls—when one’s heart covets them and their mind desires them without knowing contentment, then their conduct brings harm to their body and disgrace to their name.

**[Exegesis]:** Fine and beautiful things, when one clings to and indulges in them, become the cause of birth and death. Within the path of cultivating the Dao, they greatly obstruct progress.
Thus, the Sage holds to the *fù* (abdomen),
*[Note:]* Guarding the five *xìng* (nature/essence), eliminating the six *qíng* (emotions), moderating the *zhìqì* (intentional *qi*), and nurturing the Godly Spirit.

And does not indulge the eyes,
*[Note:]* The eyes should not gaze wantonly, for wanton gazing scatters vital essence outward.

**[Exegesis]:** Thus, following the previous text—
The Sage embodies the sacred precepts: whatever is within the abdomen, whatever is external to the eyes, they embrace the Dao and cultivate its *virtue/power*, filling their inner being. Therefore, they hold to the abdomen.
Internally, they perceive the formless *color*; they return to listen to the soundless *sound*. All sensory faculties are empty and pure, untouched by dusty realms—so they do not indulge the eyes.

Thus, they abandon the former and take the latter.
*[Note:]* Abandoning the eyes’ wanton gazing, taking the abdomen’s nurturing of the Godly Spirit.

**[Exegesis]:** Abandoning the ears and eyes’ blindness and deafness, taking the abdomen’s true wisdom.

Favor and disgrace are like sudden alarm,
*[Note:]* Both receiving honor and enduring humiliation bring alarm.

**[Imperial Annotation]:** When one clings to them, they are filled with dread; when one is bound by them, they are filled with sorrow. Not having set their minds to honor or disgrace, they are both alarming.

**[Exegesis]:** Favor refers to the name of glory and love; disgrace refers to the shame of punishment. *Ruò* (like) means *as*; *jīng* (alarm) means *fear*.
When one receives favor, they grow idle and joyful; when one suffers disgrace, they are startled and troubled in spirit—so receiving favor fills the heart with alarming joy, while enduring disgrace fills the heart with alarming dread.
Though joy and fear differ, they are both forms of alarm—so it is said that they are like alarm.

Yet the emotions of joy and fear are not the true nature—so those who attain the Dao liken poverty and wealth to the cold and heat of the seasons, compare honor and disgrace to the coming and going of fortune, and remain unshaken by death and life—what then is the power of honor and disgrace to alarm them?

Great misfortune is like the body itself.
*[Note:]* *Guì* (great) means *fear*; *ruò* (like) means *until*.
One fears great misfortune until it reaches the body—so it is alarming.

**[Exegesis]:** Great refers to noble rank, but also to one who forgets their body in their own nobility. It refers to afflictions such as worries, old age, and illness.
Common people only know to boast of their own bodies, yet fear great misfortune—unaware that great misfortune is their very body. They seek nourishment and sustenance, yet ultimately return to decay and ruin. So to fear the body is to fear great misfortune, and to fear great misfortune is to fear the body—both are rooted in the body itself.

**[Cai’s Commentary]:** Those who cling to the body are attached to their own form, unable to let go or forget. They worry over their body’s labor, fear its hunger and cold, and ponder its decay—this is great misfortune. Thus, it is known that clinging to existence brings suffering, and preserving the body is the root of all misfortune. Those who fear their own body are no different from those who fear great misfortune.»

Thus the *Xi Sheng Jing* says:
*"The body is the root of affliction—pain, itch, cold, and heat—
the origin of great misfortune, arising from clinging to the body."*

What is meant by *honor and disgrace*?
**Note:** *Honor* refers to exaltation and dignity; *disgrace* refers to shame upon the body.
This is a self-directed inquiry to awaken understanding in others.

Honor is lowly,
**Note:** *Honor and disgrace* are both base.
To gain it is like a shock,
**Note:** Those who gain honor in shock—
like one in high station fearing peril—
noble yet dare not be arrogant, wealthy yet dare not be extravagant.
To lose it is like a shock.
**Note:** *Loss* means losing favor and suffering disgrace.
*Shock* means fearing that calamity will return.

**Jie’s Explanation:** It means that when one attains the Dao, all spirits gather within the abdomen, murmuring and conversing with one’s children—knowing that the five spirits and vital *qi* flow freely, hence *"to gain it is like a shock."* To lose it is like a shock means that when *qi* is lost and essence depleted, the spirits stagnate and one part fails—hair turns white, teeth fall out, and death comes, while mourners wail and call the soul from the roof, shocked by the heavenly spirits—hence *"to lose it is like a shock."*

**Cushion:** The world is inverted, valuing honor as supreme, while wisdom sees it as base. Why? Because gaining honor brings startled joy, losing it brings startled dread—both shocks stem from the same *honor*, hence it is lowly.

This is called *"honor and disgrace as shock."*

**Note:** Explaining *"to gain it is like a shock, to lose it is like a shock."*

**Yu’s Commentary:** Honor and disgrace cycle—honor is the root of disgrace.
Ordinary minds are deluded, shocked by disgrace yet not by honor.

What is meant by *"fearing great misfortune as the body"*?
**Note:** Returning to self-inquiry—why fear great misfortune as the body?
My great misfortune arises because I have a body.
**Note:** My great misfortune comes from having a body—
to labor over it, to fear hunger and cold,
to indulge emotions and desires—
thus inviting calamity.

**Cushion:** This answers the prior question.
Clinging to the body, unable to forget it,
worrying over its labor, its hunger, its cold—
this is great misfortune.
Thus, those who prize the body are no different from those who prize misfortune.

Hence the *Xi Sheng Jing* says:
*"The body is the root of affliction—pain, itch, cold, and heat—
I am bound by the body, knowing it as great misfortune—
this is its reality.*
When I have no body, what misfortune do I have?
**Note:** If I have no body, attaining the Dao naturally,
lightly ascending to the clouds, entering and exiting without hindrance,
communing with the Dao and spirits—
what misfortune could there be?

**Yu’s Commentary:** If one knows that heaven and earth harmonize without being mine,
detaching from form and discarding intellect,
realizing the body is not the body,
uniting with the Great Way—
what misfortune could there be?

**Jie’s Explanation:** Forgetting the body to nourish the spirit is called *"no body."*

**Cushion:** *"No"* here means *sitting-forgetfulness*, losing the self,
enjoying the body while detached from form—
thus the body has no body,
not annihilation but transcendence.

Thus, those who prize the body as the world’s master
may temporarily rely on the world,
**Note:** Those who exalt their body while belittling others,
desiring to rule the world,
can establish themselves but not endure.
**Cushion:** Unaware that the body is great misfortune,
they cling to it, exalting themselves,
always seeking to dominate others—
such people may briefly dwell in the world
but cannot long govern it.

Those who love the body as the world’s master
may truly entrust themselves to the world.
**Note:** Those who love their body not for themselves
but as parents to all people—
if a ruler governs with this virtue,
they may entrust their body to the people above,
enduring without blame.

**Cushion:** *Rely* means temporary dwelling;
*entrust* means enduring commitment—
speaking of preserving and cherishing the body without reckless indulgence.
With humility to govern oneself,
yielding and following others,
they may transform and entrust themselves to the world—
hence the *De Jing* says: *"Love yourself but do not exalt yourself."*

That which cannot be seen is called *yi*,
**Note:** *Yi*—without color—
speaking of absolute uniformity,
unable to be perceived by sight.
**Cushion:** *Yi* means level—
the Dao’s subtlety cannot be grasped by the five colors,
hence it cannot be seen.
If it had color, it would be uneven—
only without color is it perfectly level.

That which cannot be heard is called *xi*,
**Note:** *Xi*—without sound—
speaking of absolute silence,
unable to be perceived by hearing.
**Cushion:** *Xi* means sparse—
its essence is not the five tones,
hence it cannot be heard by the ear.

That which cannot be grasped is called *wei*.
**Note:** *Wei*—without form—
speaking of absolute formlessness,
unable to be held or touched.
**Yu’s Commentary:** *Wei* means subtle.
The Dao has no shape, hence cannot be grasped.
Yet within its formlessness, it manifests—
thus it is called *wei*.

**Cushion:** *Wei* means subtle—
its essence is not material,
hence cannot be touched or held.

**Zang’s Commentary:** When speaking of *yi, xi, wei*,
it refers to *jing, shen,* and *qi*—
*jing* is the name for luminous wisdom,
*shen* is the name for unfathomable function,
*qi* is the name for form and appearance—
these three are unified as one.

The Sage does not see this *jing*,
does not hear this *shen*,
does not grasp this *qi*.
Since it cannot be seen, heard, or grasped,
it should be called *without color, without sound, without form*—
yet why speak of *yi, xi, wei*?

The Supreme Dao, though called *without color*,
is not utterly nonexistent—
if utterly nonexistent, it would be like the Great Void,
leading to nihilistic views.
Now it is clarified:
*Without color yet colored*,
*without sound yet sounding*,
*without form yet forming*—
hence it is called *yi, xi, wei*.

The so-called **Three-in-One**—
these three cannot be defined or explained.
**Annotation:** The three refer to *yi, xi, wei*.

That which cannot be defined or explained is *without color, without sound, without form*,
words cannot describe it,
script cannot transmit it—
one must **receive it in stillness**,
**seek it with the Dao/Way**,
it cannot be forced through definition or inquiry to be obtained.
Hence, they are merged as one.
**Annotation:** *Hun* means "to blend" or "to unite."
Thus, they are united in three names yet one.

**Exegesis:** When true and responsive, it scatters one as three;
when responsive and true, it blends three into one—
one and three, three and one, are not different,
hence it cannot be defined.

**Further Explanation:** This true and responsive duality manifests as the Three Vehicles,
the meaning of which is fully expounded in the *Opening Topic Scroll*.

Its uppermost aspect is **unfading**,
**Annotation:** Saying one exists in the heavens above,
yet its radiance is not **glittering and bright**.

Its lowermost aspect is **unobscured**,
**Annotation:** Saying one exists in the mortal realm below,
yet it is not **darkened and clouded**,
illustrating the Dao’s infinite reach above and below.
**Exegesis:** The Supreme Dao is subtle and profound,
neither foolish nor wise—
ascending beyond the Three Pure Ones,
it does not **augment its clarity**,
descending into the Nine Abysses,
it does not **add to its darkness**,
as it is said: **"neither increasing nor diminishing"**,
is this not so?

**Endless and nameless**,
**Annotation:** *Shéngshéng* means "endless motion."
That which is nameless is not a single hue—
it cannot be distinguished as **blue, yellow, red, white, or black**;
not a single sound—
it cannot be heard as **the six tones** (官商角征羽);
not a single form—
it cannot be measured by **length, shortness, size, or smallness**.

**Exegesis:** The Dao penetrates into the skin, flesh, bones, and joints,
hence it is said: **endless and nameless**.

Returning again to **the Great Void**,
**Annotation:** *Wu* means "substance" or "essence."
It must be returned to **that which lacks essence**.

**Exegesis:** *Shéngshéng* means "rectitude," akin to the meaning of *string and ink*.
The Sage, though able to **self-rectify**,
can also **rectify others**,
thus the endless and nameless cannot be grasped by name or sought by logic,
hence it is nameless.

**Further Explanation:** *Shéngshéng* depicts **motion and transformation**,
saying the Supreme Dao **turns and transforms** heaven and earth,
**molds and nurtures** living beings,
yet sight and hearing cannot trace it—
hence it is nameless.

Returning again means **reverting to the source**,
the Great Void is the **wondrous origin**.
When responding to circumstances and descending to guide,
it becomes **visible and audible**;
when reverting to its root origin,
it is **without name or form**.
Hence, it is said: **returning again to the Great Void**.

This is called **the form of the formless**,
**Annotation:** Saying one lacks shape and form,
yet it can **give shape and form to all things**.

This is called **the image of the substance-less**,
**Annotation:** Saying one lacks material essence,
yet it can **establish forms and appearances for all things**.

**Exegesis:** The wondrous origin is subtle and profound,
hence called **the formless**.
It leaves no trace of substance,
yet can **generate all things**,
hence it is called **the form of the formless** and **the image of the substance-less**.

This is called **dim and indistinct**,
**Annotation:** Saying one is **dimly present and indistinctly absent**,
neither fully here nor fully gone—
it cannot be described.

**Exegesis:** The Dao is **not fixed in existence or nonexistence**,
response is not **fixed in absence or presence**,
neither absent nor present,
yet **absent yet present**,
its presence and absence are indeterminate—
hence it is called **dim and indistinct**.

When **welcomed**, it is **not seen in its beginning**,
**Annotation:** One lacks **start and end**,
and **cannot be anticipated**.

「When one discards emotions and renounces desires,
one returns to the self—**the Way of Oneness** with one’s true nature.
**Annotation:** The *Dao* is the **form without substance**,
saying it is **comprehensible in appearance yet not in reality**,
for though it lacks material form, it manifests in response to all things.
If one were to say it **has** form, its shape is unseen;
if one were to say it **lacks** form, all things arise from it.

**Master Gu** says: Its coming is **unforeseen**,
suddenly present yet **unseen in its beginning**—
hence the saying, **"when welcomed, one does not see its start."**

**Annotation:** It is **neither fixed in ancient nonexistence nor in modern termination**;
**Annotation:** The Dao is **not ancient without beginning**,
nor is it **modern without end**.
One follows it and **finds no trace**—
it cannot be seen.
**Master Gu** says: The Dao **has no beginning**,
hence when welcomed, one does not see its start.
The Dao **has no end**,
hence when followed, one does not see its conclusion.
**Annotation:** The Dao is **not modern without end**;
**Annotation:** **Master Gu** says: Its departure **leaves no trace**,
mixed and boundless.

The **Sage holds the ancient Dao**,
to **govern all things in the present**.
**Annotation:** The Sage **clasps the ancient Dao**,
**the Way of Non-action**, to **govern the present world of action**—
thus returning to **primordial simplicity and purity**.
**Annotation:** *Exegesis*: **"Holds"** means **"preserves."**
The Sage **preserves the Dao of the Great Ancient**,
**the Way without name**, and **refines the present age**.
**Master Gu** says: Though ancient and modern differ,
the Dao remains **constant and enduring**.
Only those who **grasp it** can **govern all things**.
To **know the ancient beginning** is called **the Dao’s framework**.
**Annotation:** When one **knows the Dao of the Upper Ancient**,
**the beginning of the Way without name**,
this is called **grasping the fundamental principles of the Dao**.

**Annotation:** The ancient beginning is **the Way without name**;
if one knows **the beginningless and endless Dao**,
yet **both beginning and ending**—
neither ancient nor modern,
yet **both ancient and modern**—
and **applies the ancient Dao** to **govern the present world**,
one may truly be said to **grasp the Dao’s fundamental framework**.
**Master Gu** says: The Sage **preserves the ancient Dao of Non-action**,
**governing the present world of action**,
**calming the restless customs of the age**,
and **returning to the ancient simplicity and purity**.
**Refining and tempering** the transformations—
this is **attaining the Dao’s framework**.

In the ancient times, those who **excelled in the Way of the Sage**,
**Annotation:** Referring to **the Sage who governs the Way**;
**Annotation:** *Exegesis*: **"Ancient"** means **"of old,"**
**"excelled"** means **wise and virtuous**,
**"for"** means **to cultivate and study**.
The previous chapter **clarified holding the ancient to govern the present**,
and this **applies governing the present to draw upon the ancient**,
hence **citing those of old who cultivated the Way with wisdom**,
to **establish a model for the Sage**.

**Subtle and profound**, **penetrating the Mysterious**,
**deep beyond recognition**.
**Annotation:** The **virtue and power of the Dao** are **profound and distant**,
unable to be **known or discerned**—
**inner vision is blind**, **reversed hearing is deaf**,
none know **its extent**.
**Master Gu** says: **"For"** means **"affairs"**;
speaking of those in ancient times who **excelled in taking the Dao as their affairs**,
**even in slight and subtle words**,
the **profound and refined Dao** is **unfailingly discerned and illuminated**,
yet its **virtue and power are deep and vast**,
beyond recognition.
**Annotation:** *Exegesis*: **"Subtle and profound"** is **the wisdom that enables cultivation**,
**"penetrating the Mysterious"** is **the realm of what is cultivated**—
when **wisdom and realm converge**,
both **become profound**,
and **cannot be known by the mind alone**—
hence the admiration.

For **only what cannot be recognized** is **worthy of being described**.
**Annotation:** Referring to the following sentence;
**Master Gu** says: **Only the virtue and power of the Dao are difficult to recognize**,
hence **forcefully describing its appearance** to make it clear.
**Annotation:** *Exegesis*: **"Appearance"** means **form and semblance**;
**this Sage-wisdom, which cannot be recognized**, is **most worthy of guiding all things**—
yet **desiring to draw near and include all beings**,
one **forcefully describes the formless principle** as **the appearance of cultivation**.

**Cautious as winter crossing a river**,
**Annotation:** When undertaking any matter, one must **add weight and caution**—
**cautious as winter crossing a river**,
for the **heart finds it difficult**.
**The *Xiang Er*** says: **"Cautious"** is **like hesitation**,
**the bearing of movement and stillness**—
**always should one stand in awe**.
Winter crossing a river is **fearful**;
**to fear the four neighbors**,
**daring not to act wrongly**,
**for fear they know**—
this is how **those who follow the Dao and honor its precepts** **show humility**.

**Annotation:** *Exegesis*: **"Hesitation"** is **fear**;
speaking of **those cultivating the Dao**,
**fearing the world’s conditions**,
like **crossing a river in winter’s ice**,
**the heart trembles in dread**,
**fearing to sink**—
this **illustrates the purity of intent and deeds**.

**Like fearing the four neighbors**,
**Annotation:** Their **advance and retreat are hesitant and constrained**,
as if **one commits a crime**,
**fearing the four neighbors know**—
this **illustrates the purity of speech**.
**Master Gu** says: The **four neighbors** are **birth, death, old age, and sickness**,
also **the four demons**—
when one **does not uphold precepts**,
**delights in violating the law**,
the **four demons record it**,
hence one **must be cautious**,
**as if fearing others’ knowledge**—
**still and quiet**.

**Solemn as a guest**,
**Annotation:** Like a **guest facing the host**,
**solemn and without contrivance**.

「○Exegesis: *Yǎn* is the appearance of **dignity and solemnity**.
The *Records of Ritual* states: **"Solemn as though in contemplation"**, meaning the **sage** should **restrain and invigorate** the body and mind,
**not indulging in laxity**,
as if a **guest facing the host**,
**unable to be frivolous**—
this **illustrates the purity of bodily conduct**.

○Master Gù says: It is the **appearance of reverence and restraint**,
speaking of the **sage’s reverence and restraint**,
**doing nothing artificial**,
like a **guest before the host**,
**not daring to be careless**,
always **harmonious without clamor**.

**Dissipated like ice about to thaw**,
○Exegesis: *Huàn* means **dispersion**, *shì* means **extinction**.
It refers to **removing emotions and desires**,
**daily emptiness and voidness**.

○Master Yù says: Though **one is solemn as a guest**,
**doing nothing contrived**,
yet **not clinging to things**,
so **dissipated like spring ice melting**.

○Exegesis: As in **prior cultivation of wisdom and clarity**,
**delusions and stains disperse**,
like **winter ice encountering spring**.

○Master Sōng Líng says: Though **externally dignified as others**,
**internally ever harmonious and unrestrained**,
**no longer clinging or stagnant**,
**dissipated like frozen ice melting**.

**Sincere as uncarved wood**,
○Exegesis: *Dūn* means **genuine and thick**,
*Pǔ* means **formless and unadorned**—
**internally preserving spirit**,
**externally without ornament**.

○Exegesis: *Dūn* is **sincerity and depth**,
*Pǔ* is **plainness and simplicity**—
since the **three conducts** are **pure and cold**,
**delusions and burdens removed**,
one’s **virtue and actions become sincere and harmonious**,
**discarding superficiality for reality**.

**Vast as a valley**,
○Exegesis: *Kuàng* means **broad and spacious**,
*Gǔ* means **emptiness and void**—
**without moral reputation**,
**all-embracing and inclusive**.

○Exegesis: When **dust and burdens are exhausted**,
**the mind is pure and empty**,
so the **Dao is broad and vast**, encompassing like a valley.

**Mixed as turbid water**.
○Exegesis: *Hún* means **preserving the true nature**,
*Zhuó* means **not manifesting clearly**.
With the **crowd in uniformity**,
**not self-exalting**.

○Master Yù says: **"Blending brilliance and obscuring traces"**,
like **turbid water yet clear**.

○Exegesis: *Hún* is **unity**,
*Hé* is **maintenance**,
*Zhuó* is **artificiality**.
Though **the mind is pure and unadorned**,
**obstacles and burdens long dissipated**,
yet **in the midst of turbid confusion and artificiality**,
**it does not differ from dust and filth**.
**Turbid water, when still, gradually clears**.

○Exegesis: *Xú* means **gradual**.
Though **dwelling in desire’s midst**,
**engaged in worldly affairs**,
yet **untainted by defilement**,
**the heart ever tranquil**,
**leisurely and clear**.
**Though in motion, it remains still**—
this **illustrates stillness amid activity**.

○Master Gù says: *Zhuó* is the **appearance of dullness**,
meaning **one obscures wisdom and brilliance**,
**not allowing scrutiny**,
like **turbid water**,
**reflecting nothing**.

○Master Wáng says: **Hiding luminosity and concealing radiance**,
**externally indistinguishable from others**,
**mixed like waves and dust**—
thus it is said **"like turbid water"**.

**All seven matters above**,
are the **virtues of the ruler**.
The *Transmission* says: **"The ruler bears defilement—this is the Way of Heaven."**
**Rulers who attain the Dao** are thus referred.

If **discussing conduct in the world**,
*Hún* means **harmonizing with the crowd**,
*Zhuó* means **artificiality**.
Though **the heart is pure and simple**,
yet **in the midst of confusion and artificiality**,
**it does not differ from dust**.
As for **"turbid water, when still, gradually clears"**,
it **illustrates the heart**—
**when the heart is disturbed, cut off from its roots**,
**gradually it becomes pure and correct**.
**Tranquility endured long**,
**motion arises slowly**.

○Exegesis: **Who can endure tranquility long**,
**gradually prolonging life**?
Though **tranquil**, yet **stillness and motion intertwine**,
though **responding to things in motion**,
**the heart remains ever leisurely and transforming**—
this **illustrates stillness amid activity**.

○Master Wáng says: **When waves are mixed, turbidity arises**,
**when stillness is gradual, clarity emerges**,
**when agitation is extreme, death follows**,
**when tranquility is enduring, life sustains**.
**The wise know turbidity can be cleared**,
so **they harmonize with the waves** and then **transform**,
**they grasp danger’s potential for safety**,
so **they move gradually** to **nurture life**—
**none but those who embody the Dao’s good transformation** can thus be.
Thus it is said **"Who can?"**.
**Life and transformation are subtle and profound**,
**not achieved quickly**,
requiring **the heart’s singular focus**,
**accumulated diligence over time**,
**not seeking it daily and attaining it**—
thus **"gradual emergence"**.

**Those who preserve this Dao**,
**do not desire excess**.
○Exegesis: **Preserve** this **gradual emergence of life**,
**not desiring luxury, extravagance, or overflow**.

○Exegesis: *Bǎo* means **to uphold**.
Speaking of **those who uphold the unified Dao of stillness and activity**,
**they do not dwell in the middle while full and replete**—
this is **the Way of the Middle**.

**Only those who do not overflow**,
can **endure decay without renewal**.
○Exegesis: *Bì* means **to hide one’s brilliance**.
*Xīn chéng* means **precious and renowned**.
**Alone, the sages of the Middle Way**,
**within the decayed and turbid**,
yet **able to compassionately save all beings**,
**accomplishing great virtue**—
this is **the repeated completion**.

○Master Lú says: This **further explains the prior meaning of gradual clarity**,
**harmonizing with the worldly** and then **attaining stillness**,
**gradually preserving its clarity**,
**settling oneself** and then **responding in motion**,
**gradually ensuring life’s fullness**.

**Attaining emptiness’s utmost**,
○Exegesis: *Zhì* means **to reach**.
**Those who cultivate the Dao**,
**abandoning emotions and desires**,
**the five internal organs pure and still**,
**reach emptiness’s utmost**.

○Master Lú says: **Eliminating concerns and deliberations**,
**attaining emptiness’s utmost**.
Yet **the heart remains ever still**,
**steadfast in guarding tranquility**.

**Steadfast in guarding tranquility**.
○Exegesis: **Guarding purity and stillness**,
**conducting with sincerity and depth**.

○Master Lú says: **Emptiness’s utmost is the Dao’s fruition**.
*Dǔ* is **the Middle**.
Speaking of **those who wish to attain the Dao’s profound and empty fruition**,
must **still the heart**,
**guard the Middle Way’s singular path**—
then **it may be obtained**.

**All things arise together**,
○Exegesis: *Zuò* means **to generate**.
**All things generate together**.
**I observe them and see their return**,
○Exegesis: **I speak of observing all things**,
**none failing to return to their roots**—
**one should ponder deeply the importance of their origin**.

○Commentary: *Zuò* means **the dynamic function**—
**speaking of all sentient beings**,
**all engaging in taking and discarding**,
**chasing after external objects with greed**,
**yet the Sage’s profound insight sees them all returning to the wondrous root**—
**though in motion, they are still**.

○Master Gù says: **The still observation of their return to True Nature**.

○Master Wáng says: **The essence of perpetual generation**,
**its nature inherently serene**—
**yet when it receives and contains form**,
**the Six Emotions stir externally**,
**speaking and acting as they will**,
**reckless deeds invite calamity**.
**The Perfect One observes its original nature**,
**knowing it can be transformed and restored**—
**thus it is said: *‘I observe their return.’***

**All things flourish in abundance**,
○Commentary: *Yún-yún* means **luxuriant growth**—
