**each returns to its root**.
○Commentary: **Speaking of how all things**,
**though withering and falling**,
**each returns to its root and regenerates**.

○Commentary: *Yún-yún* is **the appearance of multitude**—
**Speaking of why sentient beings cannot align with the Sage**,
**though moving without motion**,
**acting without intent**,
**it is only because they are ever driven by restless striving**.
**If one can return to the root and origin**,
**then it aligns with the Sage’s illumination**—
**this is the exhortation**.

**Returning to the root is stillness**,
○Commentary: *Stillness* refers to **the root**—
**serene, yielding, humble, and lowly**,
**thus it does not perish again**.

○Commentary: **Only by returning to the origin**,
**does the restless striving cease**.

○Master Gù says: **Grasses and trees wither and fall**,
**returning to the root is stillness**,
**humans and things transform**,
**returning to True Nature is tranquility**.
**This is called *‘restoring the Mandate.’***

○Commentary: **Speaking of tranquility**,
**it is restoring the true nature and wisdom-life**,
**having cast aside the clamor of dust**,
**the heart and spirit concentrated in stillness**,
**thus returning to True Nature**,
**restoring the wisdom-life**.

**Restoring the Mandate is constant**,
○Commentary: **Restoring the Mandate to prevent death**,
**is the Dao’s eternal practice**.

○Commentary: **Returning to the nature and Mandate**,
**doubtlessly concentrated and serene**,
**no longer subject to birth and death**,
**thus it is called *‘constant.’***

○Master Hé Yàn says: **Restoring the Mandate should make one immortal**,
**this is the Dao’s eternal principle**.

○Master Lú says: **Those who understand the constant principle of restoring the Mandate**,
**are those who see the Dao’s clarity**.

**Knowing the constant is clarity**,
○Commentary: **Those who know the Dao’s eternal practice**,
**are called *‘clear.’***

○Master Yù says: **Guarding stillness and restoring the Mandate**,
**can be called *‘constant.’***
**Those who know how to guard the constant**,
**are even more clearly enlightened**.

○Commentary: **Having known the principle of returning to the true and constant**,
**wisdom illuminates, revealing nothing hidden**.

○Master Wáng says: **The constant Dao is difficult to know**,
**thus knowing it is called *‘clarity.’***
**Not knowing the constant, reckless action brings misfortune**.

○Commentary: **Not knowing the Dao’s eternal practice**,
**while fabricating deceitful schemes**,
**loses one’s divine insight**—
**thus misfortune**.

○Commentary: **Not knowing how to cultivate the nature**,
**return to virtue**,
**and align with the true and constant Dao**,
**then one constantly arises with delusive thoughts**,
**creates karma in response to phenomena**,
**moving toward the path of death**—
**all actions bring misfortune**.

○Master Gù says: **Not knowing**—
**speaking of knowing yet acting recklessly**,
**those who act recklessly lose the Dao**—
**thus misfortune**.

○Master Sūn says: **Unable to guard stillness**,
**the Heavenly Mandate loses its constancy**—
**this is the source of misfortune**.

**Knowing the constant is tolerance**,
○Commentary: **Those who know the Dao’s eternal practice**,
**can abandon passions and desires**,
**embracing all without exclusion**.

○Master Yù says: **Knowing the true and constant Dao**,
**one must utterly attain emptiness and stillness**,
**like a boundless valley**,
**containing all without limit**.

○Commentary: **The body attains the concentrated constancy of the Middle Way**,
**awakening to the dual emptiness of conformity and deviation**,
**thus able to contain all things**.

**Tolerance is impartiality**,
○Commentary: *Impartiality*—**equitable and just**,
**since one contains all things without partiality**,
**no evil can withstand it**.

○Master Yù says: **Embracing all things impartially**,
**responding to all things without bias**,
**since there is no private malice**,
**thus it is impartial and just**.

○Commentary: *Impartiality*—**equitable and upright**.
**Since one contains all things**,
**thus it is impartial and just without favor**.

**Impartiality is sovereignty**,
○Commentary: **Impartial and just without bias**,
**one can rule the world**,
**rectifying the body makes the form unified**,
**the spirit myriad**—
**together harmonizing within oneself**.

○Master Yù says: **If one is impartial and just without bias**,
**all things will turn toward them**.

○Commentary: *Sovereignty*—**to be followed**.
**Only by containing all things**,
**being impartial and just without bias**,
**can one establish the great law**,
**becoming the refuge for all living beings**.

**Sovereignty is Heaven**,
○Commentary: **Having become the sovereign ruler**,
**transforming all things**,
**one covers and nurtures all sentient beings**,
**aligning with the boundless sky**.

**Heaven is the Dao**,
○Commentary: **Having aligned with the great law**,
**covering all things equally**,
**and mirroring the Dao’s boundless transformation**.

**The Dao is longevity**,
○Commentary: **Aligning with the Dao**,
**equal in measure to the boundless void**,
**no coming, no going**,
**the heart merging with the supreme Dao**,
**neither extinction nor generation**—
**since this aligns with the principle**,
**it is said to be *‘longevity.’***

**Longevity means no peril in death**,
○Commentary: **Death**—**extinction**.
**Peril**—**danger**.
**Having attained sovereignty and impartiality**,
**harmonizing with Heaven and the Dao**,
**the four virtues complete**,
**virtue and the Way vast and enduring**,
**no calamity, no blame**,
**dying with Heaven and Earth**,
**yet never in danger**.

「Hiding the origin and erasing the traces,
is called *‘segmenting the body’*。
○Commentary: **Responding to sensations and seizing opportunities**,
is called *‘no peril’*.
○Further Explanation: **Traces** have rise and fall,
thus *‘segmenting the body’*—**the body itself is extinguished**,
since there is originally no birth or death,
thus *‘no peril’* also.

**The Supreme Sovereign**,
the lower knows of His existence;
○Commentary: **The Supreme Sovereign** refers to the nameless Lord of primordial antiquity.
The lower knows of His existence—**the lower knows there is a Sovereign above**,
yet sees no trace of his subjects’ service,
pure and unadorned in simplicity.
○Exalted Explication: **The Supreme Sovereign** is the ancient, pure Lord.
The lower knows—**his subjects know a Sovereign exists above**,
revering him like Heaven,
yet without the traces of deliberate governance and action,
thus the people lack virtue yet still honor him.
○Commentary: **This Supreme Sovereign** is the present **Lord of the Dao of the Mysterious Heaven** in the **Jade Capital** above.
The **Lord of the Dao** abides in the **Golden Gate** within,
concentrating his spirit in distant contemplation,
as the constant place of response.
Those of keen insight,
with clear and sensitive faculties,
awaken to the wondrous, primordial stillness,
transcending form and name,
descending from the origin to manifest traces,
thus he bears titles,
yet does not cling to the illusory generation of forms,
hence the lower knows of his existence—
they know of the **Supreme Sovereign’s** title by origin.

**The next level**, they are close to him and praise him;
○Commentary: **His virtue is visible**, his benevolence and favors can be spoken of,
thus they are close and praise him.
○Exalted Explication: **When virtue is pure yet declining**,
the Sovereign governs with good teachings,
his kindness is seen, hence they are close to him,
his achievements are high, hence they praise him.
○Commentary: **Those of moderate insight**,
with somewhat dull faculties,
cannot forget words to attain principles,
must cling to forms and study methodically,
thus they immerse themselves in scriptures and teachings,
cling to what is close and praise what is apparent,
generating interpretations from texts and shared conventions.
○Reflection: **This is the age of antiquity’s middle phase**,
growing distant from the Dao,
fixating on words and clinging to forms—
its virtue is still visible.
When they are close and praise above,
then below they trust and believe in him,
below then praises and honors him above—
his deeds can be spoken of,
thus songs of joy resound on the roads.

**The next level**, they fear him;
○Commentary: **He establishes forms and laws to govern them**.
○Exalted Explication: **When virtue further declines**,
the Sovereign rules with many flaws,
the people cannot bear his commands,
thus he drives them with punishments and retribution,
hence they fear him.
○Reflection: **Those of dull faculties**,
with hearts deceived by illusions,
cannot discern clearly,
thus they mock and slander.
○Commentary: **Mockery**—**deception**.
Those of low faculties,
with dull and coarse natures,
indulge their hearts in chasing sensory objects,
their entanglements deepen daily.
They only fear worldly authority and punishments,
not the underworld’s scrutiny and judgment,
thus they mock and slander the sacred words,
destroying faith and disbelief—
hence the scripture says: *‘The lower hear the Dao and laugh at it.’*

When trust is insufficient,
○Commentary: **The Sovereign’s trust is insufficient toward the lower**,
the lower then has deceitful and cunning people.
○Exalted Explication: **The Sovereign’s trust is insufficient toward the lower**,
the lower then responds with disbelief,
and deceives their Sovereign.
○Reflection: **Those who fear and mock him**,
all stem from the Sovereign’s insufficient trust,
thus the lower has people who do not believe.

○Commentary: **It is because their faculties are low and their obstacles heavy**,
their trust in the Dao is insufficient,
thus they doubt and slander the sacred texts,
incurring the crime of disbelief.

**Yet they still value words**,
○Commentary: **Speaking of the Supreme Sovereign’s governance**,
his actions are still held in higher regard than words,
fearing he may stray from the Dao and lose naturalness**.

○Commentary: **They still value worldly, superficial, and false words**,
thus they do not believe in the Dao’s true and authentic teachings,
hence their confusion deepens daily,
and their crimes and obstacles grow ever greater.

When deeds are accomplished and affairs fulfilled,
○Commentary: **This means the world is at great peace**.
The people say *‘it is natural for me.’*
○Exalted Explication: **When deeds are done yet not grasped**,
affairs fulfilled yet without action,
the people use them daily without knowing,
saying *‘it is natural that I have accomplished and fulfilled them.’*

○Commentary: **When the functions of punishment and relief are fulfilled**,
the nurturing of life and affairs is complete,
yet the supreme virtue subtly permeates,
success not claimed—
the people use them daily without knowing,
all say *‘it is natural that I have attained such things,’*
unaware of the sacred grace bestowed.

○Reflection: **Transformation does not come through words**,
the silent merit subtly covers all,
each thing roams freely,
beating earthenware in self-awakened joy,
using them daily without knowing,
rejoicing in reliance without master,
unaware of who has wrought this—
thus they all say *‘it is natural for me.’*

When the **Great Dao is abandoned**,
**there arises benevolence and righteousness**.
○Commentary: **In the age of the Great Dao**,
each family has filial children,
each household has loyal and trustworthy people—
benevolence and righteousness are unseen.
When the **Great Dao is abandoned and not practiced**,
evil and rebellious deeds arise together,
then **benevolence and righteousness** can be transmitted as the **Dao**.

○Exalted Explication: **When purity is diluted and simplicity dispersed**,
the **Great Dao does not prevail**,
daily **benevolence and righteousness** emerge,
small achievements arise and are completed,
yet **purity is diluted, simplicity dispersed**,
elaborate flaws spring from deliberate action.

○Commentary: **This is the abandonment of the ancient teachings**.
Speaking of the **Great Dao’s** age,
the five virtues are not manifest,
**benevolence and righteousness** arise,
**purity and simplicity** then decay—
hence *Zhuangzi* says:
*‘Dispersing virtue and the Dao to create benevolence and righteousness is the fault of the sages.’*
And again:
*‘They rely on one another to moisten, they support one another with oil—
it is not as good as forgetting one another in the rivers and lakes.’*

When wisdom emerges,
**there arises great falsehood**.
○Commentary: **The wise Sovereign**,
devalues virtue while valuing words,
devalues substance while valuing ornament—
the lower then responds by creating **great falsehood and deceit**.

○Exalted Explication: **Using wisdom**,
he will establish laws.
When laws emerge, deceit arises,
thus there is **great falsehood**.

○Commentary: **Wisdom**—**sacred wisdom**.
The upper uses **sacred wisdom** to govern things,
laws and decrees multiply,
the lower then employs countless deceits to evade the net of punishments,
stealing **sacred wisdom** to fashion false instruments—
the harm of wisdom is fully shown in *Zhuangzi’s* texts.

○Reflection: **In the age of the Great Dao**,
there is no knowledge, no desire,
all things cherish virtue,
external use unseen—
when the Dao’s merit ceases,
deceit then flourishes.
Wielding wisdom startles the dull,
displaying talent moves the common,
**purity disperses, simplicity departs**,
deceitful emotions arise together—
thus it is said: **great falsehood**.

「When the six bonds of kinship are fractured,
**filial piety and benevolence** arise.
**Explication:** The six bonds of kinship severed,
then relatives and kin fall out of harmony,
and thus **filial piety and benevolence** emerge to nurture one another.

○**御 (Yu) says:** All stem from losing the Dao,
so there are **partial names** in its place.

○**疏 (Shu) says:** The six bonds of kinship—
father and son, brothers, husband and wife.
In a world of **purity and harmony**,
partial affection is not evident.
When the six bonds fall into disorder,
**filial piety and benevolence** then stand out,
hence in the age when Zhou’s **virtue/power** declined,
**Jiao (浇, King Jie of Xia)** arose in competition,
and **Zeng Min (曾闵, Zeng and Min)** were praised for their lofty conduct.

When a state is in chaos,
**loyal ministers** appear.
**Explication:** When laws and decrees fail,
superior and inferior blame each other,
evil factions vie for power,
and thus **loyal ministers** arise to rectify their ruler.

This is to say:
In the age of the **Great Dao**,
there is no knowledge of **ren (仁)** and **yi (义)**—
people are naturally without desire for propriety,
yet they remain inherently clean;
no need for **zheng (贞, integrity)**,
yet they are naturally upright;
no need for **zhong (忠, loyalty)**,
yet they keep their word.

In the **Great Dao’s** time,
**ren (仁)** and **yi (义)** vanish,
**filial piety and benevolence** fade away—
just as at noon, when the sun is brightest,
all stars lose their luster.

○**疏 (Shu) says:** The sovereign of myriad chariots,
holding the four seas as his home,
thus when a state is only well-governed,
loyalty and sincerity remain unseen.
In times of chaos,
**upright integrity** then shines forth—
hence **Long Feng (龙逢)** was renowned for his loyalty under **King Jie of Xia (夏桀)**,
and **Bi Gan (比干)** was praised for his virtue under **King Zhou of Shang (殷纣)**.

**Discarding sacred wisdom**,
**Explication:** Discarding the **sacred wisdom** of rulers,
returning to the original, guarding the root—
the Five Emperors (五帝) drew symbols,
**Cang Jie (仓颉)** created writing,
yet this was not as good as the Three Sovereigns (三皇) using knotted cords without script.

**Abandoning cleverness**,
**Explication:** Abandoning **cleverness**,
returning to **non-action (无为)**.
The people’s benefits multiply a hundredfold;
**Explication:** Agriculture prospers,
public affairs are without private gain.

○**御 (Yu) says:** Discarding the **sacred wisdom** of rulers,
then their teachings fade away,
and **transformation (化)** is smooth without contrivance.
Abandoning the **common folk’s** cunning and deceitful schemes,
people return to **purity and simplicity**.
When people return to **purity and simplicity**,
artifice does not arise,
**self-aggrandizement and partiality** cease.
People in harmony with **Heaven’s Dao**,
things lose no part of their nature—
thus there is **a hundredfold increase in benefits**.

○**疏 (Shu) says:** Discarding the **sacred wisdom** of named sages,
abandoning discriminating cleverness,
people all return to their roots,
their benefits become boundless—
*a hundredfold* is but an approximation of the great number.
Hence *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Rui’s wine was thin, yet Han Dan (邯郸) was besieged—
when sages arise, great thieves appear."*
And again:
*"Tian Chengzi (田成子) stole a kingdom using sacred wisdom."*

**Discarding ren and abandoning yi**,
**Explication:** Discarding **ren (仁)**’s display of favor,
abandoning **yi (义)**’s emphasis on empty words—
the people return to **filial piety and benevolence**;
**Explication:** **Virtue/power**’s transformation is pure.

○**御 (Yu) says:** Discarding **ren (仁)** as universal love,
abandoning **yi (义)**’s rejection of material gain,
then people return to **great filial piety and benevolence**.

○**疏 (Shu) says:** Discarding **ren (仁)**’s partial inclinations,
abandoning **yi (义)**’s rigid adherence to forms,
people all follow their true nature,
without artificiality or pretension.
**Filial piety** springs from **Heaven’s natural order**,
**benevolence** flows from spontaneous virtue—
returning to **purity and simplicity of antiquity**,
hence it is said the people **return to their original state**.
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"Ren and yi are the decayed huts and straw dogs of former kings."*
And again:
*"If you eliminate Zeng and Shi’s (曾史) conduct,
the world will all be ren."*

**Discarding cleverness and abandoning profit**,
**Explication:** Discarding clever deceit,
sealing the path of greed,
closing the door to power struggles—
thieves and robbers will not exist;
**Explication:** When the upper echelons are fair and just,
the lower levels have no private or evil schemes.

○**御 (Yu) says:** If one **boasts of partial skill**,
there will inevitably be **contention for profit**,
thus discarding cleverness means people no longer contend,
abandoning profit means people find self-sufficiency.
When self-sufficient, they will not become thieves.

○**疏 (Shu) says:** Discarding **cleverness** from divergent paths,
abandoning **profit-seeking**,
each thing guards its proper measure—
thus there is no theft or robbery.
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"If a carpenter spits on his fingers,
the world will all be clever."*

These three cases,
**Explication:** Referring to the three matters above
that should be discarded.

The text is insufficient in its teachings.
**Explication:** It is insufficient because the teachings cannot guide the people.

○**Shu says:** These three matters refer to the three prior absolutions. This text is a sudden teaching of the Great Vehicle, the study of the superior ones, whose principles are profound and distant, insufficient to instruct those of lower capacity. Therefore, it is ordered to have its proper associations.

**Explication:** As in the following sentence.

○**Yu says:** These three matters merely require abandonment; they do not yet demonstrate practice. Thus, it is said that this text is insufficient for instruction, and further associations are ordered—referring to the following passages.

○**Shu says:** *Association* means to entrust. The people of lower capacity cannot yet bear the Great Vehicle’s teachings, so they must first have associations to arouse their learning. The associated text is the four lines below:

Seeing simplicity, embracing sincerity,
**Explication:** *Seeing simplicity* means to see and guard simplicity, not to adorn with artifice.

*Embracing sincerity* means to embrace one’s natural simplicity and demonstrate it to the world, so it may be emulated.

Few in private,
**Explication:** To be upright and without self-interest.

Few in desires,
**Explication:** To know contentment.

○**Shu says:** *Seeing simplicity*—rejecting ornamentation. *Embracing sincerity*—returning to reality. *Few in private*—uprightness and justice. *Few in desires*—quelling greed.

Abandoning learning,
**Explication:** Abandoning learning that is not true or does not conform to the Dao’s teachings.

Without worry.
**Explication:** Removing superficiality, then there is no worry or trouble.

○**Jie Jie says:** This means abandoning all scriptures and returning to the One.

○**Yu says:** Abandoning the learning of contrivance, then one becomes pure and simple.

○**Shu says:** *Worry*—trouble. Abandoning the learning of relative forms, one attains the principle of non-action, and thus troubles are exhausted, so there is no worry.

How far apart are *yes* and *ah*?
**Explication:** Both are responses, yet how far apart they are—criticizing the times for valuing superficiality and demeaning substance.

○**Shu says:** *Yes*—respectful assent. *Ah*—a lazy response. The *Records of Rites* says: When the teacher summons, do not answer. *How far apart*—not distant, but people do not understand the distinction between compliance and defiance, recklessly generating joy or anger. Hearing *yes* brings joy; hearing *ah* brings disdain, unaware that *yes* and *ah* issue from the same mouth, not far apart.

How far apart are good and evil?
**Explication:** *Good* is praise, *evil* is slander—how different are they? The times detest the loyal and upright, favoring deceitful flatterers.

○**Shu says:** Conforming to desire is good; opposing the heart is evil. Since compliance and defiance are both empty, beauty and ugliness are meaningless. Moreover, *yes* and *ah* arise from one mouth, while good and evil stem from one heart. Those who forget know they are not distinct; those who cling are divided like the liver and gall. Yet those entangled in contrivance are many, and within the distinctions of right and wrong, within the *yes* and *ah*, lies the root of all troubles. To abandon them is to have no worry—these two pairs briefly illustrate what must be abandoned in the learning of attachment.

What people fear,
**Explication:** *People*—the Daoist practitioner. What they fear is fearing not to abandon learning in rulers.

Cannot but fear.
**Explication:** Nearby are those who kill the virtuous through flattery.

○**Shu says:** This is what the practitioner fears—life and death, and all worldly entanglements.

**Explication:** They will surely perish. Earlier, it was ordered to abandon evil, showing that beauty and ugliness are both empty, lest one become attached to this text and be stained by worldly affairs. Hence, this sentence is issued as a warning.

How vast and endless it is!
**Explication:** The people of the world are chaotic and unrestrained, desiring to study but clinging to false teachings, with no end.

○**Shu says:** The *River Above* text reads *vast*, while others say *wild and boundless*—a distant, dim vastness. *Endless*—lamenting that it does not cease. The hearts of sentient beings are deeply entangled for a long time, their attachments firm, with no time of rest.

All people are exultant,
**Explication:** *Exultant*—indulgent in lust and desire.

Like feasting on the grandest sacrifice,
**Explication:** Like a starving man craving the finest sacrificial meats, never satisfied.

Like ascending a terrace in spring.
**Explication:** In spring, yin and yang interact, and all things respond. Ascending a terrace to gaze upon it, one’s desires are unrestrained.

○**Shu says:** Many are entangled, hence *all people*—another term for *sentient beings*. *Exultant*—joy and delight. *Feasting*—banqueting. *Grandest sacrifice*—ox, sheep, and pig. Sentient beings delight in sounds and colors, their attachments extreme, like a starving man feasting on the finest sacrificial meats, finding such joy, or like ascending a terrace in spring, gazing upon forests and fields, finding their hearts fully at ease.

I alone am serene, with no signs yet.
**Explication:** I alone am serene and tranquil, with no traces of desire.

○**Shu says:** *I*—the Old Master’s self-reference. *Serene*—stillness. *Signs*—manifestation. The Sage, though in the world, has a still and serene mind, untouched by the surroundings, with no inclination toward green hills.

Like an infant before it smiles.
**Explication:** Like a child before it can laugh at others.

○**Yu says:** I alone am serene and tranquil, with no traces of desire, like that infant before it can laugh.

Towering yet without destination.
**Explication:** Towering like a remote village, with nowhere to go.

○**Yu says:** The Perfect One has no mind; movement follows things, taking and giving nothing. Like a traveler with no destination.

○**Shu says:** *Infant*—the uncarved block. *Smile*—laughter. *Towering*—vastness. *Destination*—movement. The Sage acts without deviating from stillness, remains untouched in defilement, like that infant before it can laugh, its form and mind both still. Its wisdom is vast and respectful, its capacity boundless—like an empty boat with no mooring.

All people have excess,
**Explication:** People have excess wealth for luxury, excess intelligence for deceit.

I alone seem abandoned—
**Explication:** I alone appear cast aside, as if insufficient.

○**Shu:** The multitude clings to possession, thus indulging in excess. The Sage’s wisdom embodies emptiness, thus abandoned and untaken.

I hold the heart of the simple fool—
**Explication:** I do not follow the ways of the world, steadfast in the One, unmoved, like the heart of the simple fool.

Pure and undivided—
**Explication:** Without distinction.

○**Shu:** Pure and undivided—unmixed. The Sage’s wisdom is empty and clear, pure and refined, like the simple fool’s heart, undivided.

The vulgar shine brightly—
**Explication:** Bright and discerning.

I alone seem dim—
**Explication:** As if obscured.

○**Yu:** I conceal myself.
○**Shu:** The vulgar’s hearts are dim yet sharp in distinction, boasting their cleverness. The Sage’s wisdom is empty yet luminous, bright as sun and moon, yet veiled—thus seeming dim.

The vulgar are keen and hasty—

The vulgar are keen and hasty—
**Explication:** They are quick and sharp in discernment.

○**Yu:** Establishing laws and institutions.

I alone am **mènmèn**—
**Explication:** Without cutting off or restricting.

○**Shu:** Keen and hasty, this is the heart of distinction. **Mènmèn** is broad and unhurried, the wisdom of undivided discernment. Though the vulgar’s hearts are dull in perception, they cling to judging right and wrong, recklessly hastening in urgency. The Sage’s wisdom perceives form yet transcends it, knows emptiness and illusion, ever abiding in stillness.

Like the vast sea—
**Explication:** I alone drift and flow like the river and sea, boundless in extent.

○**Shu:** The *Héshàng* [River Above] text reads *hǎi* [sea]. **Huì** means dark, **zhǐ** means to stop or dwell. Though the Sage’s wisdom is truly luminous, it drifts like darkness seeking to reveal itself—bright yet not dazzling. Thus, this saying emerges.

Though mingling with the dust of the world,
Yet ever serene and still—
And not fixed in this stillness—
Thus, no true dwelling or halting.

Like drifting without end—
**Explication:** I alone drift and drift, as if rising and unbound, my spirit and intention soaring in the realm of the divine.

○**Yu:** My heart is still, severed from worldly learning, as if without attachment or fixation.

All others have **yǐ**—
**Explication:** *Yǐ*, worldly striving.

I alone am dull, like the vulgar—
**Explication:** I alone have no striving, seeming vulgar and lacking.

○**Yu:** **Wán** means undivided; **bǐ** means insufficient, yet my heart truly comprehends. Though appearing insufficient, thus I say *like the vulgar*.

○**Shu:** The vulgar are mired in sensory desires, never resting. Though they may take different paths, they all share the same worldly attachments. The Sage’s wisdom penetrates the false and the real, never distinguishing. Neither seeing what is right nor knowing what is wrong, like the dull and vulgar, yet in truth, it is thus.

I alone differ from others—
**Explication:** I alone am distinct from people.

○**Yu:** Others are bound by emotions and desires; I have no tainted heart.

And I alone honor **shímǔ**—
**Explication:** *Shí*, to employ; *mǔ*, the Dao/Way.

I alone hold the Dao/Way in highest regard.

○**Shu:** Others distinguish and cling to sensory pleasures; I alone drift in forgetfulness. Distinction leads to attachment to sounds and colors; forgetfulness leads to reverence for the true Dao/Way—thus my difference.

The **vast virtue’s** appearance—
**Explication:** *Kong*, great/extreme; the bearing of one with great virtue.

One with great virtue encompasses all without exception,
**Explication:** able to receive filth and turbidity, to dwell in humility.
Only the Dao/Way is followed.

**Explication:** *Wei*, alone; one with great virtue does not conform to worldly practices but follows the Dao/Way alone.

○**Shu:** The great/extreme virtue’s bearing—how does it appear?
Only in following the Dao/Way does its form manifest.
**Alternative:** *Róng*, to encompass; great virtue’s profound harmony
thus moves the heart and shapes conduct, following the Dao/Way alone.
In speech and silence, actions and reticence, all are Dao/Way’s domains.

The Dao/Way, as a thing—
**Explication:** The Dao/Way’s relation to all things:
It alone comes and goes in their midst.

○**Yu:** This reveals the Dao/Way that the great virtue follows—
neither having nor lacking, beyond naming or verification,
hence it is *hunhuáng*.

○**Shu:** Of the supreme Dao/Way as a thing,
It neither has nor lacks, yet though it has, it lacks;
though it lacks, it does not lack.
Its presence and absence are indeterminate,
thus it is *huánghū*.

Why speak of "thing"?
To clarify the Dao/Way does not depart from things,
things do not depart from the Dao/Way;
outside the Dao/Way, there are no things,
outside things, there is no Dao/Way.
The Dao/Way and things are one in function,
one in essence—
also, enlightenment is the unity of things and Dao/Way,
delusion is their duality.
Things and Dao/Way are neither one nor two,
yet one while not one—
not Dao/Way, not things,
yet Dao/Way and things are not two.
Hence, one is not one,
and things are not dual.

○**Gù:** It seeks to define presence,
yet has no color, no sound;
to say it lacks presence,
yet it has emotion, has faith—
for its essence cannot be defined,
thus it is *hunhuáng*.

Within *hunhuáng* there is form,
**Explication:** Though the Dao/Way is *hunhuáng* and shapeless,
it alone sets patterns for all things.

○**Yu:** *Hū*, lacking; *huáng*, having.
Its traces appear as daily forms.
From the void descending into manifestation,
it becomes the form of all things.

Within *hunhuáng* there is substance,
**Explication:** Though the Dao/Way is *hunhuáng*,
within it there is unity—
nurturing and transforming, establishing form through *qi*.

○**Shu:** Within it is substance—
this is the divine mystery,
the wondrous thing named.
Though neither lacking nor having,
it contains an unfathomable substance.
Within it is form—
this is *qi*, though neither form nor color,
it becomes form and color,
thus it is *qi*.
Speaking of the Dao/Way’s manifold transformations,
it is neither lacking nor having in things,
neither lacking nor having in form.

Within *yǎomiǎng* there is essence.
**Explication:** Though the Dao/Way is *yǎomiǎng* and formless,
within it there is essence—
the subtle interplay of yin and yang
to establish form and spirit.

○**Jiě:** Refers to the original *qi* in the brain transforming into essence.

○**Yu:** *Hū* and *huáng* are presence and absence,
*yǎomiǎng* is unfathomable.
In generation and transformation,
the essence is wondrously preserved.

○**Shu:** *Yǎomiǎng*, profound and distant.
Within it is essence—*wisdom*.
The Dao/Way, though profound and distant, *hū* and *huáng*,
possesses wondrous essence and spirit—
its wisdom shines without bounds,
its divine power unfathomable.

Its essence is most true,
**Explication:** The Dao/Way’s essence, *qi*, and spirit are wondrously true,
without adornment.

○**Shu:** Its true essence is unmixed,
not empty or false.
Among the three-in-one, it emphasizes essence
to reveal essence as the root of *qi*, color, and spirit’s function.

○**Gù:** The supreme Dao/Way’s essence and spirit are utterly true, without falsehood.

Within it there is faith.
**Explication:** The Dao/Way hides its function, conceals its name,
its faith resides within.

○**Jiě:** Refers to *essence*.
If essence does not transform and blood does not store,
then one becomes a true person.
In one’s conduct, within it there is faith.

○**Shu:** The Dao/Way has no mind,
yet responds to stimuli without fail—
faith like the four seasons,
never out of step.
From ancient times to the present,
its name does not depart,
**Explication:** *Zì*, from; from ancient times to the present,
the Dao/Way remains ever-present.

○**Jiě:** *Name* refers to the Dao/Way;
the Sage does not die, his name endures.

○**Shu:** Time has ancient and present,
yet the Dao/Way has neither coming nor going.
Since its name does not depart,
it suffices to show it has no coming—
the text is concise.

With this, it observes all things at their beginning.
**Explication:** *Yuè*, to receive/embody; *fǔ*, initial.
The Dao/Way can embody and bestow,
all things begin to arise—
they receive *qi* from the Dao/Way.

○**Shu:** *Yuè*, to survey; *fǔ*, initial.
Though the supreme Dao/Way has neither coming nor going,
it also goes and comes—
thus it surveys ancient and present,
responds to all ends and beginnings.

How do I know all things arise thus?
**Explication:** How do I know all things receive *qi* from the Dao/Way?

With this.
**Explication:** *This*, the present;
with the present, all things gain the Dao/Way’s essence and *qi* to arise.
Human actions, movements, and dwellings
without the Dao/Way would not be.

○**Shu:** A rhetorical question posed.
Laozi asks: How do I know the affairs of beginning and end?
Only through this pure and faithful Dao/Way,
the joyous and *hū* *yǎomiǎng* method,
do I know it.

Bending brings fullness,
**Explication:** *Qū*, to bend oneself to others;
not asserting oneself brings fullness.

○**Shu:** Flexible and compliant,
not clinging to things’ emotions—
gentle and weak, humble and harmonious,
preserving life’s Dao/Way,
thus *Zhuangzi* says:
I walk in curves, yet my feet remain unharmed,
this sentence forgets compliance.

Perverting brings rectitude,
**Explication:** *Yǎng*, to bend oneself while extending to others;
long-term, one attains rectitude.

○**Shu:** *Yǎng*, to err/transgress.
Knowing transgression and error are not dual,
one can receive slander without retaliating—
thus, perversion gains true rectitude.
This sentence forgets praise and blame.

Hollow brings abundance,
**Explication:** *Dì*, earth; hollow and low,
water flows into it—
humble and low, virtue returns to it.

Worn brings renewal,
**Explication:** *Bì*, to receive harm/thinness;
self-sacrifice for others, the world respects it—
long-term, one attains renewal.

○**Commentary:** *Dì*, to be low/humble.
Humble and yielding, retiring oneself to lowliness,
not competing with others—
thus virtue and conduct overflow abundantly.
This sentence forgets the high and the low.

**Explication:** *Bì*, to be worn/harmed.
To endure dishonor and vileness while not coveting glory and favor,
one’s virtue renews daily—
this again forgets honor and disgrace.

**Moderation brings gain,**
**Explication:** *Shǎo*, to take little—then one gains much.
The Dao/Way of Heaven favors the humble,
the divine illuminates the empty.
**Excess brings confusion,**
**Explication:** *Duō*, to be excessive.
The vulgar, with many thoughts and desires,
walk without steadfastness—
thus they are confused.

○**Commentary:** *Shǎo* refers to the complete practice of *Yǎng* (bending/extending),
unaware of high and low,
dwelling in the singular middle.

*Duō* refers to being stuck in the duality of *Wéi Shùn* (compliance/transgression),
never leaving the two extremes.

To embody the singular middle—then gain is attained;
to cling to the two extremes—then confusion arises.

Thus the sage holds the One as the model for all under Heaven.
**Explication:** *Bào*, to guard/hold.
*Shì*, law/pattern.
The sage guards the One and thus knows all things,
making it the law/pattern for all under Heaven.

○**Commentary:** It means to focus on the One Dao/Way,
to guard the balance of the self.

○**Commentary:** *Shì*, law/pattern.
The sage upholds this singular middle Dao/Way,
guiding all beings by its path—
thus becoming the exemplar for all under Heaven’s cultivation.

**Not self-illuminating—then clarity,**
**Explication:** The sage does not see the world as through his own eyes,
but uses the sight of all under Heaven to perceive it—
thus attaining clarity.

○**Commentary:** *Wù* (things) and *wǒ* (self) alike are empty,
no perception of self or form—
thus wisdom shines brightly.

**Not self-righteous—then brilliance,**
**Explication:** The sage does not assert his own correctness while rejecting others—
thus his virtue/power shines in the world.

○**Commentary:** Self and others are equal;
not asserting correctness in oneself or in things—
thus his virtue/power is manifest and evident.

**Not self-praising—then merit,**
**Explication:** *Fá*, to take/claim.
The sage’s virtue/power flows naturally,
not claiming its own beauty—
thus it benefits all under Heaven.

○**Commentary:** Though he may be merciful to all beings,
he humbles himself as their dog (servant),
attributing merit to others—
thus he attains great merit.

**Not self-glorifying—then longevity.**
**Explication:** *Jīng*, to magnify/boast.
The sage does not exalt himself—
thus his virtue/power endures without peril.

○**Commentary:** Only by attributing merit to others,
not boasting or magnifying—
thus his virtue/power lasts far and wide.

**For only the non-competing—
none under Heaven can compete with them.**
**Explication:** *Zhēng*, to contend.
This praises those of the four virtues,
who grasp compliance/transgression subtly,
cultivate themselves with humility—
and do not contend with others—
thus all ranks under Heaven cannot contend with the non-competing.

**The ancients’ saying—"Flexibility brings wholeness"—
was it idle speech?**

**Explication:** *Qū*, to bend.
*Zéng*, to follow.
The ancients said that bending and yielding in compliance
preserves one’s Dao/Way—
this saying is true, not idle.
Only by following the teachings and practicing accordingly,
not contending with others—
one attains complete virtue/power,
and all things return to oneself.

Thus, **wholeness is indeed attained and returned.**

**Explication:** *Chéng*, reality/truth.
Those who practice bending and yielding
truly preserve their body and mind,
returning to their parents (nature)—
no harm comes to them.

**Sparse speech is natural.**
**Explication:** *Xī*, to love/minimize.
Those who love minimizing speech
embody the Dao/Way of Nature.

○**Commentary:** *Xī*, few/sparse.
If one loves few words and sparse speech,
then actions align with Nature’s Dao/Way.

○**Commentary:** *Xī*, to minimize.
Minimizing speech is to forget speech.
The ultimate of Nature is the *Chóng Xuán* (profound emptiness).
Dao/Way seeks to clarify the ultimate,
which transcends speech.
Speech itself deviates from principle—
only by forgetting speech and discarding teaching,
can one aptly meet the profound emptiness.

**Gale winds do not last a morning,
torrent rains do not last a day.**

**Explication:** *Piāo*, drifting.
*Fēng*, wind.
*Zòu*, sudden.
*Yǔ*, rain.
It says that swiftness cannot endure,
violence cannot persist.

○**Commentary:** Drifting winds and sudden rains
do not even last a morning—
how could they endure a full day?
It likens those who cling to teaching, lost in doctrine,
to rash actions in pursuit of swift rewards—
their resolve is fleeting and cannot last long,
hence the comparison to gale winds and torrent rains.

Day is the total of morning,
morning is a division of day—
the division illustrates the newness of each moment,
the total signifies the span of a hundred years,
both convey impermanence.

○**Commentary:** Mountains and valleys communicate,
giving rise to gale winds.
Yin-qi erupts,
forming torrent rains.
When yin and yang lose their rhythms,
then gale winds and sudden rains occur.
If humans lose the middle harmony,
then they become violent and swift.
Ruining the body and losing substance—
this cannot be sustained.
Sudden violence is not constant,
Nature cannot endure it long—
thus it is said: "They do not last a morning."

○**Commentary:** Heaven disperses yang-qi downward,
which becomes wind;
Earth ascends yin-qi upward,
which condenses into rain.

○**Commentary:** Heaven is vigorous, Earth is yielding,
the divine qi is uniquely absolute—
this causes sudden violence and swiftness,
yet even this cannot last a full day,
let alone the vulgar, who are born in the morning and die in the evening,
and whose many words harm others—
how could they endure?

○**Commentary:** The greatest forms are Heaven and Earth.
The most expansive qi are yin and yang.
When yin and yang clash,
Heaven and Earth intertwine—
gale winds and sudden rains arise,
yet cannot last long.
How much more so for humans,
so small and fragile between Heaven and Earth,
who perish swiftly from excessive speech—
their swift extinction is known.

**Who causes this?**
Heaven and Earth.

Heaven and Earth cannot endure long—
*[Note: They cannot last from morning to evening.]*
How much more so for humans?
*[Note: Heaven and Earth are supremely divine; when they combine to produce gale winds and sudden rains, they still cannot last from morning to evening. How much more so for humans, who seek sudden death?]*

○ **Exegesis:**
*"Who"*—this is a rhetorical question asking who causes the winds and rains.
The answer is: The Two Principles of Heaven and Earth generate and transform with great divine power.
Even gale winds and sudden rains cannot endure long—
how much more so for the vulgar, whose reckless actions
cannot last?
Thus, those who practice the Dao—
*[Note: "From" here means "to engage in." Humans should act in accordance with the Dao’s tranquility, not like gale winds and sudden rains.]*
○ **Yu’s Commentary:**
Those who practice the Dao should not cling to rigid teachings.
Those who embody the Dao awaken to it and forget speech—
thus, they align with the Dao itself.
○ **Exegesis:**
"Following the Dao" means yielding to it.
"Affairs" refers to worldly matters.
Those who embody supreme virtue act in accordance with both principle and reality,
with the Dao and with things—
thus, they naturally remain empty and fluid,
just as Confucius said: *"Only the Dao is worth following."*

○ **Cai’s Commentary:**
If all actions conform to the Dao,
then the Dao is attained—
this is the harmony of Dao’s operation.

○ **Wang’s Commentary:**
To reverse conventional teachings and conform to the Dao,
one must abandon all burdens and merge with emptiness—
this is called aligning with the Dao.
The Dao responds accordingly.

*[Note: This is because one’s actions and understanding merge,
allowing perception and wisdom to converge naturally.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
"Dao attained" means attaining the Dao.
Those who align with virtue also attain virtue.
*[Note: "Virtue" here refers to those who cultivate virtue.
Those who align with virtue act in accordance with it.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
The Dao is the eternal principle;
virtue is the highest virtue.
Those who embody both teaching and practice, forgetting speech,
act in harmony with supreme virtue—
thus, virtue naturally resonates and responds in kind.
The Dao is the essence of virtue;
virtue is the manifestation of the Dao.
Speaking of essence, we call it Dao;
speaking of manifestation, we call it virtue—
hence the two expressions.

Those who align with loss also lose.
*[Note: "Loss" refers to acting selfishly and failing others.]*
Those who align with loss also lose.
*[Note: Those who act in accordance with loss also delight in loss.]*
Those who align with the Dao also find the Dao delights in them.
*[Note: Those who align with the Dao also find the Dao delights in them.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Those who act recklessly, cling to teachings, and remain deluded
ultimately align with those who act against principle—
thus, they cannot attain empty-hearted unity.
As for those who claim to speak of the Dao yet fail—
this is akin to losing the Dao itself.
Thus, the *Western Ascension Scripture* says:
*"In past lives, you did not study;
now you consort with those who stray."*

Those who align with virtue also find virtue delights in them.
*[Note: Those who align with virtue also find virtue delights in them.]*
Those who align with loss also find loss delights in them.
*[Note: Those who align with loss also find loss delights in them.]*

Lacking faith is met with disbelief.
*[Note: If the ruler lacks faith in his subjects,
the subjects respond with disbelief.]*
There is disbelief.
*[Note: This speaks of natural affinities:
dragons gather where dragons are,
winds follow tigers,
water flows to moisture,
fire seeks dryness—
these are the laws of nature.]*
○ **Yu’s Commentary:**
Clinging to speech and rigid teachings,
one fails to awaken—
this is the root of insufficient faith.
Aligning with loss, loss seeks them—
this is the manifestation of disbelief.
○ **Exegesis:**
Unable to forget speech and instead seeking principle through words,
though one claims to have faith in the Dao,
one’s understanding remains insufficient.
Thus, clinging to speech and rigid teachings,
one fails to grasp the true source—
hence, in the realm of the Triple Mystery,
one harbors doubt.

Those who advance arrogantly cannot stand.
*[Note: "Advance" here means greedy for power and fame,
seeking glory and honor—
such actions cannot sustain one’s conduct or the Dao.]*
Those who strut cannot walk.
*[Note: Those who consider themselves noble and domineer over others
are despised by the crowd,
preventing them from proceeding.]*
Those who self-display are blind.
*[Note: People who admire their own appearance or actions,
believing them virtuous,
are unaware of their ugliness or vulgarity.]*
○ **Yu’s Commentary:**
Flashing talent and boasting of oneself
only invites criticism—
thus, one remains unseen.
○ **Exegesis:**
This is because one cannot forget the self.

Those who insist on being right are not illuminated.
*[Note: Those who insist on their own correctness while rejecting others
are despised by the crowd,
preventing their virtue from shining.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
The crowd despises them—
thus, their virtue remains hidden.

Those who boast of their own merits achieve nothing.
*[Note: Those who constantly praise their own achievements
lose the true credit of having helped others.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Whenever they act, they immediately claim credit,
and others refuse to acknowledge their contributions—
thus, they achieve no true merit.

Those who pride themselves cannot endure.
*[Note: Those who boast of their own greatness
cannot last long.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Arrogance and vanity, contempt for others—
this is short-sightedness.
How could such virtue endure?

Such people bring only excess consumption and superfluous actions to the Dao.
*[Note: "Excess" here means greed.]*
If such boastful, self-praising individuals govern a state,
they daily extort surplus taxes and luxuries for their own gluttony.
○ **Exegesis:**
"Excess consumption" refers to leftover food—
"superfluous" refers to useless flesh, as in *Zhuangzi*:
*"Hanging warts and parasitic growths."*
Such prideful, self-righteous people,
in their Daoist practice,
are like leftover food and parasitic growths—
how despicable they are.

People may come to hate them.
*[Note: Such individuals in power act harmfully,
so all fear and despise them.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Superfluous flesh is useless;
boastful actions are meaningless.
Those who cultivate the Dao would never harbor such vain ambitions.

Thus, those who embody the Dao do not dwell [in any one place].
*[Note: Those who follow the Dao do not remain fixed in any realm or state.]*

There exists a primal unity that coalesced before Heaven and Earth came into being.
*[Note: This refers to the Dao—formless and indistinct, yet giving rise to all things before Heaven and Earth existed.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
The "primal unity" is the Dao.
The Dao is neither a thing nor not a thing,
neither formless nor formed,
yet it generates all existence—
thus it is both indistinct and distinct.
As *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The divine and the sovereign spirits,
giving birth to Heaven and Earth,
yet not aging—*
*Silent and boundless,
self-sufficient and unchanging."*

Silent and void,
it stands alone without peer,
unchanging in its transformations.
*[Note: Silent—without sound; void—without form.
Alone—without counterpart.
Unchanging—its transformations are constant.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Silent—no sound.
Void—no form.
Alone—no dependence on another.
Unchanging—no alteration or decay.
The Dao’s essence is profound and obscure,
its form and sound extinguished,
neither dependent nor altered—
this is the singularity beyond mere singularity.

Its circulation is boundless and unceasing,
*[Note: The Dao permeates Heaven and Earth without obstruction,
neither scorching in the sun nor rotting in the shade,
penetrating all things without peril or failure.]*
○ **Exegesis (Node Analysis):**
The Sage embodies the Dao within the body,
flowing through limbs, joints, orifices, and pores—
thus its circulation is boundless and unceasing.

It can be the mother of all under Heaven.
*[Note: The Dao nurtures all things,
its vital essence like a mother to her children.]*
○ **Exegesis (Node Analysis):**
The Sage who guards the Dao and embodies unity
is the mother of all under Heaven.
○ **Exegesis:**
The Dao is everywhere—
its circulation is boundless,
its presence universal—
thus there is no peril or failure.
It opens and harmonizes yin and yang,
establishes Heaven and Earth,
nurtures all beings,
and gives birth to all sentient life—
thus it is the mother.

I know not its name,
so I call it the Dao.
*[Note: I see no form to name the Dao,
yet all things arise from it—
thus I name it the Dao.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
The Dao cannot be grasped by intellect;
its name was first mentioned in the opening chapter:
*"The nameless is the origin of Heaven and Earth."*
Taking its pervasive generative virtue,
I name it the Dao.

I forcefully name it **Great**.
*[Note: Unable to name it truly,
I call it Great—
vast and without limit,
encompassing all without exclusion.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Its essence knows no bounds—
thus it is called Great.
Not *great* in the ordinary sense—
thus it is called *Great* by force.
Names summon the essence,
characters express the virtue—
the Dao is its function,
the Great is its substance—
thus it is named *Great* and called *Dao*.
Men usually name first, then characterize,
but here the order is reversed
to contrast the Dao with the common.

The Great is called **Passing**.
*[Note: Its greatness is not like Heaven’s fixed position above
nor Earth’s fixed position below—
it moves on, without permanence.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
*Passing*—to go.
With great power, it guides all beings
to the Sage’s realm.

*Passing* is called **Distant**.
*[Note: Distant—extending to the boundless,
its qi permeating Heaven and Earth without obstruction.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Ascending beyond the Three Realms,
reaching the Three Purities.
*Distant*—to return.
*[Note: Return—restoration.]*
Having perfected the Dao within,
attaining the distant Sage’s realm,
it returns to the mortal world
to compassionately save all beings.
*Alternative Exegesis:*
When lost, the Three Purities are the Three Realms;
when enlightened, the Three Realms are the Three Purities—
thus its return to the mundane is the Great Void Heaven above.

Thus, the Dao is Great,
Heaven is Great,
Earth is Great,
and the Sovereign is Great.
*[Note: The Dao is Great—encompassing Heaven and Earth without limit.
Heaven is Great—covering all without exclusion.
Earth is Great—bearing all without failure.
The Sovereign is Great—controlling all without overreach.
Within the realm, there are these four Great ones,
and the Sovereign occupies one of them.]*
○ **Exegesis (Command):**
The Sovereign is the master of humanity,
the fate of all things resting on his rise or fall.
To warn and admonish him,
it is said the Sovereign occupies one of the four.
○ **Exegesis:**
Within the domain, these four Great ones exist,
and the Sovereign, with his transformative virtue,
follows Heaven and Earth in occupying one place.
As *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The Dao was never confined by boundaries—
speaking of ‘within the realm’ is to say
though confined, it is not confined,
though not confined, it is confined—
discussing confinement,
though confined, it is not confined—
encompassing all without limit."*

○ **Exegesis (Node Analysis):**
The lungs are Great,
the heart is Great,
the liver is Great,
the spleen is Great—
kidney water generates the One,
and the Sovereign governs from the One,
as the spleen’s qi spreads unity in all directions.

The Sovereign should model himself after Earth—
*[Note: The ruler must emulate Earth’s stillness, gentleness, and yielding nature.]*

The cultivation of the five grains yields abundance,
and digging yields sweet springs—
laboring without complaint,
with merit yet unclaimed.

Earth follows Heaven,
*[Heaven is serene and unmoving,
bestowing without seeking return,
nurturing all things yet taking nothing.]*

Heaven follows the Dao,
*[The Dao acts with effortless clarity,
its subtle qi moving unseen,
and all things form without contrivance.]*

The Dao follows *ziran* [自然].
*[The Dao’s nature is spontaneous—
it follows nothing, transcending all imitation.]*

○ **Exegesis (Commentary):**
The ruler must emulate Earth—
calmness in action, for only then
does wisdom arise.
Thus the next passage states:
*"Agitation loses sovereignty—
when still as Earth,
he must next follow Heaven’s purity,
nurturing all without partiality."*

Heaven has the Three Lights—
a metaphor for wisdom’s illumination—
Earth is the gate of *ding* [定, steadfastness],
Heaven the gate of wisdom.
Having aligned with Heaven,
he must next follow the Dao’s boundless openness,
embracing all things.

Having aligned with the Dao,
he must then follow *ziran*’s profound mystery—
the realm of *Chóngxuān* [重玄].
The Dao is the path,
*ziran* is its essence—
to grasp the essence is to master the path,
hence the saying, *"to follow."*

Further elucidation:
The Dao’s nature is *ziran*—
it follows nothing,
transcending all cultivation,
thus it is said, *"the Dao follows ziran."*

*Chóng* [重] is the root of *qīng* [轻]—
*[For rulers, neglecting weightiness brings disrespect;
in self-cultivation, neglect brings loss of vitality—
flowers and leaves are light and fall,
roots are heavy and endure.]*

○ **Commentary:**
This warns against recklessness,
for it is written:
*"The noble does not act lightly, lest he lose authority."*

Stillness governs agitation—
*[A ruler’s agitation brings ruin;
in self-cultivation, agitation brings peril—
the dragon’s stillness allows transformation,
the tiger’s agitation invites early death.]*

○ **Commentary:**
Stillness is non-action;
agitation is desire—
desire brings death,
non-action brings longevity.
Stillness masters movement,
thus it is the ruler’s essence.

Hence the sage moves through life
yet never departs from *zhīzhòng* [辎重].
*[Zhī: stillness. Zhòng: weightiness.]*
The sage walks the Dao all day,
never straying from stillness and weightiness.

○ **Commentary:**
The noble and enlightened move through the world,
yet remain rooted in weightiness and stillness—
this is motion in tranquility.

Though adorned with palaces and halls,
their repose is transcendent.
*[Palaces: the royal halls.
Halls: the consorts’ quarters.
Transcendent: detached, uninvolved.]*

○ **Commentary:**
Those who embody weightiness and stillness,
though surrounded by splendor and comfort,
dwell in emptiness and detachment,
untainted by worldly pleasures.

How then does the sovereign of ten thousand chariots—
*[Naihe: a lament for the times, a sorrowful exclamation.
Ten thousand chariots: the king.]*
—abandon the greatest treasure, the realm?
*[The realm is the supreme position of authority.]*
How can the ruler indulge his body in excess,
squander his vital essence,
and lose his throne?

○ **Commentary:**
*Naihe* means *"how is this possible?"*
The king, ruling a thousand-li domain,
commanding ten thousand chariots,
must uphold weightiness and stillness—
yet he indulges in licentiousness and debauchery,
imposes heavy taxes and unrest,
pursues sensual excess and ruinous desires,
neglects his inner chambers—
his lament is profound,
thus it is asked: *how is this possible?*
And yet he treats his body with such levity,
risking the realm’s collapse.

*[The ruler, though noble, must guard the One for longevity—
seeing yet not learning,
he treats his body lightly,
and thus meets his end.
Even with jade goblets and gold carvings,
what use are they to a lifeless corpse?]*

Lightness brings loss of ministers,
*[The ruler’s indulgence alienates his ministers;
self-neglect dissipates vital essence.]*
agitation brings loss of sovereignty.
*[The ruler’s haste undermines his authority;
agitation depletes his spirit.]*

○ **Exegesis:**
Those consumed by restless thought
cannot attain longevity.

○ **Commentary:**
Indulgence and agitation grow worse by the day—
externally, the nation falls;
internally, the body perishes.
Loyal ministers flee,
the throne collapses—
loss of ministers,
loss of sovereignty.

The virtuous path leaves no trace,
*[The true practitioner seeks virtue within—
no need to step outside,
no need to leave the hall,
thus no tracks remain.]*

○ **Commentary:**
To act without action,
to move without form—
this is the virtuous path,
a subtle accord with cultivation.
When mind and wisdom merge,
no traces appear—
this reveals a pure life.

The virtuous word has no flaws,
*[The virtuous speaker chooses words with care,
leaving no blemish or reproach in the world.]*

○ **Exegesis:**
The enlightened teach without obstruction,
transcending literal forms,
seeking meaning beyond symbols,
forgetting speech in its essence—
thus their words are without fault.

○ **Commentary:**
Speech that is not spoken,
spoken yet not spoken—
all day speaking yet never speaking,
nor ever not speaking—
this is the virtuous word.
As *Zhuangzi* says:
*"When words suffice, one speaks all day yet exhausts the Dao."*

Flaws are defects,
reproach is censure.
Speech and silence are one,
thus no blame attaches to the mouth—
this reveals a pure tongue.

The virtuous plan requires no calculations,
*[The virtuous strategist acts without scheming,
needing no abacus or reckoning.]*

「The virtuous strategist who calculates through the Dao/Way
holds fast to the One without deviation,
though myriad affairs arise,
yet needs no abacus or reckoning—
the path is clear.

○ **Exegesis:**
The subtle insight of all dharmas as One in emptiness and illusion,
abandoning neither emptiness nor illusion,
then the false body becomes true—
effortless clarity,
no need for scheming or calculation,
this reveals the purity of intent.

The virtuous closure knows no latch or bolt that can be opened,
*[The virtuous who seals through the Dao/Way restrains emotions and desires,
guarding the spirit—
no door or barrier can be forced open,
nor can it be unlocked.]*

○ **Commentary:**
*[The Dao/Way seals the mind,
not like a door with a latch or bolt that can be pried open.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
This refers to closing the breath and firming the spirit,
sealing the celestial *yin* (天牝),
retaining the earthly *yang* (地牡)—
thus it is said, "no latch or bolt."

○ **Exegesis:**
*[The virtuous transcend both speech and action,
not entering any false path—
the mind is free from entanglement in phenomena,
phenomena bear no burden of arising thoughts,
though no latch or barrier exists,
how could it ever be opened?]*

○ **Exegesis:**
*[Externally, there are no desirable objects;
internally, there is no desiring mind—
when the root of desire is employed,
employed yet unstained,
this is non-closure yet closure,
though closed, not closed—
no need for latches or bolts,
thus it cannot be opened.]*

The virtuous knot requires no rope or binding that can be undone.
*[The virtuous who binds through the Dao/Way
merely binds the mind—
no rope or cord can force its release.]*

○ **Exegesis:**
*[The noble one perceives true reality,
fundamentally abandoning all attachment—
if one can abandon attachment,
then the mind aligns with the Dao/Way,
though no rope or cord exists,
how could it ever be undone?]*

○ **Exegesis:**
The noble ones of the first rank
prioritize others over themselves,
harbor vast compassionate vows,
transform and guide all beings—
their resolve is unyielding,
their bonds unbroken,
not constrained by worldly ropes or cords,
thus they cannot be undone.

Yet such vows are manifold,
yet they reduce to five:
1. The vow of awakening intent,
   *[to enter my path and embrace the natural Dao/Way.]*
2. The vow of subduing the mind,
   *[to conquer all obstacles and delusions.]*
3. The vow of knowing the true mind,
   *[which bears nine grades,
   yielding birth to the nine officials.]*
4. The vow of emancipating the mind,
   *[which bears three grades,
   yielding birth to the Three Pure Ones—
   the Sage, the True, and the Holy.]*
5. The vow of the peerless mind,
   *[to ascend directly to the Dao/Way’s fruit,
   even attaining the Great Void.]*

The virtuous knotting binds these five minds
without ever faltering—
this is the vow’s firm resolve.
○ **Exegesis:**
*[This refers to binding the soul and spirit,
the Sage holding fast to them—
thus it is said, "unbreakable."]*

The Sage ever holds the soul and spirit,
thus it is said, "unbreakable."

Therefore, the Sage is always virtuous in saving beings,
*[for the Sage’s constant teaching of loyalty and filial piety
seeks to rescue the essence of human nature.]*

○ **Exegesis:**
The Sage is one whose three actions are pure,
whose six senses are liberated.
To awaken vast vows,
to rescue and guide all beings,
the Sage remains in the world,
responding to each need—
compassionate, equitable, and boundless,
never abandoning any.

And yet, no being is abandoned;
*[for the Sage guides the noble and lowly to their proper place.]*

Always virtuous in saving all things,
*[for the Sage teaches the people to follow the four seasons,
thus sparing all creatures from harm.]*

Thus, no thing is abandoned.
*[The Sage does not deem stone less precious than jade,
but views them as equal.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
First, the Sage’s virtue extends to all sentient beings,
then, to all things—
saving beings directly through the common folk,
saving creatures through the four seasons—
this is called *clinging to clarity*.

*[The Sage’s virtue in saving all things
is called clinging to the clarity of Heaven’s Dao/Way.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
*Clinging* means to uphold, to employ.
Thus, the Sage’s virtue in praising the Sage’s constancy,
benefiting all without abandon,
may be called upholding and employing the Sage’s Way.

The virtuous are teachers to the unvirtuous;
*[those who practice virtue
the Sage takes as teachers for humanity.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
The virtuous are those who follow the clarity of the Way,
ever bearing a great mind,
prioritizing others over themselves,
guiding all beings—
truly the master craftsmen of the path.

The unvirtuous are the material for the virtuous;
*[material, here meaning resource or means.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
*[The Sage always transforms and guides the multitude,
using even the unvirtuous as a field of merit,
for the benefit of all beings
thus perfects my virtue.]*

The Sage does not esteem their teachers,
*[for there is no reliance.]*
nor cherish their resources,
*[for there is no purpose.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
*[If one retains the notion of teachers and resources,
this is not yet the ultimate.]*

Now, the reason the Sage esteems teachers
is to preserve the form of learning;
once the form of learning is empty,
naturally there is no esteem.
The reason the Sage cherishes resources
is to preserve the form of teaching;
in teaching, forgetting teaching,
thus no resources are cherished.
Esteem and cherishing both forgotten,
the Dao/Way transforms naturally.
Though one may seem deeply deluded,
*[though one may think oneself knowing,
this person is truly great delusion.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
*[There is no transformation.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
The Sage guides the multitude,
encouraging them to study and cultivate.
Now, the Sage’s equality with the vulgar
discards the notion of teachers and resources—
thus, from shallow to profound,
gradually ascending to all wonders,
this is called *essential subtlety*.

*[One who grasps this meaning
is said to know the subtle and essential Dao/Way.]*
○ **Exegesis:**
Discarding teachers and resources,
the Sage and the vulgar are one—
though one may seem deeply deluded,
according to principle,
they truly attain essential subtlety.

Knowing one’s strength as *yang*,
guarding one’s weakness as *yin*,
one becomes the Dao/Way of the world’s valleys.
*[Strength as *yang* symbolizes the exalted,
*yin* the humble—
though one knows their own nobility,
they must also retain humility,
abandoning the rigidity of *yang*,
embracing the harmony of *yin*—
thus all under Heaven will follow,
as water flows into deep valleys.]*

○ **Exegesis:**
*Valley* means path.
*Yang* is the name for the vigorous and harsh,
*yin* for the gentle and tranquil.
Knowing the vigor of *yang* leads to ruin and death,
*yin*’s gentleness brings longevity—
thus, discarding *yang* and guarding *yin*,
one attains the essential path of the world’s harmony.

○ **Exegesis on *Nodes*:**
The *yang* is the *hun* soul—
the worthy scholar follows the Way,
guarding natural spontaneity,
drawing in and closing the *qi*,
embracing and holding the *hun* and *po* souls.
Constant virtue never departs—
*[For when one remains humble as a deep valley,
virtue abides and does not stray.]*
Return once more to the state of an infant.
*[One must return one’s mind to the innocence of an infant,
serene and without knowledge.]*

○ **Exegesis on *Nodes*:**
*Valley* means *mouth*—
when one practices *yi* (one-ness) and closes the *qi* in stillness,
there is no action.
*[Thus, one does not age;
returning to the state of an infant.]*
*[Exegesis:]* *Depart* means to disperse;
the infant is a metaphor for undifferentiated wisdom.
When one constantly guards stillness,
virtue does not disperse,
and thus returns to its original nature,
reverting to wisdom without distinction.

Guard its darkness,
and become the model for all under Heaven.
*[White symbolizes brilliance,
darkness symbolizes obscurity—
though one knows their own clarity,
they must also guard obscurity,
as if in darkness with nothing to see—
thus, they become the standard for all under Heaven,
and virtue remains constant.]*
*[Exegesis:]* White is luminous clarity.
Darkness is obscurity.
*Model* means *law*.
To display oneself openly and flaunt before others
is certain to provoke resistance—
not the conduct of the wise.
Withdrawing brilliance and concealing traces,
retiring into obscurity,
one does not offend against things—
thus, they are the virtuous person.
Those who know the benefits and harms of light and dark
can serve as the grand exemplar for cultivators.

To become the model for all under Heaven,
virtue never falters—
*[For when one becomes the standard for all under Heaven,
virtue abides within them,
never deviating.]*

○ **Exegesis on *Nodes*:**
This means the Dao is practiced in *yi* (one-ness) to attain transcendence,
becoming the model for all under Heaven.
Return once more to the *Wuji* (Non-Extreme).
*[When virtue does not deviate,
longevity and eternal life are attained,
and the body returns to the boundless.]*

○ **Exegesis on *Nodes*:**
This means constantly guarding the *sheng* (life) practice in *yi* (one-ness) without limit.
*[Exegesis:]* *Deviate* means to stray.
*Wuji* is the Dao.
When one constantly abandons brightness and guards darkness,
virtue does not stray,
restoring purity and returning to the ultimate Dao.

Know its glory,
guard its humiliation,
and become the valley of all under Heaven.
*[Glory symbolizes nobility,
humiliation symbolizes filth—
though one knows their own honor,
they must also guard humiliation,
as if in filth—
thus, all under Heaven will follow,
like water flowing into deep valleys.]*
*[Exegesis:]* Glory is favor and nobility.
Humiliation is lowliness and disgrace.
Dwelling in glory and nobility,
one may rise to arrogance and extravagance,
and suddenly—
as good fortune turns to misfortune and lust to ruin—
one’s former glory becomes the foundation of calamity.
If one knows that rise and fall are inconstant,
that nobility is the beginning of disaster,
one must restrain themselves
and not indulge in dissipation.
Dwelling in splendor yet ever as lowly,
nobility is rooted in humility,
and height is founded on the lowly.
Thus, knowing glory and humiliation,
all under Heaven will follow,
like rivers converging in the empty valleys.

To become the valley of all under Heaven,
virtue is then complete—
*[Complete means to cease;
when one becomes the valley for all under Heaven,
virtue finds its fulfillment within.]*
Return once more to *Pu* (the Uncarved).
*[One must return to the simplicity of the unadorned,
no longer adorned with artifice.]*

○ **Exegesis on *Nodes*:**
This means guarding naturalness.

*[Exegesis:]* *Pu* is the true origin.
From knowing *yang* to ultimately guarding humiliation,
these three methods of cultivation
are the essentials of the Dao.
Just as the empty valley contains all sounds,
so too does the constant Dao’s virtue
thus find fulfillment,
and one can return to true emptiness,
reverting to the wondrous origin.

*Pu* disperses into *qi* (vessels)—
*[Vessels mean use;
when the *Pu* of all things disperses,
it becomes functional.]*
The Dao disperses into the numinous,
flowing as the sun and moon,
manifesting as the Five Phases.
The Sage employs it,
and becomes the ruler of officials.
*[The Sage, ascending in use,
becomes the primal leader of all officials.]*
*[Commentary:]* When virtue is internally harmonized,
one returns to *Pu*.
When virtue is applied,
it disperses into *qi* (vessels).
Once engaged in form and function,
there must be refinement and coarseness—
thus, the Sage employs it to become the talents of the multitude and the leaders of officials.

*[Exegesis:]* Disperses means to distribute.
*Qi* means use.
*Officials* refers to the sovereign.
*Leaders* refers to teachers and masters.
Having returned to simplicity and restored purity,
reverting to the wondrous origin,
one must descend from the origin to benefit the people.
Spreading this purity and harmony as transformative use,
the *Xisheng Jing* says:
*The Dao thus disperses and distributes,
then becomes the ruler to govern the world,
the teacher to guide the customs.*
The Sage employs this *Pu*,
dispersing it into methods of governance and guidance.
Thus, the great measure knows no harm.
*[The Sage governs the world with the great Dao,
harming nothing;
governing the body with the great Dao,
harming no spirit.]*

*[Exegesis:]* The supreme Sage’s divine power is unimaginable,
subduing all within the Three Realms.
As the sovereign of vast universes,
they are called the *Great Measure*.
Yet they refine all beings,
molding life—
extending merit to things,
not ruling as a butcher.
Thus, it is said there is no harm.
*Harm* means to slaughter.

Desiring to take all under Heaven—
*[Desiring to become the sovereign of all under Heaven.]*
Yet to act—
*[Desiring to govern the people with deeds.]*
I see that this is unavoidable.
*[I see that this is contrary to the Dao and human nature—
the Dao abhors turmoil and filth,
human nature abhors excess desire.]*

*[Exegesis:]* When one is about to govern all under Heaven and transform the people,
one must empty the mind and forget desires.
If one governs with deeds,
the moment one attempts to transform,
one will fail—
and this failure is already evident.

All under Heaven is the divine vessel—
*[Vessel means thing;
humans are the divine things of all under Heaven.]*
The divine vessel loves tranquility,
and cannot be governed by deeds.

*[Exegesis:]* All sentient beings possess vitality,
all capable of receiving the Dao,
thus called the *divine vessel*.

The divine vessel is the **Imperial position**—
If one governs with **non-action** and tranquility,
then all beings and things flourish in harmony,
but if one governs with **deeds** and forced intervention,
then all sentient beings lose their **inherent nature**—
thus, it cannot be governed by deeds.

Those who **act** (with deeds) will **corrupt** it,
*[Exegesis:]* Forcing governance upon it will spoil its **natural simplicity**.
Those who **cling** will **lose** it.
*[Exegesis:]* If one rigidly enforces teachings,
one will **fail** to grasp its **true essence**,
giving rise to **deceit and falsehood**.

*[Exegesis:]* To stagnate and drown in **deeds** leads to **self-destruction and ruin**,
to fixate on desires and greed brings **loss of state and nation**.

Thus, things sometimes **operate by themselves** or **follow others**,
*[Exegesis:]* Whatever the **Sovereign** does,
the **subjects** will inevitably follow.

*[Exegesis:]* Things—**all things**—are uncertain.
Sometimes they **act by their own nature**,
sometimes they **conform to others**.
This illustrates that **authority and influence** are inherently unstable—
sometimes **one prevails**, sometimes **one falters**.

Sometimes they **warm**, sometimes they **chill**,
*[Exegesis:]* **Warm** (mild) and **chill** (harsh) are complementary.
Where there is warmth, there must be cold.
*[Exegesis:]* Warmness symbolizes **wealth and honor**,
coldness symbolizes **poverty and lowly status**.
Things may first be **wealthy and honored**, then **poor and lowly**,
just as the **scarlet summer** blazes with heat,
and the **dark winter** is piercing with cold.
The **Heavenly** alternates between **gentleness and severity**—
human fortunes of **wealth and honor** are equally transient.

Sometimes they **strengthen**, sometimes they **weaken**,
*[Exegesis:]* Where there is **strength**, there must be **frailty**.
---

「○ *Exegesis*: For great strength cannot endure long before decline—
thus the next chapter states, *"When things are at their peak, they grow old,"*
and the *Xi Sheng Jing* says: *"The flourishing must decline."*
This is the principle of **flourishing and decline being unstable**.

Sometimes they **settle**, sometimes they **peril**.
*[Exegesis:]* **Settle** means **stability**; **peril** means **danger**.
Where there is stability, there must be danger—
this illustrates that rulers must not act arbitrarily,
whether governing the state or cultivating the self.
○ *Exegesis*: The *He Shang* text originally renders *"or settle"* as *"or connect."*
**Connection** means continuity; **ruin** means decay.
Continuity and connection bring completion,
while decay brings ruin—
this demonstrates the instability of stability and danger.

Thus *Zhuangzi* says: *"What is completed is also destroyed."*
By enumerating these eight principles—
their instability reveals that all things are impermanent—
those who govern the state or cultivate the self
must not cling to rigid actions or fixed outcomes.

Therefore, the Sage **discards excess, extravagance, and excessiveness**.
*[Exegesis:]* **Excess** refers to greed for sounds and colors,
**extravagance** refers to carriages, attire, and drink,
**excessiveness** refers to official halls and pavilions.
By abandoning these three, one abides in **moderation**,
acts with **non-action (wuwei)**,
and the world transforms naturally.
○ *Exegesis*: The Sage, embracing the Dao, subtly embodies emptiness and falseness,
thus does not cling to the eight principles above,
but instead practices the three below:
Excess beautifies sounds and colors,
extravagance adorns garments and ornaments,
excessiveness expands halls and pavilions.
By removing these three delusions,
dwelling in the **central unity (zhongyi)**,
governing the state ensures enduring blessings,
cultivating the self ensures longevity and clear vision.

For those who **assist the ruler with the Dao**—
*[Exegesis:]* Meaning the ruler strengthens themselves with the Dao—
they do not **coerce the world with arms**.
*[Exegesis:]* By assisting the ruler with the Dao,
not relying on arms,
following the Dao and trusting in **virtue/power (de)**,
enemies submit naturally.
○ *Explanation*: This refers to—
the art of war itself.»

...when words err, arms must follow,
then **spirit (shen)** surges and **soul (hun)** and **vital essence (po)** combine,
**divine intelligence (shenming)** grows discontented and agitated—
then it abandons the body.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "以" means "to use."
"佐" means "to assist."
"人主" means "the sovereign/ruler."
Those who **use the Dao to assist the ruler**—
should **lay down arms and cultivate culture (yanshu xiuwen)**,
guide them with **virtue/power (de)**,
and not **overuse military force to coerce the world**.
The **internal wars of body and mind**—
that is, the **three afflictions (sanpo) and six senses (liujin)** as arms.
Their deeds return to themselves.
○ *[Exegesis:]* "好还" means—
when one steadies the heart and calms the will to act with **central unity (zhongyi)**,
this returns to their own person.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "还" means "return" or "recompense."
If one **uses external arms**,
and harbors resentment toward enemies within,
or **applies the three poisons (sanduo)** internally—
then they invite the **three sufferings (santuo)** as recompense.
This is inevitable,
hence it is said that their deeds return to themselves.

Where the **army (shi)** treads,
thorns and brambles grow.
○ *[Exegesis:]* Fields lie fallow, crops unplanted.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "师" means "army."
Where **warhorses and soldiers** march,
they harm people and destroy things—
only thorns and weeds sprout,
no grain grows.
It is the same as where the **desire-heart (yuxin)** roams unrestrained—
only evil deeds multiply,
no good fortune arises.

After a great army,
there must be years of calamity.
○ *[Exegesis:]* Heaven responds with **harmful qi (ezhiqi)**,
then the five grains are blighted,
the five grains exhausted—
then people suffer.

The **virtuous (shan)** act with **resolute sincerity (guo)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* Those who practice goodness
should act with **resolute sincerity** alone,
that is, with **restraint (xiuxi)**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "果" means "resolute."
"已" means "to cease."
The **cultivated Daoist** resolutely ceases
this violent heart and does not wield arms.

Do not **dare to take strength (qujiang)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* Do not **dare to claim resolute sincerity** as a means to seize power.

**Resolute yet not arrogant (guo er wu ji)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* **Resolute yet humble**, not boasting of oneself.

**Resolute yet not self-praising (guo er wu fa)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* **Resolute yet self-effacing**, not seizing glory for oneself.

**Resolute yet not proud (guo er wu jiao)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* "骄" means "to bully."
**Resolute yet not bullying others.**

**Resolute yet not forced (guo er wu deyi)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* **Resolute yet utterly sincere**, not acting under coercion or compulsion.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "骄" means **to disrespect superiors (mangshang)**,
"矜" means **to boast before inferiors (kuaxia)**.
