The **cultivated Daoist** must **practice modesty (qianxun)** and **yielding (ran)**,
not **seizing strength or violence (ziquiangbao)**,
nor **bullying or arrogating power (mangkuai)**.
They must also **avoid self-aggrandizement (jiakuang)** toward things,
not **boasting before inferiors (kuaxia)**.
"伐" means "to take."
**They must acknowledge others' worth (tuirenzhiwu)**,
not **seize glory for oneself (ziqui)**.
Those who act under compulsion—
like **Emperor Yao (Yao Di) attacking Zhongzhi (Congzhi)**,
or **Yu the Great attacking Youxu (Youxu)**—
these were deeds not of their own choosing,
not **delighting in war or conquest (lebingfa)**.
"勿强" means—
before the **knotted cords (jiehuang)** era,
**resolute sincerity** was the **determined cessation of arms**.

This is **resolute yet not strong (guo er wujiang)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* **Resolute yet not relying on arms or fortified might (qujiangbianjia)** to oppress others.**

○ *[Exegesis:]* When enemies invade,
if one **acts under compulsion**,
then they **use arms to repel them**—
but this is **resolute in response to aggression (guoyingdi)**,
not **resolute in seizing strength (guoqujiang)**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "物壮则老" means—
when **plants and trees reach their peak (zhuangji)**,
they wither and fall,
and when **people reach their peak (zhuangji)**,
they grow decrepit and old.

○ *[Exegesis:]* Those who **overuse strength** cannot endure long.
○ *[Exegesis:]* Just as **extreme heat must give way to cold (yanji zeleng)**,
this is the **constant principle of nature (lizhihengshu)**.
When **plants and trees reach their peak (zhuangji)**,
they wither and fall—
this is an inevitable truth.

○ *[Exegesis:]* This is called **"not the Dao (bu dao)"**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* Those who **wither and fall** are those who **sit idle, not practicing the Dao**.
○ *[Exegesis:]* Those who **do not practice the Dao** will **perish early**.
○ *"已" means "death."*

○ *[Exegesis:]* When **plants and trees reach their peak (zhuangji)**,
they wither and fall—
when **arms grow strong (bingjiang)**,
they bring decline.
Since they **do not practice modesty and yielding (buqianran)**,
they are **not true Daoists (feizhendao)**—
they must **cease and desist (zhiqi)**,
not **persist in such ways**.

Now, **fine arms (jia bing)**—
are **inauspicious instruments (bu xiang zhiqi)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* "祥" means "good."
Arms **disturb the spirit (jing shen)** and **muddy harmonious qi (zhuo heqi)**,
they are **not good instruments (bu shan zhiqi)**,
and should not be **cultivated or adorned (bu dang xiu shi)**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "佳" means "ornate."
"祥" means "good."
Though **frosted spears and dazzling halberds (shuangegeliangri)**,
or **jewelled swords gleaming in the sky (baojianhuangtian)**,
these are **tools of calamity (cong huang zhiwu)**,
not **instruments of peace (fei tai ping zhiqi)**.
Internally, they are the **arms of the three poisons and six senses (sanpo liujin zhi bing)**—
if one **sharpened the senses (moxi liujin)**,
yet **greedily took from the dusty world (qianqu chenjing)**—
these are **not good deeds (bu shan zhi xing)**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* Things **often turn against those who wield them (wu huo e zhi)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* When arms are **used, they bring harm (bingdong zhi you hai)**,
so **all things (wanwu)** hold no good fortune for them.
Thus, **those who practice the Dao do not dwell there**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* The **gentle and yielding (zhi)** are **valued in peace (gui zuo)**,
while **strength and force (gangqiang)** are **valued in war (gui you)**—
this shows that the **Dao of war (bingdao)** and the **Dao of the gentleman (junzidao)** are opposed,
and what is **valued differs**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* With **virtue and moral character (junzi renwu zhi de)**,
one is a **gentleman (junzi)**.
The **left (zuo)** is **yang (yang)**—
**bringing auspiciousness and life (zhu sheng)**.
The **right (you)** is **yin (yin)**—
**bringing inauspiciousness and death (zhu xiangsi)**.
When the **gentleman dwells in a peaceful age (pingju zhi shi)**,
they **value the left (yongwen)** and **cultivate culture (xiuwen)**,
while in **times of chaos and disorder (huangluan zhi shi)**,
they **value the right (yongwu)** and **employ arms (bing)**.
This illustrates that **true wisdom (ren shizhi)** values **longevity (changsheng)**,
but **tactical wisdom (quan zhi)** sometimes must **kill (shou shi)**—
hence the scripture says:
**"Thousand upon thousand are beheaded,
million upon million are dismembered."**

○ *[Exegesis:]* "兵者，不祥之器" means—
○ *[Exegesis:]* "非君子之器" means—
arms are **not instruments the gentleman cherishes**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* The **gentleman relies on virtue and moral character (daode)** as their **talents and tools (caiqi)**,
not on **military strategy (bingmou)**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* "阴强则杀身之斧也" means—
**when strength is used in darkness (yinqiang)**,
it becomes the **axe that slays oneself (shashen zhi fu)**.
Hence it is said that **arms are inauspicious instruments**.

When **arms are used under compulsion (bu deyi er yong)**—
○ *[Exegesis:]* When **facing decline and ruin (shao feng huangui)**,
and **desiring to protect the people (yuxia wanmin)**,
one **uses them to defend oneself (yongzhi yizhi)**.

○ *[Exegesis:]* Arms are **not instruments of the gentleman or the virtuous (fei junzi shanren zhiqi)**,
but they **must be used to pacify evil and rescue the afflicted (bixiang qiannan)**,
and **cannot be stopped (bu de zhi)**—
even **tactical wisdom (quan zhi)** must act thus.

**Serenity and simplicity (tian dan)** are the highest.

---

**Annotations:**
- **丧礼** (sangli) as "funeral rites"
- **肉饲乌鸢** (rouci wuyuan) as "flesh fed to carrion birds"
- **血流绛草** (xue liu jiangcao) as "blood flowing over crimson grass"
- **匹夫厘妇** (pifu limifu) as "even commoners and ordinary women"
- **横尸巨野** (hengshi juyě) as "corpses sprawled across vast plains"
- **散骨长源** (sangǔ chángyuán) as "bones scattered along the rivers"
- **朴** (pǔ) as "unadorned simplicity" (or "primordial integrity" in Daoist context)

---

**Translation:**

**Annotations:**—
**When victory is obtained, yet beauty (mei) is not deemed advantageous.**
○ *Exegesis:* **Serenity and non-action (tian dan wuwei) are the highest.**
The **gentleman’s heart** values **non-action (wuwei)**,
hence **using arms is not considered beautiful (mei).**

**It is likened to the traveler**—
though **tactical wisdom (quan zhi)** is employed,
**true Dao/Way (zhidao)** must be held supreme,
and **action should not stray from sincerity (zhen).**

**If one finds beauty in it, then one delights in killing.**
○ *Exegesis:* **If one takes fine arms as beautiful (mei),**
it is **the love of war and slaughter (aile gan gē) that kills.**
**For those who delight in killing cannot achieve their ambitions in the world.**
○ *Exegesis:* **As a ruler, to delight in killing—**
this **cannot be tolerated by the world (tianxia gongji)**,
for **calamities (weiyue)** are many,
and **one cannot fulfill their desires (bu de yi).**
Hence, **one should not arbitrarily execute punishments (bu de màng xing).**

○ *Exegesis:* **The rites of mourning are held on the left (shang zuo),**
**for the left governs life and position (shengwei).**
**Evil is held on the right (shang you),**
**for darkness (yin) governs killing.**

**The lesser general (pianjiangjun) is stationed on the left,**
○ *Exegesis:* **The lesser general is humble and occupies a bright position (yangwei),**
**for they do not specialize in killing.**
○ *One Family’s Commentary:* **The general is one in essence,**
but **names vary with their duties (dongjing yiming).**
When **still, they attend in residence (shizhi shi ju),**
hence they are called **lesser (pian).**

**The greater general (shangjiangjun) is stationed on the right,**
○ *Exegesis:* **The greater general, in their exalted position on the right,**
**holds authority over killing (zhi zhu sha),**
**for they wield threats and punishments (dongyong weixing),**
**and assume command outside the gates (qimen zhuan sha),**
**acting by **self-tasked Dao/Way (ziran zhi dao)****,
hence they are called **greater (shang).**
**Though they are supreme, they dwell on the right,**
**for killing belongs to darkness (yin).**
**This is likened to the rites of mourning.**
○ *Exegesis:* **The greater general on the right—**
**funeral rites are also placed on the right,**
**for the dead are honored in darkness (yin).**
○ *Commentary:* **Left is the auspicious position (jwei),**
**right is the mourning position (sangwei).**
**Now, the lesser general is placed on the left,**
**while the greater general is stationed on the right,**
**to show that though they hold authority over life and death,**
**they are treated with the solemnity of funeral rites (sangli).**

**When many are killed, one mourns with sorrow and weeping,**
○ *Exegesis:* **The harm to one’s own virtue (de) is thin,**
**unable to transform others with Dao/Way (bu de yidao hua ren),**
**yet harming the innocent (wu gui zhi ren).**
○ *Exegesis:* **With the reverence for living beings (shengling zhi gui),**
**yet war causes slaughter,**
**one feels pity (cexin zhi xin),**
**hence they mourn with sorrow and weeping.**
○ *Commentary:* **In battle, destruction and slaughter are inevitable,**
**so the greater general mourns with sorrow,**
**pitying the wounded and slain,**
**not because they employ Dao/Way,**
**but fearing that innocent lives may be recklessly harmed.**
**One man’s lament is called "呼嗟" (huxie),**
**the enemy is "敌人" (diren),**
**and the many are the soldiers (shizu).**
**The harm to one’s own virtue is thin,**
**yet unable to transform others with Dao/Way,**
**and harming the innocent—**
**then the heart is filled with sorrow and pity,**
**and they weep,**
**making clear that they do not delight in it.**

○ *Exegesis:* **One soldier’s lament (yifu huxie) causes the Dao/Way to falter.**
**One village’s discord (yili weihe) alters the harmony of yin and yang.**
**Zou Yan’s frost (Zou Yan jiangshuang) descends,**
**and the walls of Qí’s wife (Qici cheng hui) are destroyed,**
**even commoners and ordinary women (pifu limifu)**
**feel the shifts of heaven and earth,**
**how much more so when**
**corpses sprawl across vast plains (hengshi juyě),**
**bones scatter along the rivers (sangǔ chángyuán),**
**flesh is fed to carrion birds (rouci wuyuan),**
**and blood flows over crimson grass (xue liu jiangcao)?**
**Pitying the mutilated and wounded—**
**how could one not grieve?**

**Victory in battle is treated with funeral rites.**
○ *Exegesis:* **In ancient times, when victorious in war,**
**the general occupied the mourning master’s position (sangzhu zhi wei),**
**wearing plain garments and weeping,**
**to show that the gentleman values virtue (de) over arms (bing),**
**and though compelled to punish inauspicious acts (bu xiang zhu sha),**
**their heart does not rejoice in it,**
**comparing it to mourning (bi yu sang).**
**Knowing that future generations will not cease from using arms,**
**hence they mourn with sorrow and pain.**
○ *Exegesis:* **The brave warrior enters battle and gains victory,**
**victory brings them rank,**
**yet the right is not auspicious—**
**hence they are treated with funeral rites.**
**But if arms are merely regarded as inauspicious instruments,**
**why then must they don plain garments as a sign of mourning?**

○ *Commentary:* **The gentleman and the virtuous (junzi shanren)**
**value the ability to employ Dao/Way.**
**When matters cannot be avoided (shi bu de yi),**
**they are then treated with funeral rites.**
**Though martial prowess is inferior to cultural refinement (wu bu ji wen),**
**it clearly shows that expedience (quan) is inferior to principle (shi).**
**In governing the state, one must use force when compelled (bu de yi yong wu),**
**and employ expedience when necessary (bu de yi xing quan),**
**by this standard of measure,**
**one knows that expedience is weaker than principle.**

**Dao/Way is always nameless.**
○ *Exegesis:* **Dao/Way can be dark (yin) or bright (yang),**
**can relax or tighten,**
**can preserve or perish,**
**hence it has no fixed name.**
○ *Exegesis:* **Dao/Way’s application is its constancy (yongyong wei chang),**
**constancy can respond to all things,**
**yet its response is not of human making,**
**hence it is said that Dao/Way is always nameless.**
○ *Commentary:* **The principle of empty and boundless harmony (xutong zhi li)**
**is always still and settled (chang zhan ningran),**
**neither form nor sound,**
**without name or character.**
**Silent and solitary, it transcends the four extremes (chao si ju zhi duan),**
**dim and elusive (huanghuo xiyi),**
**separate from all contrariness (li bai fei zhi wai),**
**how could it be sought through words and symbols (bi yu yin xiang qiu)?**
**How could one measure it with the mind’s intelligence (an yong xin zhi ce)?**
**Hence the following text says,**
*"Heaven and earth began..."*
**and again,**
*"I do not know its name."*

**Though unadorned simplicity (pǔ) is small,**
**none under heaven dares to serve it as a subject.** #1

「Commentary:
The Dao/Way is simple and unadorned though small,
subtle and formless beyond measure—
none under heaven dares to subject it as a lord.

○ *御 says:* "Simple" refers to the wondrous, singular origin,
hence it is called *small*.
Yet when it manifests and fulfills its function,
it becomes supremely vast—
thus none dare to make the Dao/Way their subject.

○ *Cū says:* "Simple" means pure and unadorned.
"Small" means subtly profound.
The simple Dao, though in its own fineness,
can cultivate yin and yang,
order the myriad things,
and sustain the daily lives of the people—
yet they remain unaware,
and none dare to claim it as their own.
Again it is said:
*"Things have no high or low—
the Dao is present, and thus it is honored."*
Thus, the recluses—Púchū, Xùyóu, Wángnǎi, and Nièquē—
these sages,
though reduced to poverty as common folk,
were never subjects of the Son of Heaven
nor friends of the feudal lords—
this is their very essence.

If lords and kings could uphold it,
all things would come of their own accord in submission.

Commentary:
If lords and kings could guard the Dao/Way and act without contrivance,
all things would spontaneously submit and follow its virtue.

○ *Cū says:* When a ruler aligns with the Dao’s virtue
and harmonizes with the order of yin and yang,
then the four seasons proceed like jade candles,
the seven luminaries move without fault,
and auspicious signs—strange plants, sweet dew—
abound, leaving no want in the land.
How could this not be so?
The people need no command to be naturally balanced.

Commentary:
*Balanced* means harmonious.
*None* means without.
When virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth,
and clarity unites the three luminaries,
the people require no edicts—
they are spontaneously at peace.
The power of the Dao is beyond reckoning;
its guardianship brings such benefits.

When the Dao first established names,
Commentary:
*The Dao* refers to the Way,
*names* to the myriad things.
The nameless Dao governs the named,
the formless governs the formed.

○ *Cū says:* The named arises from the nameless,
the named illuminates the nameless—
only then can one guide the lost
and lead them to awakening.

When names have already been established,
Commentary:
*Exhausted* means fully realized.
When the named things are complete in their desires,
they rebel against the Dao and forsake virtue,
and thus bring ruin upon themselves.

Heaven too will know when to stop,
Commentary:
If one follows the Dao and practices virtue,
Heaven itself will know when to cease.

○ *Cū says:* The Dao has no designation,
yet it descends to establish names—
its intent is to guide beings toward their true path,
returning them to their root.
Once the metaphor is understood,
one must cease clinging to names
and seek the reality beyond—
lest one be ensnared by the snare,
losing sight of fish and hare alike.

Knowing when to stop is how one avoids peril.

Commentary:
To know when to stop is to invite the aid of the numinous—
then there is no longer danger.

○ *Cū says:* Once the snare is forgotten,
the subtle truth is attained.
When names converge with reality,
there is no peril.

The Dao/Way in the world is like rivers and valleys to the sea.
Commentary:
The Dao/Way in the world,
harmonizing with all things,
is like rivers and valleys flowing into the sea.

○ *Cū says:* The sea excels in receiving all rivers—
the Sage’s emptiness receives all beings.
Thus it is said:
*"The sea is king of the rivers because it excels in descending."*

To know people is wisdom,
Commentary:
To discern people’s likes and dislikes—
this is wisdom.

○ *Cū says:* To perceive the nature of others—discerning their subtleties, whether profound or shallow—without failing to know them fully: this is *other-heart wisdom*. To know oneself is clarity.

*Commentary:* When one knows their own virtues and flaws, this is *listening in silence* and *gazing inward without form*—thus it is called clarity.

○ *Yuè says:* The wise employ their intelligence to discern things, while the enlightened illuminate subtleties with radiant insight. Intelligence may have its limits, but enlightenment knows no bounds.

○ *Cū says:* To know one’s past lives—both merit and sin—and the retributions of the three ages with perfect clarity: this is *host-fate wisdom*.

To overcome others is strength,
*Commentary:* Those who overcome others do so only through coercive power.

○ *Cū says:* Intelligence and strength overcome others; might subdues beings. Thus one subdues celestial demons and eliminates heterodox paths—this is *subduing-demons wisdom*.

○ *Gù says:* Talent and capability may overcome others, but strength alone is fleeting. Those with strength must have adversaries. If one has adversaries, strength is not yet absolute. But to master one’s nature and temper emotions—that is *self-overcoming*. Those who overcome themselves have no rival within themselves, and thus may be called truly strong.

*Self-overcoming is strength.*
*Commentary:* When one conquers their own desires, none in the world can contend with them—thus they are strong.

○ *Yuè says:* Those who can conquer others are merely strong in power. But those who conquer their own hearts, softening them, alone preserve their true strength.

○ *Cū says:* To subdue one’s own mind, to master all entangling desires—when desires no longer arise, wisdom grows ever stronger: this is *severing-delusion wisdom*.

To know contentment is wealth,
*Commentary:* When one knows sufficiency as sufficiency, they preserve fortune and blessing eternally—thus they are wealthy.

○ *Cū says:* To guard one’s portion and know contentment is not merely to avoid poverty in wealth, but to be rich in virtue and morality. Further, to discern presence and absence—when all realms are illuminated, wisdom knows no lack, and thus is called *wealth*—this is *contentment wisdom*.

To act with vigor is resolve.
*Commentary:* When one exerts strength in doing good, they are said to have resolve in the Dao, and the Dao responds in kind.

○ *Jié says:* To practice the Dao without rest, day and night.

○ *Yuè says:* Those who know contentment lack greed—thus they are wealthy. Those who act with vigor never falter—thus they possess resolve and principle.

○ *Cū says:* *Resolve* is principle. When resolve is strong, great vows arise—prioritizing others over self, sacrificing one’s life to benefit beings, even in the final moments, never retreating.

To never lose one’s place is longevity.
*Commentary:* When one preserves their vital essence, cherishing the heavens’ refined *qi*, they endure.

○ *Jié says:* To never lose one’s singular essence.

○ *Cū says:* Responding to circumstances with appropriate remedies, administering medicine to suit each ailment—all in perfect accord, never straying from what is fitting. Thus, in interacting with beings, one remains enduring and unyielding.

To die yet not perish is longevity.
*Commentary:* Eyes do not gaze wantonly, ears do not listen indiscriminately, the mouth does not speak recklessly—thus no resentment lingers in the world, and one attains true longevity.

○ *Jié says:* Referring to those who achieve *corpse dissolution*—dying only to be reborn.

○ *Cū says:* When both deeds and vows are complete, inner and outer paths aligned, logically there should be no death or birth, no untimely end or extended life. Yet it is said, *"To die yet not perish"*—to clarify that death is not true death, and non-longevity is true longevity. The body may depart, but the Sage’s essence remains unchanged—thus it is called *longevity*.

○ *Yuè says:* Death is the end of one’s appointed fate; perishing is the reversal of heaven’s will; longevity is the fulfillment of one’s allotted span. To know others and overcome them invites calamity, but contentment and vigor align with the natural order. To die yet not perish—this is the completion of one’s span, and thus may be called *longevity*.

The Great Dao is boundless,
*Commentary:* The Dao is vast, like floating and sinking, presence and absence—visible yet unseen, spoken yet indistinct.

○ *Yuè says:* The Great Dao is boundless, unrestrained yet responsive to all things, neither favoring left nor right.

Its left and right are boundless.
*Commentary:* The Dao can turn left or right, fitting all circumstances.

○ *Cū says:* *Boundless* means unrestrained, also universal. The Dao is empty and mysterious, boundless in compassion, responding as it is moved—though neither yin nor yang, it adapts to left and right, revealing that square and round conform to things, exhausting all possibilities without fail. Hence *Zhuangzi* says: *"There is left and right, there is right and wrong."*

All things rely on it to live,
Yet it does not decline.
*Commentary:* All things depend on the Dao for existence, yet it never refuses or resists.

○ *Cū says:* All things rely on the ultimate Dao for their formation, compassionately saving and guiding, never weary of effort. Further, things themselves do not decline—they arise naturally.

Its achievements are unnamed.
*Commentary:* The Dao does not claim credit for its accomplishments.

○ *Yuè says:* All things rely on the harmonious *qi* of the Dao for transformation, yet the Dao does not decline in its labor. When its functions are complete, it does not claim ownership.

○ *Cū says:* It nurtures all forms of life, its subtle work unseen, yet its merit is attributed to the things themselves—thus it does not claim the name of achievement.

It clothes all things yet is not their master—
This may be called *small*.
*Commentary:* The Dao, though nurturing all things, does not rule like a sovereign.

○ *Yuè says:* *Clothing* is akin to nurturing.

○ *Cū says:* It clothes all beings, molding life—yet its divine function remains hidden, not assuming mastery. Humbling itself before things, it is rightly called *small*—though not truly small, but *small in name*.

All things return to it yet it does not rule—
This may be called *great*.
*Commentary:* The Dao is vast as the empty sea, where all rivers converge. It does not rule as a sovereign—its meaning has been explained before. Since all things turn to the Dao, it rightly bears the name of *great*—though not truly great, but *great in name*.

Thus, the Sage never claims greatness,
*Commentary:* The Sage follows the Dao, concealing virtue and hiding renown, not indulging in grandiosity.
And so achieves true greatness.
*Commentary:* The Sage embodies the Dao, acting without speech yet transforming all things, and thus attains true greatness.
*Yuè says:* The Sage follows the Dao and forgets merit, never seeking to magnify itself, and so achieves true greatness.
*Cū says:* The Sage who embodies the Dao forgets self for things, retreats in stillness and humility, never claiming greatness—only prioritizing others over self, forgetting merit and greatness. Thus, it becomes the leader of all beings, alone holding the highest honor in the Three Realms, and attains true greatness.

Holding the Great Symbol, all under Heaven turns toward it.
*Commentary:* *Holding* means upholding. *Symbol* refers to the Dao. The Sage upholds the Great Dao, and thus all people align their hearts and turn toward it. Governing the self, Heaven sends divine clarity, coming and going within it.
*Cū says:* *Holding* means to grasp, yet it is grasping without grasping, grasping nothing. The Great Symbol is like the law of the Great Dao—just as the Sage who forgets greatness upholds the formless Great Symbol, always benefiting things, making all under Heaven turn toward it.
As in *Zhuangzi*: "Wang Dai stood without teaching, sat without ruling, empty in departure, full in return." Confucius led all under Heaven to turn toward him.
Coming and going without harm,
It brings peace, stability, and harmony.
*Commentary:* When all things turn without harm, the realm is secure, families are at peace, and great harmony prevails. Governing the self without harming the divine, the body remains secure and long-lived.
*Yuè says:* Things turn without harm, and thus dwell in great peace.
*Cū says:* *Great* means vast. The common people turn to the most sagely, all gaining benefit without loss. The benefit they gain is the law of peace and stability. Without harm, it reveals the removal of suffering; peace and stability speak of sharing in joy.

Joy and delicacies—
The passing traveler stops.
*Commentary:* *Delicacies* means beauty. The traveler is one. If one takes delight in the Dao’s beauty, they will stop and stay.
One is the absence of excess, dwelling in emptiness, like a passing traveler.

The Dao, when spoken of, is without taste.
*Commentary:* The Dao, entering and exiting the mouth, is insipid—unlike the five flavors of sour, salty, bitter, sweet, and spicy.
*Cū says:* The utmost Dao is empty and silent, its essence subtle and elusive. Thus, its speech is insipid and without allure, unlike music or fine foods that delight the senses. Music has sounds to please, delicacies have flavors to nourish—what is pleasing and satisfying makes even passing travelers stop and linger. But the Dao’s speech is insipid, and few scholars pause to reflect.

Ah, how deeply the world’s folly is obscured!
They can indulge in pleasing sounds,
Yet who can savor the tasteless taste?

It cannot be seen when gazed upon,
*Commentary:* *Sufficient* means attainment. The Dao is formless—unlike the five colors of blue, yellow, red, white, and black that can be perceived.
*Cū says:* The utmost Dao is profound and dark, transcending form and color. Unlike worldly teachings, it cannot be recognized by the eyes.

It cannot be heard when listened to,
*Commentary:* The Dao is unlike the five tones—*gong*, *shang*, *jiao*, *zhi*, and *yu*—that can be heard.
*Cū says:* The great sound is subtle and without pitch. It is not like the tones of *gong* and *shang*, nor can it be heard like bells and drums. The Sage’s speech follows this same principle.

Its use cannot be exhausted.
*Commentary:* Using the Dao to govern the realm brings wealth and prosperity to the people; governing the self extends life—there is no end to its use.
*Yuè says:* With the Dao in stillness, there is no need for spoken instruction. Thus, it cannot be seen, cannot be heard. Pure winds prevail, all things flourish abundantly, and each year’s harvests overflow—so its use cannot be exhausted. *Exhausted* means finished.
*Cū says:* The utmost Dao’s words are insipid and distant, neither sound nor color, transcending sight and hearing. Like a mirror’s reflection, things come and it responds; like the Sage’s wisdom, it responds intuitively. Thus, the use of the non-use is difficult to exhaust.

When one would gather it,
One must first disperse it;
*Commentary:* First, one opens and disperses to indulge in extreme decadence.
*Cū says:* *Gather* means to contract, to restrain. To enact governance and transform all things, making them contract and not be corrupted by luxury, one must first open and indulge them, pushing them to extreme decadence. This was how Laozi, upon entering the Western Regions, first allowed their fierce and unrestrained nature to flourish before transforming them with the Dao.

When one would weaken it,
One must first strengthen it;
*Commentary:* First strengthening it is to invite disaster.
*Cū says:* To make them return to the Dao’s gentle weakness, one must first push them to extreme strength, then guide them toward yielding softness.

When one would abolish it,
One must first establish it;
*Commentary:* First establishing it is to let their arrogance and excess reach danger.
*Cū says:* To abolish their brutality, one must first indulge their ferocity. Once their ferocity reaches its peak, it can be abolished.

When one would take from it,
One must first give to it.
*Commentary:* First giving to it is to indulge their greed.

The *Cū* says:
To indulge their evil first is called *giving* to them.
Later, to make them return to the Dao and goodness is called *taking* from them.
Thus, the Old Master first revealed the common and confused,
So as to indulge their obstinate and perverse nature.
Later, when they witnessed the divine transformations,
The wicked were subdued, and only then could their ferocity be taken away,
And they were led back to the virtuous Dao.
These four paired principles share similar logic—
Only differing in that the initial is shallow and the latter profound.

Those of superior capacity, upon hearing the truth, immediately awaken.
Those of inferior capacity require reliance on expedient means—
This is the *Increased Treatment of the Profound*.
This is called *subtle clarity*.

*Commentary:* These four matters—
Their Dao is subtle, yet their effects are clear.

*Yuè* says:
The Classic states: *"True speech seems contrary."*
The *Yì* says: *"Compliance to act with expedience."*
Expedience is contrary to the Classic yet harmonizes with principle.
Thus, the noble employ expedience when it aligns with principle,
While the petty use it for deceit and guile.
Confucius said: *"One may entrust them with uprightness, but not with expedience."*
Indeed.

Thus, the Old Master in the previous chapter demonstrated the Great Image—
This is called *truth*.
This chapter follows with contraction and expansion—
This is called *expedience*.
To measure the roots of sentient beings,
He thus demonstrated both expedience and truth,
Reversing appearances to reveal essence,
So that they must attain the realm of life and fate.
Yet the deluded claim this is not the intent of the Dao and its virtue—
How deeply they err and fail to awaken!

When one seeks to contract and restrain the desires of sentient beings,
First expand them, indulging their extravagant hearts,
So that they become ensnared in love and desire—
Then contraction and restraint may follow.
The principles of strength and weakness are similarly applied.
This Dao is exceedingly subtle, yet its effects are clearly manifest—
Hence it is called *subtle clarity*.

The *Cū* says:
Though the expedient Dao above is subtle and profound,
Its verification is nonetheless evident.
Gentleness and weakness overcome rigidity and strength.

*Commentary:* Gentleness and weakness endure,
While rigidity and strength perish.

The *Cū* says:
Gentleness and weakness are *true wisdom*,
Rigidity and strength are *expedient wisdom*.
To teach and transform sentient beings,
One must employ expedient wisdom.
In definitive terms, *true wisdom* prevails.

Fish cannot be removed from the depths,
*Commentary:* Fish removed from the depths
Gain softness by losing rigidity—
They can no longer be controlled.

A state’s sharpest tools must not be shown to the people.
*Commentary:* Sharp tools refer to the expedient Dao.
Those who wield state expedience must not reveal it to officials,
And those who practice the Dao of self-cultivation
Must not reveal it to the unworthy.

The *Cū* says:
*Detachment* means *loss*.
Sharp tools are *authority*.
Fish hidden in water, expedience concealed in the heart—
Thus nothing harms them.
Fish lost from water are seized by others,
Expedience revealed to others is controlled by others—
Hence it must not be shown to others.

*End of Volume Three of the Annotations on the Dao De Jing*
#1: Original text reads *"giant"* (巨), corrected to *"subject"* (臣).

*Volume Four of the Annotations on the Dao De Jing*
Narrated by the *Chéngshì* of Wú Commandery, *Gù Huān*.

The Dao is ever without action yet without failing to act.
*Commentary:* The Dao is always without action.
*Exegesis:* The Dao of profound stillness is quiescent and without action, yet from its essence arises function, responding to things and transforming them—hence it is said to be without failing to act. The first sentence is the root, the second the manifestation; this clarifies root and manifestation, the interplay of stillness and motion.

*Cai* says: The Dao’s essence is congealed and still, its true realm unmoving—hence it is said to be without action. Yet it responds to things and moves; nothing is left unused—hence it is said to be without failing to act. If lords and kings could uphold it, all things would spontaneously transform.
*Commentary:* If lords and kings could uphold the Dao, all things would spontaneously transform, benefiting themselves.
*Yu* says: The wondrous root is pure and pristine, hence always without action. Things rely on it to thrive, yet it is without failing to act. If lords and kings could uphold the Dao’s non-action, all things would spontaneously transform. The ruler’s non-action brings the world to simplicity and purity.
*Exegesis:* Even sovereigns of ten thousand chariots, rulers of the five ranks—if they could uphold this Dao, the eight extremes and myriad lands would naturally follow its transformation. The same applies to commoners. The reason this passage emphasizes lords and kings is to show that rulers lead transformation, benefiting and expanding for all.
*Du* says: Virtue can subdue things; the Dao is present, and thus the sovereign is honored. Kings who uphold the Dao do not command—yet all is balanced. Dukes and marquises embrace virtue without severity, yet all is transformed.
*Luo* (Rushen) *Inner Explanation* says: The mind attains emptiness yet does not lose its resources; all spirits follow transformation, and evil is subdued, returning to the right path.

When transformation stirs to act, I shall restrain it with the nameless Primal Simplicity.
*Commentary:* "I" refers to the self; the nameless Primal Simplicity is the Dao. All things transform, benefiting themselves.
*Yu* says: When people have followed the ruler’s transformation—non-action, pure and pristine—and yet still desire to act and do, I shall restrain them with the nameless Primal Simplicity, which is the Dao.
*Exegesis:* "Act" means to begin cultivation. "Primal Simplicity" is the Dao. It says that when beings first begin cultivation, they must have desires for fruition. Once such desires arise, one must settle their mind with the nameless, pristine Dao, so that it is not stained by the worldly—this is to dispel the "having" with the "non."
*Gu* says: "Act" means deceit arises. It says that when a lord upholds the Dao, all things follow transformation, yet someone from within this harmony might harbor deceitful intentions. The Master says, "I shall restrain them with the Dao." Again: "Make their hearts chaotic, so that there is no distinction." Again: "The nameless Primal Simplicity is instruction." The nameless Primal Simplicity also does not desire; not desiring is stillness.
*Commentary:* It says that when a ruler restrains with virtue and the Dao, the people will also not desire, hence one must guide them with purity and stillness.
*Exegesis:* Not only must one not desire the "having" of laws, but one must also not desire the "non" of this nameless purity. Earlier, the "non" dispelled the "having"; now, the "non" dispels the "non." Having and non are both dispelled, departing from the single Middle Way. The world will then spontaneously rectify itself.
*Commentary:* Stillness is cessation. Earlier, the nameless dispelled the having; next, not desiring dispelled the non. Having and non having are both dispelled, not desiring is then extinguished, and not desiring removed, the single Middle is effaced. This is dispelling again and again, the mysterious beyond the mysterious—what is called probing the subtle, seeking the hidden, exhausting principle, and perfecting nature. Then all dharmas are without exception true and genuine; even weeds and tiles are all the utmost Dao—hence it is said the world spontaneously rectifies itself. This one sentence seals the gate of all mysteries.
*Yu* says: The nameless Primal Simplicity also does not desire—it says that when the ruler has restrained with the nameless Primal Simplicity, the common people must not cling to this namelessness, lest they seek to advance and lose their root, falling back into action. Hence, even this nameless Primal Simplicity must be forgotten. Not desiring desire, not desiring is also forgotten—pure and pristine, the world spontaneously rectifies itself.
Again: The Dao is ever without desire, delighting in purity, hence it makes heaven and earth ever rectified. Heaven and earth are the Dao’s subjects; when the ruler follows the Dao and issues precepts, the hearts of all subjects will spontaneously rectify.

The highest virtue does not claim virtue, hence it has virtue;
*Commentary:* The highest virtue refers to the ancient sovereign without name—his virtue is great yet unnamed, hence it is called highest virtue. "Not claiming virtue" means he does not teach virtue to the people; he follows nature, nurturing human life and vitality, his virtue unseen—hence it is said he does not claim virtue. It says his virtue aligns with heaven and earth, harmonious *qi* flowing, and the people are thereby preserved.
*Exegesis:* "Highest" means elevated, a name for transcendence. "Virtue" means attainment, with the sense of achieving. It says those of highest virtue intuitively grasp the Dao’s realm; their insight is obscure yet unified, attainment and loss both forgotten. Attainment and loss forgotten—hence it is said they do not claim virtue. Insight obscure yet unified—hence it is said they have virtue. Having virtue dispels loss; not claiming virtue dispels attainment.

The lower virtue does not lose virtue, hence it has no virtue.
*Commentary:* The lower virtue refers to the named sovereign—his virtue does not reach that of the highest, hence it is called lower virtue. "Not losing virtue" means his virtue is visible, his deeds praiseworthy. Since he bears a name and title, it is said he has no virtue.
The highest virtue acts without action, yet there is nothing it does not act upon;
*Commentary:* It says the Dao’s law is tranquil, without contrivance, hence there is no need for a name.
*Exegesis:* "Act upon" means to use. The highest virtue acts without action, reaching the root of congealed stillness, yet its manifestation is empty and wondrous—this is called non-action yet action; action is precisely non-action.

The lower virtue acts with contrivance, yet there is something it does act upon.
*Commentary:* It speaks of a sovereign who enforces teachings and policies, all for his own sake, and claims a name for himself. ○*Exegesis:* Since his heart does not forget virtue, he is called "for it." Yet he still has affairs to attend to, hence it is said he acts with contrivance.

The highest virtue acts with benevolence, yet there is nothing it does not transcend.
*Commentary:* The highest virtue refers to a sovereign who practices benevolence without equal, hence it is called the highest virtue. "Acts with benevolence" means to extend compassion. When deeds are accomplished and affairs established, there is nothing to cling to as a means. ○*Exegesis:* Benevolence is compassion—it arises from a mind of nurturing. Since he does not value reciprocal kindness, it is said he transcends contrivance. Though the highest virtue is not as profound as the lower virtue, in terms of moral rectitude, benevolence surpasses it, hence it is called "transcend contrivance." Within the Three Jewels, compassion is paramount, so among the Five Virtues, benevolence is also placed first.

The highest righteousness acts with contrivance, yet there is something it does act upon.
*Commentary:* It acts with righteousness to sever and cut away—his actions are for his own sake, killing to establish his authority, taxing the people to sustain his own indulgence. ○*Exegesis:* Righteousness upholds the correction of evil and the punishment of wrongdoing, as seen in the scriptures’ precepts and disciplines. Yet the essence of these precepts, when applied to lower beings, still involves contrivance; they have not yet reached the realm of empty and wondrous non-action, hence it is said he acts with contrivance.

The highest propriety acts, yet none respond to it.
*Commentary:* It refers to a sovereign whose propriety is without equal. "Acts" means to establish rites, institutions, and ceremonial authority. It speaks of propriety being ornate yet inwardly decayed, adorned with falsehood and excessive detail—any movement departs from the Dao, and thus it cannot be responded to. ○*Exegesis:* Propriety emphasizes ceremonial decorum, as seen in the scriptures’ teachings on fasting, rituals, and etiquette. Moreover, the highest Dao is boundless and tranquil, its great form without substance; yet now it is devalued, the simple esteemed over the elaborate, the plain over the ornate, and only ceremonial authority is upheld. Though it may be a gradual teaching, it fails to align with true principle, not only contradicting the Dao internally but also unable to harmonize with the external world, hence it is said none respond to it. ○*Lu’s Saying:* The rites of jade and silk are used as tokens for betrothal, hence it is called "highest." Rank and title are conferred in due measure, hence it is said "acts." Yet with so many ceremonial details, the jade and silk are exhausted, and the people cannot sustain the sovereign, hence it is said "none respond to it." Because none respond, punishments are enforced. Then, he raises his arm in anger and drags others into conflict.
*Commentary:* It speaks of how, when propriety is too burdensome to be followed, the sovereign and the people grow resentful, hence he raises his arm in anger and drags others into contention. ○*Yu’s Saying:* Propriety emphasizes reciprocity and mutual expectation. Because of its shallow observance, he raises his arm in anger and drags others into conflict, desiring to make clear the flaws of teaching propriety. Hence, *Zhuangzi* says: "Rujiu is thin, yet Handan was besieged"—this is its case. Thus, when the Dao is lost, virtue alone remains.

「○*Commentary:* Virtue here refers to the **highest virtue** (上德). When the **Dao/Way** declines and the people grow weary, its transformative power fades, and only then does **virtue/power** manifest. It responds to things according to their time, but **virtue/power** of the lower order appears only when the **Dao/Way** is lost.

When **virtue/power** is lost, **benevolence** (仁) emerges,
　　○*Commentary:* It means that as **virtue/power** wanes, **benevolence** and love take its place.

When **benevolence** is lost, **righteousness** (義) arises,
　　○*Commentary:* When **benevolence**’s compassion fails to prevail, **righteousness**’s distinctions then arise.

When **righteousness** is lost, **propriety** (礼) is practiced.

　　○*Yu’s Saying:* Those who lose the **Dao/Way** lose the **highest virtue**, for the **highest virtue** aligns with the **Dao/Way**, hence it is said "lose the **Dao/Way**."

Now, **virtue/power**, **benevolence**, **righteousness**, and **propriety** are merely the names of refined and coarse customs in different times. Thus, when the **Dao/Way** declines, **virtue/power** appears; when **virtue/power** is lost, **benevolence** remains; when **benevolence** fades, **righteousness** stands forth; when **righteousness** is gone, **propriety** is resorted to—these are all fitting measures for their times. Therefore, when discussing **propriety** in the age of simplicity and purity (淳朴), it is either madness or perversity; when **propriety** is forgotten in the age of artificiality and corruption (浇醨), it is either foolishness or slander. If one can dissolve and transcend these layers, they should abandon **propriety** to uphold **righteousness**, abandon **righteousness** to uphold **benevolence**, abandon **benevolence** to uphold **virtue/power**, and forget **virtue/power** to return to the **Dao/Way**, thus restoring simplicity and purity. Then, the **highest virtue** will have nothing to rely upon.

　　○*Commentary:* From the **Dao/Way**’s decline in the age of artificiality and corruption to its uttermost extreme, unable to curb evil, **propriety** is then employed for ritual and etiquette. Though it may forcefully establish ceremonial decorum, its superficiality only grows worse.

**Propriety** is the thinning of **loyalty and trustworthiness** (忠信),
　　○*Commentary:* It means that **propriety**, though it regulates external forms, neglects its roots—**loyalty and trustworthiness**—and thus these virtues grow ever more shallow.

And the beginning of **chaos**.

　　○*Commentary:* **Propriety** values outward display over inner sincerity, so the upright and honest dwindle while licentiousness and disorder flourish.

　　○*Yu’s Saying:* **Propriety** is meant to rescue **chaos**, yet it esteems rigid knowledge. Those who cling to **propriety**’s outward forms—who prize jade and silk, who revere bowing and kneeling—are the very ones who become the starting point of **foolishness and ignorance**.

　　○*Commentary:* Those who claim to know without true understanding are called **pretense of knowledge** (前识). This is the case of those who have lost the **Dao/Way**’s substance but cling to its superficial trappings. Such **pretense of knowledge** is the origin of **stupidity and darkness**.

　　○*Gu’s Saying:* **Pretense of knowledge** refers to those who do not await deep inquiry. Now, the pure and subtle **Dao/Way** is profound beyond comprehension, formless and colorless—its truth lies within itself. Yet those who rely on elaborate discourse and ornate displays, who flaunt ceremonial splendor and arrayed beauty, these are not the **Dao/Way**’s true essence but its superficial adornments, the beginning of **stupidity**.

When **stupidity** first takes root, it has not yet encountered the **Dao/Way**’s true reality, so it is led astray by **pretense of knowledge**. The **Dao/Way**’s superficiality corrupts its very beginning. The *Sequence of Families* says: This interpretation is the most refined.

Therefore, the **great man** (大丈夫) dwells in the **thick substance** (厚),
　　○*Commentary:* **Great man** refers to one of **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**—a leader who sustains the people with courage, compassion, and wisdom. His aspirations are vast and universal, hence the name **great man**.

But not in the **thin veneer** (薄).

　　○*Yu’s Saying:* **Artifice** (有为) is the **Dao/Way**’s thinness. **Propriety** and **righteousness** are the **virtue/power**’s superficial adornments. Thus, the sage dwells in the **Dao/Way**’s **non-artifice** (无为), which is its **thick substance**.

　　○*Commentary:* The **great man** of the **Dao/Way**, the transcendent scholar, should anchor his emotions in **virtue/power**’s true essence, not fixate on the **pretense of knowledge**’s superficial trappings. Hence, he discards the latter for the former.

　　○*Commentary:* Discarding the **thin veneer** means severing **benevolence** and **righteousness**. Taking the **thick substance** means returning to simplicity and purity. Moreover, the **highest virtue** of the sage embodies **non-duality** (无分别), so how could he ever fixate on distinctions or be entangled in discarding and taking? Indeed, it is not a matter of discarding what is not to be discarded, nor of taking what is not to be taken. The same holds true for the **Dao/Way**’s duality of superficiality and substance.

In ancient times, those who attained **Oneness** (一):
　　○*Yu’s Saying:* **Oneness** is the **wonderful harmony of pure and subtle qi** (冲和之妙气).

　　○*Commentary:* **Ancient** refers to the past. It speaks of those who attained the **Dao/Way** in bygone ages, and their condition is fully described in the following text. This is **Yu** citing the past to elucidate the present.

The heavens attained **Oneness** with **clarity** (清),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It means that the **muddy pellet** (泥丸) within the human head emits **pure qi**, which descends to illuminate, hence it is said "with **clarity**."

　　○*Commentary:* The heavens absorb the **Dao/Way**’s **qi**, thus gathering **yang** to form the sky, which remains **clear and elevated** above.

The earth attained **Oneness** with **tranquility** (宁),
　　○*Commentary:* The earth, too, absorbs the **Dao/Way**’s **Oneness**, thus congealing **yin** to form the earth, which remains **tranquil and still** below.

　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It means that the **elixir field** (丹田) does not indulge in licentiousness or disorder, and **spirit** (神) resides within the body, hence it is said "with **tranquility**."

The **spirit** (神) attained **Oneness** with **aliveness** (灵),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* **Spirit** refers to the **heart** (心). The **heart** is the **alive spirit** of the five **zang** organs (五藏), hence it is said "with **aliveness**."

　　○*Commentary:* It means that the **spirit** attains **Oneness**, hence its ability to **transform and manifest without form**.»

The **Dao/Way** dwells in **yin**, dark and unfathomable,
　　○*Commentary:* By its **Oneness**, it achieves boundless **aliveness** and **wisdom**, hence *Zhuangzi* says: *"The spirits and the **Dao/Way**’s **Imperial Sovereign** (神帝) generate the heavens and the earth."*

The **valleys** (谷) attained **Oneness** with **fullness** (盈),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* The **valleys**—that is, the **Huaxi Palace** (华池宫)—can summon the **elixir spring** (醴泉), hence it is said "with **fullness**."

　　○*Commentary:* The **valleys** (谷) are mountain basins. By attaining **Oneness**, they can nourish flowing springs and fill the **Han moats** (汉壑) with abundance.

The **ten thousand things** (万物) attained **Oneness** with **birth** (生),
　　○*Commentary:* All sentient and insentient beings, collectively called the **ten thousand things**, share **Oneness** with the **Dao/Way**, hence they come to **birth** and **manifestation**.

　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It means that all things, though of varied forms, equally attain **Oneness** with the **Dao/Way** to bring forth life, hence it is said "with **birth**."

The **feudal lords and kings** (侯王) attained **Oneness** to govern the **world** (天下) with **rectitude** (正).
　　○*Commentary:* Feudal lords and kings alike observe the **Dao/Way** to transform, and by embracing **non-action** (无为) to attain **Oneness**, they can administer the world’s **affairs** (政). The examples are **Fangxun** (放勋) and **Wenming** (文命).

　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It refers to the **spleen** (脾). Positioned in the **central yellow** (中黄), it governs the four directions through **Oneness**, hence it is said "with **rectitude**."

The **Dao/Way’s** **manifestation** (致) is thus,
　　○*Yu’s Interpretation:* When things attain the **Dao/Way**, they are named accordingly. The **Dao/Way** persists, names stand; the **Dao/Way** fades, reality perishes. Hence, the heavens’ **clarity**, the earth’s **tranquility**, the **spirit’s** **aliveness**, and the **valleys’** **fullness** all arise from **wondrous application** (妙用) of the **Dao/Way**, thus it is said "its **manifestation**."

　　○*Commentary:* **Manifestation** (致) means **attainment**. It unifies the preceding six affairs, all of which attain **Oneness** with the **Dao/Way**. The *Inner Resolution* says: "This is the **sixfold practice** (六种行门) of a single individual."

　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It means that the heavens, earth, humans, **spirit**, **elixir waters**, **springs**, and the **ten thousand things** all attain **Oneness** to manifest, hence it is said "its **manifestation**."

The heavens, without **clarity**, would fear **rupture** (裂),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* If the **marrow pellet** (泥丸) does not attain **Oneness**, then the **brain** (脑) will wither, the **head’s white** (桔头白) will fade, and the **teeth** (齿) will fall out, hence it is said "rupture."

The earth, without **tranquility**, would fear **outburst** (发),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* If the **elixir field** (丹田) does not attain **Oneness**, then the **essence-qi** (精气) will dissipate, hence it is said "fear **outburst**."

　　○*Commentary:* **Outburst** (发) means **change and movement**. If the earth cannot apply the **Dao/Way** to remain **tranquil**, then disasters like **collapse** (崩沦) may arise. In ancient times, the **Qishan mountain** (岐山) collapsed, causing the **three rivers** (三川) to tremble—this is the case.

The **spirit**, without **aliveness**, would fear **extinction** (歇),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* If the **heart** (心) is disordered and does not uphold **Oneness**, then the body will **hollow out** (空) and age prematurely, hence it is said "fear **extinction**."

　　○*Commentary:* **Extinction** (歇) means **decay**. If the **spirit** does not rely on the **Dao/Way**, then **wisdom and application** (智用) will grow **stupid and sluggish** (愚怠), lacking **aliveness**, and will fear **extinction** and **obliteration**.

The **valleys**, without **fullness**, would fear **exhaustion** (竭),
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* If the **Huaxi Palace** (华池宫) loses its **Oneness**, then it will lack **vital fluids** (津液), hence it is said "exhaustion."

　　○*Commentary:* **Exhaustion** (竭) means **blockage**. If the **valleys** (川谷) deviate from **Oneness**, then calamities will begin, causing the **springs** to dry up and the **flowing channels** (流注) to stagnate.

The **ten thousand things**, without **birth**, would fear **annihilation** (灭),
　　○*Commentary:* All the **ten thousand things** attain **birth** through **Oneness** with the **Dao/Way**; if they lose the **Dao/Way** and deviate from **truth**, then they will perish in an instant.

The **feudal lords and kings**, without **nobility and elevation** (贵高), would fear **overthrow** (蹶),
　　○*Yu’s Interpretation:* Those who attain **Oneness** must not boast of their **application**. The **Sages** (圣人) taught that their purpose is for the **living beings** (生灵), though they may elevate the heavens and earth to **clarity** and **tranquility**, their ultimate convergence lies in the **feudal lords and kings** practicing **humility** (谦和) and adhering to the **Dao/Way of female stillness** (雌静), thus avoiding **overthrow** or **annihilation**.

　　○*Commentary:* **Overthrow** (蹶) means **ruin**. If rulers cannot apply the **Dao/Way** to maintain their **nobility**, acting against **principle** (理) and **virtue** (德), then their **divine instruments** (神器) will fall into **disorder**, and their **states** (社稷) will face **peril**. The tyrants **Jie of Xia** (夏桀) and **Zhou of Shang** (殷纣) are such examples.

Thus, **nobility** must take **humility** as its foundation,
　　○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It means that those who seek **long life** (长生) must regard **original qi** (元气) as their **mother**, hence it is said "its foundation."

　　○*Commentary:* **Nobility** (贵) refers to **elevation** and **exaltation**. **Humility** (贱) means **lowliness**.

The ruler who attains **Oneness** (得一之君) thus remains humble,
while the ruler who loses the **Dao/Way** (失道之主) falls into arrogance and pride.
Hence, arrogance and pride are the foundation of lowliness,
and humility is the root of exaltation.
By measuring gain and loss,
one establishes this admonition.

**Exaltation** must take **humility** as its foundation.
○*Annotation:* It means that if one seeks true nobility,
they must take **humility** as their foundation—
just as when building walls or structures,
one must first establish a low base;
if the foundation is not firm,
the structure will inevitably collapse.

○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It means that those who cultivate **Dao/Way** to nourish **essence** (精) and supplement the **marrow** (脑) must use the **elixir field** (丹田) as their foundation—
thus, it is the foundation.

○*Gu’s Commentary:* Those who speak of **exaltation** taking **humility** as its foundation
do not attain nobility by themselves—
they are elevated by what the lowly revere.
They do not rise by themselves—
they are borne aloft by what is beneath them.
Thus, nobility depends on humility—
how could one not take **humility** as the root?

Therefore, the wise cultivate the foundation,
and thus remain humble without peril.
The foolish pursue superficiality,
and thus reach for heights only to fall.

Hence, rulers call themselves **"lonely and bereft"** (孤寡).
○*Annotation:* **"Lonely"** (孤) signifies solitude,
**"bereft"** (寡) signifies inability—
like a wheel hub (毂) that cannot be the center
for all spokes to gather.
Though kings and lords are most exalted,
they call themselves thus—
is this not taking **humility** as their foundation,
to enlighten the people?

○*Commentary:* **"Bereft"** (不毂) means unworthy.
Rulers, wise and enlightened,
know that arrogance is the root of self-destruction,
while humility is the foundation of true nobility.
Thus, they govern the common people,
shepherding themselves with humility.

○*Gu’s Commentary:* **"Bereft"** (不毂) means unworthy.
It means that **virtue** (德) is not something to be worshipped—
one cannot command the words of the multitude.
All three of these—
the ruler’s self-designation, the people’s revulsion,
and the ruler’s own humility—
are ways of taking **humility** as the foundation.
Is this not so?
○*Annotation:* **"Is this not so?"** (非乎) is merely an exclamatory rhetorical question.

○*Jie’s Interpretation:* It is not that their **Dao/Way** is **Original Oneness** (元一).
Thus, they achieve **"ten thousand chariots without a chariot"** (致数车无车).
○*Annotation:* **"Achieve"** (致) means to gather—
people gather around the chariot’s components:
spokes, wheels, hub, yoke, axle, and shaft,
yet none of these is called a chariot;
only when assembled do they become one.
Thus, rulers do not name themselves by their exalted titles,
and so their nobility is complete.

○*Commentary:* The box, cover, hub, and rim—
artificial combinations—
though called a chariot, lack true substance.
The five elements and four great forces are likewise illusory;
since the body is inherently empty,
where then would nobility reside?
Thus, when in a position of nobility,
one must practice humility.

○*Cai’s Commentary:* **"Praise"** (誉) refers to both praise and blame.
It means that a **Dao/Way**-governed ruler,
humble in self-cultivation,
forgets distinctions of nobility and lowliness,
and thus transcends both praise and blame.
Though people frequently extol his virtues,
he does not consider himself noble;
though they frequently condemn his failings,
he does not consider himself lowly.

○*Gu’s Commentary:* It means that the **noble person** (君子) establishes **moral cultivation** (道德功行) in mind and body.
Without virtue to display,
without traces to reveal,
their roots run deep and their foundations are firm—
unshaken by praise or blame,
desire or aversion.

**"Do not be polished like jade, nor rigid like stone."**
○*Yu’s Annotation:* **"Jing-jing"** (琭琭) describes jade’s appearance,
**"luo-luo"** (落落) describes stone’s appearance.
This is taking **humility** as the foundation.

**"The inverted is the movement of the Dao."**
○*Annotation:* This clarifies **strategic adaptation** (权).
**"The inverted"** refers to reversing conventional principles to align with meaning.

**"The weak is the application of the Dao."**
○*Annotation:* This clarifies **substance** (实).

The weak is taken for its yielding softness and receptive stillness—
receptive stillness is how the Sage abides in substance.
To abide in substance is the Dao’s constant application,
thus it is said: *The weak is the Dao’s use.*

All things under Heaven are born of *having*,
*—for all things arise from Heaven and Earth’s form and place.*
Having is born of *non-being*,
*—for Heaven and Earth’s mysteries, the fluttering and crawling of life,*
*all arise from the Dao, which is formless and nameless.*
This speaks of the root prevailing over the flourishing,
the weak prevailing over the strong,
humility prevailing over fullness.

○*Yu’s Commentary:*
Substance and strategy are like non-being generating having—
thus those who practice strategy must return to substance,
and those who employ having must rely on non-being.
Yet the ultimate Dao is boundless and silent,
free from names and forms.
All dharmas are of empty nature,
not dependent on each other.
If one can forget both strategy and substance,
and merge having and non-being,
then neither counting nor not-counting exists—
this may be called transcendence.

○*Exegesis:*
*Having* is the Dao’s manifestation—
the so-called *One Qi of non-being*.
The primal One is the wondrous root—
the so-called *silent and empty realm*.
All things under Heaven arise from the Dao’s manifested laws,
and these laws themselves arise from the wondrous root—
the ultimate non-being.

The superior person hears the Dao and strives to practice it.
*—The superior person, upon hearing the Dao, exerts utmost effort to practice it.*

○*Exegesis:*
The superior in wisdom, hearing the Dao,
instantly comprehends that all phenomena are empty,
thus they diligently cultivate without hesitation or laziness.

The middle person hears the Dao and holds it uncertainly.
*—The middle person, hearing the Dao,*
*seeks longevity through self-cultivation,*
*and seeks peace for the realm,*
*yet when confronted with wealth, desire, or honor,*
*they are confused by passions and abandon it.*

○*Yu’s Commentary:*
The middle person may ascend or descend,
thus they are doubtful—
sometimes holding, sometimes losing.

○*Exegesis:*
The middle in wisdom, dim in insight,
cannot fully grasp the principles.
Though they hear the Dao, they fail to penetrate its depths.
If they restrain their emotions and return to stillness,
they may briefly attain emptiness of mind,
but once immersed in worldly affairs,
they become entangled in the realm of having,
vacillating between gain and loss—
thus it is said: *uncertain holding or losing.*

The inferior person hears the Dao and laughs at it.
*—The inferior person, greedy and desiring,*
*sees the Dao’s softness as terrifying,*
*sees its simplicity as vulgar,*
*thus they laugh loudly.*

○*Exegesis:*
The inferior in wisdom, dull and obtuse,
hearing the profound and vast truths of the Dao,
fail to comprehend and deem them empty fables,
thus they scoff and laugh aloud.

If it is not laughed at, it is not the Dao.
*—That which is not laughed at by the inferior is not worthy to be called the Dao.*

○*Exegesis:*
That which is not scorned by the vulgar
is not worthy to be called the Sage’s Way.

Thus, the establishment of speech is as follows:
*—The method of establishing the Dao is as in the six lines below.*

○*Exegesis:*
*Establishment*—to set forth.
The Dao practiced by the superior is established in the following:

The enlightened one appears as if in darkness,
*—The one who illuminates the Dao*
*appears as if blind and ignorant,*
*concealing their brilliance among the common.*

The one advancing in the Dao appears as if retreating,
*—The great scholar*
*does not reveal their progress,*
*though internally cultivating,*
*externally they seem to decline.*

The one in harmony with the Dao blends with the common,
*—*Harmony*—levelness.
The great Daoist does not distinguish themselves,
appearing as if among many.*

The Sage’s virtue is like a deep valley,
*—The Sage of highest virtue*
*is like a valley,*
*unashamed by filth or dampness.*

○*Exegesis:*
The one who excels in moral cultivation
is equal to the boundless void,
thus they can embrace all things,
like an empty valley.
*[Original note:]* Those who forget virtue
do not differ from the vulgar.

The greatly pure appear as if shamed,
*—The greatly pure and virtuous*
*appear as if stained,*
*not displaying themselves.*

○*Exegesis:*
*Purity*—cleanness.
*Shame*—defilement.
The one of great purity and high conduct
blends with the dust,
does not dazzle others,
thus they can mix unnoticed—
like defilement.

Hence, the Eastern Sage purifies their Way
while staining their traces—
this is the very meaning.

The broadly virtuous appear insufficient,
*—The one of vast virtue*
*appears as if foolish and lacking.*

○*Yu’s Commentary:*
Great achievement while holding humility.

○*Exegesis:*
The one bearing great virtue,
abiding in Daoic forgetfulness,
internally overflows,
yet externally appears deficient.

Laozi’s transmission states:
*The skilled merchant hides deeply like emptiness,*
*The Sage of great virtue appears as if foolish.*

The one establishing virtue appears as if deceitful,
*—The one establishing morality*
*appears as if stealing,*
*hiding their name and progress,*
*like one who acts secretly,*
*thus the Classic says:*
*‘As if fearing their neighbors.’*

The genuine appear as if false,
*—The genuine and sincere*
*appear as if their colors fade,*
*unclear.*

○*Yu’s Commentary:*
Pure and harmonious in light.

○*Exegesis:*
*Genuine*—substance.
*False*—change.
The scholar of pure substance,
still yet moving,
true yet responsive,
their changes reveal impermanence—
thus they appear as if false.

The great square has no corners,
*—The great and square*
*has no crookedness or angles.*

○*Exegesis:*
*Corner*—angle.
The one embodying the great square
follows things naturally,
does not cling to principles,
does not establish petty angles—
as said: *‘The superior transcends all nodes.’*

Great vessels take long to mature,
*—The great vessel*
*is like the Nine Cauldrons and coral ornaments,*
*not formed hastily.*

○ *Exegesis:*
The Nine Cauldrons and coral ornaments
are not formed in a single day—
a parable for the scholar of the Great Learning,
who cannot swiftly attain enlightenment.
They must accumulate eons of cultivation and vigilance
before attaining the utmost emptiness—
thus the tale of *Ren’s Son* fishing in astonishment.

○ *Sectional Analysis:*
It speaks of steadfastly abiding in the One without peril,
and thus, in aging, attaining the Dao.

*The Great Sound is rare in voice,*
*—The Great Sound, like thunder,*
*awaits its moment to move—*
*a metaphor for cherishing qi and sparing speech.*

○ *Exegesis:*
*Rare*—as if nonexistent.
The utmost Dao, in its profound silence,
descends from its wondrous origin without a trace of sound.
The Master Blind could hear yet not perceive its voice.
The resonant *Qiong* vibrates through the cosmos,
to illuminate the manifestation as the origin—
thus it is said: *The Great Sound is rare in voice.*

*The Great Image is formless,*
*—The one embodying the Great Law’s symbol*
*is unadorned and without outward appearance.*

○ *Exegesis:*
The Dao’s symbol is formless yet manifest.
*Li Lou* gazes yet sees no shape—
its hues pervade the void,
to reveal the paradox of presence within absence—
hence, *The Great Image is formless.*

*The Way is hidden, nameless.*
*—The Dao’s depths elude all designation.*

○ *Exegesis:*
The utmost Dao, in its mysterious obscurity,
defies measurement by form or instrument—
how could mere names or speech ever explicate it?

Only the Dao
is benevolent in lending and fulfilling.
*—To fulfill*—to complete.

○ *Exegesis:*
*Lending*—borrowing.
This nameless Dao alone possesses boundless compassion,
thus stooping to rescue all beings.
It borrows their virtuous strength,
shielding all classes from poison,
nurturing the Three Realms,
through myriad expedients,
guiding them toward fulfillment.

*The Dao generates the One,*
*The One generates the Two,*
*The Two generates the Three,*
*The Three generates the Ten Thousand Things.*

*—The Dao generates the One*—the Dao’s first generation is the One.
*—The One generates the Two*—the One generates yin and yang.
*—The Two generates the Three*—yin and yang generate the three qi of harmony, purity, and turbidity, partitioning into Heaven, Earth, and Man.
*—The Three generates the Ten Thousand Things*—Heaven, Earth, and Man together generate the Ten Thousand Things, as Heaven bestows, Earth transforms, and Man nourishes them.

○ *Honor’s Commentary:*
*The Dao generates the One*—within the void, qi stirs, hence *the Dao generates*.
Before primordial qi divides, it is called the One.
*The One generates the Two*—pure and turbid separate, yin and yang manifest.
*The Two generates the Three*—the two qi circulate through the Three Realms.
*The Three generates the Ten Thousand Things*—the round Heaven covers above, the square Earth bears below, and Man governs between—what thing fails to emerge?

*The Ten Thousand Things bear yin while embracing yang,*
*—The Ten Thousand Things without fail bear yin while turning toward yang,*
*returning to the heart and seeking the sun.*

○ *Chen’s Commentary:*
Yin and yang are the Dao’s wondrous functions—
*bear* means external, *embrace* means internal.

#1 *The clashing qi harmonizes.*

「Annotations:
*The Ten Thousand Things all contain primordial qi*—
by which they are harmonized and softened.
If in the chest there is a *藏* (hidden essence),
and in the bones there is *髓* (marrow),
then within grasses and trees there is *空虚* (emptiness/voidness),
and the harmonizing qi subtly pervades—
thus they attain longevity.

○ *Honor’s Commentary:*
Yang qi is hot and solitary—
it too cannot generate the Ten Thousand Things.
Yin qi is cold and lone—
it too is insufficient to form shape.
Through the *Great Dao* it is communicated,
and by the *clashing qi* it is harmonized—
thus the Ten Thousand Things come to be.

What men detest,
are the *孤寡不谷* (lonely, widowed, and unblessed),
yet kings and lords take them as their titles.
Annotations:
*The lonely, widowed, and unblessed*—
this is the name for *谷不祥* (unauspicious blessings),
yet kings and lords take them as their titles—
dwelling in humility,
and following the *voidness*, *emptiness*, and *softness*.

○ *Sparse Commentary:*
Loneliness and widowhood are matters of misfortune—
to abhor them is human nature.
Yet kings and nobles employ them to govern themselves,
proving that nobility stems from humility,
and exalted status rests on lowly foundations,
urging those who seek the Dao
to cultivate a humble and yielding heart.
Thus, some things are diminished when increased—
Annotations:
*If one pulls, it cannot be pushed*—
*Wenzi* says: *"To vie is to lack, to yield is to have excess.
To yield is to give rise to propriety and righteousness;
to vie is to spawn violence and chaos."*
—
and increased when diminished.

Annotations:
*Those who heap highness court collapse,
and those who covet wealth invite ruin.*

○ *Master’s Commentary:*
He who diminishes himself is benefited by others.
He who benefits himself is diminished by others.
Thus, those who *朝宗* (return to the root) excel in yielding,
and the humble and weak hold the reins of *德* (virtue).
For *孤寡* (lonely/widowed) to be a title—
is it not fitting?

○ *Master’s Sparse Commentary:*
*Thus*—reiterating the previous point.
*Diminished*—meaning self-abasement.
Speaking of kings and lords who adopt the titles of *孤寡* (lonely/widowed)
to humble themselves,
the people will joyfully promote them,
revering and serving them,
and the governance thus flourishes.
Or, when increased yet diminished—
if kings and nobles indulge their own bodies,
dwell above and grow arrogant,
then those below scatter and the realm collapses.
*The Book of Documents* says:
*"Fullness invites loss, humility invites gain"—
this is what is meant.

○ *Cheng’s Commentary:*
Humility and softness,
diminishing oneself to aid others—
things will naturally gravitate toward him,
thus attaining the Dao and preserving wholeness.
Arrogance and boasting,
increasing oneself to dominate others—
things will resist him,
thus inviting peril and ruin.
Peril and ruin are *损* (loss),
wholeness and life are *益* (gain)—
the verification of loss and gain
is entirely manifest,
thus it is said:
*to diminish is to gain*,
*to increase is to lose*.

What men are taught—
Annotations:
*That the common people are taught to forsake weakness for strength,
and softness for rigidity.*
—
I too instruct them with righteousness.
Annotations:
*Saying that I teach the common people
to forsake strength for weakness,
and rigidity for softness.*

○ *Master’s Commentary:*
The *Laozi* says:
*"When a ruler desires to establish teachings to teach the people,
he must use my doctrine of softness, weakness, humility, and emptiness to instruct them."*

○ *Sparse Commentary:*
The vulgar and those of the *儒* (Confucian) teachings also extol humility and softness,
yet my school’s gate
originally reveres stillness and retreat,
values emptiness and forgetfulness—
thus it differs.

The *强梁* (unyielding/rigid) do not die naturally—
Annotations:
*The unyielding*—those who do not trust in *玄妙* (mystic profundity),
rebel against *道德* (Daoist virtue),
do not follow the *经教* (scriptures and teachings),
and rely on power and force.
*Do not die naturally*—meaning they are cut off by fate,
slain by arms,
executed by royal law,
and do not attain death by natural lifespan.
I shall make them my *教父* (teaching progenitor).
Annotations:
*Progenitor*—the origin.

○ *Master’s Commentary:*
I have seen the unyielding perish,
and the soft and weak preserved—
thus I make this teaching of softness and weakness
the progenitor of all teachings.

○ *Sparse Commentary:*
*Unyielding*—akin to *刚躁* (rigidity and impetuosity).
*Progenitor*—the origin.
Speaking of those who are rigid and unyielding—
they must surely die young,
and cannot rely on fate to die naturally.
Though the *Laozi* expounds the law in myriad ways,
and his teachings are not singular,
he always says that softness and weakness are good,
and rigidity and strength are evil—
this is the most critical point
for those who seek the Dao to grasp first.
*Progenitor*—origin.

○ *Gu’s Commentary:*
Speaking of these rigid and unyielding men—
since they do not die naturally,
I establish this teaching for them,
explaining the *无为* (effortless Daoist virtue),
and making it the foundational progenitor of their instruction.

*The utmost softness in the world,*
*drives through the utmost firmness in the world.*
Annotations:
*The utmost softness*—water.
*The utmost firmness*—metal and stone.
Water can penetrate rigidity,
and enter strength—
it flows through all without obstruction.

○ *Sparse Commentary:*
*Utmost softness*—water.
*Utmost firmness*—metal.
*Drives through*—to penetrate.
Speaking of water’s utmost softness
yet ability to break through metal’s firmness—
it illustrates that the *无为* (effortless Dao)
in its utmost softness
can shatter the burdens of *有为* (worldly striving).
Thus the following passage says:
*"The world’s soft and weak,
none surpass water—
in attacking rigidity and strength,
none can rival it."*

*Without form, it enters the formless.*
Annotations:
*Without form*—the Dao.
The Dao has no substance or quality,
thus it can emerge and enter without obstruction,
communing with the divine,
and benefiting all living things.

○ *Master’s Commentary:*
*Without form*—untainted by mundane realms,
and the heart holds nothing.
*Without obstruction*—the Dao’s nature is naturally pure and clear,
its subtle body unified and complete,
with no gaps or separations.
If one is not disrupted by desires,
and both heart and realm are still,
holding nothing at all,
then the heart aligns with the Dao,
and emerges and enters without obstruction.

○ *Sparse Commentary:*
*Obstruction*—gaps.
Speaking of those who are deluded—
they see emptiness as having form,
while those who perceive truthfully
see form as emptiness—
thus *without form*.
*Enter*—harmonious resonance.
*有为* (worldly striving) is coarse and sparse,
thus it has gaps.
*无为* (effortless Dao) is subtle and refined,
thus it has no obstruction.
Yet in both form and emptiness,
it can enter the formless principle.

○ *Che’s Commentary:*
Speaking of water and wind—
they originally have no substance or form,
metal and stone, marrow—
they too have no gaps or obstructions.
Wind and water can already
penetrate and traverse,
and their attacks are relentless.
The most wondrous Dao
is inherently formless—
karmic entanglements and coarse habits
also lack gaps or openings.
The Dao’s profound grace
has long since entered and permeated—
to trace the roots of their bonds,
to reveal the foundation of their stubbornness,
to administer the medicine of awakening,
to open the mechanism of their true nature—
this is *without form entering the formless*.」

The Master thus knew that *effortless Dao* is beneficial.

**Commentary:**
I have seen the Dao of *effortless action*, yet all things spontaneously transform and complete themselves—thus I know that *effortless Dao* is beneficial to humanity.

The teaching of *unspeaking*,
**Commentary:**
The Dao’s method is unspeaking; the Sage guides by example.

The benefit of *effortless action*,
**Commentary:**
The Dao’s method is *effortless*—to cultivate the self benefits the spirit, to govern the realm benefits the people, and it avoids toil and labor.

○ Sun said:
Softness can overcome hardness; *nothingness* can dispel *somethingness*—thus it is known that the teaching of *effortless Dao* greatly benefits humanity.
Few in the world attain it.
**Commentary:**
The world’s ruler—few can approach the Dao’s *effortless* cultivation of self and governance.

○ Cushion:
What is called *unspeaking* is not about sealing the mouth.
In principle, it is both *having* and *not having*; in teaching, it is both *speaking* and *silence*.
Both *speaking* and *silence*—thus it is called the teaching of *unspeaking*.
Both *having* and *not having*—thus it is called the benefit of *effortless action*.
The benefit, though not a benefit, is indeed a benefit; the teaching, though not a teaching, is indeed a teaching.
This is the exhaustive exploration of principle and nature—rare discourse in the world.
Worldly teachings seldom reach this, hence it is said that few in the world attain it.

○ Gu said:
The Heavenly follows in silent mystery, yet customs shift naturally—thus it is called the teaching of *unspeaking*.
The Dao’s method is *effortless*; things and people transform spontaneously—thus it is called the benefit of *effortless action*.
Which is closer—*name* or *self*?
**Commentary:**
When *name* succeeds, the *self* retreats.

○ Yu said:
*Name* is the guest of *reality*; the world pursues *name* and loses the *self*—thus the question arises: which is closer?
It is to abandon merit and *name* and preserve the true.

○ Cushion:
*Self*—the inner, the real.
*Name*—the outer, the guest.
*Na* (who)—who.
The world seeks the outer and loses the inner, covets *name* and loses *reality*.
Why? Praise and fame are originally for the *self*—if the *self* is for *name*, when the *self* dies, what use is the *name*?
The Old Master pitied this inversion, thus he reversed: *Name* and *self*—which is truly yours?
Do you covet *name* and forget the *self*?
By this measure, closeness and distance become clear—thus *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Biyí died for his name beneath Chief Yang’s hill."*

Which is more—*self* or *wealth*?
**Commentary:**
Wealth multiplies and harms the *self*.

○ Yu said:
Pursuing abundance to seek wealth—wealth obtained, the *self* lost—thus the question arises: which is more?
It is to cast away jade and shatter pearls to preserve the *self*.

○ Cushion:
Wealth is originally for the *self*—deluded ones lose the *self* for wealth.
