Thus the Old Master reproaches: *You abandon the self for wealth—wealth is more beneficial, or losing the self is more beneficial?*
Thus *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Zhoulu died for profit atop Mount Shunling."*

Which is the affliction—*gain* or *loss*?
**Commentary:**
Excessive desire for gain brings ruin in conduct.

○ Cushion:
To covet wealth excessively is to lose oneself; to desire little is to preserve the *self*.
Then *gain* is the sign of self-ruin, *loss* the proof of self-preservation.
Self-ruin is affliction; self-preservation is joy—yet the world reverses this, calling *gain* suitable and *loss* affliction.
How mistaken!
Thus the teaching says: *Gain and loss—determine which is the affliction.*

Excessive love brings great expenditure.
**Commentary:**
Excessive love of beauty wastes the spirit; excessive love of wealth invites calamity.
The more one loves, the less one retains—thus it is called great expenditure.

○ Cushion:
*Expenditure*—loss.
This is its meaning: those who love fame excessively labor their bodies and weary their minds, depleting their spirit and intelligence.
This sentence resolves *name*.

Hoarding much brings great loss.
**Commentary:**
The living hoard in treasuries; the dead are buried in graves.
The living face robbery and plunder; the dead face grave-robbing and coffin desecration.

○ Cushion:
Those who hoard wealth in treasuries must fear robbers—
not only losing their wealth but also endangering their lives.
Their ruin is deep and their calamity great.
Thus the *Book of Documents* says:
*"An ordinary man without guilt, yet his jade brings him guilt."*
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"The great scholar Luo recited:
Green wheat grows on the hillocks—
If not given in life, why hoard pearls in death?"*
Thus he struck his chin with a hammer and parted his cheeks—
lest the pearl in his mouth be harmed.
This sentence resolves *wealth*.

Knowing sufficiency brings no disgrace.
**Commentary:**
Those who know sufficiency cut off desires and avoid gain—
their bodies unshamed, their spirits at ease.

○ Cushion:
Understanding wealth’s falsity, guarding one’s portion, content and serene—
thus there is no shame.
This sentence resolves *wealth*.

Knowing when to stop brings no peril.
**Commentary:**
Knowing when to stop and stopping—
wealth and gain do not burden the body and mind,
sounds and colors do not confuse the ears and eyes—
thus one’s life is never in danger.

○ Cushion:
Having awakened to *name*’s falsity, one ceases attachment,
preserving the *self* and avoiding harm—
thus there is no peril.
The previous passage says:
*"Name has already been attained—
then one will know when to stop."*
This sentence concludes *name*.

Thus it can endure long.
**Commentary:**
If one knows when to stop, blessings and fortune abide with them.
Cultivating the self, the spirit is unwearied;
governing the realm, the people are undisturbed—
thus it can endure long.

○ Cushion:
Knowing when to stop with *name*, knowing sufficiency with *wealth*,
forgetting *name* and gain—
thus it can endure long.
This sentence summarizes *name*.

The *Dao De Jing* Commentary and Exegesis, Volume Four complete.
**Note:** This commentary appears in the *Daozang* edition of Chen Xiangu’s *Exegesis of the Dao De Jing*, Volume Lower.
Xiangu was a *Chengyi Lang* of the late Northern Song.
Thus it is known this book emerged from the late Northern Song to early Southern Song,
not the Southern Qi’s Gu Xuan’s work, nor a Tang-era compilation.

*The Dao De Jing* Commentary and Exegesis, Volume Five
By Wu County Recluse Gu Xuan

Great completion seems incomplete,

---

**Annotations:**
「注：」—*Commentary:*
「○御曰：」—*Master Gu’s Explanation:*
「○疏：」—*Exegesis:*

---

Great completion, though seemingly incomplete,
*—Commentary:* This refers to the sovereign of supreme *Dao* and *virtue*—
though appearing incomplete, like a shattered vessel,
he extinguishes his *name* and conceals his merit.
Thus his use is unexhausted.
*—Commentary:* His mind, thus governed, knows no depletion with time.
*—Master Gu’s Explanation:* Humility brings benefit, so there is no time of want or ruin.
*—Exegesis:* The one whose deeds are fully accomplished
yet forgets his accomplishment—
though fully accomplished, he seems deficient.
Only because he forgets his accomplishment
does his wealth and use never diminish through time.

Thus does *Zhuangzi* say:
*"Small completion obscures the *Dao*."*

Now, completion is not incomplete,
incompleteness is not completion—
yet today, completion seems incomplete,
forgetting incompleteness, forgetting completion.
Thus, unseen is completion as incomplete,
so it seems incomplete yet is truly complete;
unseen is incompleteness as completion,
so great completion seems incomplete.

Great fullness is like emptiness,
*—Commentary:* This refers to the sovereign of supreme *Dao* and *virtue*—
though full, like emptiness, he does not dare to be proud in his nobility,
nor extravagant in his riches.
Thus his use is inexhaustible.
*—Commentary:* His mind, thus governed, knows no exhaustion with time.
*—Exegesis:* *Chong* is emptiness; *jing* is exhaustion.
The one whose *Dao* and *virtue* are fully satisfied
yet satisfies his fullness—
thus, though full, he seems empty and void.
Only because he forgets fullness, emptiness, and voidness
does his utility never fail.

Now, fullness is not emptiness,
emptiness is not fullness—
yet today, fullness seems empty,
forgetting fullness, forgetting emptiness.
Thus, he is both empty and full,
full yet seeming empty.

Great rectitude is like bending,
*—Commentary:* Great rectitude refers to the upright practice of *Dao* and law,
yet bending, like one who does not contend with the vulgar,
as if yielding and pliable.
*—Exegesis:* The one of great simplicity and sincerity
does not display his rectitude openly,
thus blending with the common, bending with things—
hence, he seems bent.
Thus, true rectitude surpasses petty rectitude.

Great skill is like clumsiness,
*—Commentary:* Great skill refers to abundant talents and achievements,
yet seeming clumsy, he does not dare to display his abilities.
*—Exegesis:* The artisan who crafts myriad things,
the smith who forges all life—
though possessing great skill and achievement,
he forgets his craft and utility,
hiding his traces among the common—
thus, he seems clumsy.
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"He carves and shapes all forms yet does not consider it skillful."*
Thus, true skill surpasses petty skill.

Great eloquence is like inarticulateness.
*—Commentary:* Great eloquence is unquestionable wisdom;
yet seeming inarticulate, he has no verbal adornment.
*—Master Gu’s Explanation:* He does not embellish with trivial tales.
*—Exegesis:* The one whose profound insight dissects true emptiness,
measuring the utmost mysteries—
his speech is beyond speech.
He does not conform to the world’s ways.
Swift and sharp in talent, thus his speech is halting and inarticulate.
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"The unsaid eloquence"*—also, *"Great eloquence does not speak."*
Thus, true eloquence surpasses petty eloquence.

Frenzy overcomes cold,
*—Commentary:* *Sheng* means extreme.

The vitality of spring and summer ascends swiftly and fiercely above—when all things reach their utmost flourishing, cold ensues, and cold brings withering and death. Thus, it is said that one should not align with rigid excess.

*Qi* of stillness overcomes heat.
*—Commentary:* In autumn and winter, all things rest beneath the Yellow Springs—when stillness reaches its extreme, heat arises, and heat is the source of life. ○*Exegesis:* *Liao* (frenzy) is the *yang* (active, bright). Stillness is the *yin* (passive, dark). *Sheng* (overcomes) means extreme. It speaks of the four seasons’ cyclical turn: when *yin* reaches its peak, *yang* emerges; when *yang* reaches its peak, *yin* arises. *Yin*’s emergence causes all things to wither; *yang*’s emergence causes all things to flourish. It analogizes stillness as the foundation of longevity, while frenzy is the root of decay, urging practitioners to abandon frenzy and return to stillness. Again, it explains: descending from the origin to its manifestation, we see frenzy overcoming cold. Returning from the manifestation to the origin, we see stillness overcoming heat. This clarifies how movement and stillness adapt to their proper times.

*—Master Gu’s Explanation:* All things pushed to their limit invert. Movement pushed to its limit becomes stillness; stillness pushed to its limit becomes movement. Observing this, we see that extreme flourishing leads to decline, and extreme decline leads to flourishing—all phenomena in the world follow this pattern. Since it is not the norm to preserve, what should one covet or seek? Therefore, the sage takes nothing, seeks nothing, gains nothing, loses nothing, and in the five great practices of *Dao*, remains naturally pure and still, unaffected by cold or heat. Only then can he be the true master of the world, as it is said: *"He who clears and stills himself becomes the rightful master of the world."*

*—Commentary:* To clear and still oneself is to become the rightful master of the world. *Clearing* and *stillness* ensure enduring rectitude, and holding to the *Dao* means there is no end to its timelessness.

*—Exegesis:* Pure, tranquil, and at ease—one can benefit himself and others, rectify what is wrong and suppress what is perverse. Thus, he becomes the rightful master of the world. This then concludes the exposition of stillness overcoming frenzy, clarifying the distinction between excellence and mediocrity.

*—Master Jie’s Explanation:* It refers to entering deep mountains, practicing purity, and abiding in nature—then one becomes a true person. When the *Dao* prevails in the world, *—Commentary:* Meaning when the ruler of the world follows the *Dao*—

However, the warhorses are turned to plowing fields.
*—Commentary:* "Plowing" (粪) here means tilling. It signifies that when arms are not used, warhorses are instead employed to cultivate the fields. For those cultivating the self, it means channeling the vital essence of *yang* to nourish the body.

*—Master Yu’s Explanation:* A ruler who follows the *Dao* governs through *wu-wei*, his actions free from greed, thus avoiding warfare. He dispenses with the use of warhorses, allowing people to till the fields. For those cultivating the self, it means restraining the six senses—like an army—to till the heart with the *Dao*. Hence, Mencius said: *"All till their fields with dung, but none till their hearts with learning."*

When the *Dao* prevails in the world,
*—Commentary:* Meaning when the ruler of the world follows the *Dao*—

the warhorses are stabled at the outskirts.
*—Commentary:* Constant warfare keeps warhorses stationed at the borders, never returning. *—Exegesis:* The outskirts are called *jiao* (郊). A ruler without the *Dao* oppresses his people, causing chaos. Greedy for territory, he wages endless wars, forcing military horses to remain permanently beyond the borders. Moreover, those who are reckless and competitive, their hearts devoid of the *Dao*, let their six senses—like an army—gallop through the dusty realm, hence the saying that they "generate at the outskirts."

No guilt is greater than indulging desire.
*—Commentary:* Lustful desire. *—Exegesis:* "Guilt" (罪) means ruin—being overwhelmed by suffering. When one encounters alluring sights that stir the heart’s craving, it is called "desirable." Yet, if external phenomena can ensnare the mind, they become the root of sin. Thus, the previous passage states: *"Do not see what is desirable, lest the heart be disturbed."*

No calamity is greater than knowing insufficiency.
*—Commentary:* Wealth and honor cannot restrain oneself. *—Exegesis:* "Calamity" is the deepest form of guilt—when the heart clings to external phenomena, never satisfied, harming others and depleting resources. The guilt grows ever deeper, not just afflicting oneself but extending to three generations.

No blame is graver than the craving to possess.
*—Master Yu’s Explanation:* The greatest misfortune is desire; its consequences are deep, hence "blame." *—Exegesis:* "Blame" (咎) means censure. The heart’s craving for possession knows no bounds—its censure is unparalleled. Comparing the depths of guilt and calamity, the latter is heavier, while blame is lighter. To fully reveal human nature, both are made explicit.

Thus, the sufficiency of *knowing sufficiency*,
*—Commentary:* Preserving the true root—
is ever sufficient.
*—Commentary:* Free from desire. *—Exegesis:* One who adheres to their proper place, without indulging in external pursuits, is always complete, never lacking.

Without stepping outside,
one knows the world.
*—Commentary:* The sage does not step outside yet knows the world because they understand others through themselves, and others’ families through their own. *—Exegesis:* "Outside" refers to perception, discrimination, and the gates of the senses. The virtuous person, with an empty mind and inner stillness, does not let their thoughts wander into external phenomena, yet knows all under heaven. This is true illumination reflecting the mundane.

Without peering through the window,
one perceives the *Dao* of Heaven.
*—Commentary:* The *Dao* of Heaven and humanity are the same; Heaven and man are interconnected, their vital *qi* flowing together. When a ruler is pure and still, Heaven’s *qi* aligns naturally. When a ruler is greedy, Heaven’s *qi* becomes turbulent and impure. All fortune, misfortune, benefit, and harm arise from within oneself. *—Master Yu’s Explanation:* With hands resting and *wu-wei* governance, no decrees are issued beyond the door, yet the *Dao* of Heaven is known. *—Exegesis:* The window (牖) refers to the sensory orifices. The *Dao* of Heaven is the natural principle. In a state of forgetfulness, seated and still, one does not gaze through the sensory gates, yet the true heart shines brightly, revealing the natural *Dao*. This is wisdom illuminating truth.

The door allows passage in and out—
from true illumination to the mundane.
The window, when closed within—
a return to the true source.

The farther one goes,
the less one knows.
*—Commentary:* Observing others’ families from afar, others’ bodies from a distance—what is observed grows farther, and what is known grows less. *—Exegesis:* Those who are inverted cannot perceive truth; their minds follow phenomena, ever distant yet never satisfied. Their knowledge is shallow and obscured, lacking discernment.

Thus, the sage acts without going,
yet knows all.
*—Commentary:* The sage does not ascend to Heaven nor descend into the abyss, yet knows the world through the mind. *—Exegesis:* "Acts without going" means the mind does not cling to external phenomena yet knows all things in their true nature. Like a mirror hung in a hall, objects pass before it and are reflected without effort. Hence, *Zhuangzi* says: *"The sage does not use the mind to illuminate Heaven."*

Without seeing,
one names.
*—Commentary:* The ruler cherishes the *Dao*, the people cherish virtue; the ruler values martial prowess, the people value strength. The sage infers the great from the small, the external from the internal. *—Exegesis:* "Without seeing" means penetrating the emptiness of all phenomena, for there is nothing visible. "Names" refers to rectifying names and classifying all things, leaving traces and manifesting teachings. Without seeing, one diminishes emotional attachments and ornamental excesses daily. *—Master Yu’s Explanation:* Since one does not peer through the window, it is said, "without seeing." When humanity and Heaven are in harmony, one can name the era of great peace.

Without doing,
one achieves.
*—Commentary:* If the ruler does nothing, the people have no tasks; households are prosperous, and all things transform naturally. *—Exegesis:* "Without doing" means concentrating the spirit in stillness, abandoning all action. "Achieves" refers to nurturing all beings and perfecting those who learn—this illustrates stillness in motion.

For those who study,
daily increase.
*—Commentary:* "Those who study" refers to governance, teaching, rites, and music. "Daily increase" means emotional desires and ornamental excesses grow ever more.

「○ *Exegesis:* "For" means cultivating and embellishing. "Learn" refers to mundane studies. It speaks of those who cultivate mundane learning—with sharp emotions and discriminating minds, their desires thus accumulate daily. "For Dao daily diminishes" means they act in accordance with the Way of Nature, causing their desires and embellishments to be daily eroded away.

○ *Master Yu’s Explanation:* Those who study accumulate knowledge daily, while those who follow the Dao diminish their deeds. "Accumulating knowledge" is the gradual progression of mundane study, while "diminishing deeds" is the gateway to enlightenment. Thus, through accumulation, one cultivates virtue, and in forgetting virtue, one attains the Dao itself.

○ *Exegesis:* "For Dao" means cultivating the Dao. It speaks of those who cultivate the Dao—with empty minds and tranquil ease, their wisdom and virtue gradually become clear, and their delusions daily dissipate. "Diminish again, diminish again" means diminishing desires once more, and again, so as to gradually eliminate them, until reaching the state of *wuwei* (non-action).

○ *Exegesis:* Those who study cling to desires, while those who follow the Dao are hindered by the notion of *wuwei*. Though their depths differ, both suffer afflictions. Now, to correct these two extremes, there is the text on diminishing and augmenting. First, diminish and diminish desires; later, diminish and diminish the fixation on *wuwei*. With both extremes dispelled, one attains the non-action of the central path. In non-action, there is nothing that is not done.

○ *Exegesis:* This is the Dao of stillness in motion, of embodying yet applying—thus, there is nothing that is not done. The earlier section states: "The Dao is ever *wuwei* yet ever *wuwei*." To govern the realm, one must always do so without exertion—never burdening the people with laborious tasks.

○ *Exegesis:* "Govern" means to transform and guide. It speaks of those who transform and guide all under Heaven, leading them to goodness, always through *wuwei* and non-exertion. Though they respond to things and act accordingly, their minds remain undisturbed—this is stillness within motion. Hence, *Zhuangzi* says: "Who is it that wears himself out, making things his business?" When one truly has affairs, he is unfit to govern the realm.

○ *Exegesis:* When his mind is not yet empty and forgetful, treating affairs as his business and thus having affairs, this is different from true *wuwei*. True *wuwei* is distinct from having affairs; motion differs from stillness, and stillness contradicts motion. Such a person is unfit to transform and guide the realm.

○ *Exegesis:* The Sage has no mind—never altering his ways, valuing steadfastness, as if without mind. He takes the hearts of the people as his own—following what they find convenient.

○ *Exegesis:* In embodying the Dao, he penetrates and forgets all things, his mind empty and free from thought, like still ashes. Thus, he has no mind. The people are all beings. Though the Sage has no mind, he responds to all stimuli—his responses follow the sensations of things. Hence, he takes the hearts of the people as his own, yet in having no mind, his responses are also without responses.

○ *Master Yu’s Explanation:* "The Sage’s heart is without heart, yet it responds to all stimuli." Thus, those who are already trustworthy, he trusts them; those who are not, he also trusts them. When the people’s virtue is good, the Sage’s virtue is also good. When the people are trustworthy, the Sage trusts them; when they are not, he also trusts them. When the people’s virtue is good, the Sage’s virtue is also good.

○ *Master Yu’s Explanation:* Those who desire goodness and trustworthiness, I follow them accordingly. Those who are not trustworthy, I also teach them goodness and trustworthiness, so that the people are moved by my virtue to become good and trustworthy.

○ *Exegesis:* Trustworthiness means taking delight in hearing the *dharma* and precepts, while lack of trust means not rejoicing in the Dao’s teachings. The Sage, through empty practice and compassion, universally transforms and guides all beings equally, regardless of trust or doubt, so that all may benefit and universally awaken faith. Hence, the *Zhenji* of *Zhenwei* says: "Trust is the key to gain or loss." The *sutra* also states: "Trust is the pivot of learning the Dao."

One question: If the Sage has no mind, yet responds to all stimuli, and does not respond when there are none, then does he teach those who are trustworthy but not those who are not? Yet the text says that both trustworthy and untrustworthy are taught in the same way, and so too with stimuli and non-stimuli—all are responded to alike.

The answer: There is both universal and particular response. Like treating illness in the *Xuan Du* Palace of Jade Clarity, yet always saving the Three Realms—this is universal response. As for the barbarians who, upon sensing the *Zhi* of Purple Qi, receive the *Dao* from *Yin* the True Person—this is particular response.

If the response is particular, it awaits particular stimuli; if universal, it is ever-present with boundless compassion. Hence the *sutra* says: *"Ever saving all beings without abandoning any."*

**Explication:**
It means that when a person is trustworthy, trust is one; when trust is one, it trusts the person. If a person does not trust the One, the One arises from the person, so the Dao ever attains its trust.

The Sage moves through the world with constant reverence—
*Note:* The Sage, moving through the world, is ever reverent and fearful, never daring to indulge in arrogance or excess from wealth and honor.

**Commentary:** *Reverence* (*chùchù*)—the form of ceaseless fear—speaks of having no mind to respond to stimuli.

The Sage, dwelling in the world, ever spreads great compassion, protecting and saving all beings, fearing they may perish or drown. Thus, he moves with constant reverence and fear.

He muddles the hearts of the people—
*Note:* The Sage muddles the hearts of the common folk, as if making them dull and unknowing.

**Commentary:** *Muddled* (*húntūn*)—without distinction. It speaks of how ordinary people, ensnared by emotions and entangled in the world, foolishly grasp at gain and loss. The Sage desires to muddle their hearts, leaving them without distinction, and this is the meaning of his constant fear and reverence.

All the people focus their ears and eyes—
*Note:* *Focus* (*zhù*)—to use.

All the people use their ears and eyes as the Sage’s eyes and ears.

**Commentary:** The *River Above* text reads *zhù* (focus), while other versions read *yín* (indulgence). *Indulgence* means to be stained and entangled; those who are deluded and inverted, lost in the world, give free rein to their ears and eyes, clinging to sounds and colors. Later, they drift adrift in endless wandering, accumulating faults day by day. The Sage pities their suffering, so he reveals their affliction.

Among the six senses, two are highlighted to show these two afflictions are the deepest.
Thus the *Sutra of Western Ascension* says: *"Ears and eyes, sounds and colors—these are the children’s faults."* Yet by mentioning one sense, all senses are implied, for completeness’ sake.

The Sage treats them all like infants—
*Note:* The Sage loves and cares for the people as if they were newborn children, nurturing them without expecting repayment.

**Commentary:** Ordinary beings indulge their ears and eyes, drowning in the river of desire. The Sage, with constant fear and compassion, acts like one who pities infants, grieving their ignorance.

They are born and they die.
*Note:* *Born*—when emotions arise from within, the spirit is settled, the soul is still, hence *born*. *Die*—when emotions enter the chest, the spirit is confused and agitated, hence *death*.

**Commentary:** *Born*—transcending the Three Realms, free from birth and death.
*Die*—sinking into the Three Paths, drowning in birth and death. If one understands the meaning of birth and death, it is from non-being to being, releasing *birth* as *birth*; from being back to non-being, releasing *extinction* as *death*.

There are thirteen kinds of those who are born, and thirteen kinds of those who die.
*Note:* It speaks of the thirteen types of birth and death—namely, the nine orifices and four passages.

In their birth: the eyes do not gaze wantonly, the ears do not listen wantonly, the nose does not sniff wantonly, the mouth does not speak wantonly, the tongue does not taste wantonly, the hands do not grasp wantonly, the feet do not walk wantonly, the vital essence is not expended wantonly. In their death, it is the opposite.

**Commentary:** *Kinds* (*tú*)—types. The nine orifices and four passages belong to both sages and ordinary beings. The Sage forgets them, thus transcending the Three Realms. Ordinary beings cling to them, thus entering the Six Paths. Entering the Six Paths is the kind of death; transcending the Three Realms is the kind of birth.

A person’s life moves toward thirteen kinds of death.
*Note:* When a person seeks life through action, it reverses into thirteen kinds of death.

**Commentary:** When a person’s life is directed toward death, it is because they cling to these thirteen (senses), becoming entangled in the world before them.

Why is this so?
*Note:* Asking why one moves toward death through action.

**Commentary:** A rhetorical question, answered below.

Because their attachment to life is excessive.
*Note:* The reason one moves toward death through action is that their pursuit of life is too heavy, contrary to the Dao and Heaven, acting recklessly and losing measure.

**Commentary:** It speaks of how people cannot embody the Dao and forget life, instead clinging to life as real, accumulating wealth and resources, overindulging in life’s concerns, thus moving toward death. This sentence answers the prior question.

Indeed, it is heard that those who skillfully nurture life—
*Note:* *Nurture* (*shè*)—to guard, to nourish.

**Commentary:** *Indeed*—not a direct answer, akin to *"I have heard."* *Nurture*—to guard, to nourish. It speaks of how those who skillfully guard and nourish life are simple and unadorned, untouched by the world. The excellence of their guardianship is shown below.

When walking on land, they avoid rhinoceroses and tigers—
*Note:* Naturally avoiding harm, which does not reach them.

**Commentary:** *Land*—flat ground. *Rhinoceros*—a poisonous beast with one horn, resembling a wild ox, green in color. It speaks of how those who nurture life skillfully are not harmed by poisonous beasts. Internally, it means they remain calm and undisturbed by afflictions in daily life.

Entering battle, they are unharmed by armor and weapons—
*Note:* They do not love war or killing.

**Commentary:** It speaks of how those who nurture life do not enter battlefields and are not harmed by weapons. Internally, even if they enter the world, they are not injured by desires.

Rhinoceroses have no place to strike with their horns, tigers no place to place their claws, weapons no place to lodge their blades—
*Note:* Those who nurture life skillfully are not injured by rhinoceroses or tigers, nor harmed by weapons.

**Commentary:** Other texts say *zhù* (stationary). *Stationary*—to stand; *place*—to settle. Rhinoceroses, encountering objects, charge; this allegorizes the foolish. Tigers are fierce and violent; this allegorizes greed. Weapons are sharp and can harm people; this allegorizes gluttony. It speaks of how those who nurture life skillfully embody true emptiness, so even amid the world, they are unharmed by the Three Poisons.

Why is this so?

*—Asking why tigers, rhinoceroses, and weapons do not harm them.*

For they dwell in the realm of no-death.

*—Because they do not violate the thirteen forbidden grounds of death, the numinous protects them, and these creatures dare not harm them.*

*—The Way flows naturally without partiality; they do not regard death as death, so things cannot harm their life—thus, the realm of no-death.*

*—Those who perceive form as form regard the body as the ground of death. But those who practice life-nurturing skillfully forget bodily form—when form is forgotten, there is no ground for death. Without the ground of death, how could the Three Poisons take hold?*

*—The Sage embraces spirit and unity, regulates qi and stabilizes the fetal breath, thus these creatures cannot harm them. The Dao dwells in the heart, and true qi abides—there is no ground for execution.*

The Dao gives birth to them,
Virtue nurtures them,
Form shapes them,
Momentum completes them.

*—The Dao generates all things. Virtue (unity) spreads qi and nurtures them.*

*—The Supreme Dao is boundless and mysterious, generating all things; supreme virtue is compassionate and sustains all life—thus, "the Dao gives birth, virtue nurtures."*

*—It endows them with form and momentum to complete them.*

*—Thus all things reverence the Dao and honor virtue.*

*—All things arise from the Dao and virtue; hence, they are revered.*

*—The Dao and virtue are honored not by titles but by their natural operation.*

*—The Dao’s honor and virtue’s nobility require no artificial elevation—they are naturally so.*

Thus the Dao:
Gives birth to them,
Nurtures them,
Lengthens them,
Cultivates them,
Perfects them,
Matures them,
Sustains them,
Covers them.

*—The Dao’s work for all things is not merely to give birth but to lengthen, nurture, perfect, mature, sustain, and cover—fulfilling their nature and life.*

*—This mirrors the ruler’s governance of state and self.*

*—The text repeats earlier phrases to introduce what follows. The Dao is mentioned without virtue because virtue does not differ from the Dao, though the text is concise for brevity.*

As the *Western Ascension Scripture* says:
*"The Dao and virtue are indistinct and mysteriously unified."*

Lengthening refers to fostering their virtuous sprouts.
Cultivating refers to advancing their deeds.
Perfecting and maturing refers to refining their hearts with supreme teachings, so their Dao is fulfilled and their virtue perfected—this echoes prior language to lead into the next passage.
Sustaining and covering: sustaining refers to rewarding their fruits; covering refers to sheltering with boundless compassion.

The four pairs above clarify the virtue of benefiting all things; the three below clarify the Dao’s self-effacing function.

Giving birth yet not possessing,
Acting yet not relying,
Lengthening yet not controlling—
This is called the Mysterious Virtue.

*—The Dao’s operation is an unfathomable benevolence, invisible yet profound.*

*—This refers to the passage above, introducing what follows. "Mysterious" denotes depth; "virtue" signifies protective fulfillment. It points to those who embody the Dao, benefiting others while forgetting their own deeds, guiding people with the law—truly Mysterious Virtue.*

*—The Dao is the apex of yang, yang being heaven. Heaven moves, thus generating all things. Virtue is the apex of yin, yin being earth; earth encompasses, thus nurturing all things. Receiving qi is birth; nurturing form is sustenance—the forms and momentum of all things. Perfecting refers to completing them through their deeds and momentum, forming human shape.*

*—The Dao endows all beings with the four seasonal rhythms, completing all things—this explanation precedes.*

At the origin of all things lies the Dao,
Which becomes the mother of all under heaven.

*—The origin is the subtle black of primordial harmony; this mysterious qi generates all things with nourishing virtue, thus it can be the mother of all under heaven.*

*—The Dao is the primordial mother; this is its description. The mysterious Dao is the source of great wisdom, transcending names and forms, beyond all colored appearances. All things under heaven arise from it.*

---

Now the text speaks of those who boldly proclaim,
Seeking to make the Wayfarer* recognize the root and know the origin,
Thus the Classic says: *"All things originate from the Dao."*

When it says *"the mother of all under heaven,"*
It means the Dao descends from its origin,
Guiding and nurturing the teeming multitude,
Cultivating and fostering them,
Like a mother’s love for her child,
Hence the Upper Classic says: *"The Dao that bears a name is the mother of all things."*

*—Cai says: "Origin" is the root.*
All beings are sunk in the Five Desires,*
Ignorant of their root and origin,
Driven by the current, chasing after trivial ends,
Floating through life and death.
Thus the Venerable Lord Laozi raises the root to show them,
Illuminating that the world possesses the Clear and Void Qi as Virtue and Power,
As your root and origin.
Moreover, the origin has no predecessor,
The mother has merit,
Taking the Dao’s role in generating life,
Thus it is called *"origin."*
The nurturing merit is then called *"mother."*

Having known its mother,
One must also know its child.
*—The child is Unity.*
Having known the Dao,
One must also know Unity.

*—Yu says: When all things have received the Clear Qi’s nurturing virtue,*
And know their own bodies,
That is the Clear Qi’s child.

*—Cui says: The root can generate form,*
The mother can generate life.
Having known that the Dao is my mother,
I know that I am the Dao’s child,
Born from the Dao.

The Classic says: *"The Void and Natural is the true parent."*
Having known its child,
One must again safeguard its mother,
*—Safeguarding Unity,*
One must again safeguard the Dao,
Returning to Non-Action.*

*—Cai says: Having known that I am the Dao’s child,*
And thus obtain life,
I must safeguard the Clear and Tranquil Non-Action,
Returning to its mother,
Thus merging with the Dao.
The body will not perish.
*—It will not be endangered.*

*—Yu says: Having known that the body is the Clear Qi’s child,*
One must safeguard this Clear and Harmonious Qi,
Preventing its dispersion,
Then the body will not perish,
And its danger will not manifest.

*—Cui says: Having known that I am the Dao’s child,*
I must return and safeguard its mother.
But if one can return to the root and safeguard the mother,
Safeguarding the Dao to attain Truth,
Though the forms may appear or vanish,
The origin remains undangerous and unendangered.
Why so?
The Dao can generate life,
The Dao is the origin.
Life arises from the Dao,
Life is the trivial end.
Yet the trivial end can encompass the origin,
Thus one must return to its mother.
The mother can generate the child,
Thus one must safeguard its mother.
Safeguarding the mother brings enduring vision and longevity,
Returning to the root ensures the body’s safety,
Hence the exhortation.

The *Xisheng Classic* says: *"Constantly nurturing the mother, one can endure long."*

Close the *Fu* (eyes),
*—Preventing the eyes from viewing indiscriminately.*
Shut the *Men* (mouth),
*—Preventing the mouth from speaking indiscriminately.*
*—Cui says: Though the text above exhorts safeguarding the mother,*
It has not yet shown the method of cultivation and safeguarding,
Thus the following passage fully displays the means of safeguarding and returning,
Including closing and shutting.*

*Fu* is the mouth,
*—Yet among the Six Roots,*
The *Fu* is singled out,
Revealing that the Yang River* is both the Three Activities and the Six Roots,
And its sins are manifold.
*Men* refers to the Five Gates.
Closing the Five Gates,
*—Preventing contamination by dustly realms.*
The previous mention of *Fu* was a distinction among the roots,
Now *Men* is the comprehensive root.

But the meaning of closing and shutting has two aspects:
One is to sever desires and endure colors,
To take refuge in mountains and forests,
Or to turn away and shut the gates,
*—Not seeing what is desirable.*
Two is to understand that the Six Dusts are illusory and insubstantial,
The roots are not true,
Within, there is no heart capable of contamination,
Without, there are no desirable realms.
Thus, though one’s eyes see and ears hear to their utmost,
One remains ever in the Dao’s realm,
Not straying from the True,
How could one say that plugging ears and closing eyes is closing and shutting?
No, it is not so—
This is the wondrous essence of closing and shutting.

All one’s life, one is not weary.
*—When humans close their eyes to prevent indiscriminate viewing,*
And shut their mouths to prevent indiscriminate speaking,
They will not toil all their lives.*

*—Cui says: "Weary" means labor and suffering.*
The Dao has many paths,
The gates are not singular;
Some still the heart to avoid dustly realms,
Some dwell in realms to grasp the True Source,
Deep and shallow entanglements,
Indeed manifold.

Thus the *Xisheng Classic* says: *"Action brings calamity, and the self is burdened—*
This is the utmost toil and suffering."
But if one follows the earlier closing and shutting,
Dwelling in realms while attaining the True,
The heart’s nature is at ease and broad,
Free from labor and fatigue,
Thus one can live out the years,
And not weary all their lives.

Open the *Fu* (eyes),
*—To indulge in the Five Desires’ gates,*
And aid the Six Dusts’ affairs,
*—The heart and spirit grow inwardly exhausted,*
The ears and eyes are outwardly strained,
All one’s life in labor and fatigue.*

All one’s life, one is not saved.
*—Calamity is accomplished.*

*—Cui says: When the foolish act thus,*
They open the eyes and ears,
Assisting in sound and color,
All their lives entangled,
Perishing in lust,
Even the Great Sage and Great Compassionate
Could not save them from this fate.*

Seeing small is called *Ming* (clear).
*—When sprouts are yet unseen and calamity not yet revealed,*
That is the small and manifest,
Only seeing is called *Ming* (clear).*

*—Yu says: When humans, at the onset of affairs,*
See the small and change their conduct,
This can be called *Ming* (clear).*

*—Cui says: "Seeing" is observation and illumination,*
"Small" is the name for the minute and subtle.
The utmost subtle principle,
Speaking that humans, if they but observe the subtle realm,
Will daily increase in wisdom and clarity,
Hence the Upper Classic says: *"Observe its subtlety."*
Safeguarding softness brings daily strength.
*—Cui says: Having seen the small,*
Wisdom and clarity increase,
And one can apply the Dao,
Knowing softness as well,
Thus one’s virtue and deeds grow daily stronger.
At the start of learning,
There is steadiness and benefit,
There is practice and understanding,
Seeing the small is the gate to benefit and steadiness,
Applying softness is the extension of practice and understanding.
Thus among the Six Perfections,
There is practice and understanding,
The first five are practice,
The last is understanding,
Understanding is benefit and emptiness,
Practice encompasses both emptiness and substance,
Using substance to guide emptiness,
Using emptiness to aid substance,
Desiring to clarify the ability of guidance and aid,
Thus it is said: "Apply softness, see the small."*

「Use its light—
*—Cui says: To deploy its luminous gaze outward,*
And observe the world’s advantages and harms.
Then return to its clarity—
*—Cui says: To withdraw its light inward,*
Lest the spirit be dissipated outward.
○ *Commentary:* Light illuminates the external.
Yet once deployed, it is forgotten—
Concealing brilliance, obscuring traces,
Returning clarity to the dim.
Thus it is said: *Then return to its clarity.*
The following passage states: *Illuminating without dazzling.*
*Zhuangzi* says: *The brilliance of doubt is what the Sage seeks to avoid.*
Without inviting calamity upon oneself—
*—Cui says: To inwardly preserve the spirit,*
Lest it be lost or harmed.
This is called *habitual cultivation.*
*—Yu says:* "遗" (*herni*—*to invite*) means *to share with* or *to give to*.
To restore and guard the inner clarity,
Then no calamity is shared with the body—
Thus it is called *secretly applying the Dao of True Constancy.*
○ *Commentary:* Once deployed and forgotten,
The round virtue is fully practiced,
All burdens exhausted,
No lingering calamity remains.
It is also said: *No calamity is shared with the body,*
And all burdens are fully exhausted.
When deeds are complete,
All burdens are exhausted—
The transmission of the scripture and the master’s teaching
Belongs to this person,
Thus one may call him *inheriting the Dao of Constancy,*
And *continuing the True Teaching.*

*—If I were to possess even a modicum of knowledge,*
I would walk the Great Dao—
*—Cui says:* "介" (*jie*—*modicum*) means *great*.
Laozi laments that rulers do not walk the Great Dao,
Thus he sets forth: *If I were to possess even a modicum of knowledge,*
I would then walk the Great Dao,
Personally embodying the transformation of *Wuwei*.
○ *Jie Jie’s Annotation:* I have always, day and night,
Guarded the One within my body.
○ *Wang Bi and Luoshimo* say#1: "介" (*jie*—*modicum*) means *small*—
If I had even a small measure of knowledge,
I would then walk the Great Dao.

*—Yet I alone fear what is applied.*
*—Cui says:* "唯" (*wei*—*alone*) means *only*.
Only fearing what is applied,
Lest the Dao’s intent be lost—
Desiring to reward the good, yet fearing false goodness arises,
Desiring to trust the loyal, yet fearing deceitful loyalty emerges.
○ *Yu says:* The Master Laozi speaks:
If I were to be upright and boast of my knowledge,
Desiring to walk the Great Dao,
Yet it does not align with the Dao,
Thus only what is applied is to be feared.
○ *Commentary:* "使我" (*shǐ wǒ*—*If I*) is a hypothetical phrase.
Knowledge is discrimination.
"介然" (*jièrán*—*modicum*) is minute.
The Great Dao is most level and easy,
Yet if I were to arise with even a modicum of discrimination,
To knowingly walk the Dao,
This is deeply unfit.
Why?
The Ultimate Dao is empty and boundless,
Its mysteries surpass all discrimination—
Neither true nor false,
Neither real nor unreal,
True and false share the same nature,
Death and life are the same offering.
Entering the Nine Abysses without confusion,
Exiting the Three Realms without distinction,
Treading dangers yet ever secure,
Confronting great calamities without fear.
Now, to arise with discrimination,
To deviate from the Ultimate Dao—
All actions lead to the path of death,
Thus they are to be feared.

The Great Dao is most level and easy,
Yet people prefer crooked paths.
*—Cui says:* "径" (*jing*—*crooked paths*) means *devious ways*,
While the Dao is most level and easy,
Yet people prefer to follow devious paths.
○ *Commentary:* "夷" (*yi*—*level*) is upright and true.
"径" (*jing*—*devious*) is the crooked way.
The utmost principle is equal and level,
Without distinction or discrimination,
Yet those who are inverted
Turn from the true to the devious,
Thus the Great Dao is neglected,
And petty achievements flourish.

*—Jie Jie’s Annotation:* It speaks of people’s love of deceit and falsehood,
And their reckless pursuit of shortcuts.

*—Morning is most refined,*
*—Cui says:* High palaces and pavilions,
Elaborate halls and chambers.
○ *Commentary:* The ruler without Dao
Loves devious paths,
Does not uphold simplicity,
Only values luxury and excess—
Then he demolishes his old halls,
Raises new palaces,
Carves pillars and beams,
Exhausts all ornate beauty.

*—Fields are most overgrown,*
*—Cui says:* Agriculture neglected,
Unplowed and uncultivated,
Wasting the seasons.
○ *Jie Jie’s Annotation:* It speaks of people not refining their inner nature,
But instead adorning themselves morning and evening,
Thus generating illness—
Their hearts become wild and unclean.
○ *Commentary:* With taxes and labor burdens heavy,
Farmers abandon their work—
The eastern slopes lie fallow,
The southern fields overgrown with weeds.

*—Granaries are most empty,*
*—Cui says:* The five grains are harmed,
The state has no reserves.
○ *Jie Jie’s Annotation:* It speaks of not guarding the inner constancy,
Thus the five *zang* organs are depleted—
Hence it says *empty*.
○ *Commentary:* Harvests decline,
The state has no wealth—
The loom and spindle lie idle,
Granaries are left bare.

*—Wearing embroidered silks,*
*—Cui says:* Loving false adornment,
Valuing external splendor.
*—And carrying sharp blades,*
*—Cui says:* Upholding rigidity,
Martial and extravagant.
○ *Yu says:* Virtue is not cultivated,
Yet martial strength is prized.
○ *Jie Jie’s Annotation:* It speaks of loving fine garments,
Rejecting falsehood for truth—
Carrying sharp blades,
Relying on weapons for defense,
Thus falling ill from the influence of malevolent spirits.

*—Sated with drink and food.*
*—Cui says:* Excessive desires,
Never knowing contentment.
○ *Commentary:* Brocade and embroidery adorn the body,
Ganjiang and Moye guard the life,
Sweet wine and rich meats delight the palate—
Yet they do not exalt the superior virtue,
Nor care for the common people.
Alas, the gluttonous and greedy!
They reach this extreme.

*—Wealth and goods are in excess,*
This is called *plundering and boasting.*
*—Cui says:* The people lack sufficient means,
Yet the ruler has excess—
This is due to robbing and plundering
For his adornments and extravagance,
Holding displays to boast before others,
Unknowing that death and ruin will follow,
And his kin will perish with him.

*—Jie Jie’s Annotation:* It speaks of loving sweet and fatty foods
To nourish the body,
Yet abandoning the inner constancy—
Thus a hundred ailments arise,
And wealth is amassed only for the sake of death and mourning.

*—This is not the Dao!*»
*—Cui says:* If a ruler acts thus,
His death is not in accordance with the Dao.

「Again, it says *"Also, this is not the Dao!"*—
A lamenting and grieving expression.

*—Yu says:* To indulge in what is known,
Every action brings ruin—
Acting like thieves and robbers,
Yet still boasting and vaunting,
Boasting and thievery are not the Dao,
Only inviting sighs of lamentation.

*—Jie Jie’s Annotation:* It means that people do not practice *non-action* (無爲),
But only think of fine clothing and delicious food,
As if they could become thieves and robbers,
Yet they cannot stop sickness or avert death—
Thus, it is not the Dao.

*—Commentary:* Hoarding wealth and amassing riches,
Like thieves and bandits,
Both storing and accumulating,
Yet boasting and showing off,
Contrary to reason and against virtue—
This is called *"not the Dao."*

*—The one who walks the Dao with a resolute heart*
Cannot be praised—
His carriage is stripped bare,
No virtuous deeds remain.
His fields lie overgrown,
His mind neglected.
His granaries stand empty,
No moral cultivation.
He adorns himself with embroidered finery,
Delights in ornate speech.
He wears a sharp blade,
His greed as keen as steel.
He feasts on food,
Yet indulges in its flavors.
Wealth and goods abound,
Yet he does not disperse them.
Goodly foundations cannot be uprooted—
*—Annotation:* *Jian* (建) means to establish.
Those who establish themselves and their realm with the Dao
Cannot be pulled away or overturned.

*—Commentary:* *Jian* (建) means to establish.
*Ba* (拔) means to topple.
One who establishes the Dao-heart
Has firm resolve,
Reaching the Dao-field,
Unshakable and unyielding.

*—Wang and Lu say:* A carriage is made from many materials,
Only then is it called a carriage.
A ruler is formed by the people bearing him,
Only then is he called a ruler.
Yet without materials,
How can a carriage exist?
Without people,
How can a ruler stand tall?
Thus, one who loves carriages
Must value the materials,
And one who treasures the Dao-root
Must guard the people.

*—Jie Jie’s Annotation:* The Dao is truly nameless, embracing *Oneness* (抱一),
Guarding within, not seeking without—
Thus, it is said to have *"no praise"*.
*—The one who holds fast to the Dao*
Cannot be separated—
*—Annotation:* Those who hold fast to the Dao with their spirit,
Forever cannot be pulled away or freed.」

This verse follows upon the resolve of the Dao-heart,
And begins the practice of the Dao-body.
*—The one who holds fast to the Dao*
Gains the eternal *Oneness*—
Never deviating or failing.

*—Sacrifices of descendants do not cease.*
*—Annotation:* The *Master* says: *[The one who] holds fast to the Dao-root through virtue and power,*
Whose merit extends to posterity,
Loving the sweet osmanthus grove—
How much more so for their descendants?
*—The ruler’s merit lies in the Dao,*
The ancestor’s virtue lies in the Dao,
Thus, the rise of Zhou began with Hou Ji,
And was completed by Wen and Wu.
*—Zhou’s sacrifices*
Honor Hou Ji in the *Altar of Heaven* (郊祀),
And worship Wen Wang in the ancestral temple (宗祀),
So even when divination foretold thirty generations of succession,
And seven hundred years of rule,
When the main stream of the ancestral temple was destroyed and overflowed outward,
*—The sacrifices to Hou Ji and Wen Wang did not cease.*

*—Practicing the Dao in oneself,*
One’s virtue becomes true;
*—Annotation:* *[The one who] practices the Dao in oneself,*
Nurtures *qi* and tends the spirit,
Extending life and prolonging years—
*—Thus, their virtue is real.*
*—Annotation:* Through prior learning and practice,
One refines oneself until
*—The body and mind harmonize,*
*—And virtue and power are truly realized.*

*—Practicing the Dao in the family,*
One’s virtue becomes abundant;
*—Annotation:* *[The one who] practices the Dao in the family,*
*—Where the father is kind and the son filial,*
*—Where brothers are friendly and younger siblings obedient,*
*—Where the husband is trustworthy and the wife chaste,*
*—Thus, their virtue brings lasting blessings,*
*—Extending to future generations.*

*—Practicing the Dao in the village,*
One’s virtue becomes enduring;
*—Annotation:* *[The one who] practices the Dao in the village,*
*—Respects and honors elders,*
*—Loves and nurtures the young,*
*—Instructs the foolish and ignorant,*
*—Thus, their virtue covers all without exception.*

*—Practicing the Dao in the state,*
One’s virtue becomes rich;
*—Annotation:* *[The one who] practices the Dao in the state,*
*—Then the ruler is trustworthy and the ministers loyal,*
*—Ren and righteousness arise naturally,*
*—Rites and music flourish spontaneously,*
*—Governance is fair and without bias,*
*—Thus, their virtue is abundant and profound.*
*—Annotation:* The five ranks of feudal lords,
*—Purified and refined through the Dao,*
*—Bring peace within their domains,*
*—With no internal or external worries,*
*—Are they not the virtuous and sagely rulers,*
*—Whose deeds are rich and ample?*

*—Practicing the Dao in the world,*
One’s virtue becomes universal.
*—Annotation:* The sovereign who practices the Dao in the world,
*—Acts without words and transforms without teaching,*
*—The people respond to the ruler as shadows follow form,*
*—Trustful as echoes follow sound,*
*—Thus, their virtue is boundless and far-reaching.*
*—Annotation:* The *Nine-at-the-Top* sovereign (九五之君),
*—Rules by the Dao,*
*—Upright and silent, profound and taciturn,*
*—Bringing great peace to the world,*
*—Thus, all nations come to pay homage,*
*—And all quarters submit in allegiance,*
*—Their transformation reaches everywhere,*
*—Their virtue is universal.*

*—Thus, one observes oneself through oneself,*
*—Annotation:* With the Dao-refined body,
*—Observing the body unrefined by the Dao,*
*—Who perishes, who survives?*
*—One observes the family through the family,*
*—Annotation:* With the Dao-refined family,
*—Observing the family unrefined by the Dao.*
*—One observes the village through the village,*
*—Annotation:* With the Dao-refined village,
*—Observing the village unrefined by the Dao.*
*—One observes the state through the state,*
*—Annotation:* With the Dao-refined state,
*—Observing the state unrefined by the Dao.*
*—One observes the world through the world.*
*—Annotation:* With the Dao-refined sovereign,
*—Observing the sovereign unrefined by the Dao.*
*—How do I know the world is thus?*
*—By this.*

*—The Master* speaks: *How do I know of the world’s rise and fall?*
*—By measuring it against this standard,*
*—It suffices as a mirror and tortoise-shell guide.*
*—Annotation:* The *Master* says: *I know of the world’s rise and fall,*
*—Of those who practice the Dao flourishing,*
*—And those who abandon the Dao perishing,*
*—By observing this way.*

*—Annotation:* Yao and Shun’s ascension to peace was because of the Dao.
*—Jie and Zhou’s* descent into licentiousness was because of the lack of the Dao.
*—Thus, it is known:*
*—Observing the Dao-refined body against the unrefined,*
*—Observing the Dao-refined world against the unrefined,*
*—Even to the harm and benefit of families and states,*
*—The truth can be discerned.*

*—Thus, the Master* sets forth the analogy:
*—How do I know the world’s success and failure?*
*—Only by this criterion,*
*—It suffices as a mirror and tortoise-shell guide.*

*—The depth of one’s contained virtue*
*—Annotation:* *[The one who] contains and cherishes virtue and power deeply.*
*—Is likened to a newborn babe.*
*—Annotation:* The spirit’s protection and blessing for those who contain virtue,
*—Is like a parent’s care for a newborn babe,*
*—As illustrated below.*

*—Poisonous creatures do not sting,*
*—Annotation:* Bees, wasps, snakes, and viper-like creatures do not sting them.
*—Fierce beasts do not seize,*
*—Annotation:* Birds of prey do not snatch them,
*—And raptors do not strike.*
*—Annotation:* The babe does no harm to creatures,
*—And creatures do no harm to the babe,*
*—Thus, in a world of great peace,*
*—No matter noble or lowly,*
*—All have hearts of benevolence,*
*—Sharp objects return to their roots,*
*—Poisonous creatures do not harm humans.*

*—The Master* says: *The Perfect One is divine,*
*—Creatures cannot harm them,*
*—Having no intention to harm others,*
*—Thus, there is no place for venomous stings,*
*—This is the Perfect One’s contained virtue.*

*—Annotation:* Poisonous creatures are snakes and vipers.
*—Birds of prey are eagles and hawks.*
*—Fierce beasts are rhinos and tigers.*
*—Sting (螫) refers to venomous attacks.*
*—Strike (搏) refers to violent contact.*

The babe does not transgress the boundary of the present moment,
thus avoiding the three afflictions of external harm.
This illustrates the Perfect One’s profound virtue—
thus unharmed by the three poisons.

Though bones are frail and sinews supple,
their grasp is unyielding.
*—Annotation:* The babe’s sinews and bones are weak yet firm in holding,
for their intent is singular and unwavering.
*—Exegesis:* The babe’s sinews and bones are weak,
yet their grip is unshakable—
a metaphor for the Perfect One’s nature:
soft yet yielding, harmonious and humble,
though mingled with the dust of the world,
their inner purity remains unstained.
Thus, their grasp is firm—
this verse reveals the cooling of *intentional action*.

Unknowing of the union of male and female,
yet the vital essence surges—
the utmost refinement of *jing*.
*—Annotation:* The babe, unaware of sexual union,
yet their *yin* energy stirs—
a result of abundant *jing-qi*.
*—Exegesis:* The *Zìlín* says: *"Jia"* refers to the babe’s *yin*.
The babe, newly born, ignorant of copulation,
yet their *yin* swells in agitation—
a sign of *jing* overflowing the chambers.
How much more so for the Perfect One,
whose body embodies pure harmony,
effortless and empty,
though tossed in the waves of the world,
hearing sights and sounds—
yet awakened to true emptiness,
seeing no trace of union.
For their essence is most refined—
this verse reveals the cooling of *physical action*.

They wail all day yet their voice does not grow hoarse,
the utmost harmony.
*—Annotation:* From dawn till dusk,
the babe’s cry remains unchanging—
a result of abundant harmonizing *qi*.
*—Master’s Commentary:* The babe’s bones are frail, sinews supple,
yet their clenched fist is unyielding;
unknowing of *yin-yang* union,
yet their harmonizing *qi* stirs—
all from the utmost refinement of essence.
Their wailing never falters, voice unstrained—
a sign of perfect harmony.
This is the babe’s embrace of the *Dark*.
*—Exegesis:* Their cries arise without intent,
and harmony does not disperse.
How much more so for the Perfect One,
whose profound virtue penetrates the true essence—
thus able to speak without words,
though entangled in myriad things,
yet never contradicting the unsaid.
This verse reveals the cooling of *verbal action*.

To know harmony is to know the constant.
*—Annotation:* If one knows the gentle, yielding *qi* as beneficial,
then they grasp the Dao’s enduring truth.
*—Master’s Commentary:* To remain like the babe,
steadfast in softness and harmony—
this is the practice of knowing the constant.
*—Exegesis:* To recognize this speechless speech,
this pure and harmonious principle,
is to embody the eternal Dao.

To know the constant is to be enlightened.
*—Annotation:* If one practices the Dao’s enduring truth,
then daily they illuminate the profound mysteries.
*—Exegesis:* To know the eternal Dao
is to shine with wisdom’s radiance.

To nurture life is auspicious.
*—Annotation:* *Auspicious* means *growth*.
The babe’s life grows naturally, day by day.
*—Exegesis:* *Auspicious* means *abundance*.
Those deluded by worldly desires,
unaware of birth and non-birth,
indulge in worldly gains,
thickening their vital *qi*—
thus their afflictions and obstructions multiply daily.

The heart directs *qi*, making it strong.
*—Annotation:* If the heart remains focused on harmony and softness,
then *qi* solidifies within, and the form grows supple.
But if the heart is driven by delusion,
harmonizing *qi* departs,
and the body grows rigid and strong.
*—Master’s Commentary:* If the heart harbors distinctions,
*qi* loses its differentiation—
to force the heart to command *qi*
is to become a tyrant of strength.
*—Exegesis:* The heart is the spirit;
*qi* is the body.
Worldly passions invert truth,
and delusion arises from contact.
How could they know all things are empty,
the body but an illusion?
Yet they compel the spirit to dominate the form,
driving it in toil—
thus the karmic bonds of birth and death grow ever stronger.

When things reach their peak, they wither—
this is called *not the Dao*.
*—Annotation:* When all things peak, they decay and age,
and decay brings no attainment of the Dao.
*—Exegesis:* The flourishing must decline;
when things reach their height, they age—
thus, to nurture life and strengthen *qi*
proves the impermanence of existence.
Since impermanence is true,
this cannot be the true Dao.

Not attaining the Dao brings early decline.
*—Annotation:* Those who fail to attain the Dao decline early.
*Early decline* means *death*.
*—Master’s Commentary:* All things, when at their peak, decay—
thus the warning: *"Pride in strength is unaligned with the Dao,
and must be halted."*
*Halting* means *cessation*.
*—Exegesis:* *Halting* means *cessation*.

If it is not the true Dao, one must cease and not pursue it.

---

**Daoist Canon: Commentary on the *True Classic of the Dao/Way* (卷之五 complete)**

#1: Original reads *"亡"* (wáng), should be *"云"* (yún).
#2: Original reads *"伐"* (fá), should be *"代"* (dài).

**Daoist Canon: Commentary on the *True Classic of the Dao/Way* (卷之六)**
*Briefed by the recluse Gu Xuan of Wu Commandery*

The wise do not speak,
*—Annotation:* The wise value action over speech.
*—Exegesis:* Those who grasp the Dao, comprehend its subtle principles—
they know that reason transcends words,
thus they do not speak.

Hence, *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The Dao cannot be questioned,
nor can questions be answered—
this is called *wuwei* (non-action)."*
*—Rhythmic Explanation:* Meaning the Dao is beyond articulation.

Those who speak do not know.
*—Annotation:* A tongue outpaces four horses—
many words bring many calamities.
*—Exegesis:* Those who cling to verbal definitions
and seek reason in words alone
cannot fathom the Dao’s transcendence of speech and symbols,
thus they do not know.

Hence, *Zhuangzi* says:
*"If one questions the Dao and receives an answer,
they do not know the Dao."*
*—Rhythmic Explanation:* Meaning the speaker does not grasp its singularity.

The Dao is known by the Yellow Emperor.

*—Annotation:* He who blocks the *兑* (duì, "joy" or "mouth"),
and shuts his gates,
*—Annotation:* Those who block and seal
desire to cut off its source.
*—Master’s Commentary:* Those who attain enlightenment
have no attachment to the Dao,
nor do they cling to words,
thus it is said: *"He who blocks the *兑*."*
Since there is no attachment,
the gates of craving are closed.

*—Exegesis:* *Blocking* means silencing speech,
*closing* means restraining the six emotions.
*—Rhythmic Explanation:* Meaning to seal the nine orifices,
to preserve essence and guard *qi*.

*—Annotation:* When human nature sharpens its desires,
one must recall the Dao’s *wuwei*,
to blunt and halt them.

*—Exegesis:* To stop avarice and rivalry.

*—Annotation:* *纷* (fēn, "entanglement"), those who nurse grudges endlessly,
must recall the Dao’s simplicity and detachment
to dissolve and explain them.

*—Exegesis:* To release resentment and anger.

*—Annotation:* Even with singular insight,
one must harmonize it to make it dim,
not to dazzle and confuse others.

*—Exegesis:* To accommodate the ignorant and vulgar.

*—Annotation:* One must not separate oneself as exceptional.

*—Exegesis:* To blend with worldly affairs.

The preceding sentences have already been fully explained;
now, the text is briefly appended without further elaboration.
Thus, these words are repeated,
to emphasize their relevance to the path of learning.

This is called *玄同* (*xuán tóng*).
*—Annotation:* *玄* (xuán, "mysterious"), is heaven.
If one acts as above,
it is said they align with heaven’s Dao.

*—Exegesis:* Having already subdued body and mind,
they next harmonize light and obscure their traces,
thus their conduct is complete and virtuous,
and they accord with the mysterious principle.

*—Master’s Commentary:* *光* (guāng, "light"), is wisdom.
*尘* (chén, "dust"), is folly.
Ordinary beings adorn their wisdom to dazzle others,
and indulge their folly to invite suffering,
but the sage harmonizes light without dazzling,
and aligns with dust.

Hence, they cannot be approached with intimacy,
*—Annotation:* They do not take pride in honor,
nor do they lament solitude.
*—Master’s Commentary:* *玄同* is without bias,
thus they cannot be approached with intimacy.

Hence, they cannot be shunned with distance;
*—Annotation:* Their focus is on stillness, without desire,
and they bear no grudges against others.
*—Master’s Commentary:* They float universally with the multitude,
thus they cannot be shunned with distance.
*—Exegesis:* The Dao resonates with the mysterious,
and the realm of knowledge is doubly severed—
having forgotten both self and others,
they also transcend the notions of intimacy and distance.

They cannot be exploited for gain,
*—Annotation:* Their body does not crave wealth and nobility,
nor their mouth the five flavors.
*—Master’s Commentary:* Without desire,
they cannot be exploited for gain.

They also cannot be harmed;
*—Annotation:* They do not compete with the strong for advantage,
nor do they vie with the bold for *qi*.
*—Master’s Commentary:* Without desire to contest,
they cannot be harmed.
*—Exegesis:* For gain and harm
are tied to life and death,
existence and annihilation—
how much more so when they concern profit and loss.
Hence, *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Do not pursue gain,
nor shun harm."*

They cannot be elevated to nobility,
*—Annotation:* They do not govern in chaotic times,
nor serve in corrupt courts.
*—Master’s Commentary:* They embody the Dao naturally,
thus they cannot be elevated to nobility.

They also cannot be debased to lowliness.
*—Annotation:* They do not ride on power to become arrogant,
nor do they bend their will when thwarted.
*—Master’s Commentary:* They are pure without stain,
thus they cannot be debased to lowliness.
*—Exegesis:* For wealth and nobility
arise from honor and disgrace—
thus praise and blame do not move them,
nor do they tremble at favor or insult,
hence they cannot be ranked as noble or ignoble,
for nobility and ignobility are only labels of the heart.

Thus, they are noble throughout the world.
*—Annotation:* Their virtue is such
that even emperors cannot make them subjects,
nor lords bend them—
they float with the world,
adapt to survive,
and avoid harm,
thus they are noble throughout the world.
*—Master’s Commentary:* They embody the Dao without obstruction,
and forget words to let reason flow freely—
their sharpness and entanglements are dissolved,
their light and dust are harmonized—
since they defy intimacy and distance,
and transcend nobility and ignobility,
they are thus noble throughout the world.
*—Exegesis:* Having dispelled both intimacy and distance,
and emptied their minds of gain and harm,
they do not concern themselves with praise or blame,
nor do they harbor thoughts of nobility or ignobility—
thus the common folk bear them with reverence,
and all ranks gladly promote them without fatigue,
hence the world honors and values them.

They govern the realm with uprightness,
*—Annotation:* *以* (yǐ, "reach"), heaven appoints those of upright conduct
to rule over states.
*—Exegesis:* *以* (yǐ, "use"), governance means laws and teachings,
*治* (zhì, "order"), is to harmonize and regulate.
The sage leads the world and interacts with beings,
adapting to circumstances and wielding power—
the Dao is used to deploy troops and implement strategy,
wisdom to regulate the state.
This is the era of *pausing arms and refining culture*.

They use the *奇* (*qí*) to wage war,
*—Annotation:* *奇* (qí, "strange"), is deceit—
heaven appoints those who practice deception
to wage war.
*—Exegesis:* *奇* (qí), is covert deceit.
When disorder must be quelled,
war must be waged—
war does not reject deception,
it relies on stratagem and guile.
This is the era of *pausing culture and refining arms*.

War harms itself. A state is like the body. To take the world without action—
*—Annotation:* To appoint those who act without doing as rulers of the world.

*—Exegesis:* Governing the world through non-action is most noble. If one uses laws and teachings to rule, or deceit and stratagem in war, these are all contrary to the Dao. Only through non-action and non-doing can one take the world.

*—Commentary:* The Way of governance adapts to circumstances like a passing shelter or a sacrificial dog—if grasped too tightly, it only increases harm. Better to remain in non-action, centered in the *Gushu* [1], neither wielding arms nor culture, yet ruling from the hall of state with effortless ease. Thus, things transform without blemish, and the people sing in harmony. The art of governance lies here.

How do I know the world is thus? By this.
*—Annotation:* *This*, meaning the present—Laozi says, "How do I know the will of heaven is so?" I know it by what I see today.

*—Exegesis:* *This* refers to the reasoning below.

*—Commentary:* The argument assumes: "How do I know that governing the world must be through non-action?" The reasoning below reveals it.

The more taboos a land enforces, the poorer its people grow.
*—Annotation:* *Land* refers to the ruler; *taboos* mean prohibitions. Excessive restrictions breed deceit, and many prohibitions make the people cunning, deceiving one another for survival—thus poverty.

*—Explanation:* This speaks of artificial governance.

*—Commentary:* Harsh laws multiply offenders, disrupting livelihoods and deepening poverty.

Those who govern themselves cannot follow their true nature but cling to prohibitions, lacking inner virtue—thus poverty.

When the people wield many tools, the state grows confused.
*—Annotation:* *Tools* means *power*—when the people hold much power, their vision is dazzled, their ears deceived, and trust between ruler and subjects erodes, leading to chaos.

*—Exegesis:* *Tools* refers to schemes. If rulers rely too much on cunning, the people respond with deceit, deepening the state’s confusion.

When people rely on cleverness, strange devices proliferate.
*—Annotation:* *People* refers to rulers and feudal lords who favor cleverness—engraving palaces, carving garments—so strange devices arise, and the people below imitate, adorning gold and jade, embroidering colors, endlessly innovating.

*—Commentary:* *Cleverness* means deceitful schemes. *Strange devices* are war machines. If rulers favor war, the people follow, inventing strange devices to earn glory. Those who govern themselves indulge in cleverness, seizing others’ lands, and their divisive hearts only worsen corruption. This sentence further explains the use of war.

When laws proliferate, thieves multiply.
*—Annotation:* *Laws* means luxuries. If luxuries abound, farming declines, and hunger and cold follow—thus thieves multiply.

*—Exegesis:* Having lost non-action, laws become stricter, and those who exploit them become thieves—how could they not multiply?

*—Honor’s Note:* Luxuries are what laws protect. If gold and jade are prized, thieves will rise.

*—Chen’s Note:* It is natural: excessive taboos disrupt balance, many tools shift power downward, cleverness breeds deceit, and more laws only invite greater corruption.

Thus the Sage says:
*"I do nothing, yet the people transform themselves,*
*—Annotation:* *Thus the Sage says*—referring to the following.

*"I do nothing, yet the people transform themselves."* The Sage speaks of cultivating the Dao in harmony with heaven, making no artificial changes, yet the people naturally refine themselves.

*—Explanation:* This means preserving nature so that *yuanqi* [2] flows freely.

*—Commentary:* If the ruler remains detached and non-acting, the people naturally transform, abandoning vice for virtue. Laozi, being a Sage, modestly attributes this transformation to the *Shenghua* [3], hence *Zhuangzi* says: "Metaphorical speech is nineteen out of twenty."

*"I impose no tasks, yet the people grow rich,*
*—Annotation:* *I impose no tasks*—meaning no forced labor or conscription—so the people secure their livelihoods and prosper.

*—Explanation:* This means the body’s *form* and *qi* flourish when unrestrained.

*—Commentary:* With no artificial demands and no burdens, the four classes [4] settle into their work, the world harmonizes, taxes are light—how could they not prosper?

*"I value stillness, yet the people align themselves,*
*—Annotation:* The Sage says, *"I value stillness"*—without speaking or teaching, the people naturally become loyal and upright.

*—Explanation:* This means the heart remains empty of deceit, so *qi* resides within the form.

*—Commentary:* If the ruler cherishes stillness and does not disturb the people, they inherit pure customs and return to righteousness.

*"I desire nothing, yet the people return to simplicity."*
*—Annotation:* *I desire nothing*—constantly rejecting ornamentation, dressing plainly—so the people follow, becoming unadorned.

*—Explanation:* This means the mind, undisturbed by desires, remains steadfast, so *jingqi* [5] is pure and unexhausted.

*—Commentary:* If the ruler is frugal and unselfish, the people know contentment and simplicity, returning to natural virtue.

From *"Thus the Sage says"* onward, these lines collectively explain the meaning of *"taking the world without action."*

Their governance is obscure yet profound,
*—Annotation:* Their governance is broad and unrestrictive, obscure yet deep—

their people are sincere and rich.
*—Annotation:* Broad governance makes the people sincere and abundant, living in harmony.

The commentary states:
*"Mùn-mùn"* means expansive and ample.
*"Chún"* means simplicity and sincerity.
It speaks of the ruler acting without interference, governance broad and unrestrained—
so the people, following transformation, are all sincere and unadorned.

When governance is *chá-chá*,
*—Annotation:* Governance is urgent and hasty, its words pressing upon the ears—
the people become *quē-quē*.
*—Annotation:* Governance hasty and urgent—
the people, unable to sustain life, grow thin and scattered daily.

*—Commentary:* *Chá-chá* means strict and swift.
*Quē-quē* means decay and dispersion.
When the ruler favors action, governance becomes oppressive and abrupt—
the people suffer its violence, thus many perish and scatter.

To govern oneself, one must embrace emptiness, forgetfulness, and ease—
not clinging to a hasty, urgent mind.

Fate as fortune’s foundation,
*—Annotation:* *Yǐ* means "from."
For misfortune arises from fortune—
when people face calamity and repent, blame themselves,
practice virtue, and follow the Way,
fortune arrives, and misfortune departs.

Fortune as misfortune’s hidden seed.
*—Annotation:* Fortune lies concealed within misfortune—
when people grow proud from fortune,
fortune departs, and misfortune comes.

*—Commentary:* *Yǐ* means "from," *nì* means "concealed."
When governance is *mùn-mùn*, fortune is invited;
when *chá-chá*, misfortune is summoned.
These three matters stem from the mind—
fortune lies within misfortune,
misfortune hides within fortune—
their principle is not distant.

Who knows their ultimate end?
*—Annotation:* Misfortune and fortune cyclically generate—
who knows their final limit?

There is no rectitude.
*—Annotation:* *Wú* means "not"—
the ruler, failing to rectify himself, brings ruin to the realm.

*—Commentary:* *Shú* means "who."
The signs of fortune and misfortune arise from good and evil.
Karma undetermined, retribution follows—
thus the wheel turns through the Three Realms,
returning through the Six Paths,
in myriad transformations—
who knows their end?

The superior sage, mindful of saving others,
embodies this rectitude,
awakens to the Profound Mystery,
and does not become the cause of fortune or misfortune—
thus, no retribution of fortune or misfortune comes.
Hence, *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Misfortune does not arrive, fortune does not come—
misfortune and fortune are nonexistent;
who would suffer calamity?"*

Now, why the endless cycle?
Because they do not embrace the rectitude of the Way.

Rectitude returns as deceit,
*—Annotation:* *Qí* means "deceit"—
when the ruler is unrectified,
even if the people are rectified,
they in turn corrupt the ruler’s deceit.

*—Commentary:* *Qí* means "hollow deceit."
Even if one studies rectitude with sincerity,
clinging to its name while failing to forget it,
it does not align with reality—
thus it becomes hollow deceit.

Virtue returns as omens of disaster.
*—Annotation:* Even virtuous people revert to omens of calamity.

*—Commentary:* Though one acts virtuously,
still clinging to names and words,
contradicting true principle,
becoming a portent of misfortune—
thus *Zhuangzi* says:
*"To act for sound and name is to become a portent of misfortune."*

*—Metu’s Note:* If one seeks virtue yet becomes a portent,
it is the virtue of name alone.

*—Annotation:* Those who attain the Dao’s fortune
yet fail to uphold unity
soon grow proud and swollen—
evil intent resurfaces.

The people’s delusion has long endured.
*—Annotation:* The ruler’s confusion and loss of rectitude
has lasted for ages.

*—Annotation:* The world’s people cannot uphold unity,
deceived by deceit and falsehood,
losing fortune and inviting misfortune—
this has been longstanding, not just today.

*—Commentary:* The vulgar and coarse
become portents and deceitful
through their stupidity and delusion—
their error is without beginning,
not merely this life.

Thus the *Xī Shēng Jing* says:
*"Such delusion has endured long."*

Therefore, the Sage is square yet does not cut,
*—Annotation:* The Sage walks the square path of rectitude
to guide the people—
not to sever or harm them.

*—Annotation:* "Square" refers to the rectitude of unity—
guard it firmly, practice it enduringly,
never harming oneself with deceit.

*—Commentary:* The Sage embodies the Dao’s rectitude,
setting the standard for the people,
adapting to circumstances, guiding without coercion—
never ruling or forcing them to conform.

This is the Sage’s admonition,
urging all to cultivate.

Frugal yet not harmful,
*—Annotation:* The Sage’s conduct is pure and clear,
using it to transform the people—
not to harm them.

*—Commentary:* Mysteriously attuned to the empty, illusory nature of things,
free from greed or attachment,
elevated and pure,
avoiding the filth of the world.

Thus, cold and filth are both discarded,
greed and purity forgotten—
neither frugal nor impure,
neither pure nor filthy—
hence *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Great purity knows no fear,"*
and *"Frugal purity is untrustworthy."*

*—Annotation:* "Pure" refers to the clarity of *qi*.
Impurity cannot obstruct the Dao’s practice.

Straight yet not arrogant,
*—Annotation:* *Sì* means "to press"—
though the Sage bends to others,
he does not assert himself.

*—Commentary:* *Sì* means "to press."
Naturally upright and refined,
his essence untainted by deceit—
yet he bends to circumstances,
not forcing his own straightness.
Thus, the previous chapter says: *"Great straightness seems crooked."*

*—Annotation:* The function of unity is balanced—
following the heart’s flexibility,
not measuring by rigid rules.

Illuminating yet not dazzling.
*—Annotation:* Though the Sage possesses singular insight,
he remains as if obscured—
not blinding others with brilliance.

*—Annotation:* The Sage’s transformation is subtle—
not imposing square rectitude,
not staining others with impurity,
not pressing his straightness,
not dazzling with light—
but cultivating within himself,
while the world transforms naturally.

*—Commentary:* Wisdom’s light shines everywhere,
yet he conceals his brilliance,
not flaunting it before others,
forgetting even his own illumination.

Thus, *Zhuangzi* says: *"The brilliance of the slippery doubt is a trap the Sage perceives and confines."*

To govern the people—
*—Annotation:* This refers to the ruler’s desire to administer the populace.

To serve the Dao—
*—Annotation:* Serve, here meaning *use*. One must employ the Way of Heaven, follow the Four Seasons.

There is nothing better than **frugality**.
*—Annotation:* *Frugality* (啬), to cherish and conserve.

In governing the state, one must cherish the people’s wealth—
not indulge in **Qin’s extravagance**,
*—Annotation:* *Qin’s extravagance*, referring to the wasteful policies of the Qin state.
in cultivating oneself, one must cherish **qi** and **spirit**—
not abandon oneself to dissipation.

*—Annotation:* Heaven, the natural order.
*Style*, law.
There is nothing better than **moderation without excess**.
*—Annotation:* The Sage aligns with Heaven’s Way above,
transforms the common folk below—
employing the supreme law of **wuwei** (无为).

Only through **frugality** does one gain early **virtue**—
*—Annotation:* Early, to precede others;
**virtue**, attainment.

Only through **frugality** and cherishing the people’s wealth
does the populace find peace.
Cherishing **qi** and **spirit**,
one attains the Way of Heaven first.

*—Annotation:* How does one gather the people?
By wealth—
thus, through careful conservation,
those from all quarters come bearing burdens and arrive,
early serving their ruler.
*—Annotation:* Serve, to submit in service.

*—Annotation:* This **wuwei** method alone
can benefit both self and others—
applied to personal cultivation, it subdues **demons and trials**;
applied to state governance, it brings distant lands into submission.

Early **virtue** is called **deep accumulation**—
*—Annotation:* To first attain Heaven’s Way
is to deeply accumulate **virtue** within oneself.

*—Annotation:* Deep, profound and weighty.
Accumulation, layered accumulation.
To internally subdue **demons and trials**,
externally bring distant regions under control,
one must layer deeds and cultivate **virtue**—
its power profound and weighty,
only then can distant lands submit.

*—Annotation:* The Sage’s accumulated **virtue**
