draws all under Heaven to his service—
then there is none he cannot subdue.

*—Annotation:* *Kè* (克), to be capable.
*—Annotation:* Those harboring deceit and evil
are subdued through **unity of qi** and its accumulation.
*—Annotation:* *Kè* (克), to conquer, also to attain.
Layered deeds and **virtue**,
self-benefit and benefit to others,
without loss—
internally and externally subdued,
none fail to conquer.

*—Annotation:* Through **unity of qi** and its accumulation,
evil is expelled and diseases cured—
thus, none fail to conquer.

If none can conquer,
then none know its limits—
*—Annotation:* If none can be conquered,
then none can fathom the depths of one’s own **virtue**.

*—Annotation:* One who understands **corresponding principles**
finds none beyond the reach of **Dao’s profound and distant power**—
none can know its ultimate bounds.

*—Annotation:* Through **unity of qi** and its accumulation,
evil is expelled and diseases cured—
none can know its full extent,
thus one’s life endures long.

If none know its limits,
then one can establish a state—
*—Annotation:* If none can fathom the depths of one’s **virtue**,
then one can secure the land and its people,
bringing governance and blessing.

*—Annotation:* When all principles are harmonized,
their depths and distances are boundless—
thus, one can rule a state and transform the people,
becoming the sovereign of kings and lords.

The foundation of a state,
one can endure forever.
*—Annotation:* State and body are analogous;
**mother**, the Way.
If one preserves the Way within the body—
**qi** unexpended,
the Five Spirits untroubled—
then one can endure long.

*—Annotation:* As sovereign of a state,
nurturing all under Heaven,
treating all as his children,
hence called **mother**.

Though **virtue** and deeds may be exalted,
if they are achieved without attachment,
and **wuwei** remains pure and subtle,
then one can endure forever.

This is called **deep-rooted and firm-founded**—
*—Annotation:* One who takes **harmony** (熙) as root,
**refinement** (精) as foundation,
like a tree—
if its roots are not deep, it is uprooted;
if its foundation is not firm, it falls—
must deeply conceal his **qi**,
firmly guard his **refinement**,
never letting it leak or disperse.

the **Dao of enduring life and long vision**.
*—Annotation:* Those with deep roots and firm foundations
are the **Dao of enduring life and long vision**.

*—Annotation:* Root, origin.
Foundation, trace.
The root can generate the foundation—
using this analogy, the origin gives rise to the trace.
To elaborate: the origin is **deep-rooted**,
its progression is **firm-founded**.
If roots are not deep, they become precarious;
if foundations are not firm, they scatter.
It is because roots are deep that one endures long,
because foundations are firm that one’s vision lasts—
this explains why the Sage’s **virtue** and **Dao** are both perfect,
hence concluding with the **Dao of enduring life and long vision**.

*—Annotation:* Vision, illumination.

To govern a great state,
one must treat it as cooking a small fresh delicacy.

「*—Annotation:* Fresh, fish.

To cook small fish, one does not remove its entrails,
does not scrape its scales,
does not dare to prod it—
lest it disintegrate.

To govern a state with excess brings chaos below;
to govern the body with excess scatters the essence.

*—Annotation:* Cook, to boil.
Fresh, fish.
It is said that if one prods a small fish while boiling, it will disintegrate,
but if left undisturbed, it remains whole—
a metaphor: governing with *wuwei* brings great peace,
while agitation leads to ruin.

To act in the Way across the world,
its spirits lose their menace.
*—Annotation:* When one governs the world with virtue and the Way,
ghosts and spirits dare not manifest their malevolence against people.

*—Annotation:* *Lì* (莅), to oversee.
It is said that those who govern with righteousness
prevent malevolent spirits from causing harm.

*—Annotation by Gu (顾):* Spirit refers to their supernatural efficacy.

When one governs the world with the Way,
the land is at great peace—
in such times, ghosts and spirits lack the power to manifest harm.
When one cultivates the Way in the body,
true illumination attains unity—
those who attain unity are unharmed by spectral forces.

It is not that their spirits lack efficacy,
but that evil cannot enter the righteous—
thus, they cannot harm natural beings.

*—Annotation by Yu (御):* Earlier, it was said that their spirits lack menace,
not that they are extinguished and devoid of spirit—
only that they dare not manifest harm to people.

*—Annotation:* Spirit, supernatural efficacy.
It is not that these spirits lack efficacy,
but that they bring blessings to people
without harming anything.

For governing the body,
*xièméi* (馅魅) refers to restless, licentious thoughts.
It is said that when one governs the body with righteous principles,
restless thoughts cannot harm virtuous conduct—
even if the mind stirs, true illumination remains,
and thus cannot harm conduct.

*—Annotation by Jiéjiě (节解):* It is not that spirits lack efficacy,
but that the Way resides in the person—
thus, their spirit cannot harm them.

It is not that their spirit cannot harm people,
nor that the Sage cannot harm people.
*—Annotation:* It is not that spirits cannot harm people,
but that the Sage, in office, does not harm people—
thus, ghosts dare not interfere.

*—Annotation by Yu (御):* Ghosts that manifest harm are so because
the Sage has acted contrary to the Way.
Now, ghosts do not manifest harm because
the Sage acts with *wuwei* and purity.

*—Annotation:* All manuscripts originally read *yì* (亦),
but Zhang Xi’s and Master Lu’s texts read *zhī* (之).
Since *yì* and *zhī* resemble each other in script,
*zhī* was mistakenly written as *yì*—now corrected to *zhī*.

This means that it is not that these spirits do not harm things,
but that the Sage, acting in the Way, can subdue evil—
thus, ghosts do not harm people.
Power lies in the Sage’s righteousness—
hence, it is also said that the Sage does not harm people.

For governing the body, it is said that
even if the mind stirs, it cannot harm stillness—
for Sage-wisdom subdues it, so though it moves,
it cannot disturb the tranquil.

*—Annotation by Jiéjiě (节解):* It is said that when a person’s conduct aligns with divine clarity,
there are no hidden faults within—
thus, ghosts cannot harm them.
There are no outward crimes—
thus, the Sage does not punish them.

Heavenly spirits stand guard outside the body,
protecting its form within.
Original *qi* is undifferentiated—
all aid in governing the body.

One’s conduct follows Heaven above:
the head wears the luminosity of sun and moon,
stars and constellations arrayed within the body.
Spirit and breath nourish on *yùyīng* (玉英).
Thus, the two do not harm each other.
*—Annotation:* Ghosts and the Sage—
both do not harm each other.

Hence, virtue reciprocates.
*—Annotation:* Those who do not harm each other
allow people to thrive in the Yang,
ghosts to thrive in the Yin—
people preserve their life and nature,
ghosts preserve their spirit—
thus, virtue reciprocates.

*—Annotation:* The two—ghosts in the Yin, people in the Yang—
each finds its place, unharmed,
guarding its domain.
With no transgression between them,
people extend virtue to ghosts,
ghosts extend grace to people—
virtue and kindness circulate,
thus they attach to one another in harmony.

For governing the body,
one stills agitation and returns to tranquility,
fulfilling one’s conduct.
Since movement and stillness are simultaneous,
what is there to restrain?

Now it is clarified:
restraint without restraint,
response without expectation—
not responding yet responding.

A great state is the confluence of the world.
*—Annotation:* To govern a great state is like the river and sea,
which reside in the lowest flow,
not opposing the minutest currents.

The world’s gathering place.
*—Annotation:* A great state is where all under Heaven assemble.

*—Annotation by Jiéjiě (节解):* The *nìwán* (泥丸) is both great state and small state—
receiving from above, it irrigates the *dāntián* (丹田),
which is the earthly frontier.

*—Annotation:* A great state refers to a realm of ten thousand chariots,
*jiāo* (郊), the outer walls.
*Zhī* (之), to go.
It is said that a great state, vast and accommodating,
is like the river and sea—
the river and sea lie beneath all currents,
thus all streams rush toward them.
A great state, acting in the Way with humility and gentleness,
thus all nations come and pay homage,
revering virtue and filling the outer walls.

*Běn* (本) is also written as *jiāo* (交).
A great state, acting in humility,
thus exchanges with small states.

The world’s gathering place is the receptive.
The receptive, ever still, overcomes the assertive.
Soft, yielding, and harmonious—
not competing.
Women can submit to men because Yin overcomes Yang.
Their tranquility comes from not seeking first.

*—Annotation:* The world’s frontier—
echoing the preceding text to generate the following sentence.
The receptive is female, still;
the assertive is male, restless.
It is said that a great state can make all nations submit and assemble
only by employing gentle, receptive stillness—
thus overcoming restless assertiveness.
If it acts with rigidity, it cannot govern.

Thus, it is known that governing the body and the state
must prioritize gentleness and compliance.
With stillness as the foundation.
*—Annotation:* The Yin Way values tranquil stillness as humility.

Thus, a great state, humbling itself to small states,
takes them in.
*—Annotation:* Those who humble themselves
constantly attract others.

The *Commentary* states: **"以"** means **"to employ."** **"下"** means **"humility."** **"取"** means **"to assimilate and transform."** The Sage-King, ruling over great states, skillfully employs humility and gentleness, thus assimilating all nations—near and far—into submission, bringing tribute through layered tributaries. Is this not fitting?

When small states humble themselves to great states, they gather under their protection.
*—Annotation:* This means that regardless of a state’s size, if it practices humility and nurtures others, it will have no faults.

*—Master Yu’s Exegesis:* The great state takes the small as its subjects and servants; the small state takes the great as its allies and support.

*—Master Jie’s Explanation:* The *Upper Seal* (泥丸) represents the great state, while the *Lower Elixir Field* (丹田) represents the small. Practicing the *One Way*, one closes the breath and swallows saliva, letting it descend to the *Lower Elixir Field*, where it transforms into blood, blood into essence, and essence into *qi*. Through embryonic breathing, it is drawn upward to replenish the brain, then pushed and drawn outward, spreading like clouds across the four seas. Thus, the great takes from the small, and the small gathers under the great.

The small state, knowing its own lowliness, guards its place with feminine gentleness, gathering within the great state out of reverence for its virtue and moral influence.

Thus, sometimes the great takes, sometimes the small gathers.
*—Annotation:* **"下"** refers to the great state humbling itself to the small, and the small humbling itself to the great, alternately taking each other through righteousness.

*—Commentary:* **"或"** means **"indeterminate"**—when the small state employs gentleness, it gathers under the great; when the great employs humility, it takes the submission of all nations. Though both use humility, their methods differ—one gathers, the other takes.

The great state seeks only to encompass and nurture others; the small state seeks only to enter and serve.
*—Annotation:* If the great state does not lose its humility, it will encompass and assimilate the small, making them its subjects and servants.

*—Commentary:* The great state employs humility without ulterior motives—only to love and embrace the small states. The small state employs gentleness without hidden intentions—only to enter the great state’s embrace, admiring its virtue and seeking to serve.

Both achieve what they desire: the great state should thus be humble.
*—Annotation:* Great and small states each seek what they desire, yet the great state should especially practice humility.

*—Master Yu’s Exegesis:* One seeks subjects and servants; the other seeks allies and support. Both obtain what they desire, yet the great state must always guard against arrogance, hence it should be humble.

*—Commentary:* The two refer to great and small states—both encompassing and serving, each fulfilling their desires. Though both may use gentleness, the great state should particularly emphasize humility. Why? For things naturally press down the great—small things do not overpower the great. Thus, those who overpower the great should humble themselves, adhering to the principle of lowering the high.

*—Master Jie’s Explanation:* The two refer to the *Upper Seal* (泥丸) and the *Lower Elixir Field* (丹田). The *Upper Seal* desires descending *qi*, while the *Lower Elixir Field* desires ascending *qi*, together forming a single divine transformation.

The *Dao* is the profound essence of all things.
*—Annotation:* **"奥"** means **"to conceal."** The *Dao* is the hidden repository of all things, containing all within itself.

*—Commentary:* The *Dao* is the wondrous, boundless principle of emptiness and flow, the innate nature of all beings. **"奥"** means both **"profound"** and **"hidden"**—it speaks of the *Dao* generating all existence, encompassing all life, without exclusion, thus serving as the hidden repository of all things.

The treasure of the virtuous,
*—Annotation:* The virtuous person regards the *Dao* as their bodily treasure, thus daring not to stray from it.

*—Master Yu’s Exegesis:* The virtuous know that adherence to the *Dao* brings flourishing, while abandonment brings ruin. Thus, they cherish and practice it without ceasing.

*—Commentary:* Those who cultivate the *Dao* perceive true nature, obtaining the *Mystic Pearl* in the *Crimson Stream*, hence they treasure and honor it.

The refuge of the unvirtuous.
*—Annotation:* The *Dao* is the unvirtuous’ last reliance. Even in calamity or peril, they may yet repent and humble themselves.

*—Commentary:* **"保"** means **"to uphold."** The vulgar, entangled in material desires and competitive striving, cannot uphold the *Dao*. Their speech is flattering and deceitful, like market haggling—buyers seek swift purchase, sellers swift sale. Unable to guard true humility, they prioritize self over others, yet still claim noble conduct, thus distancing themselves from the *Dao*.

Sweet speech can be sold in the market,
*—Annotation:* Only sweet speech can be traded in the marketplace. Once the exchange is complete, the buyer and seller part ways—no further harmony remains. Sweet words and flattery are sought eagerly by buyers, swiftly sold by merchants.

Noble conduct can elevate a person.
*—Annotation:* **"加"** means **"to distinguish."** A person of noble conduct can set themselves apart from the common, yet this is insufficient to elevate the *Dao*.

*—Master Yu’s Exegesis:* Sweet and pleasing words can be traded like goods in the market; noble and lofty conduct can distinguish a person. By analogy, the Sage uses sweet, virtuous teachings and lofty, detached conduct to transform the unvirtuous—just as merchants sell goods, people follow in emulation.

*—Commentary:* The unvirtuous, with shallow hearts and hollow speech, adorn themselves with flattery like market traders seeking profit, yet they cannot uphold true humility. They place themselves before others, boast of their own nobility, and domineer over people. Such conduct, far from the *Dao*, only distances them further.

Why abandon those who are unvirtuous?
*—Annotation:* Though unvirtuous, they should be transformed by the *Dao*. In the age of the Three Sovereigns, there were no abandoned people—virtue and teaching were pure.

*—Master Yu’s Exegesis:* Even the unvirtuous can be transformed by virtue. Why abandon them?

*—Commentary:* **"奚"** means **"why."** The Sage’s *Dao* is boundless in its reach, universally salvific. Though beings may be unvirtuous, how could there be abandonment? Its compassion covers all, aiding without exclusion.

Thus, the Son of Heaven is enthroned,
and the Three Ministers are appointed,
*—Annotation:* To transform the unvirtuous through teaching.

*—Commentary:* Having aided without exclusion, the proper order of the world must be established. Thus, the Son of Heaven is enthroned to govern all affairs, and the Three Ministers are appointed to discuss the *Dao*.

To harmonize *Yin* and *Yang*,
and to refine and flourish in governance,
with intent on guiding all classes like a vessel,
and protecting all life from poison.

Since the opening of the cosmos, the True Sovereign has manifested in the world, embodying the *Dao* and, through expedient means, assuming the role of emperor, establishing an administration to emulate the Three Pure Ones.

Though one may present a jade tablet held aloft—
preceded by a team of four horses—
it is not as good as simply sitting and advancing this *Dao*.
*—Annotation:* Even if one arrives with a high-capped carriage bearing a perfect jade tablet, it is still inferior to sitting in quietude and cultivating this *Dao*.

*—Commentary:* *"Jade tablet"* refers to holding a round, flawless jade tablet. *"Four horses"* denotes a single carriage. In ancient times, when summoning a scholar, one would first purify oneself and present the jade tablet, then dispatch a carriage drawn by four horses. Though such offerings bring wealth and honor, they pale in comparison to the effortless, forgetful cultivation of this *Dao*. Why? For cultivating the *Dao* grants longevity, while wealth and honor are but great calamities. Hence, Guangchengzi said: *"Human life in the world is like a cloud soaring in the sky—its presence fleeting, vanishing in an instant. Measured by this standard, it is better to withdraw far from such things."*

*—Master Gu’s Note:* To maintain a spotless reputation is called "noble conduct," but one who prides himself in conduct may surpass others—yet this is not the ultimate.

*—Master Sun’s Note:* Fine words can outvalue all goods, and noble conduct can elevate one above others. Yet the *Dao* expressed in words is bland, without flavor. The Sage acts without action, teaches without speech. How could he adorn speech to please the ears of the people or embellish conduct to dazzle the eyes of all things?

Why then was this *Dao* held in such esteem in antiquity?
It is not that seeking brings attainment,

「Annotations: The reason antiquity held this *Dao* in such high regard was not that one could seek it daily and attain it through distant travels, but rather that it could be found within oneself. ○Master Gu notes: There is no need for distant searching—one seeks it daily, but its accumulation lies within the self, and its attainment rests in the heart. Its profound enlightenment resides within me. Would one only seek to avoid guilt when already guilty? ○Annotations: "Guilt" refers to suffering in turbulent times under tyrannical rulers, where arbitrary punishments prevail. Cultivating the *Dao* can resolve resentment and spare one from the evils of the multitude. ○Kumarajiva and others say: By dedicating oneself to the virtuous *Dao*, one avoids all evils. Thus, it is held in highest esteem by the world. ○Annotations: With profound *Dao* and virtue, nothing escapes its nurturing embrace—preserving life, governing the realm with ease, and remaining serene in non-action. Hence, it is worthy of the world’s reverence. ○Master Yu notes: The *Dao* lies in enlightenment, not in seeking. Unlike wealth or silk, which can be sought daily and obtained, it is said that one does not seek it daily to attain it. Once enlightened, there is no burden of guilt—why wait for guilt to arise before seeking release? Thus, it is worthy of the world’s reverence.

○Exegesis: Why did the sages of old hold this *Dao* so precious? Simply because it is not a matter of seeking it day by day to obtain it. The enlightened see the *Dao* before them, while the deluded wander countless kalpas without comprehension. Even if, from beginningless time, one has been bound by birth and death, by guilt and retribution—once returned to the source, all is instantly freed. Such divine power is beyond measure, hence the world reveres it as the object of all inquiry.

Non-action (*wuwei*), ○Annotations: Achieved through natural unfolding, requiring no artificial contrivance.
○Exegesis: In perfect harmony with the heart’s essence, it never loses its true illumination. Illuminating all action, it is itself the very nature of non-action. Thus, non-action is action, and action is non-action.

○Concise Analysis: To say "one does not act" means one does not act for the self.

Non-doing (*wu shi*), ○Annotations: Refraining from preparation, eliminating distractions and unnecessary affairs.
○Exegesis: "Affairs" refers to sensory phenomena—colors, sounds, material forms—all things. In perfect alignment with true emptiness, one’s illumination does not contradict stillness. Though engaged in affairs, there is no attachment to them.

○Concise Analysis: To say "one does not do" means one does not act for others.

Flavorless (*wu wei*), ○Annotations: Deep contemplation and foresight—savoring the *Dao*’s essence.
○Exegesis: "Flavor" denotes attachment and clinging. With both root and object emptied, color and taste both cease. Though one may still engage with taste, there is no lingering obstruction.

Great and small, much and little, ○Annotations: A call to observe restraint and discipline.»

「One wishes to invert greatness into smallness, abundance into scarcity—this is the Dao/Way of nature. Returning resentment with virtue.

○Annotations: Cultivating goodness and the Dao/Way, extinguishing calamity before it arises.
○Concise Analysis: For those who act without doing (*wu wei*), engage in affairs without attachment, and savor taste without lingering—if greatness and smallness, abundance and scarcity, are both kept within their proper bounds, then there is no transgression to provoke resentment. If one’s mind arises with chasing sensory phenomena and exceeds one’s natural portion, harming one’s true nature, then there is neither greatness nor smallness—all become resentment and anger. Now, since one abides by one’s portion and maintains perfect harmony, one can thus return resentment with virtue.

○Exegesis: Resentment is anger. Returning means recompensing. From the beginning of existence onward, great and small offenses, abundant and scarce deeds—if one seeks to cry out and eliminate them all at once, one must first apply the three kinds of virtuous mind: *wu wei*, non-attachment to affairs, and non-lingering with taste. Then the Three Worlds are emptied, all dharmas are tranquil and void, and the unity of self and other is realized—what lingering remains? Hence the previous chapter states: "By what virtue/power can one be freed from crime?" This is what it refers to.

Endeavoring the difficult in the easy, ○Annotations: To seek to accomplish difficult matters, one must first practice in easy ones before they are perfected.

○Exegesis: *Tú* means to plan and measure. *Nán* refers to retribution for misdeeds. *Yì* denotes the initial mind.

It states that those who wish to plan and dispel the hardships of evil paths must first observe the initial mind. If there is no action, there will be no retribution.

The great surpasses the small.
○Annotation: To accomplish great deeds, one must begin with small ones—calamities arise from the small.

○The Master said: Those who indulge their passions and desires find all actions difficult and all matters great. Now, if one seeks to plan and measure their difficulties, to undertake their greatness, they must act when their nature is yet undispersed and their boundaries untransgressed—that is, when they are still in their easy and small beginnings.

○Exegesis: *Xiǎo* means small. Great calamities originate from the minute. This clarifies that to extinguish evil fruits, one must first eliminate the causes.

Great undertakings under heaven must begin with what is easy.
Great affairs under heaven must begin with what is small.
○Annotation: This elucidates the earlier precepts.

Thus, the Sage never undertakes what is great.
○Annotation: This reflects humility.

○Exegesis: *Zuò* means to initiate. The hardships and retributions of the world necessarily arise from the easy mind—speaking of how a single thought of the initial mind can easily create karma. Hence, great calamities must begin with the small, growing from the subtle to the manifest, gradually forming great conflicts. Therefore, to remove evil retributions, one must first restrain the initial mind.

Thus, one can achieve the great.
○Annotation: All under heaven converge upon it.

「Lightly given promises are bound to lack trustworthiness. ○*Annotation*: One does not lightly speak.

○*Exegesis*: It speaks of those who lightly promise—losing the substance of self-respect. ○*Commentary*: Here, a metaphor is introduced. *Nuo* (诺) means "to assent or agree." *Guǎ* (寡) means "few or scarce." Those who lightly assent to others’ requests will inevitably be few in truthfulness, later incurring the blame of untrustworthiness.

To act lightly will bring many hardships. ○*Annotation*: One must not neglect potential calamities. ○*Commentary*: Lightly assenting to others leads to few truths; acting lightly in deeds will later bring many hardships.

○*Commentary*: This combines the metaphors. Lightly assenting to others leads to untrustworthiness, just as lightly committing sins will invite the heavy retribution of the Eight Difficulties (八难). Therefore, those who cultivate the Dao must be solemn and deliberate, never reckless.

Thus, the Sage finds even difficulty hard,
○*Annotation*: The Sage weighs actions and matters with great deliberation, seeking to block their origins.
and so never faces difficulty.
○*Annotation*: The Sage’s lifelong absence of hardship arises from deep avoidance of harm.

○*Commentary*: *Yóu* (犹) means "still" or "even." The Sage of true Daoic cultivation has no difficulty or ease, yet still finds difficulty hard and does not act upon it, thus avoiding blame for hardship. How much more so for the common folk who indulge their passions!

This is a general precept for the Sage and the vulgar alike.
Hence, Guo Xiang’s annotation on *Zhuangzi* states: "The Sage, though entering fire, does not feel its heat, yet never once stepped into the fire." Again, he says: "Avoiding fire is not fleeing its heat—this is the very meaning."

Its tranquility is easy to uphold,
○*Annotation*: In governing oneself and the realm, the tranquil is easily maintained.
○*Commentary*: *An* (安) means "tranquil," *chí* (持) means "to uphold." When the Sage’s spirit is still and undisturbed, no desires have yet arisen—at such times, it is very easy to cultivate and guard against them.

its latent state is easy to plan against;
○*Annotation*: When desires or calamities have not yet taken form, they are easy to forestall.
○*Commentary*: *Zhào* (兆) means "signs or indications," *móu* (谋) means "to plan or strategize." Though desires may arise in the mind, no form has yet manifested in the external world. At this moment, one should restrain the mind and return to stillness, for the karmic deed has not yet taken root—thus, it is easy to plan against and consider.

its frailty is easy to shatter,
○*Annotation*: Calamities and chaos have not yet stirred in the morning or evening, nor have desires shown in form or expression—like something brittle, they are easily broken away.
its subtlety is easy to disperse.
○*Annotation*: Before matters become manifest, they are small and easily scattered.

○*Commentary*: *Cuì* (脆) means "brittle or fragile." Like a gourd whose fruit has not yet hardened, it is fragile and easily broken. When the initial mind arises with defilements, it is the same—karmic deeds are shallow and thus easily removed.

To act upon it before it arises,
○*Annotation*: When one seeks to do something, one must act before its sprouts take root.
○*Commentary*: *Wéi* (为) means "to cultivate or study." The Sage’s cultivation and guarding are constant, even before desires arise—this explains *its tranquility is easy to uphold*.

to govern it before it falls into disorder.
○*Commentary*: *Luàn* (乱) means "disorder," referring to the mind being ensnared by karmic deeds and thus clouded and disordered. Now, though the mind may sprout defilements, no form has yet appeared in the external world. At this very moment, it is easy to align with reason—this explains *its latent state is easy to plan against*.

A tree with arms’ embrace grows from a hair’s end;
○*Annotation*: From small to great.
○*Commentary*: A hundred-surrounding tree (百围之木) originates from a hair’s tip (毫微). This metaphor illustrates how threefold heavy conflicts (三涂重衅) arise from a single defiled thought. The tree is used as a metaphor because, though it grows from nothingness, it is not inherently "nothing"—it is formed by the four dusts (四尘). Likewise, defilements arise from emptiness, yet are not truly "nothing," for they are shaped by the four dusts as well.

Troubles and vexations are thus of no inherent substance,
○*Annotation*: They emerge from emptiness.

nine-tiered towers are built from piled earth;
○*Annotation*: From low to high.
○*Commentary*: *Céng* (层) is akin to "levels." A nine-level tower (九级之台) begins with a single basket of soil (累土). This metaphor illustrates how nine nights of calamity (九夜之祸) arise from a single defiled thought—it deeply warns all learners to be cautious.

and a thousand-li journey begins beneath one’s feet. ○*Annotation*: From near to far.

The *Commentary* reads: The *River-Upper* text states "thousand-li," but here it speaks of "hundred *rèn*."[^1]
For seven *chi* is called a *rèn*, and a lofty peak of a hundred *rèn* in height begins with its first step.
Treading the eightfold gates of suffering arises from a defiled mind.
The prior analogy has yet to be fully realized.

The one who acts brings about its ruin.
*Annotation*: To act in affairs corrupts spontaneity; to act in principle corrupts benevolence and compassion; to act in form corrupts spirit.

The one who clings loses it.
*Annotation*: Clinging to gain leads to its cessation; clinging to enmity forfeits one’s life; obstinacy yields nothing, while yielding returns it.

The *Master’s Response* says: All worldly attachments are deluded and clinging; unable to forget or release, every movement becomes attachment. The clinger seeks possession, yet reason dictates loss.

The *Commentary* explains: The Supreme Dao is empty and boundless, without action or attachment. Worldly minds are inverted, with attachment and contrivance. Action leads to peril, hence "ruin"; clinging contradicts reality, hence "loss."

Thus, the Sage acts not, and so knows no ruin;
*Annotation*: The Sage does not indulge in superficial adornments, nor covet material gains or harm others, and thus knows no corruption.

acts not in clinging, and so knows no loss.
*Annotation*: The Sage teaches the ignorant with virtue and aids the poor with wealth, holding nothing back, and thus loses nothing to others.

Yet the people, in their pursuits, often nearing completion, bring ruin upon themselves.
*Annotation*: "From" means "to act." The people’s affairs, nearing fulfillment, grow greedy for position and fame, indulgent in excess, and thus self-destruct.
The *Commentary* states: "Near" means "close." All people engage in contrived endeavors, restless and competitive, distinguishing and selecting without foresight. Ever seeking near-completion, they begin but do not finish, hence their frequent ruin.

The *Book of Documents* says: Few have beginnings, but fewer still have endings.
To be as vigilant at the end as at the beginning brings no ruin.
*Annotation*: The end must mirror the beginning; do not grow slack.

The *Commentary* warns: This admonishes learners to fortify their Dao-heart, steadfast from beginning to end, lest there be ruin. Within the Six Perfections, this is the fourth.

Thus, the Sage desires what others do not;
*Annotation*: The Sage desires what others reject—when others seek display, the Sage hides brilliance; when others seek ornamentation, the Sage seeks simplicity; when others lust after wealth and form, the Sage seeks virtue and power.

values not the hard-to-obtain treasures;
*Annotation*: The Sage does not dazzle with adornments nor devalue stone while esteeming jade.

learns what others do not learn;
*Annotation*: The Sage learns what others cannot—while others learn cunning, the Sage learns spontaneity; while others learn to govern the world, the Sage learns to govern the self, guarding the true Dao.

and reverses the errors of the multitude.
*Annotation*: The multitude learns in reverse—errors become ends, reality becomes superficial. To reverse is to restore the original and true.

The *Master’s Response* says: The Sage does not seek excessive learning.

The *Commentary* elaborates: The Sage’s luminous insight comes from Heaven, differing from common wisdom. Knowing all phenomena as empty and illusory, no desirable object appears. Thus, within desire, no desire-form is seen. Having no desire for what others desire, how could jade and silk be valued?

Further, comprehending true and false as undifferentiated, the Sage neither learns nor fails to learn. Learning is not learning, and not-learning is learning—no distinction between learning and not-learning. Hence, the *Western Ascension Sutra* says: *"I learn nothing, yet I illuminate spontaneity."*

The *River-Upper* text reads *"reverse,"* while other editions say *"prevent."*[^2] To prevent is to guard against the calamities of those who cling to contrivance, unable to forget learning.

To aid the natural unfolding of all things,
*Annotation*: Teaching people to return to their original and true nature, desiring to assist the natural essence of all things.

yet daring not to act.
*Annotation*: The Sage’s movements follow naturally, daring not to contrive, lest they stray from the root.

The *Commentary* explains: "Aid" means "assist" and "guide." All beings inherently possess a correct and natural disposition, yet delusion and attachment cause them to stray from the Dao and lose authenticity. Now, the Sage intends to teach the method of "learning not to learn," guiding them to return to their roots. Though the Sage exhausts principle and fulfills nature, there is neither action nor inaction.

Now, when the Sage is said to "not act," it is to assist and guide the multitude, restoring them to their natural principles. Hence, it is said, "daring not to act."

---
[^1]: *Annotation*: A *rèn* (仞) is a traditional unit of measurement (~7.2 meters).
[^2]: *Annotation*: "复" (*fù*, reverse) vs. "备" (*bèi*, prevent).

「吴郡征士顾欢述
The recluse Gu Xuan of Wujun says:

古之善为道者，
The ancients who excelled in cultivating the Dao,

注：谓古之善以道治身及治国者。
*Annotation*: refers to those ancient sages who skillfully governed themselves and their realm through the Dao.

非以明民，
were not those who enlightened the people,

注：不以道教民明智奸巧也。
*Annotation*: nor those who taught the Dao to the people to sharpen their cunning and cleverness.

将欲愚之。
but sought to make them simple.

注：将以道德教民，使质朴不诈伪也。
*Annotation*: They aimed to instruct the people with virtue/power, so that their innate simplicity would not be corrupted by deceit.

○御曰：人君善为道者，
The Imperial Commentary states: The sovereign who excels in cultivating the Dao,

非以其道明示於人，
does not openly display his Dao to the people,

将导之以和，
but guides them through harmony,

使归复於朴，
restoring them to their original simplicity,

令如愚也。
making them appear as though they were simple.

○疏：为道，犹修道也。
The *Commentary* explains: "Cultivating the Dao" is the same as "practicing the Dao."

言古昔善修道之士，
Those ancient sages who practiced the Dao—

实智内明，
though inwardly possessed of profound wisdom,

无幽不烛，
penetrating all hidden depths,

外若愚时，
yet outwardly appearing simple—

不耀於人，
did not flaunt their wisdom before others,

闭智塞聪，
concealing their intelligence and dulling their perceptiveness,

韬光晦迹也。
hiding their brilliance and obscuring their traces.

民之难治，
The people are difficult to govern,

以其智多。
because their cleverness is excessive.

注：以其智太多而为巧伪。
*Annotation*: Their excessive wisdom leads to cunning and deceit.

○御曰：君将明道以临下，
The Imperial Commentary states: When the sovereign seeks to clarify the Dao and rule over his subjects,

人必役智以应上，
the people must employ their cleverness to meet his expectations,

智多则诈兴，
and the more they rely on their cleverness, the more deceit arises,

是以难理。
making governance arduous.

○疏：治，理也，智，分别也。
The *Commentary* explains: "Govern" is the same as "order," and "wisdom" here refers to "discrimination."

言众生所以难治者，
The reason the people are difficult to govern is that

为心多分别，
their minds are filled with excessive discrimination,

不能虚忘，
and they cannot empty themselves of it through forgetfulness,

故难化也。
thus rendering them hard to transform.

故以智治国，
Therefore, when wisdom is used to govern the state,

国之贼；
it becomes the state’s ruin.

注：使智慧之人治国政之事，必远道德，妄作威福，为国之贼也。
*Annotation*: Those who are wise in worldly affairs, when entrusted with state governance, will stray from virtue/power and arbitrarily wield authority, becoming the state’s ruin.

不以智治国，
But when wisdom is not used to govern the state,

国之福。
it becomes the state’s blessing.

注：不使智慧之人治国之政事，则民守正直，不为邪饰，上下相亲，君臣同力，故为国之福也。
*Annotation*: Then the people uphold righteousness, unadorned by deceit; the ruler and subjects are in harmony, and the sovereign and ministers unite in effort, making it the state’s blessing.

○御曰：若不用巧智之臣，
The Imperial Commentary states: If the sovereign does not employ clever and cunning ministers,

但取纯德之士，
but instead selects those of pure virtue/power,

使偃息蕃魏，
allowing them to rest and flourish like the grass and weeds,

弄丸难解，
while the trivial matters of governance remain unsolved like a child’s plaything,

自然智诈日薄，
then cleverness and deceit will naturally diminish day by day,

淳朴日兴，
while sincerity and simplicity will flourish,

人和则年丰，
and when the people are harmonious, the land will yield abundant harvests,

故是国之福也。
thus becoming the state’s blessing.

○疏：若用明智察俗以理物者，
The *Commentary* explains: If one employs those who are clever and discerning in worldly affairs to govern the people,

既乖淳和，
they will contradict the natural harmony of the Dao,

人同贼害，
and the people will align with the harm of deceit.

言无为至虚至静，
It is said that the Sage who governs through non-action attains supreme emptiness and tranquility,

忘心遣智，
abandoning the mind and relinquishing cleverness,

尸居玄默，
remaining as still as a corpse in profound silence,

以斯御世者，
and ruling the world thus—

其唯上德乎。
this is what is called "supreme virtue/power."

以此格量，
By this standard of measure,

胜劣可见，
the superior and inferior become apparent,

治国既尔，
and if this is the way to govern the state,

身亦宜然。
then it is also the way to govern oneself.

知此两者，
Understanding these two principles—

亦楷式。
is also a model.

注：两者谓智与不智也，常能知智为贼，不智为福，是治身治国之法式也。
*Annotation*: These two refer to wisdom and non-wisdom. If one always knows that wisdom is ruinous and non-wisdom is a blessing, this is the model for governing oneself and the state.

○御曰：役智诈则害於人，
The Imperial Commentary states: Employing cleverness and deceit harms the people,

任纯德则福於国，
while entrusting to those of pure virtue/power blesses the state.

人君能知此两者，
If the sovereign understands these two principles,

委任纯德之臣，
and delegates authority to ministers of pure virtue/power,

是亦为君楷模之法式也。
then this too is the model for sovereigns.

○疏：两者前文智与不智也。
The *Commentary* explains: These two principles are wisdom and non-wisdom from the previous text.

能知智为贼，
If one knows that wisdom is ruinous,

不智为德，
and non-wisdom is virtue/power,

则可为修身之楷模，
then one can serve as a model for self-cultivation,

治国之洪范也。
and as the grand standard for governing the state.

○节解曰：谓去智守愚，
The *Summary of the Dao* states: This means abandoning cleverness and preserving simplicity,

则病除气盛，
so that ailments are dispelled and *qi* flourishes,

此治身之法式也。
this being the model for governing oneself.

常知楷式，
If one always understands these models,

是谓玄德。
then this is called "Daoist virtue/power."

注：玄，天也，能知治国治身之法式，是谓与天同德也。
*Annotation*: "Daoist" (*Xuán*) is the same as "Heavenly." Those who understand the models for governing the state and oneself are said to align with Heaven’s virtue/power.

○疏：常能知无分别为治身之楷式者，
The *Commentary* explains: Those who constantly understand that non-discrimination is the model for governing oneself

可谓深玄之大德也。
can be called the "supreme virtue/power of profound Daoism."

玄德深远，
The virtue/power of the profound Dao is deep and boundless,

注：玄德之人深不可测，远不可极也。
*Annotation*: The person of profound Daoist virtue/power is unfathomable in depth and boundless in extent.

与物反，
and contrary to all things.

注：玄德之人与万物反异，万物欲益於己，玄德欲施与人。
*Annotation*: The person of profound Daoist virtue/power is opposite to all things; while all things seek to benefit themselves, Daoist virtue/power seeks to give to others.

○节解曰：谓一之深远，
The *Summary of the Dao* states: This refers to the depth and boundlessness of the One,

乃在太清，
which resides in the *Ta Qing* (Supreme Clarity),

降下绛宫，
descending into the *Jiang Gong* (Vermilion Palace),

入子形中，
entering the form of the child,

物皆枯死，
while all things wither and die,

子独长生，
the child alone endures eternally,

故与物反也。
thus being contrary to all things.

○疏：冥真契道谓之玄德，
The *Commentary* explains: "Daoist virtue/power" is the name for the mysterious truth that aligns with the Dao.

穷源极际谓之深远，
"Deep and boundless" refers to exhausting the source and reaching the utmost limits.

深远之智，
The wisdom of profound Daoism,

智乖於俗，
is contrary to worldly cleverness,

故与物反。
thus being contrary to all things.

然后乃至大顺。
Only then does one attain *Da Shun* (Great Harmony).

注：玄德之人，与万物反异，故能至大顺，顺天理也。
*Annotation*: The person of profound Daoist virtue/power, being contrary to all things, thus attains Great Harmony, which aligns with Heaven’s principles.

○疏：顺有两种，一顺於理，二顺於俗，
The *Commentary* explains: There are two kinds of harmony: one is harmony with principle, the other is harmony with the world.

顺理则契於妙本，
Harmony with principle aligns with the wondrous root,

顽俗则同尘降逵。
while harmony with the world aligns with dust and descends to the mundane.

问曰：前言反物，后言顺俗，
*A Question* asks: Earlier it says "contrary to all things," later it says "harmony with the world."

则前反后顺，
If "contrary" precedes "harmony," then

文岂类乎。
does the text not seem contradictory?

答曰：前言反者，
*The Answer* states: The earlier "contrary" refers to

此言明，
this explicit teaching—

不反而反，
not being contrary yet being contrary,

后言顺者，
the later "harmony" refers to

亦不顺而顺，
also not being harmony yet being harmony,

不顺而顺，
harmony that does not contradict principle,

顺不乖反，
and principle that does not contradict harmony—

不反而反，
not being contrary yet being contrary,

反不乖顺，
and not being harmony yet being harmony—

亦何所嫌也。
so what is there to object to?

问曰：经称大顺，
*A Question* asks: The text speaks of "Great Harmony,"

顺亦有大小乎。
does harmony not have degrees of greatness and smallness?

答曰：夫以顺对违，
*The Answer* states: Harmony is contrasted with defiance,

虽顺而小，
though it may be small harmony,

今不顺不违，
but when one neither defies nor aligns with worldly matters,

而违而顺，
yet both defies and harmonizes—

顺不异违，
harmony is not different from defiance,

违不乖顺，
and defiance is not contrary to harmony—

所以出处语默，
thus, in conduct and speech, one follows the Dao silently,

唯道是从，
conforming only to the Dao,

和光同尘，
blending one’s brilliance with the dust,

而恒顺於理，
yet always harmonizing with principle,

不问顺与不顺，
without regard to harmony or defiance,

不顺与顺，
or defiance and harmony—

一时皆顺，
all are harmonious in the present moment,

是故无顺无不顺，
thus there is neither harmony nor defiance,

而能顺能不顺，
yet one is capable of both harmony and defiance,

乃至非顺非不顺，
even reaching beyond harmony and defiance,

故名为大顺也。
thus it is called "Great Harmony."

江海所以能为百谷王者，
The reason the rivers and seas can be sovereigns of all streams is that

以其善下之，
they excel in humility.

注：江海以卑下，故众流共归之，若人民归就王者也。
*Annotation*: The rivers and seas, being lowly and humble, thus gather all streams, just as the people gather to a sovereign.

○疏：王，往也。
The *Commentary* explains: "Sovereign" means "to go toward."

百谷，百川也。
"All streams" refers to the hundred rivers.

言江海所以百川之所归往者，
The rivers and seas are where the hundred rivers converge because

以其善居洼下之地也。
they excel in dwelling in lowly and humble places.

人能退身谦下，
If a person can retreat in self, humble and lowly,

虚柔容物者，
and empty, yielding, and accommodating all things,

亦为苍生之所归往也。
then they too will be where the common folk converge.

故能为百谷王。
Thus they can be sovereigns of all streams.

注：以其卑下故能为百谷王也。
*Annotation*: Because of their humility and lowliness, they can be sovereigns of all streams.

○疏：此结成其义，
The *Commentary* concludes its meaning:

故经云：海为百川王。」
thus the text says: "The sea is the sovereign of all rivers."」

Thus the Sage desires to be above the people,
*The Annotation* explains: "Desires to be above the people."

yet speaks of humility;
*The Annotation* explains: "Follows the Way of the rivers and seas, abiding in humility and modesty."

They wish to have all beings
reside above themselves,
so they display a gentle demeanor,
constantly humbling themselves.

They desire to lead the people,
*The Annotation* explains: "Desires to be before the people."

yet place themselves behind them.
*The Annotation* explains: "They lead others while placing themselves last."

*The Commentary* explains: "To have beings before oneself, one must retreat in self and reside behind all things, hence the text says: *All who have not attained the Dao, I always do not hold in hope.* The former advances yet ends in arrogance, while the latter guards humility yet secretly progresses. Thus the Sage, though above the people,
*The Annotation* explains: "The Sage, though above the people as their ruler, does not oppress them with their exalted status, hence the people revere them without feeling burdened."

*The Commentary* further elucidates: "The Sage is tranquil, unassuming, and free from self-interest, with few desires. As their ruler, they govern with light taxes and minimal labor, so the people are not weighed down. Alternatively: The Sage’s virtue extends to all things, so the people bear and uphold them without chaos."

and before them,
*The Annotation* explains: "The Sage, though before the people, does not obscure them with their brilliance, so the people love them as they would parents, never harming them."

*The Commentary* explains: "As rulers who stand before all things, they bring harmony to the realm, making the people sing and strike the earth in joy. The world is peaceful and prosperous, their benefits vast, yet none are harmed. Alternatively: The Sage’s virtue is boundless, the people willingly embrace it, cherishing their blessings—how dare they harm?"

thus the world delights in upholding them without tiring.
*The Annotation* explains: "The Sage’s love and benevolence are deep, treating the people as their own children, hence the world takes joy in supporting them as their ruler, never wearying."

Because they do not contend,
*The Annotation* explains: "The heavens do not weary of the Sage, for the Sage does not compete with others for precedence."

the world cannot contend with them.
*The Annotation* explains: "All speak of my Dao as great, yet I feign foolishness as if unworthy."

*The Commentary* explains: "The *River Above* text reads *xìao* (肖), while other families render it *xiào* (笑). *Smile* refers to the Sage’s natural harmony and profound enlightenment, so all beings cannot but revere them as the Great Sage. Yet why does it seem so? Only because, in dealing with others, they are humble and yielding, never boasting or scoffing, hence the result."

*Master Gu* states: "Because they do not align with others, it says *unworthy* (不肖). The Sage’s Dao is exalted and virtuous, indeed worthy of greatness, yet they conceal their traces like dust, their presence indeterminate—neither appearing wise nor foolish, hence *unworthy*."

For only the great
seem unworthy.
*The Annotation* explains: "Only those whose virtue is truly great would harm themselves by boasting, so they feign foolishness as if unworthy, making no distinctions or divisions, not demeaning others while elevating themselves. If *worthy* (肖) were long-standing,
*The Annotation* explains: *Worthy* (肖) means *good* (善). It refers to those who excel in debate and benevolence, yet elevate themselves in speech, implementing meticulous governance—such as the petty scholars of old."

their refinement is indeed shallow.
*The Annotation* explains: "Only the petty-minded are refined in debate and benevolence, not the noble."

*The Commentary* explains: "The Sage alone, with their vast virtue, does not boast or scoff before others. If they did, they would long be among the petty. *Worthy* (肖) means *good*—the people cannot follow the good Dao because of the Sage’s greatness. The Sage’s greatness is why they cannot practice the good Dao, for they must long remain humble and lowly."

*Master Gu* states: "The Great Sage is beyond comparison. If their form had a fixed nature and their wisdom a constant measure, then their Dao would be constrained and rigid—this is the mark of the petty, not the sublime Great Sage."

I possess three Treasures,
treasures that I hold fast.
*The Annotation* explains: "The Sage says, *I possess three Treasures*, which they embrace and rely upon."

*The Commentary* explains: "Thus the Sage’s virtue is great, yet they do not boast or align themselves with others, because they depend on the Three Treasures, which are precious and held firm."

The first is **cí** (慈, compassion),
*The Annotation* explains: "Loving the people as their own children."

*The Commentary* explains: "Compassionate thoughts for all beings, delivering them from suffering and bringing them joy—this is the Dao’s Treasure."

The second is **jiǎn** (俭, frugality),
*The Annotation* explains: "Taxing and taking as if from oneself."

*Master Yu* states: "Compassion means broad salvation; frugality means sufficient resources."

*The Commentary* explains: "Content with little, knowing satisfaction, guarding one’s portion without greed—this is the scripture’s Treasure."

The third is **bù gǎn wéi tiānxià xiān** (不敢为天下先, not daring to be first under heaven).
*The Annotation* explains: "They hold to humility, never taking the lead."

*The Commentary* explains: "Humility and feigned weakness, placing others before themselves, retreating to benefit others—this is the Teacher’s Treasure."

*Master Jié* states: "It refers to the Treasures of **jīng-qì-shén** (精气神) within a person’s form, which they preserve to sustain life."

Compassion makes them **yǒng** (勇, courageous),
*The Commentary* explains: "Within them lies great compassion, outwardly extending to all beings, hence their courage to enter the Three Realms and rescue all."

frugality makes them broad;
*The Annotation* explains: "The sovereign’s body, if frugal, makes the people’s daily use vast and great."

*The Commentary* explains: "All phenomena are empty and illusory; they unite without greed, maintaining simplicity and purity, hence their virtue is vast."

and not daring to be first under heaven,
*The Annotation* explains: "Not daring to be first under heaven."

thus they can fulfill their role as vessels of the long-lasting.

「The *Annotation* explains: "To fulfill the role of *long-lasting vessels* refers to those who have attained the Dao. I can be the foremost among those who embody the Dao."

*The Imperial Commentary* states: "The virtues of compassion and frugality, when tempered with humility, grow ever brighter. When their brilliance is shared with others, none will weary of it, hence they can become the sovereign vessels of the long-lasting."

*The Commentary* elaborates: "It is only because of their courage to rescue the common folk that they retreat from the world to dwell alone in the Celestial Capital, becoming the foremost among all the sages. When descending to the mortal realm, they occupy the august position of the Nine Ranked, the sovereign of the divine vessel. Now, however, they abandon their compassion and courage—
*The Annotation* explains: "In this age, they combine the seven aspects of compassion into mere martial prowess."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "They discard compassion and instead revel in fierce valor."

They abandon their frugality and vast virtue—
*The Annotation* explains: "They combine their frugality into mere extravagance."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "They forsake their simplicity and instead indulge in greed for worldly things."

They abandon their humility and instead seek to be first—
*The Annotation* explains: "They combine their self-effacement into a rush to lead."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "They abandon the mind of retreat and instead hasten toward advancement."

Thus, they court death.
*The Annotation* explains: "Such actions inevitably lead one to the path of destruction."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Summing the three transgressions above—rejecting the Three Treasures—they plunge into the Three Abysses, hence the declaration: *Thus, they court death.*"

Now, compassion: in war, it brings victory; in defense, it ensures steadfastness.
*The Annotation* explains: "Those who embody compassion inspire the people’s loyalty, uniting hearts and minds. Thus, in war, they conquer their foes, and in defense, they remain unshaken."
*The Imperial Commentary* states: "When leading armies with compassion, soldiers are moved by gratitude, ensuring swift triumph."
*The Inner Explanation* adds: "The six senses are like soldiers; if they are not ensnared by the six dusts, they achieve victory."
Again, when defending with compassion, the people’s hearts align, making the defense unbreakable.
*The Inner Explanation* adds: "Guarding with a compassionate, empty mind fortifies the Dao-heart, rendering it impervious to assault."

When Heaven intends to save them, it shields them with compassion.
*The Annotation* explains: "Heaven rescues those who practice compassion, endowing them with its nature to aid themselves."
*The Imperial Commentary* states: "To wage war and defend with compassion is not merely to unite the people—Heaven’s Way is profound, and it too will save and protect them."
Victory in war is Heaven’s salvation.
Steadfastness in defense is Heaven’s guardianship.
All this is because of compassion, hence it is said: *When Heaven intends to save them, it shields them with compassion.*
*The Commentary* elaborates: "The Celestial Sovereign of the Dao rescues and protects those who practice compassion, using compassion itself to guard them."

Thus, within the Three Treasures, compassion takes precedence, and among the Four Virtues, it is paramount. Truly, compassion’s meaning is vast!

The ancient wise warriors did not rely on force,
*The Annotation* explains: "It is said that moral cultivation is valued over martial might."
*The Imperial Commentary* states: "士 (*shì*) means *affairs*. Those who govern well through the Dao prioritize virtue, hence they do not emphasize force."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "The ancient wise cultivated the Dao in stillness and retreat, never abandoning compassion for martial valor."

The skilled warriors do not rage,
*The Annotation* explains: "Those who wage war through the Dao suppress evil in their hearts and cut off misfortune before it arises, harboring no anger."
*The Imperial Commentary* states: "If conflict is unavoidable, one must respond—but compassion makes it skillful, hence no reliance on wrath."
*The Sectional Explanation* adds: "This means *closure*—harmonizing the essence, so all spirits rejoice."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Those who penetrate the emptiness of all phenomena can subdue enemies before they arise. Their wondrous body transcends both compliance and defiance, hence they experience neither joy nor anger."

The skilled conquerors do not contend,
*The Annotation* explains: "Those who conquer through the Dao draw near with benevolence and seek the distant with virtue, never clashing with enemies, who thus submit of their own accord."
*The Imperial Commentary* states: "Military triumph lies in harmony; harmony means no contention."
*The Sectional Explanation* adds: "This means *unifying the mouth*—all ghosts bow in submission, hence *no contention*."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "If one is untouched by the dusts, it is called conquering enemies. Since there is neither joy nor anger, what is there to contend over?"

The skilled leaders place themselves below others.
*The Annotation* explains: "Those who skillfully employ others always practice humility."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Those who embody humility are naturally followed by others, hence they can command their use."

Thus, the virtue of non-contention—
*The Annotation* explains: "This refers to the preceding point of placing oneself below others."
—is the very Dao of not contending for the realm.
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Not contending with others allows one’s own virtue to flourish; this concludes the section on skilled conquerors who do not contend."

This is called employing the strength of others.
*The Annotation* explains: "Those who place themselves below others can employ the strength of their ministers."
*Zhang’s Explanation* adds: "If one delights in leading others, they forget their own toil."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Through humility and yielding softness, one does not exert dominance over others, yet others submit and serve one’s purposes; this concludes the section on skilled leaders who place themselves below others."

This is to align with Heaven,
*The Annotation* explains: "Those who practice this can match Heaven’s virtue."
*The Imperial Commentary* states: "The virtue of skilled conquest is non-contention, and employing others is using their strength. Thus, one can align with Heaven and claim the imperial title—this is the supreme Dao of antiquity."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Align (*pèi*) means *to harmonize*. Heaven refers to *nature*."

The **extreme** is the **fruit of the Dao**.
It speaks of those who cultivate the Dao with skill—
profoundly embodying true emptiness,
penetrating the interplay of opposition and compliance,
not contending with things—
thus harmonizing with the ultimate principle of nature,
and attaining the **supreme Dao** of antiquity’s origin.
This is the **conclusion**.

On wielding arms, there is a saying:
*The Annotation* explains: "Chen’s martial teachings—
Laozi despised the age’s use of arms,
so he framed this principle under his own words."
*The Commentary* elaborates: "Wielding arms refers to **strategic wisdom**,
subduing and transforming beings—
also how the Sage employs the six senses
to engage with the dusty realm."

There is a saying—
its meaning lies in the following text.

I dare not act as the **initiator**,
*The Annotation* explains: "Initiator (*zhǔ*) means *first*—
I dare not first raise arms."
as but the **guest**.
*The Annotation* explains: "Guest means *yielding without leading*—
in wielding arms, one must follow Heaven’s movement before acting."
*The Chapter Explanation* states:
"Upper arms refer to **speech**,
lower arms refer to **subtle action**.
Empty speech harms oneself,
so one speaks in humility and restraint.
Lightly employing subtle action depletes one’s essence,
so one does not dare to lead but yields instead."

*The Annotation* explains:
"‘Master’ refers to **my own body**,
‘guest’ refers to **past defilements**—
speaking of how the roots and defilements are both empty,
and **self** and **phenomena** are all illusory.
Since there is no **self** to act upon,
nor any **defilements** to be stained,
I dare not advance an inch for fear of retreating a foot."

*The Annotation* explains:
"Advance" means **taking**,
"retreat" means **letting go**.
‘Little’ signifies **nothing**,
‘much’ signifies **something**."

Once **discriminating wisdom** and **phenomena** are both abandoned,
and **roots** and **defilements** forgotten,
the **Mystic Oneness** is realized,
free from the duality of **self** and **other**—
thus, there is nothing in **non-action** to be sought,
nor anything in **action** to be embraced.

This is called **"actionless action."**
*The Annotation* explains:
"If one does not cease, they become a **scourge to the world**—
though they may be executed, they are not **arrested** in their **non-action**."

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
"Retreating a foot as a **guest**,
not contending with things—
though one may respond to hostility,
it aligns with **actionless action**."

*The Annotation* explains:
Having abandoned all **deliberate action**,
and forgotten all **phenomenal perceptions**,
there is no **action** to be called **action**.

**Brandishing arms without arms,**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Though one might raise their arms in great anger,
it is as if they had **no arms to brandish**."

*The Chapter Explanation* states:
"Brandishing arms is meant to display anger,
but the virtuous warrior does not grow angry,
so it is as if they had **no arms to brandish**."

**Grasping weapons without weapons,**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Though one might wish to seize and wield them,
it is as if there were **no weapons** to hold."

Why?
To harm another’s people brings **retribution from Heaven**,
to face a **tyrannical ruler** is to risk **self-destruction**.
*The Master’s Commentary* states:
"Wielding weapons is meant to display killing,
but now **compassion** is the master—
so though one might grasp, it is as if there were **no weapons**."

*The Annotation* explains:
Detached from form and wisdom,
there are **no weapons** to be used.

**Yet no enemy remains,**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Though the mind may still **pursue** and **draw near**,
there is **no enemy** to engage."

**No greater calamity than despising the enemy,**
*The Chapter Explanation* states:
"This refers to those who **indulge in reckless conflict**—
they invite **ruin and disaster**."

*The Annotation* explains:
"Despising" means **contempt and aggression**,
"enemy" refers to **past phenomena**.
To **despise** and **cling to defilements**
brings the **karmic retribution of the Three Realms**—
thus, the **great calamity** arises.

**Despising the enemy risks losing my treasure,**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Near" means **imminent**,
"treasure" refers to **the body**."

*The Chapter Explanation* states:
"Treasure" means **vital essence and breath**—
to **despise the enemy** and **fight repeatedly**
leads to the **loss of vital essence**."

*The Annotation* explains:
"Near" means **exhaustion**,
"treasure" refers to the **Three Treasures** from the previous chapter.
Combining **compassion** with **courage** is called **provoking the enemy**,
which invites **great disaster**—
thus, the **Three Treasures** are utterly lost.

Therefore, when **arms are raised in conflict,**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Two armies **raise arms** against each other."

**One who acts with compassion will prevail.**
*The Master’s Commentary* states:
"Antagonize" means **to provoke**—
when two nations **raise arms** against each other."

*The Annotation* explains:
"If" means **when**,
"compassion" is **pity**.
"Antagonize" means **to provoke**—
speaking of how **roots and defilements** press upon each other,
raising **glances and expressions** in response.
Yet with a **great compassionate mind**,
one **emptily mirrors** the **phenomena before them**—
thus, **self** and **phenomena** are both forgotten,
all **dharma** becomes a **wondrous realm of victory**.

My words are **easy to know, easy to practice.**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Laozi says my words are **concise and easy to know**,
**brief and easy to practice**."

*The Annotation* explains:
"My words" refer to the previous chapter—
what is spoken is **not this**, yet it is **easy to know**.
**Actionless action** is **easy to practice**.

Yet **none in the world can know them, none can practice them.**
*The Annotation* explains:
"None" means **not**.
Laozi says they are **easy to know, easy to practice**,
yet the **multitude of beings** are **deluded and dull**—
none can **grasp** the **profound subtlety** of my teachings,
nor **follow** them in practice.

**Words have a root, affairs have a sovereign.**
*The Annotation* explains:
"My words have a **foundational origin**,
affairs have **rulers and subjects**—
those who do not know this are not without **virtue**,
but their **hearts have not returned** to the Way."

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
"Root" means **origin**,
"sovereign" means **master**.
Though **words** may diverge in **paths**,
they share the **root of emptiness**.
Though **affairs** may differ in **direction**,
they share the **mastery of stillness and simplicity**."

*The Commentary of Cai* states:
"Words take **non-speech** as their root,
affairs take **non-action** as their sovereign."

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
"Words" pertain to **principle**—
when **principle** is attained, **words are forgotten**,
so **non-speech** is the root.
"Affairs" pertain to **achievement**—
when **achievement** is complete, **no one claims credit**,
so **non-action** is the sovereign.

*The Annotation* explains:
"Words" are **scriptures and teachings**,
"sovereign" is the **Son of Heaven**.
Though **teachings** may be vast,
their **root** lies in the **Mystic Darkness**,
though **affairs** may be complex,
their **order** comes from the **sovereign**.
This **essence** is raised to show **ease in knowing**.

**Only the ignorant fail to know me.**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Only because the world is **ignorant**,
my **virtue and power** remain hidden—
**profound and wondrous**—
thus, they are **unknown**."

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
"Only because the world lacks **enlightenment**,
they fail to grasp my teachings of **non-speech and non-action**."

*The Annotation* explains:
"Not knowing me" is like **not knowing myself**.
Only because **beings are ignorant and dull**,
they cannot grasp my **profound words**.

**Few know me, thus I am precious.**
*The Annotation* explains:
"Few" means **rare**.
Only those who **grasp the Dao** can truly know me—
thus, I am **precious**.

*The Annotation* explains:
Most **ordinary minds** are **superficial and deluded**,
few truly **embody** the **sage’s teachings**.

**Then, this is the *Law* [法則].**
To follow my teachings and walk according to this *Law*,
to attain the sacred fruit—this is what is **precious**.

Thus, the Sage wears coarse cloth while treasuring jade within.
*The Annotation* explains:
Wearing coarse cloth means **humble exterior**,
treasuring jade means **rich interior**.
He conceals his treasure and virtue,
not revealing them to others.

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
Wearing coarse cloth means **obscuring his outward form**.
Treasuring jade means **illuminating his inner essence**.
Thus, few truly know me.

*The Explication* says:
The Sage values **virtue and power** while despising his form—
wearing skins and fur,
embracing the **One** within,
not coveting office or rank,
nurturing spirit and clarity within,
refining *qi* into jade,
solidifying essence into gold,
thus able to transform and ascend to the **Purple Palace**.

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
Coarse cloth is the **garment of the common**, jade is the **precious treasure**.
Ordinary minds truly do not know—
they feign wisdom outwardly like a **sheep in tiger’s skin**,
this is the **disease of virtue**.

*The Annotation* explains:
To **know** the Sage’s words,
to **understand** the Sage’s teachings,
to **practice** and **realize** them fully—
this is how one becomes a Sage.
Coarse cloth is the attire of the rustic,
jade is the precious gem—
outwardly blending with the dust,
wearing coarse cloth.
Inwardly, the heart is **empty and tranquil**,
treasuring jade.
Treasuring jade—
though dwelling in the vulgar world, it remains **ever true**.
Wearing coarse cloth—
the heart is **empty and concentrated**, engaging with others.

**Knowing while feigning ignorance** is **supreme**.
*The Annotation* explains:
To **know** yet speak of **not knowing**—
this is the **height of virtue**.

*The Annotation* explains:
The Sage **harmonizes with the Recondite Mystery**,
his outward form does not stray from the **Root**,
he **penetrates emptiness and distance**,
knowing yet **without knowing**,
returning to the **Root’s emptiness and concentration**—
thus called **supreme**.

Hence, *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Who knows that you do not know?
How could I know?"*
**Not knowing while feigning knowledge** is **disease**.
*The Annotation* explains:
To **not know** yet speak of **knowing**—
this is the **disease of virtue**.

*The Explication* says:
Descending from the **Root** to outward form,
**not knowing** yet **knowing**,
engaging in affairs to relieve suffering—
thus called **disease**.

Only by **disease of disease** does one **not fall ill**.
*The Annotation* explains:
Only because the Sage **suffers from the disease of others’ forced knowledge**,
he does not **suffer himself**.

*The Annotation* explains:
The Sage has no **flaw of knowledge**,
while the common mind is **entangled in distinctions**—
suffering from the **disease of others’ disease**,
thus he speaks to the vulgar, expounding teachings,
his intent to **rescue the drowning**.
Already **not ill yet ill**, **ill yet not ill**—
hence it is said: **not ill**.

Thus, the Sage does not fall ill,
because he **suffers from the disease of others**—
thus he does not fall ill.

*The Annotation* explains:
The Sage has no **disease of forced knowledge**,
for he **constantly suffers** with those who bear this disease,
out of **compassion for them**—
thus he does not suffer himself.

The Sage possesses **enlightened, penetrating wisdom**,
yet **appears ignorant**,
to make all under heaven **simple, sincere, and true**,
each guarding their **pure nature**.

The petty man does not **understand this intent**,
yet **presumptuously acts in forced knowledge**,
seeking to **display himself**,
internally **injuring his spirit**,
shortening life and wasting years.

*The Master’s Commentary* states:
The vulgar, **grasping at forced knowledge**,
distinguishing and clinging—
wandering yet never returning—
truly lamentable.

Only the Sage **truly knows the Mysterious Root**,
**penetrates speech and teaching**,
alone able, through **compassionate benevolence**,
to **pity** the petty man’s **disease of forced knowledge**,
for he himself is **without disease**.

*The Annotation* explains:
The Sage can **empty and forget**,
**transcend both phenomena and wisdom**,
**penetratingly discern** while **riding the *qi***,
**ascending on clouds**,
**Root and form both empty and tranquil**—
what **disease** could bind him?

---
*The Annotated Commentary on the Dao De Jing* concludes **Volume Seven**,
begins **Volume Eight**.

**Volume Eight** of *The Annotated Commentary on the Dao De Jing*
continues:

Master Gu of Wu Commandery expounds:
If the people **do not fear minor harm**,
then **major harm** shall come.
*The Annotation* clarifies:
**"Harm"** refers to **calamity**.
If men **do not fear petty calamities**,
then **great calamities** shall come—
great calamities being **death** itself.
Those who fear it must **preserve their essence and nourish their spirit**,
**align with Earth and receive from Heaven**.

*The Annotated Commentary* elucidates:
**"Harm"** means **punitive authority**—
the laws and prohibitions of all the scriptures.
**Major harm** refers to the **three evil destinies**—
the cruel punishments of the **Northern Underworld (Tàlú and Lúchēng)**.
It speaks of how the **common folk**, unendured by scriptural precepts,
who **recklessly commit evils**,
shall surely face **the winds of retribution and great punishments**.

**Do not narrow their dwelling**—
*The Annotation* explains:
**"Dwelling"** refers to **the abode of the spirit**,
which must always be **broad and gentle**,
never **hasty or confined**.

**The *Jiéjiě* Commentary** states:
**"Do not narrow"** means, while **still alive in the mundane world**,
to **reside in deep mountains and vast marshes**,
where one may **cultivate one’s nature**.

**Lord Lu expounds:**
**"Do not narrow"** means to **banish passions and discard desires**,
so that **the empty chamber may give birth to purity**.

**Do not satiate their birth**—
*The Annotation* explains:
**"Birth"** refers to the **spirit**,
which arises from **essence and depends upon emptiness**,
delighting in **clarity and purity**.
If **diet is unregulated**,
or if one **abandons the Dao for sensual pleasures**,
then **perverse obsessions fill the body**,
**shortening life and scattering the spirit**.

**The *Jiéjiě* Commentary** states:
**"Satiate"** means to **reject**—
to **reject the love of *qi***,
for one **treasures essence as the treasure of life**.

**Lord Lu expounds:**
That which **the body generates** is **the spirit**.
**"Do not satiate"** means to **hold few private attachments and desires**,
so that **the spirit may not grow weary or exhausted**.

*The Annotated Commentary* concludes:
**"Satiate"** is **to abandon**.
**"Narrow"** is **to constrain**.

**"Residence"** is **settled abode**.
**"That which is generated"** is **the heart**.

It is said that those who cultivate the Dao must **settle the heart without attachments**, lest they **constrain it with narrow, inferior desires**, abandoning what they generate. Why? Though the body is **empty and illusory**, it is the **vessel for receiving the Dao**. One must neither **indulge in attachment** nor **reject with aversion**, for **indulgence clings to the realm of conditioned existence**, while **aversion drowns in the illusion of emptiness**. To **neither cling nor be defiled**, dwelling in the **Middle without dwelling in the Middle**—this is the **true practitioner of the Dao**.

Only **the one who does not satiate** is thus **unsatiated**.
**Annotation:** Only the one who **does not satiate the spirit**—who **cleanses the heart of filth**, **discarding desires**—is **unsatiated by the spirit’s dwelling**.

**Commentary:** The **domain of cultivation** is **neither existence nor emptiness**; thus, one **can cultivate it**. **Wisdom too** is **neither attached nor averse**; when **wisdom aligns with the domain**, it is **unsatiated**.

Thus, the **Sage knows himself**—
**Annotation:** **Knowing one’s own gains and losses**.
**without self-display**—
**Annotation:** **Not revealing virtue externally**, but **concealing it within**.
**Commentary:** When **wisdom aligns with the Dao’s domain**, one **penetrates the true source**, thus **knowing oneself**. The **Four Great Elements** are **borrowed and assembled**; in **sitting-forgetfulness**, the body **decays unnoticed**, hence **no self-display**.

**Self-love**—
**Annotation:** **Loving one’s body** to **preserve essence and qi**.
**without self-aggrandizement**—
**Annotation:** **Not seeking honor or nobility in the world**.
**Commentary:** **Nurturing true nature**, **not lightly staining it**, hence **self-love**. **Humility, quietude, and retreat**, **prioritizing others over oneself**—this is **not self-aggrandizement**.

Thus, **discard the former, take the latter**.
**Annotation:** **Discard self-display and self-aggrandizement**; **take self-knowledge and self-love**.
**Commentary:** **Discarding self-display and self-aggrandizement**, **taking self-knowledge and self-love**—this is **to instruct others through example**, hence the **language of discarding and taking**.

**Boldness in daring** leads to **death**—
**Annotation:** **Boldness in action** kills the body.
**Jiéjiě Commentary:** **Greed for sexual union**—seeking **sensual gratification**, **life and death alternate**—this is **boldness**. **Contempt for the Dao and neglect of the spirit** lead to **premature death**—this is **daring**. Both are **self-destruction**.

**Boldness in not daring** leads to **life**—
**Annotation:** **Boldness in refraining from action** preserves the body.
**Commentary:** **Boldness** is **fervent advance**; **daring** is **resolute action**. **Death** is **annihilation**; **life** is **longevity**. **Advancing the heart resolutely**—if **attached to worldly affairs**, it leads to **annihilation**; if the **heart is empty and indifferent, not daring to cling or defile**, then it is **longevity**.

These two—
**Annotation:** **Referring to daring and not daring**.

**Some bring benefit, some bring harm.**
**Annotation:** **Preserving life** is **benefit**; **destroying life** is **harm**.
**Commentary:** **"Some"** means **indeterminate**. **The two** refer to **daring and not daring**. **Speaking of resolute action without daring**—if one does not dare, it leads to **longevity**, hence **benefit**. If one **advances the body** and **clings to defilement**, it leads to **annihilation and death**, hence **harm**.

**What the Dao/Way abhors,**
**Annotation:** **Abhors action.**
**Who knows its reason?**
**Annotation:** **Who can know the Dao/Way’s intent**, hence **not daring to transgress it.**

**Commentary:** The **natural order**—**rewarding virtue, punishing licentiousness**—when **examining cause and effect**, none know its **ultimate arbiter**. Thus, even **sages find it difficult**.

**Annotation:** **Speaking of the sage’s enlightened virtue**, yet even they find **boldness and daring difficult**—how much more so those without sagehood who dare to act?

**Explanatory Resolution:** **Saying that the Dao/Way constantly abhors boldness and daring**, yet **loves timidity and weakness**, **cherishing life and qi**—who knows the Dao/Way’s intent?

**The Dao/Way of Heaven**—
**not contending yet naturally triumphant,**
**Annotation:** **Heaven does not contend with humanity over nobility**, yet **humanity naturally fears It.**

**Explanatory Resolution:** **The Dao/Way values harmony.**

**Commentary:** The **natural order**—though it **does not contend with things**, yet **those who are humble, retiring, and self-effacing** will **inevitably gain victory and good fortune.**

**Not speaking yet naturally responsive,**
**Annotation:** **Heaven does not speak**, yet **all things move in response to the seasons.**

**Explanation:** **"Heaven speaks not—how could it speak?"** Yet **rewarding virtue and punishing licentiousness**, there is **no deviation**, hence **"naturally responsive."**

**Commentary:** **The retribution of fortune and calamity** is **like echoes and shadows**—**without words, yet good and evil must respond.**

**Not summoned yet coming of its own accord,**
**Annotation:** **Heaven does not call forth the myriad things**, yet **all bear yin and turn toward yang.**

**Explanation:** **"The Dao/Way of Heaven does not summon the myriad things to obey It**, yet **things cannot defy it**—they come **naturally in accordance with Heaven’s command."**

**Commentary:** **The retribution of good and evil** follows **one’s deeds**—**without summons, it must come of its own accord.**

**Serene yet cunning in its planning.**
**Annotation:** **"Serene"** means **vast and expansive**; though **Heaven’s Dao is vast and broad**, it **calculates human affairs**, **cultivating virtue or punishing vice**, and **each receives its due.**
