**Commentary:** **"Planning"** means **reckoning**. **Speaking of the hidden principles**—**without labored explanation**, yet **virtue and vice are calculated with precision, not a hair’s breadth amiss.**

**Heaven’s net is vast and wide,**
**sparse yet never failing.**
**Annotation:** **Heaven’s net**—**vast and boundless**, though **sparse and distant**, yet **in overseeing human virtue and vice**, **nothing is missed.**

**Commentary:** **"Vast"** means **broad and expansive**. **"Net"** is **law and decree**. **Saying that Heaven’s natural net** is **vast and distant**, yet **deeds great or small, merit or fault**, **reward and retribution are without error.**

**Sun’s Commentary:** **What is called "naturally responsive"**—**Heaven’s Dao does not speak, yet responds naturally.**
**Understanding "naturally responsive"**—**it lies not in much speech.**

**Lu’s Commentary:** **Silent and empty**, hence **"not speaking"**—**yet when moved, it responds**, hence **"naturally responsive."**

**People do not commonly fear death,**
**Annotation:** **Rulers impose harsh punishments**, and the people **cannot endure life**, hence **they do not fear death.**

**Those who govern themselves**—**indulging desires harms the spirit**, **greed for wealth destroys the body**, yet **the people do not know to fear.**

**How can one use death to frighten them?**
**Annotation:** **If the ruler does not mitigate punishments** and **teach people to abandon their desires**, how can one **establish laws** and **frighten them with death?**

**Commentary:** **This condemns the deep delusion of common sentiment**—**chasing worldly attachments**, **unafraid of life and death**, though **precepts and warnings are clear**, yet **there is no terror**—hence the **sigh of "how can one?"**, expressing **deep pity.**

**Explanatory Resolution:** **Saying that people do not seek life’s pleasures in desires**, **hearing virtue yet not practicing it**, **knowing vice yet not abandoning it**—**how can they recognize benefit?**

**If the people were to constantly fear death,**
**Annotation:** **One must first eliminate what harms them**, then **teach people to abandon their greedy desires.**

**and those who act strangely, I would seize and kill them—who would dare?**

**Annotation:** **If one governs by the Dao**, yet the people **do not comply**, instead **acting deceitfully and falsely**, then **according to the king’s law**, one **seizes and kills them**—who would dare to transgress?

**Laozi laments that rulers do not first govern by virtue and the Dao**, but **instead impose punishments.**

**Explanation:** **If all people followed pure transformation**, **not daring to indulge desires**, **constantly fearing death**, yet **only those who act strangely**—**even if I could seize and kill such deceitful ones**, who would dare to do so?

**Commentary:** **The heart of greed and desire** **daily breeds deceit**, **firmly clinging to delusion**, thus **making it habitual**—**the underworld’s officers capture them**, **seize and kill them**, **with sharp blades and tortures**, **evidence clear and verified**—if there are **those with insight and awareness**, who would **still commit crimes?**

**There are always those who kill in place of the executioner.**
**Annotation:** **The executioner**—**Heaven, high above and overseeing from afar**, **inspects human transgressions**; **Heaven’s net is vast and wide, sparse yet never failing**—this is so.

**Explanation:** **Such deceitful ones**—**Heaven’s net does not fail them**, hence **there are always Heaven’s executioners who kill them.**

**Explanatory Resolution:** **Referring to the mouth**—**to its left is the "Executioner of Yin"**, to its right the **"Executioner of Killing."** If one **commits yin transgressions**, the **Executioner of Yin reports to the Highest**, **examining the soul and spirit**; if one **speaks evil**, the **Executioner of Killing reports to the Lord of Life**, who **records it**, and **when guilt is full, death follows.**

**Commentary:** **This penal net has its own overseers**—the **Three Officials and Nine Departments**, the **One Hundred and Twenty Bureaus**, the **Northern Capital’s prisons and the Underworld’s offices**, the **Eight Hardships and Five Torments**, each with its **designated officers.**

**Those who act as executioners in place of Heaven**—
**this is called substituting for the Great Carpenter in felling wood.**
**Annotation:** **Heaven’s Dao is supremely clear**, its **oversight is constant**—**like spring growth, summer flourishing, autumn harvest, winter storage**, the **celestial stem’s rotation**—**governing all in sequence**. If a ruler **attempts to usurp killing**, it is **like a clumsy man substituting for the Great Carpenter in felling wood**—**laborious yet ineffective.**

**Commentary on "Restraining and Severing" (節解) says:**
The **Dao/Way of Heaven** rewards the virtuous and punishes the wicked, **governing the body of the people**—
**like the Great Carpenter crafting vessels, the virtuous are perfected, the wicked are ruined.**

**Those who substitute for the Great Carpenter in hewing wood**—
**how few escape unharmed by their hands!**
**Annotation:** When a ruler **enacts punishments**, it is **like a clumsy man substituting for the Great Carpenter in hewing wood**—
**his measurements and angles are flawed, and he harms himself in the process.**
**Those who substitute for Heaven in killing**—
**they lose the **proper order and principles** (紀綱), and if they lose these, they **suffer retribution in return.**

**Commentary on "Restraint" (御) says:**
When a ruler **assigns punishments**, substituting for the **officers of execution**—
**how could it be that he alone spares the people, but also that he himself loses Heaven’s harmony?**

**Commentary (疏):**
All living beings **create karma** (造業), each reaping their own **fruits of retribution** (報).
**Punishments and suffering** (刑罚苦楚) have their own **underworld overseers** (冥司).
**The unenlightened** (無識), **arrogant and self-serving** (師心專辄), who **presume to substitute for the Underworld’s officers** (冥司) in **inflicting slaughter** (行誅戮)—
**what difference is there from a mediocre, clumsy artisan wielding an axe?**
**The clumsy artisan wielding an axe**—
**this is not his craft, and he not only fails in his work but also harms his own hands.**
**To forcibly substitute for the officers of execution**—
**its meaning is the same; not only does he abuse his authority (滥職), but he also invites disaster (獲其衅) upon himself.**

**Commentary on "Restraining and Severing" (節解) says:**
**Human governance** (人治) over the body varies in effectiveness—
**like artisans shaping vessels, some are skilled, some are clumsy.**
**The skilled bring the vessel to perfection; the clumsy harm themselves in the process.**

**The people’s hunger**—
**it arises from their rulers’ excessive taxation.**
**Annotation:** The people’s **suffering from hunger and cold**—
**it stems from their rulers above consuming too much while taxing them heavily.**
**Commentary (疏):**
**Hunger** (饥) refers to **lacking inner moral cultivation** (內無道德).
**Above** (上) means **the heart** (心), for the heart is the master of the **five viscera and all actions** (五藏百行).
**Taxation** (税) means **excessive accumulation**; **consumption** (食) means **indulgence in sensory pleasures** (滋味).
**The reason people fail to cultivate virtue**—
**their hearts are entangled by external desires, and they greedily indulge in sensory pleasures.**

**Commentary on "Restraining and Severing" (節解) says:**
**Hunger** (饥) refers to **insufficient qi** (氣不足).
**The qi is insufficient** because of **excessive craving for fine flavors** (坐口嗜美味).
**Excessive consumption** (食多) means **hoarding wealth** (實畜積), which leads to **obstruction** (僻).
**Thus, defenses are erected in all directions.**
**This is why there is hunger.**
**Annotation:** This is why the people **all regard their rulers as greedy** (化上為貪),
**rebelling against the Dao/Way and defying virtue** (叛道違德), and so **suffer hunger.**

**Commentary (疏):**
**This ties together the previous reasoning.**
**The people are difficult to govern**—
**it is because their rulers are overbearing** (有為).
**Annotation:** The people are **untreatable**—
**because their rulers above have excessive desires and love to interfere** (多欲，好有為).
**Commentary (疏):**
**The reason all actions become chaotic and difficult to manage**—
**is solely because their hearts crave interference** (贪有為).
**If the heart does not interfere**, then all actions are easily governed.
**Thus it is known: "Ten thousand phenomena arise from the single mind."**
**This is why they are difficult to govern.**
**Annotation:** This is why their people **regard their rulers as interfering and deceitful** (化上有為),
**making governance all the more arduous and unmanageable.**

「○**Jiéjiě** says: The common people are called **"one hundred pulses"** (百脉), the reason their **vitality fluctuates without harmony** (盈缩不和平)—
**it is because their mouths do not uphold the single principle** (口不含一), their lips are parched, and their **bodily fluids are exhausted** (液竭), thus their **pulses lose their vital essence** (脉失精泄).

**People’s disregard for death**—
**it is because their pursuit of life is excessive** (求生之厚).
**Annotation:** The reason **people lightly court death**—
**is that their obsession with living is too profound** (求活之事太厚), **valuing profit at the expense of their own peril** (贵利以自危).
**Commentary (疏):** Those who **do not complete their natural lifespan** (不终天年) and **lightly enter death’s domain** (轻入死地) are **bewildered by the Dao, inverted in their understanding** (迷顺颠倒), unable to **awaken to true insight** (悟达). They **do not realize that all things—self and world—are illusory** (物我俱幻), that **birth and death are simultaneous** (即生即死). **Life is marked by excessive craving** (生既多贪), and the **six senses** (六尘) amass **abundant karmic resources** (厚资) from the **four great elements** (四大), thus hastening their demise.
**Jiéjiě says:** This refers to **those who do not dread death’s approach**—
**it is because they indulge their physical form excessively** (养形太厚), **gratifying their mouth and appetite** (恣其口腹), **losing the Dao of life** (失其生道), hence their death.
**Thus it is known: "People’s disregard for death"**—
**because their pursuit of life is too excessive** (求生太厚), **they lightly court death’s domain** (故轻入死地).

**Now, only those who do not cling to life** (无以生为者) **are truly virtuous in honoring life** (贤於贵生).
**Annotation:** Only those who **do not make life their primary concern** (无以生为务者) are virtuous—
**when titles and emoluments do not sway their intentions** (爵禄不干於意), and **wealth and gain do not enter their hearts** (财利不入於心), then **even the Son of Heaven cannot command them as subjects** (天子不得臣), nor **nobles as companions** (诸侯不得友). **Thus they are truly virtuous in honoring life.**
**Commentary (疏):** *Virtue* (贤) means **excellence in harmony** (善也). The **mystic sages** (玄悟之士) **do not cling to form or emptiness** (非色灭空), nor **lie idle in forgetfulness** (咯焉坐忘). Instead, they **transcend the notion of life itself** (生无生相), **rejecting the clamor of worldly desires** (膻躁荣利) and **spurning the corruption of mortal affairs** (厌秽风尘). Therefore, **only those who do not cling to life** (独不以生为者) **can truly safeguard and honor those who do.**

**Jiéjiě says:** This refers to **those who can mourn life before it is lost** (忧生於未生) and **guard form before it decays** (忧形於未形)—
**only by contemplating non-action** (但念无为) and **abandoning artificiality** (舍自然) do they **attain true virtue and honor the Dao** (为贤贵道), **securing their own life** (安其生).

**Now, the nature of human life is soft and frail** (人之生也柔弱).
**Annotation:** When **humans are born, they are infused with harmonious qi** (含和气) and **embrace vital spirit** (抱精神), thus soft and frail.
**Their death, however, is rigid and unyielding** (其死也坚强).
**Annotation:** When **humans die, their harmonious qi is exhausted** (和气竭), and their **vital spirit departs** (精神亡), thus rigid and unyielding.

**All things—grasses and trees—are soft and brittle when they live** (万物草木之生也柔脆).
**Annotation:** When **harmonious qi remains** (和气存也).
**Their death, however, is withered and decayed** (其死也枯槁).
**Annotation:** When **harmonious qi departs** (和气去也).
**Commentary (疏):** When **life persists with its mandate** (生存有命), then **all limbs are soft and frail** (诸节柔弱). But **at death**, **bone and flesh become rigid and unyielding** (骨肉坚强).
**Grasses and trees** are likewise—
**when alive, they are pliable and tender** (生时软脆), but **when dead, their branches and trunks wither** (条柯枯槁). **This softness in life** (生而柔软者) **is because harmonious qi remains** (和气存也). **This rigidity in death** (死而坚强者) **is because harmonious qi departs** (和气离也).
**This analogy serves to illustrate** (举此有识为辩) **the superiority and inferiority of things** (其胜劣者也).
**Now, rigidity and unyielding are the marks of death** (夫坚强者死之徒), while **softness and frailty are the marks of life** (柔弱者生之徒).
**Annotation:** Observing the **two precedents above**, it is clear—
**rigidity and unyielding belong to death** (坚强者死), while **softness and frailty belong to life** (柔弱者生).
**Commentary (疏):** *Tú* (徒) means **kindred or category** (类也). Thus, **those who act rigidly and forcefully** (行刚强者) **contradict the harmonious principle** (乖於和理), **and so align themselves with death** (故与死为徒). **Those who act softly and frailly** (行柔弱者) **conform to harmonious qi** (顺於和气), **and so align themselves with life** (故与生为徒).
**This is a unified metaphor.**

**Thus, strong armies do not prevail** (是以兵强则不胜).
**Annotation:** Even **mighty armies** (强大之兵), **reckless in battle and delighting in slaughter** (轻战乐杀), **with poisonous flows and lingering grudges** (毒流怨结), **are ultimately undone when many weak stand as one** (众弱为一), **for their strength is their own undoing** (强故不胜).
**Jiéjiě says:** *Army* (兵) here refers to **the mouth** (口)—
**when the mouth is strong, it is exhausted by others** (口强为人所穷), and **when the yin is strong, it is overpowered by the female** (阴强为女所侵).
**The Master responds:** *The softness of life* (生之柔弱) **is the fullness of harmonious qi** (和气全也). *The rigidity of death* (死之坚强) **is the dissipation of harmonious qi** (和气散也).
**To clarify, guarding softness and frailty preserves life and extends years** (守柔弱者全生保年), while **acting rigidly and forcefully destroys the body and forfeits life** (为强梁者亡身失生).

**When trees are rigid and strong, their branches and leaves are crushed** (木强则共).
**Annotation:** When **trees are strong and robust** (木强大), their **branches and leaves are pressed beneath** (枝叶共生其上).
**Commentary (疏):** *Army* (兵) here refers to **the three activities and six senses** (三业六根). **Those who indulge their passions** (纵恣六情), **forcefully dominate their surroundings** (强暴前境), and **consume without restraint** (食取无厌) **will inevitably suffer grave calamity and misfortune** (必遭重殃大祸), hence their defeat.
**It is like a sturdy tree** (譬树木粗强), whose **branches are crushed beneath its own weight** (故枝条共压其上), or **like a grand beam** (亦犹梁栋宏壮), whose **rafters and tiles press down upon it** (故椽瓦压其上).
**The strong are pressed low, while the weak rise high** (强大处下，柔弱处上).
**Annotation:** When **constructing things or achieving feats**, **great trees are placed below** (大木处下), and **smaller things above** (小物处上). **This is the Dao of Heaven—suppressing the strong and elevating the weak** (天道抑强扶弱), **the natural order’s effect** (自然之效).
**Jiéjiě says:** This refers to **the hair’s softness and frailty** (发以柔弱), **which is worn down by others** (为人所戴), and **the bones’ rigidity and strength** (体骨坚强), **which are labored upon by others** (为人所劳).
**Commentary (疏):** The **rigid and strong trees dwell below** (坚强之木居下), while **the soft and weak branches ascend above** (柔弱之条处上). Thus, **the rigid and strong are consigned to the Nine Abysses below** (居九幽之下), and **the soft and weak are elevated to the Three Pure Ones above** (处三清之上).

**Now, the Dao of Heaven is like drawing a bow** (天之道，其犹张弓乎？).
**Annotation:** The **Dao of Heaven is profound and distant** (天道杳邈), and **analogies are used to elucidate it** (举物类以为喻).
**The Master responds:** The **Dao of Heaven is mysterious and far-reaching** (天道玄远), and **cannot be made clear without metaphor** (非喻不明), hence the **bow analogy to reveal its function** (举张弓以彰其用).
**The high are pressed low, the low are lifted up** (高者抑之，下者举之), **the overabundant are diminished** (有余者损之), **and the insufficient are supplemented** (不足者与之).
**Annotation:** This describes **the harmonious adjustment of drawing a bow** (言张弓和调之义如是), **only then can it be used.**
**The Dao of Heaven presses the high and lifts the low** (天抑高举下), **diminishes the strong and supplements the weak** (损强益弱), **thus the Dao of Heaven** (天之道).
**Commentary (疏):** To **lift the low and press the high** (举下使高，抑高令下), **both must be balanced** (高下调适), **only then can the arrow be released** (方可运箭). **The proud and arrogant, with excess** (傲慢有余者), **will inevitably meet ruin and defeat** (必招损败). **Those in harmony but lacking** (和不足者), **are granted fortune and goodness** (与其福善). **Thus the Dao of Heaven** (天道如是), **and it is likened to drawing a bow** (故似张弓).

**The Dao of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements deficiency** (天之道，损有余补不足).
**Annotation:** The **Dao of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements the humble** (损有余而益谦), **for it always values moderation and harmony as supreme** (言天道常以中和为上).
**Commentary (疏):** *Bestowing fortune upon the virtuous and calamity upon the licentious* (福善祸淫) is the **natural principle** (自然之理), **as previously expounded** (仍前生后). **This passage restates the same meaning.**

**Now, human conduct is not so** (人之道则不然).
**Annotation:** Human **conduct opposes the Dao of Heaven** (人道与天道反).
**They diminish the deficient to enrich the excessive** (损不足，奉有余).
**Annotation:** **Worldly folk diminish the poor to enrich the rich** (损贫以奉富), **plunder the weak to empower the strong** (夺弱以益强).
**The Master responds:** The **Dao of Heaven applies evenhanded measures** (天道平施), **distributing surplus to the lacking** (裒多益寡), but **humans pervert Heaven’s ways** (人则违天), **flipping the principle of diminishing the deficient** (翻损不足者).
**Commentary (疏):** In **human affairs**, **worldly conduct favors superficiality and deception** (好行浮伪), **pursuing trends and competing for gain** (趋势竞利). **This defies Heaven and contradicts nature** (违天背理), **plundering the deficient poor and lowly** (割损贫贱之不足) to **supply the excessive rich and noble** (供奉富贵之有余).

「Who can have surplus to bestow upon the world, only those who embody the Dao. **Annotation:** It asks, who can occupy a position of surplus and, out of self-restraint, bestow upon the world’s deficient? Only a ruler who embodies the Dao can enact this.

The **Master responds:** The **Dao of Heaven** is evenhanded, yet the **virtue/power of the sage** does not accumulate. **Annotation:** The sage’s virtue/power is bestowed as benevolence, yet he does not rely upon or expect repayment. When his deeds are complete, he does not dwell in their fruits. **Annotation:** When his achievements are fulfilled, he does not claim their rewards.

**Annotation:** The sage, by pushing his virtue/power outward and not dwelling in its results, does not wish to display his wisdom. **Annotation:** The sage’s reason for pushing his virtue/power outward and not dwelling in its results is to prevent others from recognizing his own wisdom.

**Annotation:** The sage who embodies the Dao often rescues the world, yet though he bestows both wealth and virtue, he does not rely upon his own efforts. Even with great achievements, he does not dwell in their rewards, for his intent lies in humbly withdrawing himself, pushing virtue/power outward, concealing his brilliance and obscuring his traces, not wishing to display his own wisdom and ability.

Among all things in the world, none are as soft and weak as water, **Annotation:** Water is soft and weak—when contained, it is round; when shaped, it is square. If dammed, it stops; if released, it flows.

**Annotation:** All things in the world differ in their nature, yet none are as soft and weak as water. By this, it illustrates the Dao’s gentle and yielding nature, its adaptability and capacity to nurture all things, like water permeating and nourishing everything.

Yet when it comes to overcoming hardness and strength, none can surpass it, **Annotation:** Water can erode mountains and topple ridges; it can wear down iron and dissolve bronze. Nothing can overcome water and achieve success.

**Annotation:** Though water is soft and weak, its utility is boundless. It attacks and overcomes hardness and strength—none are more effective than it. This is because the Dao breaks through obstacles without force; its meaning is likewise.

**Annotation:** Water is the inner essence, that is, the saliva in one’s mouth. When one rinses and swallows it, it can attack evil and dispel malice, penetrating to the marrow. Like the *qū bò* (麴蘗) that dissolves rice and medicinal herbs, nothing can surpass its efficacy.

**Annotation:** In overcoming hardness and strength, nothing can replace water. **Annotation:** There is no substitute for water in overcoming hardness and strength.」

The Sovereign replied: *"To attack strength with hardness, both hardnesses are damaged; to govern strength with softness, the strong are damaged while the soft remain intact. Thus, in overcoming hardness and strength, nothing can replace water."*

**Annotation:** It is said that water can attack hardness—an unaltered principle across all ages. The Way of non-action overcoming existence is an unchanging art through the ages.

Thus, softness overcomes hardness, and weakness overcomes strength.
**Annotation:** Water can extinguish fire; yin can consume yang.
The tongue is soft, the teeth are hard—yet the teeth perish before the tongue.
None in the world understand this,
**Annotation:** Knowing that softness and weakness endure, while hardness and strength are broken.
None can act accordingly.
**Annotation:** They shame humility and modesty, yet crave force and arrogance.

**Annotation:** This refers to how a person, by containing essence and drinking qi, governs the blood and vital forces—common practice yet unrecognized by the masses.

**Annotation:** Since water can attack metal and stone, it is known that softness and weakness overcome hardness and strength—a truth understood by both wise and foolish alike, yet no one in the world follows it.

Thus, the Sage says:
**Annotation:** Referring to serving those below.

Bearing the filth of the realm—this is called the steward of the ancestral altars.
**Annotation:** A ruler who endures the impurities of the realm, like the sea that does not reject the tides, can long preserve the ancestral altars and become the sovereign of the land.
**Annotation (御):** Drawing in the faults of all under heaven—this is bearing the realm’s filth. Calling oneself "lonely and bereft" (孤寡) and "not the grain" (不谷)—this is bearing the realm’s misfortunes. With such virtue, the ancestral altars are served, and thus all under heaven turn to him.

**Annotation (疏):** *Guó* (国) refers to the domain of the state; *gòu* (垢) means disgrace. Those who embody the Dao in the world, humble and yielding, accommodating and compliant, enduring personal humiliation without contending with others, are fit to be stewards of the ancestral altars and shepherd the people.

**Annotation (节解):** The human form’s left spirit (*arcan*) is the ancestral altar (*shè*), the right spirit (*pò*) is the grain altar (*jì*). Governing the vital breath (*yī qì*) makes one the sovereign.

To bear the realm’s misfortunes—this is called the King of all under heaven.
**Annotation:** A ruler who takes upon himself the people’s faults and endures the calamities of misfortune can reign over all under heaven.
**Annotation (节解):** The realm (*guó*) refers to the body. When breath enters the spleen, transforming dregs into essence and renewing the old with the new, nourishing the Five Spirits, it reigns over the viscera.

**Annotation (疏):** *Bùxiáng* (不祥) means "not good." The Sage’s discernment is vast and penetrating, knowing good and evil. If misfortune arises, he blames himself and takes the fault upon himself—this is worthy of ruling all under heaven. In ancient times, when King Tang said, *"I cut down a thousand li to come,"* this was his deed.

Earlier, it was said that the ancestral altars could be entrusted to the feudal lords; now it is said that all under heaven should belong to the Son of Heaven. This is upright speech, though the world may take it as paradox.

---

**Annotation (节解):** The Way speaks of what common folk desire—purity and cleanliness as supreme, fragrance and beauty as highest—yet the Dao takes receiving filth as its master, dwelling in the low to rule. This is said to be contrary to the world.

**Annotation (御):** Though the words appear contrary to common custom, why? The world loves beauty and honor, yet now it is said to receive filth and misfortune—thus it seems contrary. But *"if"* (若) is used to clarify that what is contrary is not fixed opposition: though it seems contrary, it is always in harmony with the Way. Though it appears contrary, it remains true, conforming to the world’s nature while transcending it. Though it seems contrary, it is not truly contrary—this is why *"if"* is added.

Earlier, it was said that the **Way’s virtue** is profound and far-reaching, contrary to things, and then it attains great harmony—this is its meaning.

To reconcile great grievances,
**Annotation (御):** *"To reconcile"* (和) means to harmonize. The greatest grievance against oneself is **emotional desire** (情欲). The ruler seeks to guide the people through speech, harmonizing their desires so that none remain—thus it is said, *"To reconcile great grievances."*

There must be lingering grievances.
**Annotation (御):** When laws are established, traces of governance remain, and these traces become grievances that affect the common people.
**Annotation (疏):** Grievances are **discriminative perceptions** (慧). They are all **dharma** that awaits opposition—whether of existence or non-existence. Though one may discard the extremes of existence and non-existence, attain neither existence nor non-existence, and harmonize the two edges into the **middle path** (中一), one still clings to **emptiness** (玄), failing to penetrate its true essence. Though this is **non-attachment** (无待), it cannot fully escape **attachment** (不待). This is **selective attachment** (待独), not yet **absolute freedom** (独独)—thus lingering oppositions persist.

How can this be considered good?
**Annotation (御):** If lingering grievances remain, it cannot be considered good.
**Annotation (疏):** *"How"* (安) means *"why."* Though one may discard the two extremes, one still clings to the middle—why can it not be fully good?

**Annotation (节解):** This refers to the mouth. When one sings, there must be response. If people practice the Dao, the Dao will come to meet them. When one sings of goodness, the good will respond in harmony—then there will be no lingering grievances. But people of today indulge in **evil thoughts** (邪想). Evil thoughts follow them, and they call upon **evil spirits** (邪鬼). The spirits come to respond, and when one sings of evil, the evil harmonizes—how can such a thing be considered good?

Thus, the Sage holds the **left契** (left契),
**Annotation (御):** The left契 is the **heart** (心). The heart is the **yang viscera** (阳藏), binding with the present realm (境) to establish trust—thus it is called the left契. The Sage knows that establishing guidance will inevitably leave traces, and traces will breed lingering grievances. Therefore, he upholds this heart, making it pure and tranquil, and transforms the people below, so that they have no desires. Without need for punishment or blame, the heart naturally aligns with **non-action** (无为).

**Annotation (疏):** 契 is **trust** (信) and **harmony** (合). Left is **yang** (阳). The Sage’s heart, dwelling in **Dao/Way**, resonates with **wisdom** (智), in perfect accord. Through this, he can make all things harmonize, transcending the myriad faults. Thus, he attains the meaning of **longevity and eternal vision** (久视长生).

To hold the left契 is to not hold yet hold. Like an emperor grasping the left契, by which he governs all affairs, the Sage holds the **perfect契** within the cycle, thus able to guide the myriad multitudes. And when it is said that he does not blame, though he may guide all beings, he forgets their faults. The people are like **sacrificial dogs** (刍狗), and he does not blame them for their shortcomings.

**Annotation (节解):** The left契 is **yang virtue** (阳德). The Sage does not reconcile great grievances but only cultivates his own virtue, never blaming others for their faults. Thus, the virtuous ruler governs through **trust and harmony** (司契), while the unvirtuous ruler governs through **traces and laws** (司彻).
**Annotation (疏):** 司 means **to govern** (主), and 彻 means **traces** (迹). The Sage’s perfect契 does not cling to discriminative words. The unvirtuous ruler, however, remains stuck in the **nets of governance** (筌蹄), unable to fully transcend them. Since traces are not erased, it is said that he governs through **traces** (司彻).

**Annotation (节解):** This refers to people practicing **moral cultivation** (行道德). The **Heavenly Command** (司命) bestows their **fate** (算). If people practice evil, the Command will **kill and destroy their records** (同杀彻其籍)—this is its meaning.

The **Heavenly Dao** has no favorites, always aligning with the good.
**Annotation (疏):** The **natural principle** (天真) of the Dao knows no favoritism. Those of **highest virtue** (上善) naturally harmonize with it—just as before, the Sage held the left契 without blaming others.

**Small realms, few people** (小国寡民).
**Annotation (御):** Though the Sage may govern great realms, he still regards them as small, teaching **frugality** (俭约) and avoiding **extravagance** (奢泰). Though the people are many, they are treated as if few, not daring to burden or exploit them.

**Annotation (疏):** Realm (国) is **domain** (域). The heart dwells in **emptiness** (空)—thus it is said *"small realm."* This is the practice of **Hinayana** (小乘) and **few desires** (寡欲). Instruments (器) are the **weapons of the hundred** (什恶兵器). Though the six **roots** (根) exist, their use is **unexhausted** (不穷). When it is said *"the hundred and the ten"* (什伯), it refers to the greater numbers. *"Not using"* (不用) means **resting the roots** (息诸根), not staining them with the dust of the world.

**Annotation (御):** 什 is **the five** (伍), and 伯 is **the great** (长). This chapter clarifies that people are **naturally harmonious** (含淳和), without seeking or demanding. When there are those whose talents and capacities fit to be **leaders of the five or the ten** (什伍伯长), they are still not **used** (用).

「○**Jiéjiě** says: *"Small realm"* (小国) refers to the **body** (形), *"the hundred and the ten"* (什伯) refers to the **Five Zang Organs** (五藏).
The heart and kidneys are *"the five"* (什), the liver and lungs are *"the great"* (伯).
When one **closes the mouth and cultivates the breath** (闭口行气), then the *"hundred and the ten"* (什伯) **qi** flourishes without **unnecessary use** (无不用).

To make the people **cherish death** (重死)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** If a ruler can **benefit the people and remove their harm** (兴利除害), allowing each to **find their rightful place** (得其所), then the people will **cherish death and hold life dear** (重死而贵生).
And **not relocate far** (不远徙)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** If governance is **not burdensome** (不烦), then the people will **settle contentedly in their occupations** (各安其业).
Thus, they **do not move far** (不远迁徙), leaving their **usual dwellings** (常处).

○**Yù** says: With **few thoughts and few desires** (少思寡欲), they **do not lightly expend their life** (不轻用其生), **devoting themselves to the root** (敦本) without **seeking anything** (无求), so they **do not relocate far** (故不远迁徙).

○**Cù** says: All **actions** (诸行) are **heavy with stillness** (重静), **knowing contentment and abiding by their allotment** (守分), so they **attain their natural lifespan** (得终其天年) and **do not depart from their present realm** (不远遂前境).

Though there are **vessels and conveyances** (舟舆), they **do not ride them** (无所乘之)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** In **purity and non-action** (清净无为), they **do not indulge in elaborate luxuries** (不作繁华) and **do not enjoy travel and recreation** (不好出入游娱).

○**Cù** says: *"Vessels and conveyances"* (舟舆) refer to the **Three Yanas** (三乘教法).
The **boat** (舟) relies on **water** (水) to **ferry across** (济渡)—
This illustrates that **teachings** (教) are like a **boat navigating all beings** (舟航万物), **crossing the ocean of birth and death** (度於生死海).
Thus, the *Xīshēng Jīng* (《西升经》) states:
*"Long abandoned the sea of birth and death"* (久旷生死海),
*"Cutting off the flow of greed and love"* (隔绝贪爱流).
The **carriage** (舆) moves on **land** (陆) with **wheels** (轮转)—
This illustrates that **teachings** (教) can **transform the ordinary into the sacred** (转凡成圣), **achieving insight beyond words** (得理忘言).
Thus, they **dispense with teachings and do not rely on them** (能遣教而不用).

Though there are **armor and weapons** (甲兵), they **do not deploy them** (无所陈之)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** They **harbor no resentment toward the world** (无怨恶於天下).

○**Cù** says: Though one possesses **bodily and mental armor and weapons** (身心兵甲),
In the state of **"sitting and forgetting"** (车体坐忘),
With **external phenomena emptied** (物境既空),
What need is there for **utterance or exposition** (陈说)?
This sentence **reverses and clarifies** the earlier *"the hundred and the ten"* (什伯) **instruments unused** (之器而不用).

To make the people **return to knotting cords** (复结绳) and **employ them** (而用之)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** To **abandon culture and return to simplicity** (弃文反质), **keeping faith without deceit** (信不欺).

○**Yù** says: *"Vessels and conveyances"* (舟舆) are meant for **relocation** (利迁徙),
*"Armor and weapons"* (甲兵) are meant for **waging war** (徇攻战).
Since both are **unwanted** (无欲),
They **do not ride or deploy them** (故无所乘陈).
Returning to **primordial simplicity** (反朴还淳),
They **revert to the use of the Three Sovereigns** (归於三皇结绳之用).

○**Jiéjiě** says: This means that **Dao/Way-qi** (道气) **naturally flows and functions** (自流行),
As **bodily use** (形中之用),
Like the **trustworthiness of knotted cords** (结绳之信).

○**Cù** says: Having **emptied the heart and verified the principle** (虚心证理),
**Discarding teachings and forgetting the raft** (舍教忘筌),
They **resort to knotted cords** (寄结绳),
**Showing their return to the origin** (示其反本).
This sentence **repeats the earlier theme** of *"though there are vessels and conveyances, they do not ride them"* (虽有舟舆无所乘).

**Simple food** (甘其食)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** To **enjoy simple nourishment** (甘其蔬养), **not plundering the people** (不渔食百姓).

○**Cù** says: **Taste is the Dao/Way** (味至道).
**Beautiful attire** (美其服)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** To **adorn simple garments** (美其恶衣), **not valuing the five colors** (不贵五色).

○**Cù** says: **Virtue/power shines through the body** (德莹身).
○**Jiéjiě** says: This means to **find sweetness in the qi of food** (甘食其气),
And **beauty in the Dao/Way of attire** (美服其道).

**Peaceful dwelling** (安其居)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** To **settle in humble thatched homes** (安其茅茨之居),
**Not favoring ornate dwellings** (不好文饰之屋).

○**Cù** says: **All places are peaceful** (所在皆安), **stillness in motion** (动而寂).

**To delight in their customs** (乐其俗)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** To **rejoice in simplicity and authenticity** (乐其质朴之俗),
**Not altering them** (不转移).

○**Cù** says: **Harmonizing with the world** (和光人世),
**Stillness in motion** (寂而动).

○**Jiéjiě** says: This means to **settle the body and delight the spirit** (安形乐神),
**Singing while embracing the belly** (抱腹而歌吟).

**Neighboring realms are visible** (邻国相望), **the sounds of chickens and dogs are heard** (鸡狗之声相闻)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** They are **nearby** (相去近).

○**Cù** says: The **chicken** (鸡) **marks the dawn** (司晨),
The **dog** (狗) **wards off night** (警夜)—
This illustrates that **all teachings** (一切言教)
**Alert to the long night of ignorance** (警长昏之夜),
**Awaken the dawn of wisdom** (思智慧之晨).
This **teaching** (言教) **is close to realizing the One Mind** (近证一心),
Thus it is said that their **sounds are heard** (故言相闻).

**The people do not interact until old age** (民至老不相往来)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** They **lack emotional attachments** (其无情欲).

○**Jiéjiě** says: *"Neighboring realms"* (邻国) are the **two ears** (两耳).
The **two ears** (两耳) **face each other** (相望),
**Preserving their tranquility** (保其安).
They **guard their spirit-qi** (使神气各有所守),
**Not interfering with one another** (不来往相干),
Thus **even in old age, they remain keen** (故至老常能聪明).

○**Cù** says: **Uniting with principle and embodying truth** (会理体真),
**The common is the Dao/Way, the Dao/Way is common** (即俗即道),
**Not coming from the realm of birth and death** (不从生死而来),
Nor **going toward any realm of the Dao/Way** (亦无道境可往).
With **the two domains as one** (二域既一),
They **do not interact** (故不相往来),
And **the two boundaries are without boundary** (二际无际).

**True words are not ornate** (信言不美)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** *"True words"* (信言) are **honest speech** (实言).
*"Not ornate"* (不美) means **simple and unadorned** (朴且质).

○**Yù** says: *"True words"* (信言) are **sacred teachings** (圣教).
**Honest and true words** (信实之言),
**Do not exceed the common** (不出於俗),
Thus they are **not ornate** (故不美).

**Ornate words are not true** (美言不信)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** *"Ornate words"* (美言) are **embellished speech** (滋美之华辞).
*"Not true"* (不信) means **decorative and full of emptiness** (饰伪多空虚).

○**Yù** says: *"Ornate words"* (美言) are **worldly teachings** (世教).
**Sweet and beautiful words** (甘美之言),
**Align with the common** (动合於俗),
Thus they are **not true** (故不信).

○**Cù** says: **Truth** (信) is **reality** (实).
**Ornate** (美) is **superficial and gaudy** (浮艳).
Those of **highest virtue/power** (上德之人),
**Dwelling in true emptiness** (冥真契道),
Their **uttered teachings** (所说言教)
Are **real without embellishment** (实而不华).
**Superficial and gaudy words** (浮艳之言),
Are **novels of the common world** (俗中小说),
**Contradicting ultimate principle** (既乖至理),
Thus they are **not trusted** (所以不信).
Hence, **Zhuangzi** says:
*"A dog does not bark well for being good, nor does a man speak well for being virtuous"* (犬不以善吠为良，人不以善言为贤).

○**Jiéjiě** says: This means that **those who trust in Dao/Way-speech** (信道言者),
**Do not adorn it with worldly matters** (则不美於俗事),
And **those who adorn worldly matters** (美俗事者),
**Do not trust in Dao/Way-speech** (则不信於道言).

**The wise do not seek extensive knowledge** (知者不博)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** *"The wise"* (知者) refer to **those who understand the Dao/Way** (知道之士).
*"Not extensive"* (不博) means **abiding in the One** (守一元).

**The extensive do not understand** (博者不知)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** *"The extensive"* (博者) refers to **those with many experiences** (多见闻).
*"Do not understand"* (不知) means **losing the true essence** (失真要).

○**Yù** says: *"The wise"* (知者) are **those who have awakened insight** (了悟).
*"The extensive"* (博者) are **those with much hearing** (多闻).

○**Cù** says: Those who **understand the Dao/Way** (知道之人),
**Forget words and abandon learning** (忘言绝学),
Thus they **do not seek extensive knowledge** (所以不博).
**Scholars of broad learning** (博学之士),
**Are ensnared by names and teachings** (耽滞名教),
Thus they **do not understand** (所以不知).
Hence, the *Xīshēng Jīng* (《西升经》) states:
*"Worldly people only know of heaven and earth, the myriad things,
But do not know their own source of birth"* (世人徒知有天地万物，不知己之所由生),
This is **great ignorance** (是大不知).
Again it states:
*"If you know the One, all matters are complete"* (子能知一，万事毕).
**Zhuangzi** says:
*"Culture extinguishes substance, extensive knowledge drowns the heart"* (文灭质，博溺心).

**The good do not argue** (善者不辩)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** *"The good"* (善者) **cultivate themselves with the Dao/Way** (以道修身),
*"Do not argue"* (不辩) means **not adorned with rhetoric** (不文彩).

**The argumentative are not good** (辩者不善)—
**Annotation (Zhù):** *"The argumentative"* (辩者) refer to **those skilled in speech** (巧言).
*"Not good"* (不善) means **the harm of verbal governance** (舌政患).

**Earth has jade** (土有玉), **digging its mountains** (掘其山),
**Water has pearls** (水有珠), **muddying its springs** (浊其泉).
**Excessive speech destroys the self** (辩口多言亡其身).

「○**Commentary (Shū):** *辫 (biàn)*, **to distinguish** (bié yě).
Those who truly grasp the **Dao/Way** (至道 zhìdào) **do not argue** (故无可分别 yě), for they **point and scold at Heaven and Earth** (指骂天地 zhǐmà tiāndì) as if nothing could be distinguished.

Moreover, **Confucians and Mohists** (儒墨 rúmò) **cling to right and wrong** (是非 shìfēi), for **they stubbornly distinguish** (坚执分别 jiānzhí biéyì) **because they have yet to attain the Dao/Way of virtue** (良由未证善道 yìng yóu wèi zhèng shàndào yě).

**The Sage does not hoard** (圣人不积),
**Commentary (Shū):** The **Sage accumulates virtue** (积德 jīdé) **but not wealth** (不积财 bù jīcái). With **virtue, they teach the ignorant** (有德以教愚 yǒudé yǐ jiāoyú), and with **wealth, they relieve the poor** (有财以施贫 yǒucái yǐ shīpín).

**Master Yuè says:** Hoarding (积 jī) refers to **clinging stubbornly to doctrines and teachings** (执滞言教 zhīzhì yánjiào), **amassing knowledge or possessions** (有所积聚 yǒusuǒ jījù).
The **Sage comprehends speech and transcends speech** (了言忘言 liǎoyán wàngyán), **awakens to teachings and discards teachings** (悟教遣教 wùjiào qiǎnjiào). **They hold no fixed distinctions** (一无执滞 yīwú zhīzhì), thus it is said they **do not hoard** (故云不积 yě).

**Commentary (Shū):** *积 (jī)*, **to amass** (jù yě).
The **Sage possesses wealth to benefit beings** (有财惠物 yǒucái huìwù) and **virtue to teach people** (有德教人 yǒudé jiàorén), yet they **do not hoard** (故不积 yě).
**The more they give to others, the more they possess** (既以为人己愈有 jì yǐ wèirén jǐ yùyǒu), **the more they share with others, the more they have** (既以与人己愈多 jì yǐ yǔrén jǐ yùduō).
**Commentary (Zhù):** When they **bestow wealth and practice almsgiving** (既以财贿布施与人 jì yǐ cāihuì bùshī yǔrén), their **wealth grows ever more abundant** (而财益多 ér cái yìduō), **like the radiance of the sun and moon** (如日月之光 rú rìyuè zhī guāng), **endless without depletion** (无有尽时 wúyǒu jìnshí yě).

**Commentary (Shū):** *既 (jì)*, **to exhaust** (jìn yě).
*为 (wèi)*, **to transform and bestow** (施化 shīhuà yě).
Though they **exhaust their own virtue and power** (己所有道德 jǐ yǒudé) **to teach others** (尽持教人 jìnhú jiàorén), their **heart is without pettiness or fear** (心无鄙怯 xīnwú bǐqiè), and their **virtue becomes ever more sublime and distant** (德弥高远 dé mí gāoyuǎn). Thus it is said they **possess all the more** (故言愈有 yě), for this is **virtue in action** (此德施 shì déshī).
Though they **exhaust their own treasures** (己所有财宝 jǐ yǒuzāng) **to scatter them in charity** (尽持施散 jìnhú shīsàn), the **fruits of their merit grow ever more abundant** (而果报弥多 ér guǒbào míduō), hence it is said they **have all the more** (故云愈多 yě). This is **wealth in giving** (此财施 shì cāishī).

The **Dao/Way of Heaven** (天之道 tiān zhīdào) **benefits without harming** (利而不害 lì ér bùhài);
**Commentary (Zhù):** Heaven **nurtures all things** (生万物 shēng wànwù), **loving and fostering their growth** (爱育之 àiyù zhī), **allowing them to flourish without injury** (令长大无所伤害其生 lìng zhǎngdà wúsuǒ shānghài qí shēng yě).

**Commentary (Shū):** The **natural Dao/Way of Heaven** (天然之道 tiānrán zhīdào) **gives rise to all things** (生成万物 shēngchéng wànwù), **its benefits vast and manifold** (利益弘多 lìyì hóngduō), yet **it causes no loss or harm** (有何损害 yǒuhé sǔnhài).

The **Dao/Way of the Sage** (圣人之道 shèngrén zhīdào) **acts without striving** (为而不争 wèi ér bùzhēng);
**Commentary (Zhù):** The **Sage follows Heaven’s Dao/Way** (法天 fǎtiān), **acting without contrivance** (无所施为 wúsuǒ shīwéi), **transforming all things to their natural fulfillment** (化成事就 huàchéng shìjiù). They **do not contend with the world for fame or gain** (不与天下争功名 bùyǔ tiānxià zhēng gōngmíng), thus **their sagehood remains whole** (故能全其圣功 yě).

**Commentary (Shū):** The **Sage’s highest virtue** (上德 shàngdé) **is aligned with the Dao/Way’s boundless flow** (法道虚通 fǎdào xūtōng), **their actions pure and transformative** (施为至教 shīwéi zhìjiào). Their **teachings permeate all beings** (化被群品 huàbèi qúnpǐn), **they nurture themselves with humility** (谦以自牧 qiān yǐ zìmù), **yet they do not claim credit** (成功不居 chénggōng bùjū). **They attribute their achievements to the things themselves** (推功於物 tuīgōng yú wù), thus it is said they **do not contend** (故云不争 yě).

**Master Zhāng says:** Those who speak of **having carriages and boats yet no means to ride or sail** (有舟舆无所乘之 yǒuzhōuyú wúsuǒ chéngzhī), **are like the world of non-action** (山无蹊跶 shānmí xītì), where **marshes lack bridges or paths** (泽无舟梁 zémí zhōuliáng). **They sever all coming and going** (本绝往来 běn jué wǎnglái), **so what need have they for conveyance or use** (何所乘用 hésuǒ chéngyòng).

**Again it states:** Though they **possess arms and armor**, they **have no place to deploy them** (无所陈之 wúsuǒ chénzhī).

**Master Gù says:** Arms **are used to punish rebels** (讨逆 tǎonì), and **martial might is employed to quell disorder** (静乱 jìngluàn). **Since the Sage practices the Dao/Way**, the people **turn to virtue** (下乃好德 xiànǎi hàodé), and **all naturally conform to transformation** (自然从化 zìrán cónghuà). **Why then would there be any need to deploy arms** (何事陈兵 héshì chénbīng)?

**End of Volume Eight of the *Zhēnshí* (真经) Commentary on *Dàodé* (道德)**.»
